Small rock crystal head depicting a bearded male figure, perhaps a king Early Dynastic IIIb-Akkadian, Rock crystal, 6,6 x 3,8 cm



“ It was cultural evolution that began, at that time, to mark the rhythm of genetic evolution. Two million years before, Homo Habilis had extrapolated from nature instruments foreign to its structure, choppers, to be used for both prey and predators. Then came an exceptional technological development, agriculture and its accompanying phenomena: sedentariness, urbanism, newly organised social and economic relationships, demographic development, and centralised authority. It was humanity’s great road to Damascus ”.
Giancarlo Ligabue

Inti Ligabue
Giancarlo Ligabue Fondation President
The Revolution in a Sign In my decades of dialogue with the works of art in the Ligabue
collection, one subject seemed to remain suspended – that of the tablets and seals which, simplifying for the sake of clarity, we will call Mesopotamian.
The little stone cylinders bear - along with figures of persons, animals, deities, and vegetation - messages that are brief and apparently simple, like the twitters we use today; while the clay tablets, with their labyrinthine wedges, continue to convey messages to men and gods.
I have frequently travelled with my father Giancarlo, who formed this collection decades ago, over the paths indicated by those signs.
And each time, first orienting myself, then losing my way again; attempting to understand the idea contained in those messages, transmitted in such original manner. There were pictographic symbols, numbers, motifs of celestial gods and of inferno in that age-old world.
Never before had I fully understood the true and immense human value, the amazing cultural import and the modernity of that most ancient world.
In working with the ‘Giancarlo Ligabue Foundation’ – to make visible to the public hundreds of the seals and tablets in the Collection – my thoughts returned to the first times I had viewed those imprints on clay and stone; and I saw with other eyes the messages that these objects continue to bring with them.
Those voices still locked within them belonged to people and populations that we might call ‘our contemporaries’, insofar as they present stories, symbols and gestures of a daily life still practiced and elaborated today: lists that sanction the first property rights, medical prescriptions to ease the pain of childbirth, homage paid to the deities and their intercession, contracts for purchasing animals, or tools for constructing buildings.
Mesopotamia (almost in parallel with Egypt and – at a great geographical and in part historical distance – with China, Mesoamerica and South America) has enriched the human adventure by the invention of writing.
Writing or not writing was not a chance occurrence.
My father used to explain this phenomenon to me by saying, ‘It was cultural evolution that began, at that time, to mark the rhythm of genetic evolution. Two million years before, Homo Habilis had extrapolated from nature instruments foreign to its structure, “choppers’, to be used for both prey and predators. Then came an exceptional technological development, agriculture and its accompanying



phenomena: sedentariness, urbanism, newly organised social and economic relationships, demographic development, and centralised authority.”
Today I can fully comprehend how this human revolution, that of the written word, was not only the child of an intellectual and scientific process, and of a need - but also a choice of identity and culture that has produced order, civilisation and the certainty of rights, combining social relationships in permanent and visible manner.
Our own existence lies already in the world of the Land between Two Rivers.
It is in that cultural dimension that the Western World was born. In that world and in those times, the numbers and symbols that would develop into the alphabet were ‘invented’.
Again in the words of Giancarlo, “that time, where thoughts became drawings, then signs and symbols, was humanity’s great road to Damascus.”
We still stand amazed before these objects, created over four thousand years ago, which continue to launch messages of singular, exalting beauty.
As the ‘Giancarlo Ligabue Foundation’ we have decided to bring back to life this enormous Mesopotamian library, which had remained dormant for over 40 centuries.
I have had no doubt as to who should serve as guide among those shelves, and interpreter of those labyrinthine studies conducted on books, objects, narratives, journeys, and conversations, retracing and enriching a path already taken thirty years ago with the book ‘Prima dell’alfabeto’ [Before the Alphabet].
Frederick Mario Fales, curator of the exhibition and author of this catalogue – whom I thank for his patient diligence, his keen, discerning discourse and profoundly scientific outlook - has renewed the encounter with many objects already familiar to him. And supplementing them with others, he has given us today, in collaboration with Roswitha Del Fabbro, a new and priceless testimonial to the world of Mesopotamia.
I would like to thank him even more from the human viewpoint, for having brought to fruition a cultural journey begun decades before with my father Giancarlo; a journey in which I share and am committed to divulging.
In the spirit of the first nineteenth-century ‘readers’ of those mysterious languages remained buried for millennia, this exhibition – held at the Biblioteca di Palazzo Loredan, seat of the Istituto Veneto delle
Scienze, one of our city’s vital fulcrums of knowledge and culture - is also a new cultural challenge for Venice, a city that, on each occasion, delves more deeply into its special role as interpreter of modernity. A city that was destined to be the ‘Capital of Culture’.
Moreover, this exhibition is a fine example of cooperation with various scientific institutes in Italy and abroad. Noteworthy among the many are the Musei Reali di Torino with its director Enrica Pagella, for the crucially important loan of the relief carving of King Sargon II, and the Museo Archeologico di Venezia and its director, Annamaria Larese, for the loan of some of the objects that Austen Henry Layard brought to Venice from Nineveh, the city he discovered, in the nineteenth century.
Lastly, in a note of personal gratitude, I would like to acknowledge my appreciation of Massimo Casarin and Adriano Favaro, for the great commitment they have dedicated to the cultural projects of the Foundation, and for their ardent enthusiasm in paying homage to the memory of a friend.



instantly. To address the breadth and complexity of the seals, unpublished until now, in view of the exhibition we both chose to involve Dr. Roswitha Del Fabbro, an archaeologist from Udine/ Berlin,with her invaluable experience of excavations in Syria, Turkey, and Kurdistan. We also decided to give the exhibition the same name as the previous work, Before the Alphabet. Under the wise guidance of the editor Adriano Favaro, I was given great freedom to design the descriptive system of the catalogue according to interwoven and innovative perspectives, without sacrificing the ease of understanding the whole.
So was everything that easy? Not exactly. The aging translator of cuneiform tablets soon had to realize that the archaeological publishing world was nowadays undergoing a new, gigantic boom due to IT tools; that the speed of implementation was a diktat in this world; and that texts and illustrations could be subjected to changes, shifts, and multiple revisions leading to the most appropriate result for a catalogue such as the one we had designed. However, thanks to the tireless activity of study, classification, and analysis by Roswitha Del Fabbro - who has constantly accompanied and supported me –, to the preliminary archival work by Dr. Marta Dal Martello, to the constant creative suggestions on editorial design by Adriano Favaro, to Federico Dei Rossi for the technical and graphic aspects, along with the Ubis Three team, to Lucia Berti for her precious liason work with the Foundation, and, last but not least, to the amicably perceptive and consistently strong guidance of Dr. Inti Ligabue, the President of the Foundation, the result, in my view, has exceeded all expectations.
So I offer a sincere and warm thanks to all; although we are now physically distant from Mesopotamia, the Land of the Twin Rivers can relive in the pages that follow, primarily thanks to the uniqueness, variety, and beauty of the antiquities collection of the Ligabue Foundation, and secondly, thanks go to the prodigious team work that led to the creation of this catalogue and to the exhibition that this catalogue reflects.
And finally, I am - in unison with the whole 'team' just mentioned - extremely grateful to the Presidency of the Istituto Veneto di Scienze, Lettere ed Arti, the academy of which I am honoured to be a member since 1998, for having generously agreed to host, in its highly cultured rooms of noble simplicity at Palazzo Loredan, the exhibition of which this catalogue will be the mirror, yes, but above all its projection: not only concretely as regards the wonderful three-dimensional antiquities on display, but also beyond, in the computer spheres of virtual and expanded reality, as additional assistance for their full enjoyment and first-hand perusal.
Today, the Near East has become surprisingly distant from us: for Realpolitik, for the human and cultural relations between the peoples of the Mediterranean area, as well as for me personally. Nowadays it only on television that I ever see the street signs of the places along the Upper Tigris and the Upper Euphrates where I had directed excavations, where I had found housing and machinery for rent, where I had conversed with people every day. Mosul, Jerablus, Kobanî: my memory evokes lively and cheerful marketplaces, cordial visits in people's homes, and endless negotiations – one tea after another - to obtain workers for the archaeological activities. At the time, we deluded ourselves that our stressful career paths in Italy would have benefitted from a healthy break in routine with the annual 2-3 months of physical work in Mesopotamia, in contact with the local population who placidly accepted the 'different' people who had come by airplane from the West, with their ever new instruments and their obsessions about excavation techniques...Today, I try searching in vain among the faces on TV for those I once knew amidst the refugees who are fleeing on foot in family groups, I no longer recognize their habits in the myriad of smartphones accompanying their cardboard suitcases, and I do not understand exactly how that world, kept for so long in a fatalistic sleep by iconic and paternalistic dictators, so quickly became an inferno of everyone against everyone, with missile strikes, mass conversions, and human shields. The Near East now seems to have turned into a remote planet, senseless and unfathomable, like Tlön and Orbis Tertius imagined by Jorge Luis Borges.
And us? Banned from our excavation sites, today the destinations of profit-greedy predators, we have returned to observing ancient Mesopotamia only from our libraries, although enriched by the work of interpreting its history and culture from the many impressions of its territory - vegetation, roads and distances, climates - that those decades 'in the field' have left in our mind's eye. And thus it came to pass that I walked once more along the paving stones of the Venetian streets leading to the Erizzo Palace, where Giancarlo Ligabue in the Eighties had generously opened up for me his magnificent collection of cuneiform tablets, allowing me to publish them in the form of a richly illustrated book, entitled Before the Alphabet. Alas, Giancarlo was no more the person awaiting me, but his son Inti, who is just as affectionate, passionate, and determined, and ready with an idea of great novelty and charm: to present his father's collection of Mesopotamian antiquities in an exhibition in Venice, also including the collection of cylinder seals, invaluable as to their historical and artistic value. The offer was irresistible: I agreed, gratefully and
Frederick Mario Fales
Emeritus - PARTeN Mission, Iraq Kurdistan (University of Udine) Veneto Institute for Sciences, Letters, and Arts – full member



The exhibition Before the Alphabet, presented by the Istituto Veneto di Scienze, Lettere ed Arti, is a happy occasion for different reasons. Obviously it is a pleasure to have played a role in the first initiative addressed to a broad audience by a young foundation that is taking its important initial steps with a highly cultural and refined commitment. When we are faced with new undertakings of this kind, they are always measured with likewise highly positive events, but especially so in the periods for cultural initiatives that sometimes seem to not enjoy favorable conjunctures. If this is in itself cause for satisfaction, it is all the more so in remembering that Giancarlo Ligabue, after whom the new Foundation is named, was a member of our Institute since 1985.
It will be necessary then to remember that the new Foundation, despite its youth, came from afar, previously having had the experience of the Ligabue Research Centre, founded in 1978-1979. It is precisely this 'young maturity' that has enabled the Foundation to achieve the highest level of its first major public appearance with an exhibition of special interest. The title indicates a path, in some ways quite unusual, that has come to propose a series of research studies and materials that cover a broad time span of almost three thousand years following the thread of writing common to an area such as that of Mesopotamia, so rich with centuries of extraordinary civilization.
The exhibition, whose catalogue has been curated by our member Frederick Mario Fales, presents nearly 200 pieces including cuneiform tablets, cylinder seals, and other works from ancient Mesopotamia, which Giancarlo Ligabue collected at a time when slow and dedicated collection was an important value for specialists in the field. Precisely today, when the Middle East is subject to cultural destruction by acts of war, revising the masterly collection of antiquities Ligabue carried out for decades with wisdom and patience to document the written communication and legal endorsement through the finely carved seals, along with other valuable Mesopotamian artworks, can be an effective balm for humanists and scientists, as well as being capable of attracting the interest of children and young people.
Starting with the first writings and its antecedents, from the Sumerians and the cuneiform system we reach the stage in which those amazing ancient testimonies become a substantial part of modern culture, beginning with the pioneering writings such as those in the seventeenth century by Pietro Della Valle, up to the discovery in 1975 of Ebla, with its language and studies that continue to be effected. The field of investigation is broad and the materials illustrating it have an undoubted charm. For these reasons, the Istituto Veneto di Scienze, Lettere ed Arti is pleased that the exhibition will take place in the halls of its headquarters in Palazzo Loredan.
Seeing a child's hand holding a pencil that is moving for the first time on a sheet of paper in search of a kind of writing, is like watching dancing, flying, or running; which is then followed by moments of rest, reflection, and almost even anxiety.
If I have to imagine a young scribe struggling with cuneiform writing and other expressions that have been etched for thousands of years on clay and stones, I think of a tiny being who is creating. Imitating and creating, for that breath by the nerves in a hand are describing a reality of an existence that someone else will have to reinterpret.
This catalogue, and the exhibition it illustrates, was created with the same awe and with the desire to listen to the stories belonging to a child, or a teenager. These pages that Frederick Mario Fales and Roswitha Del Fabbro have wisely and patiently filled - along with Stefano de Martino, Paolo Matthiae, Pier Giorgio Odifreddi, and David I. Owen - are like a map, or watermark, of our Western feeling towards an understanding and an outlook on life.
These documents on clay or semi-precious stones, in fact, act as the voices of poets who also recorded what they have seen, the epics of their myths and heroes;togetherwiththelistsofgoodsthatwillendupondiningtables,ofmaterials forbuilding a house, and amulets forinvoking the gods' benevolence. There had been other times that I had travelled along the route of this map that 'Before the Alphabet' now proposes in a new and modern vision. I have done so with Giancarlo Ligabue who every once in a while would share a journey with a few friends that delved into ancient times: he knew how to interpret signs and symbols that seemed - in his narratives – to have been awakened from their millennial torpors to return to dialogue with us humans.
This catalogue – which the Giancarlo Ligabue Foundation has printed with Giunti Publishers, to whom we are very grateful for their intelligent collaboration - wants to revive those moments: a rediscovery of civilizations and cultures that return to having full contact with their heirs. At last, civilization is equipped with new words that historians and archaeologists have been able to discover and offer us. Those who have the time and the desire to come to know about a distant world that existed more than five thousand years ago, will be surprised because they will learn, quite vividly, of the matrix of events that they already know about, they will explore and envision those worlds that were recounted in the Bible or Greek mythology. Thinking back to those historical roots also means understanding the initial forms of those cultures that have passed through our world, developing themselves, as the great French historian Jean Bottéro explained in his essay 'The Birth of the West', reminding us that the texts of the Mesopotamian world contain “the first serious draft of that which, recovered, amplified, furthered, and organized later on by the Greek thinkers, will become the 'scientific spirit'; the first draft of the very sort of science and reasoning which still we hold dear”.
Gherardo Ortalli
President of the Istituto Veneto di Scienze, Lettere ed Arti (Veneto Institute for Sciences, Letters, and Arts)
Adriano Favaro
Editor of the catalogue

and ending in the Arabian-Persian Gulf - the aridity is extreme, with actual risks of desertification. Remedying these risks is the annual regime of the two rivers, which have courses in a north-south direction that are more or less parallel and with comparable water supplies. With the springtime melting of snow in the high mountains of Anatolia from which both the Euphrates and the Tigris originate, the water volume increases several scores of times compared to that in the hot season, and in their flow, the two rivers convey a mass of muddy sediment and mineral content. But whereas in the hilly area of north-cenral Mesopotamia, the rivers' precipitous descent has excavated over time deep gorges and narrow canyons in the limestone terrain, the slope becomes minimal (less than 20 meters above sea level) in the last 500 km of the alluvial valley. The consequence of this state of things is that, when the two streams are at full height, they excavate mighty natural levees in the land they flow through in this southern area; the land that collapsed from the levees themselves then makes the riverbed itself rise, so that the rivers flow virtually at the same height as the banks, thereby rather easily leading to a flooding of the surrounding lands. In fact, every three to four years, an extensive area of "high water" is created all around the river basins in the southern sector for the entire duration of the flood period (March-May), and the fertile river silt spreads for many square kilometers on flat clay soil. Basically, if the Greek historian Herodotus could say that "Egypt is the Nile's gift", it could equally be said with good reason that the Mesopotamian valley was actually created by the flow of the twin Rivers and their alluvial flooding during tens of thousands of years, before rushing (now merged into a single course, the Shatt el-Arab), through a marshy area and delta, into the Arabian-Persian Gulf. Therefore, the annual flooding of the two rivers was the essential premise and the main cause of the great development of agriculture (especially cereals and date palms) and of livestock breeding (mainly
Where and when: Mesopotamia
Frederick Mario Fales
The name Mesopotamia, or "land between the two rivers", is Greek and dates back to the historian Arrian (II cent. A.D.); arising possibly along the lines of antecedents in the Aramaic language, the term refers to the entire area between the river basins of the two major rivers, the Euphrates (to the west) and the Tigris (east). The region, which geologically is a tectonic basin created by contact between the Arabian shield and the Asian landmass and then filled by huge amounts of river sediments carried by the two rivers themselves, is bounded on the north by the Taurus and Anti-Taurus mountain ranges, on the south by the Arabian-Persian Gulf into which the two rivers flow, on the west by the low rise of the vast rocky desert Shamiya (or Syro-Arabian desert) and on the east by the many parallel ranges running northwest-southeast of the Zagros mountains, which ultimately end with a flat deep-set area called Khuzistan. Therefore, ancient Mesopotamia corresponds to today's political realities of the entirety of Iraq and Iraqi Kurdistan, as well as northeastern Syria and to a lesser extent in southern Turkey, western Iran and Kuwait. The inhabitants of Mesopotamia designated this region especially with reference to two territories of the oldest historical period (2700-2000 BC), when the country of Sumer - in the southern part of the alluvial valley created by the two rivers – was comparable to that of Akkad, to the north. In this sense, Mesopotamia offers the oldest example of a cultural region recognized and consciously defined by the local culture itself. The double name "Sumer and Akkad" remained in the tradition for millennia to indicate Mesopotamia as a whole and its fundamental unity of language and culture, even when the names of the principal states had changed by then: in the south, ever since 1900 BC there was Babylonia, with its homonymous religious and political capital Babylon on the Euphrates River, while in the north, in the same era Assyria arose, centered in the religious and political capital of Assur and subsequently in other political centers (including Nineveh) on the Tigris River. Assyrian-Babylonian civilization, as a set of common cultural traditions in their political, religious, and social reflexes, was ruled continuously by locally-born kings until the sixth century BC; then it still survived as a simple linguistic-cultural whole under foreign rulers (Persians, Seleucids, Romans) until disappearing for good in the first century. A.D.
Physical features
Although with obvious differences due to a North-South extension of almost 2,500 km, Mesopotamia as a whole has an arid continental variant of the Mediterranean climate, with winter rainfall between scarce and average and long warm and dry seasons (from March to November). In the northern areas – of piedmont, hills, and steppes – of Assyria, the presence of regular winter rainfall (and even snow) can guarantee a medium to high supply of rainwater, albeit with a fair degree of variation from one year to another. Instead, in the southern half of the area - the real flood plain covering approximately 700 km

sheep and goats) which made southern Mesopotamia an area that was prosperous and famous in antiquity, with well-developed cities and powerful states in times of both peace and war. This once again calls to mind Herodotus, who considered the Babylonian cereal farming to be a product of exceptional quality and yield. However, it will be necessary to take into account different physical-geographical problems that could be caused by the very flooding; and that only an intense and constant work by humans in defense of and for the development of the environment could hold it in check. To begin with, in spite of the common area of origin and the direction of flow, the two rivers have very different hydraulic systems. After emerging from the mountains of central Turkey, the Euphrates runs more slowly and meanders broadly across the arid steppes of Syria, joined by only two northern tributaries and losing a large proportion of its volume through evaporation. Instead, the Tigris, rising in the Taurus Mountains (Eastern Turkey), has a steeper, straighter course, receives additional water contributions from various tributaries originating in the Zagros Mountains and flows into climatically cooler areas, probably having a dense forest cover in ancient times. This very difference in regime of the two rivers has an effect on the timing of the flooding, with uncoordinated full riverbeds: the Tigris is the first to flood Lower Mesopotamia, because of its more rapid and impetuous flow, while the Euphrates can flood several weeks later. But in any case, it must be noted that the flooding takes place in spring, therefore too late to benefit crops and, in fact, precisely in the phase in which excessive water can damage the summer crops (March-September). Due to the flooding, a large part of human labor was therefore involved in controlling the water and its distribution for agricultural use in the following months. For example, on the main course of the rivers, it was essential to prevent or reduce the formation of meanders and spontaneous branching. If today the site of the great city of Akkad is not to be archaeologically retrieved, this could be due to a shifting of the bed of one of the two rivers - perhaps the Tigris - which caused its erosion and then obliteration over the millennia (Wall-Romana 1990). Moreover, the increasing heat which followed the spring floods
threatened to produce a rapid evaporation of the wet layer left on the countryside: to offset any harmful drying up (with the formation of a "veil" of salt on the surface of the ground), the water thus required means of transport , with the creation of canals of varying scope and length, catch and drainage basins, and a system of dams or locks for the progressive outflow, using the slight gradient of the plain (cap. 1, cap. 3). Thus water could be brought down to individual fields, which were often elongated and divided into deep furrows: once the percolation of water into the ground was over, seed was scattered regularly into these furrows by a seeder plow (apin in Sumerian texts, epinnu in Akkadian), using animal traction (cap. 148). The device allowed saving considerable labor, because by means of a funnel, the seeds fell directly into the furrow freshly dug by the tool, in time for the winter crop (from October until spring). All these works of macro- and micro-hydraulic engineering, which involved accurate excavations and constant efficiency controls, were carried out using the raw material found in the alluvial valley: the clay soil itself with which the Euphrates and the Tigris had been filled over millennia. Instead, massive stones, completely absent in the alluvial valley and only obtainable through long-distance trade, could not be used; and in place of flint, both expensive and hard to fashion, the ancient Mesopotamian people even came to even make their own agricultural scythes out of clay, until the diffusion of metals in a later era. As for wood, the only thing was to make use of the abundant and sturdy reeds, (for fences, roofing, baskets, mats, and various uses, even boats and huts; cap. 4; cap. 6; cap. 5; cap. 2), of the tree species growing beside the water (willows, poplars, etc.) and of the tall, sturdy date palms, full of useful parts for building (trunk and branches, bark, leaves, etc.) and as food. (cap. 10, cap. 7). Any other kind of wood - and especially the precious trunks of tall trees (spruce, cypress, cedar, pine, etc.) to be used for the beams of the largest buildings – had to be imported (or directly taken) from the outer regions and brought by overland or river transport (cap. 8). Thanks to extensive work undertaken by the individual communities or in collaboration for the management of flood waters and fertile silt, the whole plain of Lower Mesopotamia came to be covered by a

dense network of canals, which extended water supply to the many rural villages surrounding the cities, thus ensuring regular harvests year after year, and with them, a range of resources that were essential to their agro-pastoral livelihoods. And this agricultural hinterland, sufficiently prosperous in itself despite high labor costs, laid the economic and social foundations for the formation of the city and the state during the fourth millennium BC, with a revolutionary leap forward. But Mesopotamian man was always alert with regard to the dangers posed by the particular nature of the Land between the twin Rivers. Thus, in the mythical Babylonian poem Atrahasis, a famine sent by the god Enki causes terrible consequences in a short time for the people of the southern plain, including lack of flooding, crop failure, and widespread salinization of agricultural land:
« The flood did not [rise] from the depths. The womb of earth did not bear, Plant life did not come forth. People were not seen about, The black fields whitened, The broad plain was filled up with salts »
Irrigation by means of canals was also carried out in stretches, and with many more technical difficulties, in areas close to rivers within the current countries of Syria and Iraq, where people struggled to wrest farm
land from the surrounding steppe. But on the other hand the fringe areas far from the rivers and from the Mediterranean Coast of the so-called Fertile Crescent, including the northern part of the steppe between the Twin Rivers (the area known as Jezirah in Arab, "the island") and the adjacent areas of northwest Syria, as well as the foothills of the northeastern Mesopotamia, practiced dry farming instead, which used only the rainfall for the cultivation of cereals and horticultural products (olives, pistachio nuts, legumes), also allowing viticulture in the highest areas. The minimum requirement for a successful harvest of barley is 200 millimeters of annual rainfall. However, the largest settlements are located mainly within areas that get at least 300 mm of rainfall. In essence, for long millennia prior to the recent industrial era, the agricultural system of Mesopotamia suffered structurally from the extensive rainfall fluctuations that could characterize climatically different years, and the ancient texts very often tell of entire regions marred by famine, which forced farmers to indebtedness or, alternatively, to abandon their fields and houses, and undertake a nomadic lifestyle.
A “bird's-eye” view of ancient Mesopotamia (ca. 3000-300 BC)
The proto-urban society that, around 3200 BC, produced the first form of writing in Mesopotamia, was already set up as a social and economic whole of remarkable structure and development, as we know from archaeological data. It brought to fulfilment a wide range of technical and social transformations that developed during the Neolithic period (VII-IV millennium): from the first results of irrigated agriculture, to the domestication of many animal species for domestic use, to the creation of craftsmanship for many different practical and ceremonial purposes, to the formation of the village as a site for community identity and as the nucleus of the working and cultural collectivity, with its small houses with a simple tripartite floor plan - in rectangular adobe bricks of clay mixed with straw - arranged according to a mutual protection scheme. The first and most important city in this period was Uruk, located on the Lower Euphrates, which extended over an area of 250 hectares and could accommodate up to 50,000 residents. It was organized into two official quarters at its center: to the west was the area of the so-called "White Temple", to the east lay what will be later called "Eanna", sepa
4
Frustoconical cup with cattle and hut
A fragment from a frustoconical cup with embossed depiction: a bull (behind which appears an ear of corn) in front of a calf coming out of a hut made of reeds, identical to a traditional mudhīf characteristic of the swamps in what is southern Iraq today.
Late Uruk/Jemdet Nasr Stone 13.9 x 20.5 x 1 cm
In the marshes of southern Mesopotamia, there were impressive structures built with reeds, which are frequently represented on seals and other art forms, such as the stone vessels. Their modern counterparts are called mudhīf, typical of the southern Iraq. These are large collective buildings, used by guests or as venues for weddings, funerals, etc. The larger reeds are tied together to form columns and then bent to create arches, which constitute the load bearing structure.

- Shuruppak). Socially, the city-states would appear to have developed from the oldest tribal communities, to which a political structure was superimposed; a certain sense of "clan" would thus have remained rooted in the population, although already subservient to functions imposed by the state. This would also explain why, at the religious level, each of these states “belonged” to a local god, who resided in the main temple (cap. 9); through its representatives, the priests, the deity appointed a ruler for the management of public affairs, especially for the control of the subsistence agriculture and the boundaries with other territories. In essence, the positions of king or supreme priest likewise appear to have arisen alike from the elite of a landed aristocracy of patriarchal foundations. Moreover, from an economic point of view, the city-states can be seen as "great organizations", which gained resources from the confiscation of agricultural surpluses in the surrounding countryside, and then took care to redistribute such goods to the class of the court specialists. On the whole, one can speak of a phase of generalized development for Mesopotamia in the Early Dynastic period including territorial expansion, increased work and technological subdivision, population growth, and the overall increase in revenue. The most famous cities of this period, from the historical or archaeological point of view, are primarily Kish, which fought against Uruk and challenged Elam on the east. Then we know of Ur, the site of the famous royal tombs, with its rich pottery, carved seals, furniture, musical instruments, and chariots; these tombs attested to the custom, almost unknown elsewhere, of collective burials. There was also Lagash, a land of vast extent (17 cities and 8 secondary settlements, with about 100,000 inhabitants), which under King Ur-Nanshe started a conflict with neighboring Umma over the possession of the rich pastures of the Gu'edenna district, with a first solution achieved through arbitration by Mesilim, king of Kish. As for the kings of Lagash, we know of Eannatum (ca. 2450 BC) who waged war all the way to Mari on the Middle Euphrates and conquered Elam; his famous "Stele of the Vultures" was the first public monument of historical narrative in bas-relief depicting the victory over Umma. Lastly, Urukagina was the king known for his enactment of a group of edicts of social reform, in which - for self-legitimation purposes - the pre-existing negative order was countered with a more "just" one, inaugurated by the current ruler (cap. 114). The text thus deals with the revocation of the former kings' usurpations which were harmful to the city's god;
rated by high walls from the rest of the urban complex. The buildings of Eanna were vast, with very diverse floor plans; innovative construction techniques and elegant décor came to embellish the complex, which brought together many adjacent structures from different periods, often rebuilt or expanded. The Eanna was initially identified merely as a place of worship, but today it tends to be seen as the first expression of a distinct political power, i.e. as the oldest palatial building in Mesopotamia. Therefore, The urban spaces of Uruk suggest all the features of an assemblage centered on administration and on the local economy and commerce, where the population had mostly given over agricultural production to the village outskirts and surrounding rural villages, diversified according to the need for irrigation or the transportation routes for commodities. Uruk is the oldest seat of political power, exercised with heterogeneous instruments (from peaceful annexation to war) and managed through a class of adepts (civil or military) according to an already complex social stratification; it can essentially be seen as the prototype for many later “royal” or capital cities of the Mesopotamian world (Liverani 1986). The subsequent history of Mesopotamia unfolds for over three thousand years; precisely this vast chronological extension makes it very difficult to render concise and understandable summaries. Nevertheless, here below, in the sections divided accordingly for the three millennia BC (13), we will attempt to show how the texts in cuneiform handwriting illuminate ever broader geographical and political horizons for each of the successive chronological phases.
The third millennium BC
The texts following the Late Uruk era, dotted with scattered archives from the pre-dynastic or Pre-Sargonic era (ca. 2700-2300 BC), have a politically fragmented and still rather small geographical horizon: Lower Mesopotamia (from present-day Baghdad to the Persian Gulf) with its many neighboring city-states and their surrounding agricultural areas, while areas further north along the Twin Rivers or to the east, beyond the Tigris appear, at least initially, as mere appendages of this area. In this culturally and religiously unified "land of Sumer," the various citystates formed a political horizon with peer-polity interactions, although individual states could attain temporary hegemonies or preferential groupings (eg. the "Hexapolis" of Uruk - Adab - Nippur - Lagash - Umma

However, Lugalzaggesi was in turn overthrown by a usurper, possibly from his own court, who deliberately bestowed upon himself the significant name of "legitimate king", Sharrum-kinum (Sargon in common usage) and resided in the city of Akkad or Agade, not far from Kish. In accordance with his name, Sargon was able to present himself in his texts as a monarch blessed by the gods and the proper successor of his defeated enemy. With the rise to power of the Akkadian dynasty, of Semitic language (ca. 2330-2150 BC), an imperial structure regarding territorial planning and forms of control came to be created for the first time in Mesopotamia: there was a central core and its external provinces, provided with a unitary scheme of administrative management. The military and economic expansion of the State outside of Mesopotamia by Sargon and then continued by his grandson Naram-Sin – to Susa in Elam, in Upper Mesopotamia on the Tigris (Assur, Nineveh), and in the steppe along the Habur River, even with raids in western Syria, in Ebla and in Arman (modern-day Aleppo) - led to the creation of some permanent nuclei of Akkadian settlers and perhaps also to a first colony of merchants that settled in Susa. The dynasty of Akkad fell around 2150 BC due to the invasion of populations from the north-eastern mountains: the Gutians (defined in Sumerian texts as subhuman barbarians) and perhaps other political-military groups from Iran, such as from Anshan. A brief period followed when local "principalities" appeared to flourish in the southern part, such as the able ensi (ruler) Gudea of Lagash, who inspired a vast legacy of statuary and texts (cap. 11). Shortly after the principality of Gudea came the formation of a new polity of imperial character, less extensive but economically far more intense than the previous one: the third Ur dynasty (ca. 2110-2004 BC). The founder of the dynasty, Ur-Namma, hailed from Uruk (cap. 12). But the actual construction of the Ur empire was the work of his successor
with the control of abuses against the lower classes, the weak, and the underprivileged; and with the exemption from tax obligations and debts decreed for the people of Lagash. In this sense, the actions of Urukagina were the forerunners of similar measures of reform and amnesty equally implemented for purposes of political propaganda by the kings of the second millennium (eg. The “Code of Hammurabi”). However, Urukagina's state suffered a serious military and political setback by the ruler Lugalzaggesi of Umma (mid-century 24th), who destroyed Lagash, devastating the Ningirsu temples. Then Lugalzaggesi went on to occupy Uruk and subjugated many other centers, such as Nippur, Ur, Larsa, Adab, and Eridu, and depending on the case, took on various royal or priestly titles. We also know that he led commercial expeditions to the Mediterranean, bringing back valuable timber, which was rare in his land. The ambitions of this king were clearly of a pan-regional order.

7
Tablet with Sumerian cuneiform script of the inventory of a palm grove
The text is a list with two entries: on the left, there is the amount of date palms (abbreviated with the term giš or "tree"), on the right is the yield of dates from the plants, measured according to the capacity unit sila (= 0.842 lt. ). This document shows that palm trees in the Ur III period gave rise to productions that were extremely variable, both in quantity and quality. Starting with the the 3rd millennium BC, the cultivation of date palms appears to have been very extensive in all of southern Mesopotamia.
Provenience Umma (?) Ur III period (ca. 2052 BC) Clay 6.5 x 4.8 x 2 cm

Provenience Umma Ur III period (ca. 2033 BC) Clay 3.9 x 3.9 x 0.9 cm

Shulgi (2094-2047 BC). Having avoided the risk of an ideological crisis of the subsequent dynasty after his father died and having steadily consolidated his throne, also with shrewd political marriages (i.e. giving his daughters in marriage to foreign kings or high-ranking members of the local élite), the king effected rapid territorial expansion of the kingdom starting with the 20th year of his reign, especially to the east of the Tigris and southwestern Iran, beginning to incorporate those territories in the State of Ur. Already in the 21st year of his reign, the king then introduced some social measures that in fact made the entire population dependent upon the Crown. That change, in turn, brought about a deep economic and administrative reorganization of the kingdom. Shulgi died at the end of the 48th year of his reign, also leaving a rich literary tradition, full of hymns of praise to his own person, which was also deified. Under the reign of two of his many children, who followed one another onto the throne of Ur, first Amar-Su'en (2047-2039) and then Shu-Su'en (2038-2030), their father's imperial policy was continued, although there were increasing external threats to the empire, especially by the semi-nomadic bands of the Amorites coming from Upper Syria and upper Mesopotamia. Shu-Su'en came to erect a wall between the Tigris and the Euphrates (muriq Tidnim) against them, as we know from a dating formula related to this king (cap. 13). But the region of Susiana to the east also created political problems: therefore trusty Zarriqum was named its governor in order to deal with them. The two sons of Shulgi continued the organizational work of administration begun by their father, in the main center of Umma on a canal to the east of the Euphrates, in the more northerly Puzrish-Dagan (today Drehem), in Lagash, and in other sites. The decadence of Ur began and became complete under their successor Ibbi-Suen (2029-2006): for internal reasons and hostile pressures from outside, from year to year a progressive defection of the main provincial centers took place, and the letters exchanged between the king and Ishbi-Erra, commander of the city of Isin, record the acquisition of an increasingly autonomous status by the latter, while in the background there was increasing pressure from the social groups of the Amorites, people of pastoral origin. Finally, Ishbi-Erra became king of Isin, thus inaugurating an independent dynasty; meanwhile the ruler of Ur also lost
Nippur, an important cultural and religious center. At the end, Ibbi-Suen remained solely at the head of the region surrounding the capital; but in 2006 BC, Elam undertook a punitive expedition against Ur, which was completely destroyed, while the king was taken prisoner.
The second millennium
The next millennium began with a radically changed situation: the fragmentation of the third Ur empire brought back to center stage the political rivalry among the kingdoms in southernmost Mesopotamia. Thus, Ishbi-Erra undertook various construction activities in his royal palace in Isin, and with the expulsion of the Elamites from Ur, extended his dominion over central and southern Mesopotamia. But the dynasty of Isin, while proclaiming to represent the imperial continuity with that of Ur, was not the only one in this new phase of political disintegration and territorial fragmentation (“intermediate period”) following the fall of the third dynasty of Ur. In parallel, a dynasty founded by a certain Naplanum came to power in Larsa, on a tributary of the Euphrates in the area between Uruk and Eridu. The dynasty of Larsa, like that of Isin, would play an important role in the Mesopotamian scenario until the eighteenth century. Other dynasties also had their origins in this same phase, in Eshnunna in the center-north and in Der, in the area bordering Elam. Thus began the political growth of the city-states located in areas on the borders of the traditional Mesopotamian playing ground, from Mari in the Middle Euphrates to Ashur/Assur on the north-central Tigris. A re-consolidation of the political spectrum around a small number of states progressively took place between the nineteenth and eighteenth centuries BC. At Larsa, the old ruling dynasty of Gungunum was subverted by an Amorite, called "sheikh of Yamutbal", even though his name was Elamite, Kudurmabuk (Steinkeller 2004: 30-34): he put his son on the throne, who was succeeded by his brother Rim-Sin. His reign, lasting sixty years (1822-1763), was the pinnacle of the political and cultural development of Larsa. Under Rim-Sin, many irrigation work were executed and significant territorial conquests were achieved. Uruk fell in 1801; Isin was subjugated in 1793. Thus, in this phase, Larsa had taken complete control of southern Mesopotamia. However, once more, its development

11
Clay nail with inscription in Sumerian cuneiform script
Commemorative inscription of Gudea (ca. 2144-2124 BC), prince of Lagash, which commemorates the construction of a temple to the god Nindaraa at Girsu, the temple quarter of Lagash.
Provenience Lagash Gutian period (ca. 2140 BC) Clay 14 x 6.5 cm
“To Nindaraa, mighty lord, his lord, Gudea, prince of Lagash, built his [=the god's] temple at Girsu.”
Gudea (2144-2124 B.C.), son of Ur-Baba, is one of the best-known figures in Mesopotamian history by virtue of the multitude of historiographic and celebratory texts drafted by him, which the French excavations at Telloh unearthed by chance. His inscriptions, also engraved on diorite statues representing Gudea himself in the round, attest to a variety of public works undertaken in Lagash (and specifically in the 'holy city' of Girsu): excavations of canals and hydraulic works; urban adjustments; the manufacture of cultic objects and apparel and, above all, the erection of shrines. The chief effort by the ensi was the reconstruction and inauguration of the eninnu, the temple of Ningirsu, main god of the city. For this deed, Gudea claimed to have entertained business relations with many surrounding areas, some of which were considerably far away: from Dilmun in the Arabian Gulf to Anshan and Elam on the high plateau to the east of Mesopotamia; from south-central Kimash to Barme in today's Kurdistan; from the Middle Euphrates up to northern Syria (Urshum and Ebla); and from the Amanus mountain range to that of the Taurus. In addition, he traveled extensively in the areas adjacent to his own to consult the gods of other Sumerian cities on the feasibility of his building project. On the other hand, any mention of bellicose activities is absent from the many inscriptions by Gudea, which have moreover been preserved in numerous duplicates. The dynasty of Gudea continued for several decades, until the absorption of Lagash as a province in the Ur III period.
31



Signs Before the Alphabet
raid against Babylon by the Hittite king Murshili I (ca. 1620-1590). The Hittite state had already reached a high level of military efficiency, which allowed for expeditions and raids even outside the borders of Anatolia. The expedition against Babylonia does not seem to have had particular consequences for the Hittites themselves. But it had implications on the south-central Mesopotamian scenario, where the Kassite dynasty settled and remained throughout the following "middle age", until 1160 BC. Around 2000 BC, after the fall of Ur, and of which it had just been a remote provincial town, the city-state of Assur, built on a steep spur on the middle Tigris and centered on the worship of a deity of the same name, intercepted trade routes that were still in progress, by organizing the redistribution of raw materials and/or finished products from its western
to the north was blocked by two other major kingdoms: the state of Eshnunna on the Diyala river, an old ally of Elam and considered dangerous by Assyria, by Mari, and even by Yamkhad (Aleppo); and the state of Babylonia. It was actually the latter that caused the final fall of Larsa under its most effective king, Hammurabi. The rise of Hammurabi (1794-1750) took place in two main phases: first of all, he obtained the neutralization of Rim-Sin, with the capture of Uruk and Isin, making himself in fact master of the southern sector of the floodplain; in the late part of his reign, he then made his move against a weakened Eshnunna and expanded his borders beyond central and southern Mesopotamia. This rise is reflected, albeit in a nutshell, in the "dating formulas" that comprised the chronological backbone of the kingdom for administrative purposes, and as such, were attested on every practical document of the period. These short sentences thereby bear witness to the fortification of the Babylonian cities in 1776; to the defeat of a coalition including Elam, Assyria, and Eshnunna in 1764; to the final conquest of Larsa after a siege of several months in 1763; and to the destruction of Mari and the defeat of the local king Zimri-Lim, who had come to power after the end of the Assyrian domination in 1759. Even in his later years, Hammurabi tried in vain to conquer Assyria: that was ruled by the mighty Shamshi-Adad I (1810-1782 BC), who - with the title of "King of all" - had formed a comprehensive political and territorial structure between the northern Tigris and Euphrates, that was continued after the his death by his weak son Ishme-Dagan. However, at the height of its power, the kingdom of Babylonia controlled virtually the whole of Mesopotamia, up to areas quite remote from the capital such as Mari and Tuttul on the north-central Euphrates, and substantial parts of the upper Tigris. Under Shamshi-ditana, one of Hammurabi's successors (1625-1594), came the sudden political collapse of the dynasty. Due to a set of factors and events that are not fully understood, the beneficiaries were the Kassites, a population coming from the Iranian plateau and which had slowly gained strength in the north-eastern part of Mesopotamia. The Kassites' conquest of the state of Babylonia was further helped by a victorious
12
Hemispherical cup with Sumerian inscription
The inscription celebrates the construction of the temple of the moon god Nanna at Ur by King Ur-Namma, founder of the Third Dynasty of Ur. He was a fervent devotee of the gods, and carried out great temple works in various cities.
Provenience Ur III period Bronze 6 x 10 cm
“Ur-Namma, king of Ur, he who has built the temple of Nanna.”
The inscription celebrates the construction of the temple of the moon god Nanna at Ur by King Ur-Namma, founder of the Ur III dynasty and is well known from brick and stone exemplars: instead, a version on a metal cup is not hitherto attested. This finding has come down to us from the deposits of metal objects, inscribed or not, from Luristan; these deposits probably originated from plunder carried out by local people in Mesopotamia in various stages, from the 3rd to the 1st millennium BC. It may be noted that the findings in the Luristan deposits also included inscriptions (equally on metal cups) much older than this one; in fact, known are texts related to the kings of the Akkad Dynasty, such as Naram-Sin (2254-2218), Sharkalisharri (2217-2193) and, later on, from the Gutian period, such as Elulu (2192-2190) and Shu-turul ( 2168-2154).
Ur-Namma was from Uruk and perhaps came from a cadet branch of the royal family; he assumed the title of 'King of Sumer and Akkad', typical of his city of origin, and established his dynasty in the city of Ur, aiming to free himself from the power of Elam in the more northern and southern areas. He was a fervent devotee of the gods and the executor of great temple constructions in various cities as well as extensive work on the canal system, but he did not seem to have greatly expanded the territory of his country. The 'Sumerian King List' (cf. ch. 7b) assigns 18 years of reign to him, of which at least 13 are well documented by administrative texts. Instead, a literary text (The death of Ur-Namma) recounts his death in battle and that his body was not recovered for burial, an event considered seriously inauspicious (Frayne 1997; Dahl 2007).

Signs Before the Alphabet
Gulf, under Ulam-Buriash (1496-1474); later, it began a long and complex 'standoff' with the nearby Assyrian power. During the 15th and 14th centuries, the Kassite kings established various relations at the international level: Kurigalzu I (1435-1406) was responsible for remarkable achievements in the sphere of foreign policy by concluding inter-dynastic marriages with the Egyptian pharaohs of the Eighteenth Dynasty and the Elamite kings, and important achievements in the construction sector, especially regarding the founding of a new capital, Dur-Kurigalzu, built to control the roads leading to the Iranian area. However, the dynasty's military and political activities were later limited to Mesopotamia and nearby Elam: although Kurigalzu II (1351-1327) resisted Assyrian oppression and defeated the Elamites by storming their capital Susa, the Assyrian king Tukulti-Ninurta I (1243-1207) managed to conquer Babylon twice, deporting the Kassite king Kashtiliash IV. The last fifty years of the Kassites' dominion was marked by a recovery of power and by public works (under Melishipak, ca. 1180); but the Elamite king Shutruk-nakhkhunde took advantage of the lull in warring with a rapid invasion and seized the major cities, putting his son on the throne, and gradually conquering the country (ca. 1160). The military ups and downs of the Kassite kingdom were mirrored by those of Assyria where, after a period of anarchy following the reign of Ishme-Dagan, for much of the fifteenth century the ruling dynasty was a vassal of the Hurrian empire of Mittani, which came to control, directly or indirectly, the vast area between the Mediterranean and the Upper Tigris. It was only after the victory over the state of Mittani by the Hittite king Shuppiluliuma I (1380-1346), that Assyria gradually emerged as a member of the "club of great powers" (Liverani 2000) in competition with each other in the Near East scenario. The backbone of the kingdom was a new ruling class with military and administrative functions, which drew its supremacy precisely from large landed property (cap. 15), under the aegis of a king newly endowed with strong political, religious, and legal powers. Having warded off the attempts of the last kings of Mittani to regain lost power, under Assur-uballit (13631328), the Assyrians inaugurated a phase of diplomatic and political relations on an international scale; Adad-nirari I (1305-1274) ushered in a period of military expansion, confronting the populations of the Iranian highland, the nomads of the Syrian steppe, and the Kassite neighbors. Then, on the occasion of the emergence of the Hurrian state of Khanigalbat, the successor to Mittani, Adad-nirari went as far as Carchemish on the Upper Euphrates, taking the enemy king prisoner and freeing him only upon the promise of vassalage. However, during a subsequent rebellion Khanigalbat was reduced to a province of the Assyrian kingdom, by then in the process of significant expansion. Territorial expansion continued under Shalmaneser I (1273-1244), who led victorious wars throughout the neighboring area, against the rising power of Nairi-Urartu, the new nomadic groups of the Akhlamu and a Hittite-Hurrian coalition. His successor Tukulti-Ninurta I (1243- 1207) brought the Middle Assyrian kingdom to its zenith, developing a system of provinces placed both to the east and the west of the Tigris, from which abundant offerings rained down with regularity - in the form of taxes and donations – to the royal palace and the temple of Assur. He led successful campaigns against the Hittites and their Syrian allies (Amurru, Ugarit, etc.); although, at the end, the Euphrates remained as a mutual boundary. Likewise prestigious was his double conquest of Babylon (see above), over which Tukulti-Ninurta ruled for seven years. However, shortly after the Kassites regained independ
neighbor Iran or from Babylonia to the lands to the northwest, and in the Anatolian region of Cappadocia. Thus, a broad commercial network came into being, gravitating around the “entrepot” (karum in Akkadian) of Kanesh - today Kültepe, near Kayseri - where the Assyrians settled, developing an unceasing caravan traffic along the approximately 800 km of routes from their country of origin for about two centuries, and founding a number of other commercial colonies, set in a radial pattern, in the Anatolian territory. Written sources concerning this phase have come almost exclusively from Kanesh: about 23,000 tablets of letters and commercial or legal documents were unearthed in the excavation of the entrepot a century ago. The texts indicate that the homeland Assur (called alum, "the city" par excellence), which had gradually become very prosperous from the proceeds of Anatolian trade, was ruled by a king with administrative functions on behalf of the tutelary deity and urban assembly, formed by the owners of the major merchant houses; the King and the Assembly also imparted directives to the Kanesh settlers. Through their warehouses in Anatolia, the Assyrians aimed at getting closer to the sources of metal which characterized the micro-Asian plateau and especially the silver and gold mines; they also imported Anatolian wool in abundance. In return, they provided the Anatolians with elaborate clothing and, above all, precious tin – a metal not found in Asia Minor – to be alloyed with copper for bronze objects. There are many kinds of documents attesting to the complexity of trade management, from transportation contracts to letters entrusting sums of silver (cap. 14), to correspondence relating to commercial lawsuits in the Assyrian courts (cap. 16). The period of Old Assyrian occupation of Kanesh extended over two separate phases. One much older set of Assyrian tablets refers to approximately 80 years in the nineteenth century BC. The site of the entrepot, outside the indigenous village of Kanesh, was later destroyed and rebuilt 30 years afterward: in fact, associated at corresponding levels are texts from the reigns of Shamshi-Adad I and his son Ishme-Dagan. However, the later period was little more than a pale shadow of the previous commercial flowering: and, moreover, the imperial attempt made by Shamshi-Adad I floundered under his successor Ishme-Dagan, who was soon reduced to ruling only the territory of Assyria. The central and final centuries of the second millennium BC had a much broader panorama than in previous ages: the political, economic, and cultural relations would then take place on a scenario that went beyond Mesopotamia and Syria and that included the entire Near East, from the Egyptian delta to the Anatolian and Iranian plateaus. This vast system of factual and ideological interrelations is reflected in the various sources of documentary archives, in different scripts and languages: there are different perspectives and information on the period from Boghazköy/ Hattusha, the Hittite capital, from the urban locations dominated by the Hurrian state of Mittani (from Nuzi to the east of the Tigris up to Alalakh on the Mediterranean), from the diplomatic archive of the Pharaohs in the Egyptian site of Tell el Amarna, and finally, from the Hittite dependence of Emar on the Euphrates and from the coastal city of Ugarit, as well as numerous smaller towns, which require careful data correlation for the complete reconstruction of this "international age". By contrast, the two states of Mesopotamia were above all devoted to local interests and reduced to a minor diplomatic prestige, especially during the early part of this period. In Babylonia, the Kassite dynasty first of all took possession of the whole region, up to the Persian
34



Tukulti-Ninurta II (890-884). But the passage to a real pre-imperial formation took place during the ninth century BC with the reign of Ashurnasirpal II and Shalmaneser III (cap. 19). After the death of Shalmaneser III, his son Shamshi-Adad V (823-811) finally got the better of an internal revolt, also thanks to Babylonian support. However, he later raged militarily against the throne of Babylonia, plunging that area into a long period of political chaos. In the early years of the reign of the very young king Adad-nirari III (810-783), the helm of the state was taken by his mother Sammu-ramat (who became legendary as Semiramis), with the help of some of the kingdom's Magnates. This king and his three sons and successors (up to Assur-nirari V, 754745) continued the expansionist drive against the feisty Aramaic states of Arpad and Damascus. Meanwhile, Urartu dangerously expanded its sphere of influence in northern Syria. Tiglath-Pileser III (745-727) ascended to the throne after a coup and a rebellion in Kalhu in 745, and moved quickly against the most difficult fronts. After a victorious pitched battle with the Urartian army for hegemony in Syria, he marched against Arpad, which was conquered in 740 after a three-year siege. The Syrian area and the Levant still remained a hotbed of anti-Assyrian revolts, requiring more retaliatory expeditions: first against the Phoenician cities, then to help Judah which was attacked by Israel and Damascus, and then to destroy the latter. On the eastern front, the king carried out sorties in the land of the Medes, bringing back rich spoils; but above all, he threatened Urartu right in its own territory, laying siege to the capital Turushpa on Lake Van. In Babylonia, the king led extensive campaigns against the Chaldean chieftain Mukin-zeri, even
ence, the able ruler was killed in a palace coup. After a period of dynastic crisis, the Middle Assyrian kingdom had one last blaze of power under Tiglath-pileser I (1114-1087 BC) who went beyond the Euphrates and reached the Mediterranean with his army. However, later on the Assyrian kings had to fall back entirely from the steppe of the Jezirah, where numerous armed formations of the Akhlamu-Aramean people, of clearly nomadic origin, were advancing.
The First Millenium
Starting in the late thirteenth century BC, a social and economic crisis of vast proportions had led to the fall of the system of empires and citystates that had characterized the ''international age": the Egyptian empire retreated from the Asian chessboard, the Hittite empire was fragmented into a series of small ("Neo-Hittite") states in southern Anatolia and upper Syria, while the Levant reorganized itself around new political and cultural groupings, such as the allied coastal cities of Phoenicia and, further south, the Philistines (a population of recent arrival among the "sea peoples" described by the Egyptians). The crisis - possibly linked to climate issues - would seem to have struck Mesopotamia with some delay: certainly, for about two centuries Assyria was reduced to its original territories around the Tigris, while many small Aramaic states were formed on the plain between the Tigris and the Euphrates. Resumption of offensive and expansion began under Adad-nirari II (911891), thanks to victories in both the north-east and to the west almost up to the banks of the Euphrates; his conquests were consolidated by

The sources already mention Sennacherib (704-681 BC) dating from from 715 or so, when the twenty-year old Crown Prince assisted his father in domestic and foreign policy. Once he sat on the throne, he had to cope with a perceived cosmic negativity connected to the death of his father within the palace and, in the Empire, with the outbreak of numerous anti-Assyrian rebellions. In Babylonia, he had to deal with tough Marduk-apal-iddina, until he finally defeated him in 700. In the Levant, the states not yet reduced to provinces were the theater of many uprisings. In 701, the king marched against Ashkelon, which was at the head of the uprising, placing new dynasties in power; the same fate befell Ekron, whose inhabitants had delivered up their pro-Assyrian king to Hezekiah of Judah and appealed to the Egyptian forces. The latter were defeated in a pitched (yet difficult) battle at Eltekeh and Ekron was returned to its king Padî. Lachish was besieged and Jerusalem was subjected to a military bloc until the surrender of Hezekiah, who gave in to renewed vassalage, upon payment of a massive tribute. Having reestablished the imperial situation which had been accomplished by his father, Sennacherib turned to building works in the Assyrian homeland and especially around Nineveh, where he had moved the imperial capital. Construction of the “Palace Without Rival" began there around 700 and ended almost a decade later. Even more impressive was the engineering feat of conveying water from the surrounding region for the city's water supply (Fales - Del Fabbro 2014). But the Chaldeans had by now reached widespread hostility: in 694, they captured the crown prince Ashur-nadin-shumi, who had been on the Babylonian throne for six years. Harsh reprisals followed that culminated in the siege, fall, and destruction of Babylon: an event which was considered sacrilegious by contemporaries. The end of his reign - also marked by revolts in mountain areas and in the Levant, backed by Egypt - was precipitated by the dynastic choice in favor of his youngest son Esarhaddon, which led to a revolt by his other sons, culminating in the assassination of the king - an event that was even remembered in the Old Testament.
taking up at the end the crown of the southern country himself, with an institutional innovation. The reign of his son Shalmaneser V (726-722) was short and marked by internal revolts against the king's tax policy. In the wake of this discontent, another son of Tiglath-Pileser usurped the throne, with the important dynastic name of Sharru-Kinu, like the ancient king of Akkad; he is traditionally indicated according to the biblical version of the name (Sargon). This king (722-705 BC) completed the siege begun by his predecessor in Samaria, the capital of the kingdom of Israel, and also reduced the status of the Aramaic state of Hama to province. Then his attention turned towards Urartu, where the king Rusa was trying to extend its influence on the scenario of the Zagros Mountains. After restoring dynastic legitimacy in the land of the Manneans as an anti-Urartian measure, Sargon organized a defensive line of Assyrian strongholds in the southern Zagros; then, in 714, he led an extensive campaign directed against the eastern vassals of Rusa, and then against the Urartian forts on Lake Urmia. During the return march, he ransacked the buffer state of Musasir where the Urartians had their main temple. There were extensive north-west campaigns in the following years, tuening into provinces the territories that were already tributaries, especially Carchemish. In Babylonia, the resumption of military intervention (710) opened a conflict against the Chaldeans and Elamites that would continue uninterrupted for forty years or so. After a series of lightning victories over the head of the Chaldeans, Marduk-apal-iddina, Sargon resumed the crown and resided in the region until 707, personally supervising the local administrative reorganization. Upon his return home, Sargon was present at the entrance of the deities in the temples of the new capital Dur-Sharruken ("Fortress of Sargon"), whose foundations had been placed in 717 and which had cost the entire empire an enormous amount of energy. Then the city was inaugurated and the New Year's Feast of 705 took place there in great pomp. But a few months later the king fell during an expedition in Anatolia and his body was not recovered to be returned to the homeland.

Signs Before the Alphabet
Meanwhile the division of the empire, which had brought Shamashshumu-ukin onto the Babylonian throne, collapsed after fifteen years, because the state of the south did not have a completely independent status, but at best, was a privileged vassal kingdom, or an important protectorate, of Assyria. So in 652, Shamash-shumu-ukin rebelled; both the "Sealand", where a nephew of the head of the Chaldeans, Marduk-apal-iddina, reigned, and Elam – the theater of various dynastic struggles – as well as diverse western polities (Syria, Nubia, the Arabs) allied themselves with the rebel. On the contrary, the Assyrians' allies included the southern cities of Ur and Uruk, as well as a part of the Chaldeans, the Arameans and the old "Akkadian" population in the region. After initial losses, the Assyrian counterattack focused on Nippur (reconquered by Ashurbanipal at the end of 651 BC); Babylon itself then came under siege (late 650). In 649, the progress of the war began to turn in favor of the Assyrians, and all the major rebel cities were placed under siege. The fall of Babylon took place in the summer of 648, and Shamash-shumu-ukin perished in the fire of his own palace. Punishment of the rebels was extremely severe: in particular, the heart of Elam was struck by the destruction and systematic plundering of its capital Susa, and even the desecration of the royal tombs. Thus fell a plurimillennial kingdom that had always been hostile to Mesopotamia. But other political entities of the Iranian plateau were ready to take its place: not only the Medes, hitherto mere vassals of Assyria, but also Parshumash, ruled by Kurash (maybe Cyrus I, ancestor of the founder of the Achaemenid dynasty, Cyrus II), which for the moment preferred to send tribute to the Assyrian king. After the defeat of his rebellious brother, the final part of Ashurbanipal's reign is poorly documented, both in official materials and in everyday documents. The reign of this king supposedly lasted 42 years, or up to 627 BC; but there is mention of him in texts from Nippur only until the 3rd month of 631. Therefore, it is possible that he had abdicated in favor of Ashur-etel-ilani, perhaps retreating to Harran. Perhaps as early as 630, three figures appear to have disputed the Assyrian throne: Ashur-etelilani, most certainly the son of Ashurbanipal, Sin-shumu-leshir, a chief eunuch, and Sinsharishkun, another son of Ashurbanipal who was on the throne at the time of the fall of Nineveh in 612. Emerging from this quarrel was a Babylonian soldier, Nabopolassar, who, after a series of
Esarhaddon ascended to the throne, supported by his mother Naqi'a/ Zakutu, after having defeated his brothers in pitched battle. His reign (680-669 BC) was marked by the work of massive reconstruction of Babylon and the surrounding major sacred cities to appease the wrath of the Chaldeans. There were only a few foreign policy interventions, and these were mostly regarding containment or control: so rebellious Sidon was destroyed and the king made a treaty of friendship with the King of nearby Tyre. A more massive military effort was aimed at extending the borders of the empire in Egypt in 673-672, ended with the chief enemy Taharqa escaping from Memphis and the division of territory between the Assyrian governors and local rulers, but triggered an immediate reaction in attempts at rebellion. Under the impetus of this substantial failure, or other personal and political factors, in 672, Esarhaddon decided to divide his crown between his two sons, designating Ashurbanipal at home and Shamash-shumu-ukin in Babylonia; the whole empire - governors, vassals, and categories of dignitaries - was called to sign an oath of loyalty to them in front of Ashur and the other great gods of Assyria; copies of this long text were found both in Assyria (Assur and Nimrud) and in the provinces (Kinalua on the Mediterranean). Perhaps as a reaction to this institutional innovation, in 670 there was a conspiracy by several Magnates, who were put to death by the king. Then Esarhaddon took once more the road to Egypt, where Assyrian rule was weak, but he fell ill and died on the journey in the late months of 669. A few weeks later, Ashurbanipal ascended to the throne, with the political support of his grandmother Naqi'a. The new ruler (668-627 [?]) normalized the situation in Egypt in the early years of his reign, ensuring the end of its dominance over the Nile Delta until 655, when Psammeticus I forced the Assyrians, by force of arms, to withdraw permanently from Egyptian territory. In the mountains around Mesopotamia, nomadic groups of Cimmerians (or Scythians) forced the rulers of Tabal and Hilakku (Cilicia), and also Gyges, king of Lydia, to ask Assyria for help; and the Manneans pressed against the Assyrian fortifications on the eastern fringes of the empire. Finally, a particular thorn in the side was Elam, which broke its good relations with Assyria and - under King Teumman - unleashed an attack in Babylonia, with the help of the Gambulu tribes on the Aramaic border. Ashurbanipal proceeded against Teumman in the 653, defeating him in a bloody battle on the Ulay River.

Tablet with Akkadian cuneiform script
Letter in Assyrian dialect concerning business affairs presumably related to the caravan trade between Assyria and Anatolia. It mentions a lawsuit taking place in the Assyrian capital, whence the sender wrote to two close friends/relatives of his in the commercial 'entrepot' of Kanesh.
Provenience Kanesh
Old Assyrian period (19th century BC)
Clay
3.7 x 4.4 x 1.6 cm
“To.. ushga and Shat-Ashshur, say: thus (speaks) Nab-Sin. We are fine: do not worry! We hear good news all the time. (As soon as) we will obtain (a decision) on our judicial affair, I will send as soon as possible (news) of our health. You need not worry!”
Letter in Assyrian dialect to two individuals by a third party, concerning business matters, presumably related to the caravan trade between Assyria and Anatolia. It mentions a lawsuit taking place in the Assyrian capital, whence the sender wrote to his close friends/relatives at the mercantile 'entrepot' in Kanesh; the tablet was found in the Anatolian archive.
For the wide commercial network in Anatolia in the Old Assyrian period, all gravitating around the 'entrepot' (karum in Akkadian) in Kanesh – today Kültepe at Kayseri – cf. ch. 1b.2. Relations with the local population at Kanesh appear to have been excellent, and carried out in the name of mutual integration. Foreigners residing there were granted extraterritoriality, and were legally dependent on their mother country while paying various taxes to the royal palace of Kanesh. In the Assyrian capital, not only the agents of the major merchants operating in Anatolia, but likewise the financiers behind the commercial enterprises themselves resided. The system involved the collaboration of many people (often linked in pools based on kinship or not) for the preparation of caravans at Assur, and once they had reached their destination after various legs of their journey, for the management of business in Kanesh.
