18
Brick with inscription in Akkadian cuneiform script
The brick presents a standard inscription by Nabu-kudurriusur, king of Babylon (605-562 BC) known as Nebuchadnezzar in the Old Testament. In it, the king states his royal title, proclaiming himself 'administrator' (zānin) of the two major Babylonian temples - Esagila, the sanctuary of the main god Marduk in Babylon , and Ezida, the Temple of Nabu and Marduk in Borsippa - and indicates his descent from the previous king, Nabu-apal-usur (= Nabopolassar).
Provenience Babylon or elsewhere in southern Mesopotamia Neo-Babylonian period Clay 32 x 32 x 6.8 cm
news of Nabopolassar's death in Babylon on August 15, 605 BC reached his son on the Syro-Palestinian field of operations. Having returned to the capital by forced marches, Nebuchadnezzar "ascended the throne of the kingdom of Babylonia" and was recognized King on September 7 (cap. 18). Only a few months later, he set out once again for the western front, where he was constantly engaged in the following years. His commitment in Syria-Palestine, where the vassal kingdoms ranged between loyalty to the Babylonian king or to the Egyptian pharaoh, increased when the king of Judah Yehoyaqim refused to recognize Babylonian authority and to pay tribute in 598 BC. His response was harsh: Jerusalem was besieged and the young king Yehoyakin, who had succeeded his father after his death that same year, was defeated. The city fell on March 16, 597 BC and suffered heavy looting; the king, his family, and his court were deported to Babylon, along with part of the population and Zedekiah was placed on the throne of Judea. However, in 589 BC, the new Pharaoh Apries gave his support to a vast anti-Babylonian coalition including Phoenicians, Ammonites, and even the kingdom of Judah. Nebuchadnezzar's reaction was immediate: Jerusalem was besieged again and fell in 587 BC. The city was destroyed, the walls razed, and the city gates burned. Solomon's temple was set on fire and its furnishings plundered. The population suffered heavy deportations (known as "the Babylonian exile", which would last seventy years). Finally, in 568 BC, taking advantage of an internal crisis in Egypt, Nebuchadnezzar invaded the Nile Delta. His campaign, which was probably just demonstrative, nevertheless yielded the result of establishing the mutual border in Gaza, in Palestine. In essence, in the 43 years of Nebuchadnezzar's reign, the Neo-Babylon kingdom reached its greatest territorial expansion, extending over much of the Near East, "from the Upper Sea to the Lower Sea". The capital city Babylonia, also celebrated
skirmishes with the Assyrian forces, ascended to the throne of Babylonia in 626, ushering in a "Chaldean" dynastic line that would last for 87 years, until the Persian conquest. News regarding the end of Assyria have come to us from a single text, the so-called Babylonian Chronicle: in 616, Nabopolassar went up the Euphrates, leading raids all the way to the Balikh. It is likely that in this same period, the first functional alliance with the Medes arose. In the summer of 614, after an abortive attempt to attack Nineveh, the army of the Median ruler Cyaxares, son of Phraortes, descended the Tigris and reached the gates of Assur, and before the arrival of Nabopolassar from the south, managed to overcome the city. The Medo-Chaldean coalition still gathered in 612 and laid siege to the imperial capital Nineveh for three months; perhaps on the occasion of an Assyrian defensive sortie, a pitched battle led to the defeat of the besieged and the death of Sinsharishkun. His successor Ashur-uballit fled with his court to Harran, the last Assyrian stronghold in Upper Mesopotamia, but the city fell in 610 BC. Nabopolassar rightly boasted about it in an inscription, thus: “The Assyrian who ruled all the people from time immemorial, and who tormented the people with his heavy yoke; I (Nabopolassar) ....blocked him from Akkad and there I raised its yoke." The new Chaldean dynasty rapidly occupied Mesopotamia and Syria-Palestine; however, taking advantage of the political vacuum created in the latter region, the Pharaoh Necho, whose father Psammetichus had bequeathed him an Egypt that was reorganized and strong, attempted an Asian policy again, going as far as the strategic stronghold of Carchemish on the upper Euphrates, where a garrison was installed. Therefore, the clash with Nabopolassar was inevitable: in 607 BC, the two armies faced each other, but Carchemish fell only in 605 BC, when the Babylonian King gave the crown prince Nebuchadnezzar command of the army. and the Fertile Crescent. However as this may be, in 543 BC the king returned to Babylon. But the political landscape had changed: the Persian King Cyrus II, who in around 550 BC had defeated the Medes, had become a real and present danger, having made conquests from Lydia up to the Indus Valley. And the Babylonian priests, hostile to Nabonidus, opened the gates of Babylon to Cyrus without a fight in 539 BC. Classical sources disagree on the fate of Nabonidus; but it is certain that, from then on, Mesopotamia would no longer be dominated by a king born in the country, nor would it be the primary focus of the imperial groups in the following centuries and in the millennia to come.

in some classical sources, reached a level of unusual splendor, with magnificent temples and palaces. The king died in 561 and ephemeral successors followed one another on the throne for five years. Finally, in 556, Nabonidus ascended to the throne. He did not belong to the Babylonian royal line and his family was originally from Harran in Upper Mesopotamia, where the Assyrian court had retreated after the fall of Nineveh. Having sought refuge in Babylonia after the end of Assyria, the family of Nabonidus must have achieved a prestigious position at court, which might not have been alien to the career of the future king; he appears to have also received a high-quality education at the scribal schools of the court (by the way, his name praises the god Nabû, patron of writing). Nabonidus ascended to the Babylonian throne when he was rather old and according to procedures that were not completely clear; however, he made reference to his predecessors in his commemorative inscriptions and his usurpation was legitimized as a manifestation of the will of the national god Marduk. He expanded his building activity in Southern Mesopotamia; it is interesting that he restored the temple of the moon god Nanna/Sin in Ur, consecrating his daughter as a priestess there. Even his military campaigns in the West in the early years of the kingdom were in the wake of the Chaldean tradition. But the references to his "Assyrian" origins were not lacking; and he gradually elevated the god Sin of Harran to the rank of supreme god. This move earned him the declared hostility of the clergy who were linked to the temples of Eanna and Ebabbar at Sippar. For this and other reasons, Nabonidus left Babylon in the fourth year of his reign, entrusting the management of the kingdom to his son Bel-sharusur, and went to the North Arabian oasis of Teima where he remained for seven years. Recent excavations have revealed large construction projects there (fortifications, a hydraulic system, etc.), which could indicate that the city was considered a true royal residence. His stay could have had foreign policy purposes, related to a deeper Babylonian penetration of Hijaz, aimed at control of the trade routes between Arabia

The inscription presents the royal titulary of Shalmaneser III of Assyria (859-824 BC) with mention of his father Ashurnasirpal II and his grandfather Tukulti-Ninurta II, and specifies that the brick itself comes from the support wall of the great ziggurat of Nimrud/Kalkhu, built by the king in the northwest corner of the citadel.
Provenience Kalkhu (Nimrud) Neo-Assyrian period (ca. 830 BC) Clay 16 cm x 34 x 6 cm
“Shalmaneser (III), great king, mighty king, king of totality, king of Assyria, son of Ashurnasirpal (II), great king, mighty king, king of totality, king of Assyria, son of Tukulti-Ninurta (II), king of totality, again king of Assyria: (Brick of the) support for the temple tower (ziggurat) of Kalkhu.”
The inscription specifies that the brick came from the supporting wall of the great ziggurat of Nimrud/Kalkhu, built by the king in the northwest corner of the citadel, for an extension of 60 square meters, with walls of stone (at the bottom) and baked brick (at the top). The building is still visible in its basic form and dimensions; it was not destroyed in the recent devastation at Nimrud/Kalkhu carried out by the Islamic State (2015-2016).
The king and his father, mentioned here, mark the passage of the Assyrian kingdom to an actual pre-imperial phase during the 9th century BC. Ashurnasirpal II consolidated his control of the borders already gainedv on the Upper Tigris and subjugated Zamua, the region east of the river, to protect trade routes to the Iranian area. However, his best efforts were lavished on the large bend in the Euphrates - strategically crucial for access to Syria where the armed resistance had its hub in the Aramaic state of Bit-Adini, governed by the skilful ruler Akhuni. The king eventually moved the political capital to Kalkhu, leaving the traditional center of Assur as the national religious capital.
His son Shalmaneser III accomplished the final defeat of Akhuni, and then - after acts of intimidation against the growing power of Urartu to the north-east – he invaded the Transeuphratic area with a huge army (853). Here he was stopped in the pitched battle of Qarqar by an equally large coalition led by the Aramaic states of Hama and Damascus. Successive campaigns in the Levant did not lead to the conquest of Damascus, the new focus of power in the area. In his later years, an internal revolt broke out in which the largest cities of the kingdom took part.
Where and when: Mesopotamia 43



Signs Before the Alphabet
millennium BC, aided the penetration of the Mesopotamian floodplain in Syria not only by the Amorites, but also by the Hurrians. The presence of Hurrians in Syria became even more numerous after the collapse of the kingdom of Upper Mesopotamia founded by Samsi-Addu. Texts from the Old Assyrian, Old Babylonian, and ancient Hittite periods document the existence of polities held by rulers with Hurrian names: a king by the name Hurrian of Kuwari appears in the tablets of ancient Shusharra = Tell Shemshara (Eidem 2014); the mythological composition about the fall of the city of Ebla, known as the "Song of Liberation", mentions three rulers of Ebla who bore Hurrian names: Arib-Ebla, Paib-Ebla, and Eshebu-Ebla (Wilhelm 1997). Tablets from the archive of Kültepe, ancient Kanesh/Nesa, record a king with a Hurrian name, Anum-Hurbi, who reigned over the city of Hashshum, located in the region of the modern city of Gaziantep (Miller 2001). Sources from phase VII of Alalah testify to a substantial presence of Hurrians, who became even more numerous in the following phase IV. Hittite texts from the Old Kingdom tell us that the Hurrians, always called only with the ethno-linguistic character name Hurri in these sources, offered a continuous and fierce resistance aimed at stemming the advance of Hittite armies towards Syria.
The Kingdom of Mittani
The kingdom of Mittani was the main Hurrian polity; the first mention of Mittani comes from an Egyptian source, and dates from the time of Pharaoh Thutmosis I (at the beginning of the 15th century BC), but, presumably, this kingdom had been formed some decades earlier. The name Mittani means "(the country) of Mitta" and refers to the eponymous founder of the Mittani dynasty. This dynasty had originally acquired control of a polity in the upper Habur river valley; after the collapse of all the political structures of western Syria conquered by the first Hittite kings, and due to the inability of the latter to establish a stable form of domination over the conquered territories, the Mittani kings extended their dominion also over most of western Syria (de Martino 2014). Kings of the Mittani dynasty bore Indo-Aryan names. This aspect of the Mittani monarchy has attracted the attention of scholars for a long time; we can assume that Indo-Aryan groups directed towards the east stopped in the upper Habur area. Some leaders of these clans may have been hired as mercenaries by Syrian potentates fighting against the Hittites, thus acquiring financial resources and control of the territory; then they became assimilated with the Hurrians inhabiting the area and adopted their language (de Martino 2014). The choice to keep even later their Indo-Aryan dynastic names, one of the few fossil relics of the ancient ethno-linguistic origin of that dynasty, may have been motivated by a desire to emphasize a specific identity and a higher rank than those of the Hurrian rulers of the polities located in western Syria. Parattarna I was the first ruler of Mittani for whom we have documentary evidence; his successor Saushtatar was the Hurrian king who fou
The Hurrians
Stefano de Martino
Department of Historical Studies. University of Turin
The oldest evidence of a political entity ruled by a Hurrian dynasty has come from the Syrian site of Tell Mozan, in the upper Khabur River valley; this is where there was the ancient city of Urkesh, home to the most important sanctuary of the Hurrian god Kumarbi. Tupkish, the king of Urkesh, dating from during the XXIV century B.C. and documented by some seal impressions unearthed at Tell Mozan, had a Hurrian name (Buccellati - Kelly Buccellati 1996; 2001). He was succeeded by Tish-Adal, who left the oldest document in the Hurrian language (Wilhelm 1998). The fall of the reign of the Third Dynasty of Ur, at the end of the third
44



ght against the troops of Pharaoh Thutmosis III at Megiddo. At the time of Saushatar's reign, the Mittani kingdom controlled a vast territory that included the upper Habur – where lay the capital Washshukkanni, near the site of Tell Fekheriye (Bonatz 2014) – , southeastern Anatolia (Kizzuwatna), western Syria (Aleppo, Alalakh, Karkemish, Tunip, Nukhkhashshe, Qatna), and southern Syria (Terqa). These were not contiguous territories, but a network of mutually connected points of administrative and military control (von Dassow 2014). Under Artatama I, a contemporary of the Pharaoh Thutmose IV, began a phase of detente with Egypt, resulting in the first of three inter-dynastic mar
riages between Mittani princesses and the Pharaohs, aimed at strengthening the alliance signed between Mittani and Egypt. The Egyptian archive of Tell el Amarna preserves part of the correspondence between the Hurrian king Tushratta and the Pharaohs Amenhotep III and Amenhotep IV (Liverani 1999). In the mid-thirteenth century B.C., the Hittite king Shuppiluliuma I moved against Mittani and conquered it. He placed one of Tushratta's sons, prince Shattiwaza, on the throne. Mittani thus became a kingdom subordinated to the Hittites, and the other Syrians countries that had been part of the Mittani confederation also ended up under Hittite control. At the time of Shattuara I, the successor of Shattiwaza, the first conflicts began between Mittani and Assyria which, with King Shalmaneser I, took possession of a large part of Mitannian territory, taking it away from the Hittite kings and turning it into an Assyrian province (Wilhem 1989; de Martino 2006). With the end of the second millennium BC, and with the economic, political, and ethnic upheavals occurring in the eastern Mediterranean in the twelfth century BC, Mittani and the Hurrians disappeared from the political and ethno-linguistic landscape of the Near East.
Hurrian Mythology
The archives of the Hittite kingdom have preserved long and complex mythological compositions of Hurrian tradition, attested both in their original versions in Hurrian and in Hittite translations. The best known of these compositions is the one that relates the conflict between different generations of gods, represented by Kumarbi, deity of the oldest pantheon, and by the Storm God (Tešob), head of the new generation's gods. This cycle consists of several episodes: the Song of birth, which describes the miraculous birth of the Storm God who emerged from Kumarbi's skull, the Song of the deity LAMMA, the Song of the Silver God who wanted to replace the sun, the Song of the Sea, the Song of the Dragon Hedammu, and the Song of Ullikummi, the stone monster. In their original version in Hurrian, these narratives were written in verse and offer fabulous descriptions marked by highly inventive images. Some episodes of the Kumarbi Cycle show close similarities with the archaic Greek literary tradition and, in particular, with Hesiod's Theogony. We do not know if an echo of Hurrian literature had reached the Mycenaeans through the Hittites already in the second millennium B.C., or whether it survived in the first millennium B.C. and was spread throughout the Mediterranean by the Phoenicians (de Martino 2013).

(Iranian plateau and the Indus Valley) and to the West (Egypt), and even an actual colonisation on the Anatolian and Syrian Upper Euphrates and in South-western Iran, with settlements of remarkable sophistication and artifacts of a certain wealth, which had substantial and lasting effects on the local culture (see fig. 1). Well investigated archaeological sites such as Habuba Kebira and Jebel Aruda (Syria) show a development of the colonization phase over approximately two centuries, resulting in architectural, funerary and commercial outputs that have suggested the existence of a real "Uruk empire" (Algaze 1993), a perspective that is now considered an overstatement. The earliest traces of writing that can be defined proto-cuneiform (through the use of drawings representing different objects and numbers) were found in the most recent archaeological levels of Uruk (levels IV and III of the Eanna, ca. 3200 BC). The characteristics of society, administration and economy of the Late Uruk period would seem per se sufficient to justify the beginning of writing in Mesopotamia. This would have been dictated by primary and practical reasons related to accounting and day-to-day administration. In effect, given the considerable complexity of the Uruk society, it is almost obvious to assume that it simply equipped itself with instruments suited to deal with the recording of vast and varied information without relying on one’s memory and for future reference. This basic idea can generally still be considered valid. However, it is based on a perspective centred on the continuity with subsequent periods, when writing would be developed into a complete system; it does not frame the actual mechanism

Frederick Mario Fales - Roswitha Del Fabbro
Early writing (late Uruk and Jemdet Nasr)
« Since the messenger’s mouth was heavy and couldn’t repeat the message, the Lord of Kullaba flattened some clay and placed the words on it, like a tablet. Up to that moment there had never been one who would place words on tablets.» (from the Sumerian epic poem Enmerkar and the Lord of Aratta)
From the beginning of the proto-urban age (3700 BC) - also called Uruk period after the leading site in lower Mesopotamia – substantial technological advances brought the valley between the Tigris and Euphrates to the forefront of Asiatic societies. These changes, which Gordon Childe described as a whole as an “urban revolution”, included first and foremost the formation of cities as central seats of power. These cities were marked by monumental buildings – high above the plain and of vast dimensions – for religious purposes, but also as evidence of a social and political control that was in the making from within, and with respect to the vast universe of the surrounding farming villages. Ambitious projects of canalisation could be organized through the central political power, so as to defeat the aridity of the land and increase the farming area of the alluvial plain. They were based on the core elements of division of labour and social hierarchies; that is, a system of power and assets management based on the existence of groups and classes differentiated from a political and economic point of view. On these same foundations, Uruk society also completed a network of long-distance commercial exchanges towards the East

Signs Before the Alphabet
and oral language is nowadays cast in serious doubt. As we will see further on, as early as a few centuries after the Late Uruk period, Mesopotamian writing began to represent an underlying language (Sumerian) through an organized set of symbols and signs – the cuneiform writing system – as it would continue to do over the following millennia, even when applied to different languages. However, no longer credible nowadays is a linear evolutionary scheme, which would directly lead from the representation of words through rudimentary drawings to the subsequent breakdown of words into phonological snippets via word-signs and syllable-signs, for the sake of representing the reality of spoken language with increasing precision (Gelb 1952). Nowadays, with the support of studies on the cognitive systems of ancient man, it is believed that writing oftentimes represented a mode of human communication that was parallel to language rather than subservient to it. To clarify this point, we may note first of all that the proto-cuneiform writing systems do not follow in any way the structure of oral languages: the documents of the time were organized above all in spatial rather than sequential hierarchies. The most archaic texts were sub-divided in vertical columns within which were rectangular boxes (cap. 20, cap. 21); inside these boxes, however, the various symbol were arranged according to the available space instead of following a reading order based on language. Moreover, the hub of these archaic texts are numbers (cap. 22) rather than other entities such as people, places or institutions. In other words, the spheres of meaning of the various signs, other than numbers, are few and
of the “birth of writing” in the Land of the Twin Rivers – of writing in the making – from a comparative and functional perspective. In the first place, it should be recalled that writing – both as concept and practical realization – was generated almost simultaneously in Mesopotamia and Egypt. From this epicentre comprising the two flood valleys of Western Asia and Northern Africa, writing spread elsewhere on a vast radius: in Elam (South-western Iran) where – still around the year 3000 BC – we find the proto-Elamite characters; in the second half of the third millennium, in the Indus culture (present day Pakistan); at the end of the third millennium in Crete (Minoan writing) and in large part of Anatolia (Hittite cuneiform), then in China at the end of the second millennium (Shang culture) and finally in Mesoamerica (first millennium BC). Some maintain that this distribution of different forms of writing over time and space demonstrates features of a slow but decisive diffusion of technology; in truth it highlights the lack of any connection – formal as well as typological – between the writing systems that were created in these different environments (fig. 2). The idea of a mono-centric development of writing in antiquity is almost completely discarded, while on the contrary the tendency is to claim that the writing systems of Mesopotamia (and Egypt), China and Mesoamerica were completely independent and, above all, were born as an answer to specific and totally different local requirements. In any case it is today difficult to establish an “universal” equation between the birth of writing and irrepressible bureaucratic-administrative needs. Moreover, the relationship between the earliest forms of writing
fig. 8

Signs Before the Alphabet
quadrangular shapes made of raw clay. To recall the content of these ‘envelopes’ without having to break the container, the required number of tokens would have been imprinted on the surface of the bullae in the fresh clay. In a theoretical further development this sort of double recording would have seemed redundant: the tokens would become obsolete, and the bulla would lose its three-dimensional volume to become a flat and compact tablet, with thin edges. The content to be communicated would then be engraved on it. This evolutionary reconstruction – from token to bulla and then the proto-cuneiform archaic tablet – is currently widely argued (Michalowski 1993). First of all, some of these tokens were discovered in a burial context, even in children’s graves; in these cases it is unlikely that these objects had a numbering function. In addition, the vastness of the documentation itself, over time and place, leads to doubt that all these objects could have the same uses, convey the same meaning, occupy the same position in a (hypothetical) evolutionary chain that led to writing. No doubt, in some cases these items might have been used to count or identify certain product categories; but in other cases, certainly more frequent, they could have been objects of religious-symbolic, or ritual, significance. As for bullae, if it is true that in many archaeological contexts they seem to precede the proto-cuneiform tablets, on the other hand this is not true in the city of Uruk, where they appear to come from the same levels as archaic tablets. In short, the bullae would be a device still in use simultaneously with the first occurrences of writing, perhaps by different social classes. And finally, a third category of documents has emerged from Uruk itself and from some of its colonies: the ‘numerical-ideographic tablets’, comprising numerical symbols and one or two signs representing different objects. Could this have been the “missing link” between the tokens, the bullae and the later tablets in cuneiform script? Further research will allow us to learn more on the subject. However as this may be, all these different aspects - technical, formal and contextual - indicate that writing was not a constituent factor of the complex bureaucracy of archaic Mesopotamia, rather - at most - one of its developments, as formalisation of a series of systems for symbolic notation that were already in place and used for various purposes, from play to ritual and business. Writing borrowed a series of pre-existing symbolic structures, for instance, some signs derive directly from tokens (Cooper 2004),
not easily identifiable, also because these present odd statistical patterns: very few of the signs appear frequently, while most are seldom found. This opens up a further possibility: the most archaic tablets might not yet have had a precise internal code, instead they possibly had to be accompanied by an oral explanation to the side. In short, even maintaining the idea that the proto-cuneiform Mesopotamian tablets had a primarily economic-administrative function, it seems like their use was largely confined to people “in the know”, who had a detailed knowledge of the context of the transactions or movements of goods being recorded. For these experts a written “memorandum” might have been sufficient to understand or remember the content carried by the tablets as for their data and related implications (Damerow 2006). To better understand, let's jump back not just in time but above all from a formal and typological perspective, in order to review the documentation that is traditionally classified as prior to writing: viz., the so-called tokens and the clay bullae. Some ten thousand of tokens were found in various archaeological sites in the Near East of Neolithic date (from 8000 to 3000 BC). They were made of clay or other materials, such as stone, and represented, on a very small scale, geometric or naturalistic shapes, such as cones, spheres, discs, tetrahedrons, cylinders, triangles, vessels, animals and more; some tokens were marked with engraved dots and lines. It has been said in the past that the tokens represented “the adaptation of an archaic way of counting, a so-called concrete counting which predates the invention of abstract numbers”, in the sense that for instance the symbol shaped as a bull’s head would represent “one bull”, that is, the object and its quantity combined (Schmandt-Besserat 1989). The clay bullae (or envelopes) would develop later, with the birth of urbanisation and more complex societies: homogeneous groups of tokens would have been contained inside hollow roundish or fig. 10

Sumerian mathematics in a 5000 year-old accounting clay tablet
Before 3000 BC, when Early Sumerians etched the images of their ‘tokens’ on clay tablets, various kinds of goods were represented by different symbols, and multiple quantities by repeating these. Three units of grain were represented by three grain signs, five oil jars were indicated by five oil signs and so on. The system had two main limitations. First of all every kind of commodity to be recorded needed its own identifying sign; this implied the need to memorise a myriad signs. The second problem concerned quantity: recording three oil jars with three identical signs was easy, but how to deal with 256 oil jars? A different system was obviously needed. The first great innovation in the development of writing was the separation of the quantity of an item from the symbol identifying it. That is, representing three units of grain with one symbol for ‘3’ followed by one for ‘grain’, and the same to write 3 sheep or 3 cows. Such a system is called a metrological numbering system, and the development of this concept in the course of the third millennium is still only partially understood. Let’s turn to other numbers: at the beginning we find some sixty different numerical signs; these can be grouped in a dozen metrological systems, for instance, a specific system to count livestock, a different one for liquid volume etc. Luckily for us Sumerian metrology has indicated all the different conversion factors, although they were all simple fractions of 60. In the sixty-base system used the unit is identified as a small cone. Ten small cones correspond to a small circle, six small circles correspond to a large cone, ten large cones to a large cone with a circle within, six of these to a large circle and ten large circles to a large circle with a small one within. The latter unity thus corresponds to 10x6x10x6x10 = 36.000.
A double sixty-base system was used instead to measure rations; this had conversion factors 10, 6, 2, 10 and 6, so that the symbol indicating the large quantity – this time a large circle containing two small ones – corresponded to a 6x10x2x6x10 = 7.200 base unit. Yet another system was in use to measure wheat. The conversion factors for this are 5, 10, 3 and 10, in such a way that the largest unit – a large cone containing a small circle – had a value of 10x3x10x5 = 1.500 units. During the third millennium these signs were gradually replaced by the equivalent Cuneiform, so that numbers could be written with the same stylus utilised for the words within the text The final phase of this development took place at the time of Ur III and consisted in the introduction of a sixty-based with ‘positional’ values. The number of signs was reduced to two: a vertical wedge derived from the small cone often employed as a base unit, and an angled wedge, derived from the small circle. The angled wedge had the value of ten vertical wedges. In the sixty-base system described above, the next unit up was the large cone. In the Ur III system this unit was represented by a vertical wedge like the base unit. In practice the couple of symbols could be repeated to infinity, always maintaining the same conversion factors of 10 and 6. Consequently a vertical wedge could could be worth 1 or 60 (6x10), or 3600 (60x60) and so on. Its exact value was determined by its place within the symbolic-numeric sequence.

Signs Before the Alphabet
modifications in the graphic system itself, both of a technical and conceptual nature. The first of such shifts concerns the shape of signs, in the sense of their progressive ‘de-naturalisation’ . A series of horizontal, vertical and oblique segments gradually came to replace the curved and sinuous lines of the primitive signs. The reed stylus used to etch the signs in the clay gave them elongated endings in angled or wedged shapes. Very soon lines and angles were used systematically in this more agile form of writing – cuneiform – destined to influence vast areas and diverse cultures of the pre-classical Near East. A change in direction of the writing also took place in the Early Dynastic period: from the original arrangement in sequential boxes within vertical columns, a 90o rotation led to the creation of horizontal lines readable from left to right, with the signs ‘lying on their side’ counter-clockwise when compared to their original concept and shape. An innovation of a radically different kind – implemented in the first half of the second millennium – led to the classification of signs and their values. Since the dawn of writing we may witness the forming of a scribal culture and environment, one that would leave a deep mark in all aspects of the intellectual life throughout pre-Greek Mesopotamia. If it is legitimate to state that in this, as in other parallel regions (Elam, Indus Valley, Crete), writing developed to facilitate the recording of goods or land assets for bureaucratic and economic purposes, it is also undoubtedly true that the class of professional writers quickly understood and employed the potential of the graphical tool for creating texts of different and varied nature, from religious or celebratory, up to decidedly literary compositions. Hence the need to have a growing number of individuals able to use this new technique was born, and professional scribes were trained to master the skills required to deal with the texts’ complexity. The scribal profession began establishing itself in society from the very early appearance of writing. However, up to the end of the third millennium BC, the Sumeric title dub-sar appears to have been used essentially to identify low or mid-level bureaucrats, although they were trained at a young age in the é-dub-ba, the school, or perhaps better (using a medieval Latin term) the scriptorium. As an example: in the many tablets from the third Ur dynasty, several officials seem to have had the function of ‘sealing officer’, but the inscription on their seals, which appear on the same tablets, describe them simply as dub-sar. In any case, by contrast, it will be observed that the ability to read and write was definitely widespread in this administrative environment, especially under King Shulgi, who opened schools at Nippur and Ur. It is certain, in any case, that the é-dub-ba of Ur III times appears to have been primarily an institution for the formation of subjects destined to work in civil service; it was a place where a common basis of ideas and attitudes were learned and exchanged between teachers or students (Michalowski 1987). In later times, dub-sar, then rendered in Akkadian with the equivalent tupsharru, indicates specifically the ‘scribe’. What we know about the scribes’ educational curriculum comes, instead, from the Old Babylonian period (19th-23rd century BC). It is found in written sources from Nippur, Ur, Isin and Sippar. In this context – where archaeology shows us that schooling was hosted in modest buildings, possibly simple ‘boarding schools’ – the public administration seems to have played a limited and secondary role. In fact, initial education consisted of exercises to become familiar
but it added many new ones; eventually all of these were integrated and codified in a technical and conceptual framework, one that diverged substantially from those where the individual elements had been conceived. We will thus continue to acknowledge the extraordinary place the Late Uruk period has in this revolutionary communication development; but we will attribute to this period only the earliest steps in the process
The birth of the cuneiform system. The Sumerian language. The application to Akkadian
« What kind of scribe is a scribe who does not know Sumerian? » (Sumerian proverb)
What was the progress of writing after the Late Uruk period? As said before, the earliest signs etched on the tablets at Uruk, levels IV and III, as well as those of more or less coeval sites such as Jemdet Nasr (for a total of around 6000 documents) were substantially pictorial. Thus, a fish is represented in a profile view, the male and female sex is rendered with schematic but precise traits, the date palm is outlined with its trunk, leaves and branches; on the other hand there are many signs from this phase that we cannot interpret. This writing form of archaic Mesopotamia was often described as ‘pictorial’ or ‘ideographic’, but in fact it presents a single and precise structural characteristic: it is effectively based on the correspondence of each sign with a word – be it describing an object, a number, or a person’s name – it is then, essentially, ‘logographic’. This system, precisely because of that characteristic, was however destined to a rapid internal mutation, one that would allow it to act as an expressive vehicle for a society that was already articulated in its social, juridical and political relations and needs (fig. 3). The evolution from the most rudimentary form of logography - the expression of simple concrete data through writing – to the rendering of associative elements of various kind, could have taken place already within the Uruk civilisation (strata IIIb, ca. 2800 BC). In fact in the name of a person from this period, en-líl-ti, “the god Enlil gives life”, we find the first example of an application of signs on clay that was intended to convey phonetic values. To express the Sumerian word ‘life’, ti, which is difficult to represent from a graphical point of view, the sign with the homophonic value ti(l), ‘arrow’ was used instead. In essence, this first ‘word play’ demonstrates that already at this stage the language underlying Mesopotamian inscribed tablets was a form of Sumerian. Once introduced, the principle of phonetization in writing must have spread at great speed: the large local archives of the following centuries - Fara/Shuruppak, Abu Salabikh, Adab, Lagash, ca. 2500 BC (cap. 23) – already show a mature application of signs with syllabic values, representing elements of linguistic relationship (verbal actions, endings, adverbial suffixes, etc.) interposed amidst signs representing whole words, albeit subject to large semantic extensions (e.g. ‘SUN’ meaning 'sun' but also ‘glow, white, day’). Writing started spreading outside of southern Mesopotamia, reaching even remote areas: the city of Mari on the middle Euphrates, Ebla in western Syria, Tell Beydar and Tell Brak in the northern Syro-Mesopotamian steppe. There is no doubt that this vast diffusion of Mesopotamian writing must be related to further important

Tablet with a contract for the purchase of a house
The deed provides for a dual mode of payment: a sum in metal, divided in turn into the 'price of the house' and a 'supplement', and a quantity of various kinds of goods (products made from barley, amounts of fat and wool, clothing).
Origin Fara/Shuruppak or Nippur
Early Dynastic IIIA (ca. 25th century BC) Clay 7.4 x 7.4 x 2 cm
This text is divided into 36 boxes of writing, distributed in six columns placed vertically side by side. Already a century before the important archives of Early Dynastic Lagash, the documents from Nippur and Shuruppak show us the mechanisms of land sales in the private sector of society. Our tablet documents this practice of buying and selling quite clearly, and has a further aspect of interest in the double method of payment: a sum in metal – divided in its turn between the "price of the house" and a "supplement" – , and an amount of various commodities in kind (mostly products based on barley, but also quantities of fat and wool).
This practice is well documented in this period, but also in later centuries of the third millennium BC: for example, a contract from the Akkadian period presents, after the price in silver, the formula "additional payment for the house", followed by amounts of barley, oil, wool, clothes and tools: thus showing a still wider range of goods than in our document.
Our text also presents a large number of individuals who were at hand for the legal deed: "Ur-eshbar, Uda-urra, Mimi (?), Umma-TUR, Ur-Sud, Lugal-USH.SIM, (are) those who have taken in the price. Ur-Nusku, the scribe; Bakhar, elder of the city; AN-lu-makh, herald; Lugal-edenne-si: (these) are the witnesses. Kinimuzu is the one who bought the house."
power with Sargon of Akkad (ca. 2350 BC). Akkadian is, as opposed to Sumerian, polysyllabic (im-me-ru = ‘sheep’; li-ša-nu = ‘language’ etc) and – just as our languages – of a inflected kind in the nouns and verbs. It has been proved (Cooper 1973) that linguistically Akkadian personal names were to be found side by side with the Sumerian ones in Mesopotamia from the earliest archives; thus, while Sumerian was culturally prevalent in the Early Dynastic age, possibly also due to demographic factors, it acted on the basis of bi-lingual population. The prevailing of Akkadian as official language due to a choice made by Sargon and his successors thus came into being because of social and political factors, but it was not the product of invasions or other external factors, as was previously believed: the Semites had long been well established in Mesopotamia and were integral part of the population, on par with Sumerians, particularly in the most northern areas. The maintaining of the existing script, albeit applied to a very different language, from the original one, was not – as it was once believed – a phenomenon exclusively peculiar to Akkadian; more or less at the same time of Sargon the scribes from Ebla, in Syria, had adopted the cuneiform script to represent the local Semitic language (‘Eblaic’) which is itself a distant relative of Akkadian (Rubio 2006). Further, in the course of the second and first millennium BC cuneiform writing was applied to yet more diverse and varied languages. In short, cuneiform is a form of writing – as the alphabet will be later in all its different manifestations, from our writing of Latin origin to the eastern scripts such as Ethiopian Ge'ez or ornate Brahmi – rather than a language (van de Mieroop 1999). Born to be applied to Sumerian, cuneiform had its first secondary adaptation for the Semitic languages. We can judge the results of this adaptation process, and without a doubt we can be astonished by the complexity that is intrinsic to it. Since, as we said earlier, the signs of cuneiform writing comprised two values, as logogram (word-sign) and syllabogram (syllable-sign carrying phonetic value), specific interventions were applied to these two types. For the Sumerian logograms the scribes applied a simple method: they kept them unchanged in their basic meaning, but attached to them Akkadian translations. As an example: the sign expressing the
with the cuneiform signs; from here, students would go on to learn the many different textual typologies – from hymns devoted to the kings or gods to disputations in the realm of wisdom, to letters of literary character up to myths and epic texts – mainly in Sumerian, at least in the south. In short, these é-dub-bas in cities that had represented the ancient foundations of Sumerian culture, resembled more a "grammar school" than a "high school" (rare were the mathematical exercises), and they were in any case functional to professional training. For us moderns, in any case, they have a value comparable to that of European medieval monasteries, in their extraordinary role of custodians of Sumerian culture, which was already alien to the common man of their times. (Robson; George). As discussed above, cuneiform writing represented since ancient times the vehicle of expression for the Sumerian language. This is a type of agglutinative language (like Turkish, Finnish, Korean and Japanese), with a predominant monosyllabic structure (for instance: udu = sheep, a = water, eme = language, du = to go). The exact kinship of Sumerian language remains unclear (as is the case for languages such as Ainu in Japan, ancient Etruscan, Basque or Burushaki of Pakistan); nowadays a possible Uralic origin has been proposed again with a new approach (Parpola 2016), placing it in the context of the linguistic group from which Finno-Ugric languages (Hungarian, Finnish, Estonian) are historically derived, together with a number of idioms spoken in Lapland and northern Russia. In any case all theories of a non-Mesopotamian origin of Sumerians (some think of India etc.) would imply a mass arrival of people from outside the valley between the Twin Rivers: but how and when this presumed arrival of Sumerians would have taken place remains a mystery. We also ignore if they overlapped a pre-existing population (called hypothetically ‘proto-Tigridian’) to which some –definitely non-Sumerian – names of the main Mesopotamian cities (Ur, Uruk, Eridu, Shuruppak etc) might refer. The second ancient language of Mesopotamia belongs instead clearly to the Semitic group (like ancient Hebrew and Arabic, from the Quran to present-day dialects): we call it the Akkadian language since it became the official language under the new dynasty that gained
56



From drawing to symbol: numbering, recording, authenticating
23
57



Signs Before the Alphabet
graphic characteristics. However, on closer inspection, the potential of the seals’ analysis is much wider: in fact they can reveal how, why, when and by whom they were employed, allowing thus the acquisition of precious information on the administrative and judicial organisation of Mesopotamian civilisations. Not only is it possible, in some cases, to determine the function of a specific seal, but also – thanks to the large amount of available specimens – to survey the variation of the seals’ application over time. The seals imprint on cretulae, bullae, clay tablets or labels, inform us on the sealing practice itself too. As far as we know the property rights were initially recorded via the use of seals engraved with a peculiar depiction, intended to easily identify who used it. Obviously property rights existed long before the invention of writing. Mesopotamian seals belonged both to kings and dignitaries of the palace administration, and to private citizens and even slaves; the latter used the sealing system in mechanisms of control not necessarily related to public management. Seals represented an object of great importance in a framework of collective management of resources. Findings from the preand proto-historic age include many more clay cretulae with seal impressions than the actual seals themselves. Cretulae were clay globes used to seal jars (fig. 3) and other containers (baskets, cloth or leather sacks) employed to carry goods or for the short-term storage of a variety of products, thus corresponding to the present-day use of sealing wax or lead seals. After imprinting the seal, the fresh clay would be let to dry. The impression of one or more seals on the cretulae guaranteed, from an administrative point of view, the correctness of opening, content removal, and re-sealing operations ; it allowed precise tracking of all handling operations and control of the assets contained within. Cretulae have an obverse and a reverse, the former side bearing the seal’s impression, the latter with the mark of the sealed object, whoich has oc casionally come down to us. The study of the backs of cretulae allows in several cases to establish what kind of object had been sealed, and to deduce the use it was intended for. The use of cretulae is known since the Neolithic period, more precisely from the seventh millennium BC at Tell Sabi Abyad, a site on the Balikh river in northern Syria. At this very early stage the main purpose was to simplify, in the absence of writing, operations of control and accounting related to the stored goods and the distribution of the common assets (mainly cereals but also other commodities). After opening the containers, the detached sealings were kept for a certain period of time near the place of distribution of the goods, to be used to compile
Sumerian word lugal, ‘king’, was equated to the corresponding Akkadian sharru, king’. For the syllabograms, Akkadians instead found themselves with a small repertory, which for the most part did not meet the requirements of their phonetic system; they thus put in place a generalized expansion of values. For the consonants, for instance, the sign ba also came to be equivalent to pa (voiceless/voiced exchange), the sign ad also stood for at and aṭ (=voiced/voiceless/ emphatic). Similarly, the ‘spectrum’ of vowels was expanded with ah that came to mean also ih and uh, or the signs in -i that also took on the value of –e. In some instances some values were apocopated (tam > ta; utu > tu) or, on the contrary, endings were added (bu > bum; ru > rum). With such differentiated interventions the cuneiform system of writing ended up acting as a functional vehicle for rendering Akkadic, while leaving unaltered its original structure in word-signs and syllable-signs. However, the adaptation process was long and complex, and resulting from several different traditions, needs and cultural interests, even partially in some contrast. Akkadian slowly established itself all over Mesopotamia, even though Sumerian was in use in the empire of the third Ur dynasty as a bureaucratic and administrative instrument up to 2000 BC. Two sets of Akkadian dialects developed over time, one in the South (called ‘Babylonian’) and the other in the North (‘Assyrian’): their relationship can, in very general terms, be compared to that of British and American English, both as for general proximity and for the presence of peculiar usages. Both dialects underwent significant evolutions over time, so as to allow us a precise distinction in parallel, starting from 2000 BC up to the fall of the Assyrian empire (612 BC); after that only Babylonian survived, gradually being replaced by Aramaic and Greek, up to its final disappearance shortly before 100 A.D. The collective perception of what had been written on ancient Mesopotamia’s tablets was born again only seventeen centuries later, in the mid-nineteenth century.
Early seals and their use. Rights and ownership
The use of cylinder seals developed side by side with the birth of urban civilisation and seems partially connected with writing technology. According to the data available today, cylinder seals engraved on stones of various kind (often precious) and the proto-cuneiform signs engraved on clay were born at the same time; their success and decline was also parallel. Traditionally glyptic studies were limited to the historical-artistic field and, consequently, focused essentially on stylistic and icono

fig. 13
checked by them at the time of shipping. The integrity of goods was thus guaranteed up to its delivery, when the recipients would examine the content and, if needed, could compare it to what was listed on the external bulla. Any interference that might have taken place in transit could then be easily detected, thanks to the fact that the cretula would be found broken upon arrival. Seals were thus invented to answer a practical requirement well in advance of the birth of writing. One use that emerged in the course of historical periods, with the advent of writing, was that of placing them on cuneiform tablets (cap. 24, cap. 25), as a guarantee of the authenticity of the message. In earlier periods the seal would be impressed on the tablet before it was written out while, starting from the age of Ur III, the exact opposite would occur: writing first and seal later. Seals were used specifically to authenticate receipts, ration lists, legal documents such as conveyances or loan contracts, or other official documents, for instance treaties and letters. On treaties and international agreements usually the involved rulers’ seals were impressed. For legal transactions the seals of various witnesses were required, often those of a scribe and an official as well. The function of the cylinder and stamp seal impressions on the clay tablets equated what, in our days, is represented by a signature associated to an official stamp on any public and administrative document. Thus, their value was as an authentication of a written document and they guaranteed the property of an individual, his involvement in a transaction, and the latter’s legal status. Sometimes, rather than impressing one’s own seal directly on a tablet, it would be impressed on the clay envelope that contained it (cap. 89). In the case of legal documents, the envelope generally held a summary of the content (or its whole text), including the indication of the involved parties and the seal impressions. Envelopes fell out of fashion in the first millennium BC.
the final accounting. In fact, they acted as receipts, testifying the delivery and the withdrawal of the goods stored in the container. The impression of the seal on clay or other materials probably represents the evolution of an older system that was used to identify individuals and groups via a distinctive hand-drawn sign; this would have been always the same and used to mark goods and products, so as to tell them apart from those belonging to others. The need to re-draw the same symbol several times may have encouraged the invention of an instrument like the seal, characterized by a chosen design, always identical, difficult to imitate and easily recognisable. The great ease of use of such a system offered, with the passing of time, an adaptable instrument suitable for a variety of needs of a society in continuous evolution, thus becoming integral part of an administration of growing complexity. The cretulae, when attached to doors with strings, would work as ‘locks’ employed to seal store rooms in public or private spaces. The doors would be secured with a string connected to a knob on the door frame; the string would be wrapped around the knob and covered with fresh clay, on which the officials in charge would then impress the seal. Access to the storeroom could only be possible by breaking the seal or cutting the string (fig. 6). Their main function was to guarantee against any kind of tampering; The integrity of the cretula testified the fact that the content had not been tampered with, acquiring a legal function similar to that of the modern seals with sealing wax. The use of several seals testified cross checks in the operation. The development of more complex and hierarchically-based societies led, in the course of time, to a functional evolution of the cretulae, which were applied to goods in transit as well. The officials in charge would imprint their personal seal on the goods to be traded: this way they indicated that the objects in the containers had been

Cylinder seal with deer, goats, and human figures
Seal with a V-shaped perforation in the upper part. The procession of deer and goats is divided into two rows. They move toward the left. There are also some stylized human figures in a sitting position among the animals.
Late Uruk/Jemdet Nasr Steatite 2.5 x 2.3 cm
In the Uruk period the sealing was essentially an institutional practice. Individuals would put a seal on behalf of the administration. It is not easy to establish when seals began to represent their owners as individuals, not just in terms of their relationship with an institution. The first inscriptions of personal names appeared on cylinder seals during the Early Dynastic II period, at the time of Fara. However, seals from that period still seem very much linked to the institutions, first to the temple and, starting from Early Dynastic IIIa, to the palace. In any case, the inscription represented a further guarantee for the acknowledgement and validity of financial transactions and administrative practices. For us, uch inscriptions provide precious information on the seal’s owner. Thanks to the inscriptions on the seals we know that some people owned several such objects, using them at the same time. The first seals that show an inscription are known only thanks to the impressions on tablets and other objects found at Ur, Jemdet Nasr and Uruk, dating to the first part of the third millennium BC (Jemdet Nasr period and Early Dynastic I). Some groups of signs and symbols seem to hint to names of cities and, for this reason, this particular category of seals is known in academic literature as ‘city seals’. Slightly later we begin to find names of gods in the inscriptions, and the first seals belonging with certainty to individuals, even though the inscriptions only contain the name of the owner, or his name and title. Hand in hand with the spread of cuneiform writing, inscriptions on the seals became longer and more complex, with the addition of family relations and the profession of the owner, who often described himself as the “servant” of a king: this information allows us to date an artefact with precision. Starting from the Ur III period inscriptions on seals could be very long and detailed. During the Old Babylonian period inscriptions became shorter, but they were more frequent compared to previous times: usually they consisted of three lines, the first one stating the name of the owner, the second his filiation, and the third describing the owner as the servant
of a deity or, less frequently, of the king. The professions, common until the Isin/Larsa period, all but disappeared. At the end of the Old Babylonian period the first prayers appear on seals, these are elaborations of a kind of inscription in which only gods' names were provided. Prayers and incantations became more common and longer in the following Kassite period, and usually the name of the owner was omitted. Starting from the Mittanian period we witness a sharp drop of inscriptions on seals. When found, these were, with few exceptions, relatively short. It is during this period that we find expressions like «seal (of) NAME» or «(seal) of NAME». The inscriptions were almost always written from top to bottom and from right to left, in such a way that the imprint had to be rotated 90° to be read. In the second half of the second millennium horizontal inscriptions appeared on the upper part of seals, even though the vertical ones did not disappear completely. Cylinder seals always had a direct link with the cuneiform writing on clay tablets, and the diffusion of these two important innovations progressed side by side. The presence of inscriptions on cylinder seals makes the relationship between these artefacts and cuneiform writing even stronger. What is often noticed is an inverse relationship between the length or relevance given to the inscription and the originality of the figurative motif on seals: the less distinctive the seal is from an iconographic point of view the more the inscription becomes necessary as a means of identification. When alphabetic writing on parchment, or other perishable materials, began replacing cuneiform, cylinder seals too were replaced by stamp seals, which were easier to be impressed on clay cretulae attached to documents. The intrinsic value of cylinder seals lies in the fact that they were usually made of semi-precious stones, which had to be imported, often from very remote areas. For this reason the re-use of such artefacts must have been very frequent and could take on various forms. The new owner could adopt the seal without changing its design nor the inscription, when present. The witnesses to a transaction sometimes made use of seals that were

Clay cone with cuneiform inscription
Commemorative inscription of Sin-kashid, king of Uruk (18651835 BC), with a dedication to the city gods Lugalbanda and Ninsun. The king presents a 'price list' of his kingdom: following a proven political propaganda scheme, he states that his kingdom has been particularly prosperous, as documented by the low prices of products and goods.
Origin Uruk Isin/Larsa period (19th century BC) Clay 9.2 x 5 cm
“For Lugalbanda, his god, and for Ninsun, his (divine) mother, (from) Sin-kashid, king of Uruk and (of the tribe) of Amnanum, which provides for Eanna (from) Sin-Kashid, king of Uruk and (the tribe) of Amnanum, which provides for Eanna: when he built Eanna, (also) E-kankal, their temple (and) place of heart's delight, he built for them. During his (text: their) kingship, 3 gur of barley, 12 minas of wool, 10 minas of copper, 3 measures of oil, were indeed sold for a shekel of silver the market rate in his country. May his years truly be years of prosperity!”
Celebratory inscription by Sin-kashid, king of Uruk (1865-1835 BC), with a dedication to the city gods Lugalbanda and Ninsun, also known as the parents of the hero Gilgamesh: the king compares himself with the latter in the designation 'mother' in addressing Ninsun. A topical piece follows (ll. 5-8), commemorating a building in the city.
Finally, Sin-kashid presents a 'price list' used in his kingdom: by following a proven political propaganda scheme, he states that his kingdom was particularly prosperous, as shown by the low prices of products and goods: barley, wool, copper, and oil in large quantities could be obtained by paying just a shekel of silver.
The inscription is relatively common: it is known from cones, tablets, even a diorite stele. The normal arrangement consists of 23 lines, thus about twice the present text; this also shows a copying error in line 1.8 ('they', as in the previous line, instead of the expected 'his'), not attested elsewhere. The document definitely had a certain popularity, and was copied on several occasions: thereby, the version of the stele found in Ezida, the temple of the god Nabu in the city of Borsippa, and here it was transcribed by apprentice scribes until 600 BC.
not theirs. In ratifying treaties the king could well use his predecessor’s seal, without altering it in any way. By using an earlier royal seal, the new king would without a doubt convey a strong signal of legitimation of his power. Generally, however, the new owner would make some changes to the seal he inherited or acquired in other ways. The inscription was obviously the part of the design that would most often be modified: it could be totally erased, but not necessarily replaced. In the case of more refined seals, often an inscription relevant to the new owner was engraved. The most drastic form of re-use implied the abrasion of the whole surface of the seal, in a way to make it possible to engrave a completely new motif. Beside their main functions, the bureaucratic-administrative and the judicial, seals could also be used as amulets – a magical value was attributed to specific stones and some decorative motifs as well, especially those depicting deities – or as ornaments and votive objects. These were drilled lengthwise (although in the Jemdet Nasr period a V-shaped perforation became popular: cat. 1691), allowing their owner to wear them using a string or necklace. They could be hung around the neck as necklace pendants, mounted on pins that were used to secure the clothes, or be part of a string of pearls tied to the pins themselves, as shown by an shell inlay found in the city of Mari (modern Tell Hariri), on the Syrian Middle Euphrates, and dated around 2500 BC. The inlay depicts a woman wearing a string of pearls attached to the pins securing her dress. The last of these is of cylindrical shape (fig. 7). On the basis of archaeological findings from the royal cemetery of Ur, where several seals were found associated with skeletons, it was suggested that this cylindrical pearl might have been a seal. According to E. Porada the main scope of the seal’s design was precisely apotropaic: the scene represented was conceived with the intent of having a favourable influence on the life of its owner. In this respect it is opportune to note that many seals seem to have been carved by non-professional engravers, possibly by
scribes – who were probably priests – for specific ritual uses. This would imply that behind the seal designs a certain ideological or spiritual conception was hidden. To sum up, Mesopotamian seals, of any type and in any period, appear to have been used, first of all, to mark belongings and, by extension, to protect what had been marked. This last feature gave the seal a more general apotropaic value: the legitimate owner and bearer, wearing the seal, felt protected. Long prayers recorded on Kassite period seals also refer back to this context.
Weights and measures
In a pre-monetary economy such as the Mesopotamian one, based on the exchange of goods, the role of weights and measures was obviously of primary importance in order to establish standards related to copper or silver, both for private merchants and for the State. The weight units have not been clearly identified in the archaic texts of the beginning of the third millennium BC yet. They are documented with certainty only from the Fara period onward. Nevertheless, measurements with weights were certainly carried out even in ancient times. Inscribed (or somehow marked) exemplars are known since Pre-Sargonic times. Very different standards seem to have been in existence since the Akkadian period.
The standard Akkadian-Old Babylonian system was thus the following (with approximate values): • barley grain ~ 0.04629629 g. • small shekel (Sumerian gin-tur, Akkadian šiqlu .sa ̆hru) ~ 0.13888 g.
• small mina (Sumerian ma-na-tur, Akkadian manu .sa ̆hru) ~ 2.777 g.
• shekel ~ 8,333 g. • mina ~ 500 g. • talent ~ 30 kg. Specimens of Babylonian weights, which have survived in considerable quantities since they were frequently found in the excavations

Duck-shaped weights
Animals represented at rest, with their necks and heads resting on their backs. One of them depicts two ducks side by side in the opposite position.
Various period - Early Dynastic, Ur III, Old Babylonian periods Various stones
Left: length 12 cm; 150 g. above: length 15 cm; 810 g. right: length 13 cm; 370 g. below: length 10 cm; 200 g.
(many of which are to be found in museums, whereas only a few were studied), provide evidence of a somewhat haphazard technological precision. These weights were certainly not conceived to provide universal standards, but rather for everyday purposes of practical measurement. In the first millennium BC the Akkadian and Old Babylonian norms remained the basis of the weight system. For instance, the inscription on a weight of Nebuchadnezzar II, king of Babylon (605-562 BC), relates that it was a copy of a weight of Shulgi, the second king of the Third Dynasty of Ur (2094-2047 BC according the middle chronology). In the Neo-Assyrian system the most significant difference is represented by the use of the double mina (ca. 1000 g) next to the traditional mina (ca. 500 g), as testified by weights that can be dated between the ninth and seventh century BC The precise moment when the double mina was introduced - thus becoming the implicit standard in Neo-Assyrian texts, except when otherwise specified – is not clear. The terms ‘mina of the king’ and ‘mina of the Land’ were found on a series of Assyrian weights from the seventh century BC. These are in the shape of bronze lions and the texts indicate official weighing standards. They are written both in Assyrian cuneiform and Aramaic alphabetic script. Perhaps the ‘mina of the Land’ is the one known from the texts as ‘mina of Carchemish’, that is, corresponding to measures in use in the western part of the Assyrian empire (Fales 2016). Both terms are applied to the ‘light’ mina and to the double, or ‘heavy’ mina.syrian and Aramaic – seventh century BC. Ancient Mesopotamians thus used groups of weights of various shapes and weight value in the conduct of their affairs, as a counterpart for exchanged goods (silver or cereals). Very strict penalties were imposed on those (especially merchants) who used false or altered weights . The weights could be made in various stones; very frequent are those in hematite. The simple barrel shape was the most common one, but weights in the shape of a duck at rest, with the neck and
head resting along the back, were widespread as well (cap. 28). Weights and measures were also rhetorically used to show the country’s wealth: texts from various different rulers tended to highlight the fact that, under their reign, the value of silver corresponded to large amounts of other goods. One example is given by a clay cone (cap. 27) displaying a celebrative inscription of Sin-kashid, king of Uruk (ca. 1865-1835 BC) with twelve lines in Sumerian.

From drawing to symbol: numbering, recording, authenticating 65



Signs Before the Alphabet
products or goods, because they were used to record the amount of transported objects. This was a control system for the goods or objects brought from one place to another. There is evidence of them among the Sumerians. But also in Elam - the 'land of God' - where, starting from the 4th millennium, an urban revolution began and where archaeologists have found hollow 'spherical bullae' , with traces on the outer surface of cylinder seals. These bullae contained 'tokens' of the conventional value corresponding to the quantity and quality of foodstuffs, animals, and objects. Writing did not exist yet. The need to count animals, goods, and things was strong, even vital: 7,500 years ago, the accounting system was already coded with the use of tokens and bullae modeled in clay. After two centuries or so, and, to avoid having to break the bullae they began to be marked on the outside by engraving the designs of the tokens that were inside: this way, the bullae did not have to be broken (except in exceptional cases) in order to control the amount of goods in transit or in arrival. It was soon realized (a few decades had gone by, and we are now around 7,250 years ago) that it was no longer necessary to use the bullae with tokens inside, but simply use loaves of clay with inscriptions that were, in practice, the prototypes of numerical digits. The 'loaves of clay' then became tablets and at the turn of the 6th millennium before the present, the digits became flanked by thousands of letters. Proto-Elamite and proto-Sumerian script - both would undergo certain phases of transformation over the centuries - developed in almost in parallel. The use of numbers and figures were more or less contemporary in both the Iranian coast of the Persian Gulf and the area between the Tigris and Euphrates, which we now call Mesopotamia and where the Sumerians (4,700 years ago) went from a pictographic writing system, which involved the use of thousands of signs, to a phonetic cuneiform method, where the use of few dozens of signs sufficed. For their calculating procedure, the Sumerians choose a compression system of '60'. They preferred it to what might be the more logical method based on 10, thereby introducing a 'sexagesimal' system based on 60 and its multiples. The Babylonians also used this method, the same one that we still practice for measuring angles and time. Probably in analogy with the revolution of the sun around the earth, completed in about 360 days, the “turn” angle is 360 degrees. Hours are composed of 60 minutes and the minutes contain 60 seconds, for a total of 3,600 seconds every hour. The sexagesimal system required naming all the numbers up to 60; so, in order not to deal with too many different names, they named the decimal units up to 10, and the tens up to 60, as we do now. This caused a mixed system of counting, sexagesimal and decimal, capable of providing importance not only to powers of 60, such as 3,600, but also to the decimal multiples, such as 600 and 36,000. In other words, a sexagesimal numbering built on the alternating basis of 10 and 6. In the Archaic period, the unit was made with a simple notch, a group of 10
Sumerian numbers
Piergiorgio Odifreddi Mathematician, logician
Calculi. That is to say, stones. Calculus, i.e. stone. The word calculus comes from the diminutive of calx, calcis, or 'construction rubble'; 'pebble'. We still use this term for kidney stones nowadays. Or in the bile. For numerical calculations, it is a term that has now become a metaphor. The word had an amplified semantic field also in Greek: psephos meant both 'pebble' and 'count'. Just as psephos tithenai meant both to 'lay a stone' and 'make an account'. Thus, lumps of clay modeled as cylinders, cones, and spheres of various sizes were found in the excavations sites in the Near East; in Turkey and India. The dates in which this counting system started are different: from the 9th millennium to the 2nd millennium BCE. And starting in the 4th millennium, these stones were collected and packed in clay containers called bullae. Our accompanying bolle (waybills) that are in use even now, derive their name from the bullae that once served as transport documents for
66



was a small circle, sixty was a thick mark, and the number 600 was a notch, equal to 60, which was contained in the circle used to indicate 10 (60x10). A large circle was used for 3,600 (60x60), and for 36,000 (3,600x10) the same large circle with a small circular sign the same size as the tens inside of it. These forms underwent a counterclockwise rotation of 90 degrees, and afterwards, there was also a transformation of the Sumerian characters (in the pre-Sargonic period - Sargon of Akkad was king from 2335 to 2279 BC), almost certainly due to a change of material for engraving the clay (from a point to a small stick, a quill of bone or ivory) which allowed to produce signs that were more visible and precise, segments of straight lines that formed more angular characters. The unit became a small vertical nail, the number ten was a punch, sixty was a large vertical nail, 600 was a large nail with a punch, the number 3,600 was four nails that formed a polygon, and 36,000 a similar polygon with an arrow. Shuruppak, a town on the Euphrates River, is the location where archaeologists found the oldest evidence of an arithmetic calculation that is is not at all trivial: a tablet that bears a division with the dividend, divisor, and remainder. Practically, this is a problem asking to divide the amount of barley contained in a barn when giving “seven sila of barley each, with the remainder of three” to a certain number of men. How many men are there? To resolve the 'school exercise' question, it turns out that it is 164,571 men, workmen to be exact. The sila (unit of measurement) was a size of just over eight-tenths of a liter and the barn contained about 970,000 liters; those numbers indicated the units of measure/capacity for both. 4,666 years have gone by since the time of that division problem - which the scribe almost certainly resolved by using the calculi in rather interesting progressive steps. The numerical cultures of the Sumerians and Elamites used an additive system, pretty much the same as the one subsequently used by the Egyptians, Greeks, Romans, and Aztecs, and many other ancient peoples. It was a system that had some advantages but also many disadvantages. The Sumerians sensed the negative aspects of the mechanism to the point that they began to introduce a notation for 10 in their sexagesimal system. But the big step in mathematics was to get to the 'positional' system, which was a rather slow and gradual path. The tablets found at Mari, a city destroyed by Hammurabi 3,761 years ago, indicate that the numbers up to a thousand were written in a 'positional' way of units-tens-hundreds. And those above this figure remained in the ancient Sumerian system. It was thus the Babylonians' turn, at a time close to that of the destruction of Mari, to change the mixed system into a definitive positional system, the first one in history. For instance, the numbers between 1 and 59 were indicated with combinations of only two symbols; a vertical nail indicated the units and a horizontal punch stood for the tens. It was necessary to differentiate between 2,300, 2,030, 2,003, 230, 203, and 23: the Babylonians chose to leave an empty space in the appropriate places. But it was not easy
to make out, and only the context or the very nature of the problem helped to interpret the numbers correctly. It was only after the Assyrian conquest and imperial restoration - that is, roughly between the ages of Nebuchadnezzar and the Seleucids (2,600 and 2,300 years ago) - that people became aware of the need to indicate not only the presence but also the absence of a basic power. And the 'empty seat' was indicated by the inclination of a double nail, a symbol already used in ancient cuneiform script to divide the text from a comment, or to indicate the transition from one language to another. Only in the 2nd century, Ptolemy began to use the initial 'o' of 'ouden', meaning empty, as a symbol; a symbol that was also used in the terminal position of a number. The numerical adventure of the cultures living in the area commonly called “Assyro-Babylonian” is full of particularities and stories related to archaeological finds, although the decisive contribution to the study of the mathematics on the tablets will take place between the '30s and '60s of the last century, over a hundred years after the interpretation of the ancient cuneiform writings. As in the case of a tablet of the Yale Babylonian Collection (YBC 7289), on display some years ago in an exhibition in New York, that has a small clay disc (dating 1900/1600 BC) bearing a square with diagonal lines. In the center, there is a number: 1,24,51,10, a sexagesimal number that corresponds to 1,41421296, the decimal number, which is the square root of 2 approximated to the millionth. From what is known of the Babylonians, they had not discovered 'the Pythagorean theorem' 1,300 years before Pythagoras himself; and moreover, the archeological finds have not brought to light any tablet which might contain the equation of Pythagoras. But they knew calculi, and they knew them quite well indeed.
