technology, a comparable complexity of logical and practical steps is faced daily, from the challenging use of computer keyboards to enter alpha-numeric data which replicate writing, with their myriad of tactile and linguistic combinations for encoding and decoding information; not to speak of the universe of different programming languages, with their complicated vocabulary, syntax and semantics, completely apart from the actual spoken and written language ... From this perspective we may be less surprised by the consideration that Cuneiform remained in use over three full millennia, and that it survived deeply relevant historical, linguistic and administrative mutations, as well as substantial variations in the applications of writing. Also, despite the introduction of more “democratic” systems of alphabetic script – such as those of Greek and Aramaic – it survived for many centuries more. In particular, cuneiform gave rise to various subsystems, some of which were extremely complicated, others quite straightforward and easy to learn. In a nutshell, cuneiform had very different meanings for completely different users; its extreme longevity
Variety of texts, languages and scripts
Frederick Mario Fales
Cuneiform script was a flexible and versatile system, one that offered an efficient instrument for communication purposes and record-keeping practice, but it also produced a complex symbolic system, one that pushed ancient intellectuals to explore and investigate the representational potential it contained. Cuneiform script was in itself quite intricated with its many signs (more than 600, with a “basic packet” of some 150 for the average scribe); each of which was associated to a variety of meanings and different readings – something that, at first sight, may undoubtedly seem excessively cumbersome and impractical to us “moderns”. We could question how to distinguish syllable-signs and word-signs, determinative signs (not to be pronounced) indicating specific semantic categories, and numerical signs, keeping in mind that some signs belonged to several among these categories. How to deal, in any case, with a system whose signs could be polysemic (i.e. with two or more syllabic values) or homophonic (i.e. with two or more signs that could represent the same syllable)? In point of fact, however, in today’s world, strongly influenced by digital

Tablet bearing Sumerian cuneiform script
The document refers to the monthly series of Umma. It is conceived as a list of rations (the latter indicated by gur, the measure of capacity for grain) with internal partitions, according to the sequence of months.
Origin Umma Ur III period Clay 5.8 x 4.9 x 2 cm
Obverse
1. the month of the middle (=4th) 2. gur
3. the month of the divisor (?) (=5th) 4. gur 5.the month of sowing (=6th) 6. gur
7. the month of the festival of the (divine king) Amar-Suen (=7th) 8. gur
9. the month the temple of the six months (=8th)
Reverse l. gur
2. the month of the festival of the goddess Lisi (=9th) 3. gur
4. the month of the festival of the (divine king) Shulgi (=10th) 5. gur
6. the month of the god Pau'e (=11th) 7. gur
8. the month of the god Dumuzi (=12th) 9. gur
The text is of considerable historical interest for the local calendars in force in different parts of the empire of the Ur III Dynasty. For this phase of history we know of four different series of month names: the calendars (l) of Ur itself, also in use at Puzrish-Dagan; (2) of Lagash, (3) of Nippur (the series that will become 'canonical' in the Akkadian translation from the Old Babylonian period onward, and which will provide the basis for the Jewish calendar), and (4) of Umma. All the series were of 12 months on the basis of lunar cycles (= 354 days) Therefore, they required the addition of an 'intercalary' month, even though such a correction appears to have been implemented with considerable irregularities in the different areas and periods, until around the 7th century BC.
Our document is related to the monthly series of Umma. It is conceived as a list of rations (the latter indicated by g u r, the measurement of capacity for barley) with internal partitions, according to the sequence of months. However, perhaps because it was a text which served as a model for the drafting of other similar documents, indications of the amount of g u r for the individual months are left 'blank'. Therefore, what remains are only the g u r signs followed, every other row, by the names of the months of Umma.
Partially damaged, the tablet retains the signs of the 4th - 8th months on the obverse, and the 9th - 12th months on the reverse. The entry for the 7th month is particularly interesting: in fact, its usual name is 'the month of the two shrines', but our document has 'the month of the (religious) festival of Amar-Suen'. This monthly event was already known, albeit sporadically attested; however, it had not yet found a 'place' in the sequence of months from Umma. From this text, therefore, we learn that the 'month of the festival of Amar-Suen' occupied - starting from a certain point during the reign of that king – the seventh place in the sequence, although without entirely or stably replacing the 'month of the two shrines'.
Finally, it should be noted that both Amar-Suen and his father Shulgi presented the 'determinative' sign for a god: they had in fact been deified, according to a practice started by Naram-Sin of Akkad (cf. ch. 7a).
Variety of texts, languages and scripts 71



Signs Before the Alphabet
and to do so over nearly three thousand years of history, makes Mesopotamia totally unique from a documentary point of view. It is worth noting that the opposite is true for the Greek and Roman civilisations, both completely laconic – albeit with some significant exceptions – especially regarding the finest details of bureaucratic and administrative organisation, thus leaving historians in the dark about several aspects of the social and economic development of these historical horizons. On the contrary it is precisely these administrative records from ancient Mesopotamia that allow, through patient and meticulous mining of the archives, a fairly accurate reconstruction of many aspects: from primary production l(agricultural produce and animal husbandry), to secondary production (food, textile, metallurgical industries and so on). In fact these documents were often made out to justify to the authorities in charge that no irregularities or unallowed appropriation of funds and goods had taken place, no matter the amount, on a daily, monthly and annual basis. From these documents we can draw detailed snapshots, e.g. of the delivery of raw materials to individual departments or workshops, of the allocation of materials to craftsmen and specialists, of the supply of finished products and their delivery for specific purposes (building activities, transport, public works of various kinds), but also for community activities such as offerings to the gods, religious festivities or war. To be sure, Mesopotamian administrative archives especially illustrate the complex activities pertaining to the power centres of the palace and the temple, with their élites including the rulers, clergy, subordinate officials operating in specific departments an offices, or at times located in capital cities and provincial districts. However, some archives, although less frequently, provide an insight in the reality of farming villages and even of pastoral communities living in the uninhabited areas of the steppe insofar as these social groups could claim the attention of the political or religious centre.
can therefore be explained by the fact that it was able to respond to varied and diversified needs, both in content and language (Veldhuis 2011). In this chapter we will attempt to give an idea of the variety of uses to which the immense corpus of texts in cuneiform script were put to, over the course of some 3000 years. We will start with a glance at the main typologies of texts in Sumerian and Akkadian from Mesopotamia and surrounding regions, such as are represented in the collection we are here dealing with, including materials from the simplest to the most refined. We will next move on to the constellation of languages that utilised cuneiform writing beyond Mesopotamia’s boundaries, in a geographic span covering all mountainous areas surrounding the Land of the Twin Rivers. Then we will look at the brief phase of the Late Bronze age, when Akkadian enjoyed the role of international and diplomatic language, much as French did in the nineteenth century and English in our time. Finally, starting from the city of Ugarit of the Late Bronze age, when the first experiment of a cuneiform alphabet took place, we will move on to the earliest results of linear alphabetic writing, in Phoenician and Aramaic: in that area of the Levant where the cuneiform universe found its deadliest rival, also because clay suitable for engraving signs was scarcer than in the Land of the Twin Rivers.
Accounting and administrative texts; juridical texts
The earliest function of cuneiform writing was the recording of administrative and accounting transactions. It is therefore not surprising to note that -at the end of the day - the "hard core" of Mesopotamian documentation of all ages consists of registers of personnel, animals and everyday consumer items; these range from the mosr simple and casual to those requested and organized by central bureaucracies of the State or the temples. This constant ability to keep a written record of realia, even in extremely varied environmental and economic circumstances,

“Messenger Text” bearing Sumerian cuneiform script
Text of the group of the socalled 'messenger texts': a very detailed list of rations which includes beer of various qualities, bread, fat, olive oil, and different types of flour. The recipients of the rations are individuals or groups: regarding the first-mentioned, their professional function is often clarified, regarding the latter, their origin is declared.
Origin Lagash
Ur III period (between 2065 and 2005 BC) Clay 5.5 x 3.3 x 1.5 cm
Obverse
52 sila of beer, 50 (sila) of the king's bread, (for the group) of guardians. 4 sila of beer, 6 sila of bread, (for the group) of the palace. 5 sila of beer, 3 sila of bread, 2 gin of oil, 2 gin of fat, for Nanna-kuzu, the leader of the armed men. 3 sila of beer, 2 sila of bread, 2 gin of oil for (the stay) in the city. one of ordinary weak beer, l0 (sila) of coarse meal, 15 gin of oil ... for Shu-irra. 50 (sila) of coarse flour, 10 (sila) of ...

Reverse
Shu-irra is responsible. 3 sila of beer, 2 sila of bread, 2 gin of oil, for Shimshikh, the superintendent of the garden. 200 (sila) of high quality beer, 200 (sila) of high grade legume flour, to Da'a'a, who went to Elam. Ur-Nigin-gar is responsible. 5 sila of beer, l0 (sila) of course flour, l sila of olive oil for those in the city; 2 of weak quality beer, 20 (sila) of flour as travel provisions, (for) Khukha the rider; Responsiibles Lu-Ningirsu, son of Ur-Baba. 2 sila of bread for a military who went to ....., Ur-Nigin-gar is responsible. 2 sila of beer, 2 sila of bread, 2..., withdrawn. On the 15th, 11th month.
Sumerian text of the group of the 'tablets of the messengers': a very detailed list of rations which includes beer of various qualities, bread, fat, olive oil, and flour of different types. The recipients of the rations are individuals or groups: of the former, the professional function is often specified, of the latter, the provenience. The type of the text shows that this tablet came from Lagash.
The 'messengers' are an administrative class of the Ur III Dynasty, perhaps of not particularly high rank, whose duties to a large extent appear to be of a temporary type, such as to be sent on missions to distant sites. In this sense, it is likely that the tasks assigned to the 'messengers' had to do with the sphere of communications between cities, or with the safety of the road network. Both options certainly apply well to the regions of Lagash and Umma, archival locations where the texts regarding this group of administrators were found: in particular, in this period, Lagash seems to have had the function of the caravan city for the urban locations of Elam, arranged in the arc of a circle on its eastern front.
In concrete terms, the 'messengers' are known to us only through the long lists of food rations, divided as to 'items', relating to individuals or small groups. The rations are in fixed amounts, and moreover generally scanty (i.e. just above the subsistence limit), and related to a few essential staples: we find beer, barley bread, onions, oil, and mixed spices, while dried fish is listed, but not continuously.
The beer must have been of the usual type in the Sumerian age, that is, obtained by the combination of barley and water with the addition of malt to give it flavour; different qualities existed ('light', 'normal', 'strong', 'royal'), of which, however, only the poorer types seem to have been assigned to the 'messengers'. Bread also had a series of internal differentiation (for example, there was 'royal bread'); onions were apparently provided in a sort of 'braid'. Overall, we are dealing with extremely basic types of food, which, however, were prepared against perishability, in direct relation to the mobility of this particular administrative group.
Moreover, they open even wider research horizons of great interest, from precise chronological reconstructions (through the dating formulae within the texts), to historical geography (reconstruction of the topography or physical peculiarities of specific areas), to prosopography (by relating names and professions, which form a wider socio-historical mesh), to metrological evaluations (weights, measures and, where possible, prices of goods and services at different times and places). All these areas of research related to the historiography of the ancient Near East are nowadays evolving fast; in fact, they owe their existence
In any case, however detailed or nuanced, the snapshots provided by the administrative documents have the advantage of being realistic, and above all immune from the pompous and self-aggrandizing propaganda that often characterizes the official documents issued by kings and rulers. Indeed they are records of everyday life, which provide the historian with endless glimpses of the technologies and materials employed, of the organisation of the workforce, both individually and collectively, and finally of the various possible ethno-linguistic identities involved in the productive and commercial processes. (cap. 30).
Variety of texts, languages and scripts 75



Signs Before the Alphabet
as offerings for the temple, or as due tax from the provinces or loot/ audience gifts from foreign states, e.g. Subartu in Upper Mesopotamia or Shimashki in neighbouring Iran (cap. 31). Among the animals transiting via Puzrish-Dagan we find cattle, goats and sheep --wild as well as bred--, but also gazelles, deer, bears, pigs, wild boars from the marshes and various species of birds. From Puzrish-Dagan, the animals were redistributed to the shrines, to the Royal Family, and as salary to government officials and military. Such a complex management required a number of bureaucrats, dedicated to collecting the animals and to providing to their well being and finally to their distribution. The central archive also kept the records relevant to the peripheral centres of collection and preliminary destination. The officials working at these peripheral centres were entrusted with very detailed record keeping; these records included the loss of animals, their natural death or stillbirth, the increased numbers of heads due to births, all elements that appear transcribed on the tablets (cap. 32). Other neo-Sumerian texts of the Third Ur dynasty come from Umma, present day Jokha They are more concerned with agriculture, canal maintenance and other matters (cap. 33, cap. 29, cap. 35). Yet more documents come from Lagash, such as texts on the “messengers” (cap. 34) who trod the communication routes with Elam. The Neo-Babylonian period (spanning the sixth and fifth centuries BC) has yielded three large accounting archives coming from the main temples of the land: Ebabbar dedicated to the god Shamash in the city of Sippar in Northern Babylonia, with around 30.000 tablets, Eanna in Uruk, dedicated to Inanna/Ishtar, with 9,000 texts and, finally, several hundred documents on clay tablets from the major temple in Babylon, the Esagila, still partially unpublished (Jursa 2004). Typologically, the tablets from these archives record the transfer of goods (cap. 36), or at least the transfer of responsibility between individuals, with the temple implicitly involved in the trans
to the exceptional availability of accountancy and administrative documents written in the cuneiform script. Virtually every stage of the historical development, and every geographical niche, of Mesopotamia have provided textual materials of administrative character. However, some specific contexts have left us totally exceptional archives, such as to enable a particularly wide-ranging and in-depth reconstruction of the socio-economic mechanisms on which they were based. A glance at these major archives may here be limited to two phases, far between in time: the age of the Third Ur Dynasty (twenty-first century BC) and that of the Neo-Babylonian empire. It is interesting to observe that they were both short-lived phases in the overall context of Mesopotamia’s political history, yet similar insofar as administrative activity, in their short duration, was extremely rich and detailed. Some 90,000 documents of economic and administrative nature illuminate the development and complexity of the state structure of the Third Ur Dynasty (Streck 2010, 53): this represents the highest number of cuneiform texts for a single century of Mesopotamian history at our disposal. From this body of evidence we can extrapolate endless indications on the different sectors of public administration of the time, and specifically on the management of agricultural resources, metallurgy, animal husbandry, river navigation and much more. We are hereby going to examine one of the two major archives from those days. The centre of Puzrish-Dagan, present-day Drehem, not far from Nippur, acted as the headquarters for the gathering and distribution of animals, an item that made up a sizeable part of Ur III state revenues. Puzrish-Dagan was founded by Shulgi in the late years of his reign (Sallaberger – Westenholz 1999:147) and continued under the rule of his sons Shu-Su'en and Amar-Su'en; Puzrish-Dagan was the centre for the gathering of a wide range of animals: wild animals, livestock and poultry delivered both as gifts to the royal family, or
35
Tablet bearing Sumerian cuneiform script
The document regards the weighing of hoes and other copper objects. The weighing was intended to control the ratio between the crude metal provided by the state apparatus and the finished product.

necessarily record it, taking instead refuge mostly behind well-worn formulas to describe the different phases or details of the transaction. Surely a myriad of transactions (perhaps the most occasional or minute) were only stipulated verbally; but the ones we have indicate that writing was a founding element, viz. that jurisprudence was expressed through the written act. Maintaining, as it has been done (Westbrook in Westbrook [Ed.] 2003), that Mesopotamian contractual texts were, in large majority, mere protocols of verbal transactions made in front of witnesses, means disregarding first of all the fundamental “testimonial” value of calling into question the great gods, who are often invoked in the texts themselves; it also ignores the importance of personal seals, sometimes added to the tablets by the witnesses to the stipulation of the act – to testify their presence – and, finally,fails to take into account the possibility, often expressly indicated, of breaking the tablet once the deed had exhausted the function for which it had been drawn up. In short, orality (any cultic act, the legal defence of the parties, even solemn oaths) certainly was part and parcel of the “ceremonial” that accompanied the legal act, but the text that was the end result, however bare and essential, had a binding value.
Lexical lists
The Mesopotamian scribal school (edubba) is clearly documented for the first time in the late third millennium BC, as functional to the learning of Sumerian with the aim of transferring traditional Mesopotamian culture from one generation to the next. However, the focus of teaching was placed on the study and copying of lexical lists of various kinds: this is a type of text that appears well established from the times of the earliest archives of Southern Mesopotamia (Uruk and Jemdet Nasr, then Fara, Shuruppak etc.). The lexical lists can, at their basic level, be distinguished between lists of signs and lists of words. The sign lists were designed to document and teach the complete inventory of signs and their correct use, organized according to the signs’ shape, in an order of growing complexity (signs with horizontal,
action. There are cases of multiple items collected in a single account. Legal documents are another category spanning the full extent of Mesopotamian history, from the Early Dynastic period onward. We will exclude here the texts of international law (such as pacts or treaties) and also the so-called “law codes”, in fact all those normative acts which had as their object one or more communities; we will concentrate instead on the huge available documentation of “transactional” nature, i.e. relevant to specific transactions involving individuals and groups in their private or sometimes corporate interests. Mesopotamian legal documents characterize every archive that has come down to us, from the vast collections found in public buildings to the modest sets of documents kept at home. They document land sales, loans with interest (cap. 37), aspects of family law (inheritance, adoption, donations of family members to the temple, etc) or the rarer records of lawsuits discussed in court; they were written on clay or, less frequently, on stone; the sentences could be of a pecuniary nature or involving indentured servitude, at times even the ordeal to settle a litigation. Their interest is thus at the same time diachronic and synchronic for the historian: on one hand he can witness the evolution of one judicial custom or another over time, on the other hand he can compare the legal documents within a single period of time to obtain a cross-section of social trends in progress. At the end of the day, the main point is that juridical Mesopotamian texts, in their variety and quantity, are a record of law in the making, seen through eyes that are strictly contemporary to the events, without secondary distortions of a literary of ideological nature. On the other hand, what we have are not deeds that “make the law”, but rather that operated within theframework of existing jurisprudence, deriving from the commonly accepted regulatory custom. And it is therefore solely by inner comparison between different contemporary deeds, with their different legal outcomes, that we can try to re-enact the “shared feeling” that could have been behind them. The Mesopotamian legal deed that has come down to us is in the shape of written text; it presupposes orality as its source, but does not
38
Clay cylinder with a list of names and professions
The document is a copy of an earlier Sumerian text, i.e. from the period of Fara or Abu Salabikh (ca. 2600-2500 BC). The original inscription is known as 'the list of names and professions'; it was found in the Abu Salabikh archives as a standardized list made into multiple copies for training the scribes of this center of southern Mesopotamia in the Early Dynastic period.
Ur III period Clay 8 x 9.8 cm
Cylinder of reddish clay, preserved for about one half. A vertical split delimits (a) a 5-column text, which in turn is divided into boxes, and (b) about half of the top lid. Drawn on the latter is a rectangle containing a further inscription, also divided into lines separated by horizontal rulings. A second, horizontal, split has damaged the individual columns of text: also on this side, therefore, the document is reduced to about half of its original length, as can be ascertained by the existing parallels.
The text engraved on this clay cylinder can be dated palaeographically as from the beginning of the Ur III period (21st century BC), although some of the signs were no longer in use at that time. Instead, from the point of view of content, the document is a copy of a Sumerian text from a more ancient period, i.e. the period of Fara or Abu Salabikh (ca. 2600-2500 BC).
The criteria for the order of the list known as “names and professions list” - attested on large square tablets with 12 columns - seem to have been a sequence of three elements: a personal name, a professional title, and a place name, although the complexity of the Sumerian language in this archaic phase gives rise to some doubt. Moreover, we do not know just how much the list of Abu Salabikh reflected cases taken from the real world (i.e. actually existing human or geographical entities), and how much, instead, could have been the result of random juxtapositions, for the sole purpose of promoting the ability of the compilers in the scribal art.
Variety of texts, languages and scripts 79



Signs Before the Alphabet
With the full establishment of the Akkadian language in spoken and written use, and thus well aware of the risk of obsolescence for Sumerian, the scribes of the first centuries of the second millennium (Old Babylonian period) proceeded to make out a vast series of bilingual lexical lists. Such lists were compiled, in their simplest form, on the model of the earliest mono-lingual Sumerian lists, with the simple addition of further columns containing the equivalents in Akkadian: for example, the list called (from its incipit) IZI=ishatu was ordered according to the shape of Sumerian signs, both simple and composite. Other bilingual lists presented greater complexity: the 32 tablets (ca. 10,500 entries) from the SIG7.ALAN = nabnitu series were based on the Sumerian correspondences with verbal or nominal Akkadian roots, according to an organisation “from head to toe”, related to the parts and functions of the human body; while erim-hush = anantu (7 tablets) was centred on the multiplicity of Akkadian synonyms to render a given Sumerian term. This said, we must however note that the main difference between the horizon of monolingual Sumerian texts and that of Old Babylonian times does not reside so much in the addition of a second language as in the organisation of a true learning curriculum for the scribe: in fact, scribes from the Hammurabi period structured the sequences of lists to be studied in the edubba in a way that would introduce students, step by step, to aspects of growing complexity in the writing system. To sum up, the lexical lists represent an important product of Sumero-Akkadian culture. By themselves they demonstrate that “words are things”, i.e. that giving/finding a name for each aspect of reality, and thus being able to put it down in writing, represented one of the most effective and precise means to appropriate that aspect. These texts have obviously little in common with dictionaries the way we conceive
angular, vertical lines, etc., with elements in growing number). Word lists were instead organized mostly by meanings, thus opening the way for thematic breakdowns, such as lists of professional names, birds, stones, place-names, etc. These lists are among the earliest known; in some cases – such as names of professions – there may be some internal groupings in a hierarchic light, but elsewhere their order is more casual and, at most, simply provided by lexical and semantic similitude. Such lists were copied over and over in the course of millennia: naturally what did change was the ductus (the way signs were written) in the various periods, but not the content that gradually became more archaic and remote from practical contemporary use (cap. 38). In some lists primary words (such as “sheep”) were not the only ones listed, but their qualifying attributes appeared as well, for instance “fattened sheep” or even “sheep eaten by a lion”: from this we can understand that the pupil not only would find entities attested in administrative and account-keeping documents, but also in narratives of a literary nature. The thematic principle was not, however, the only one behind the lists” organisation. Some combined sign lists could be organized according to an acrographic principle, that is, an organisation starting with the first sign: for instance, all combinations of words or sentences, where the sign a, “water”, came first, like in the following example:
a šu min bal “second irrigation” a šu eš5 bal “third irrigation” a bal-bal “irrigation water” a bal-bal-bi-še3 “water for libation” a-bal-e “to pour water” illu (=A.KAL) “flood water” (Veldhuis 2013, 9).

Cone with inscription in Sumerian cuneiform script
Commemorative inscription of Gudea, recalling the construction of the temple of the god protecting the city, Ningirsu, which was called é-ninnu. The sanctuary is provided here with an additional name, 'bright anzubird', with reference to the lion-headed eagle frequently attested in Sumerian mythology and contemporary figurative art. The deity is also presented with an epithet, 'strong hero of the god Enlil', thus, as an important divine figure in the presence of the absolute head of the Sumerian pantheon.

Signs Before the Alphabet
the military feats now leads to yearly reports: thus the modern historian manages to capture the rhythm of annual war campaigns, from departure to return, even managing to evaluate the kilometres travelled daily by the Assyrian army (Fales 2001). In parallel, royal self-aggrandizement increases as well, by now mercilessly: the Assyrians never lose a battle, in the field or through siege; enemy kings are all to be blamed for serious character faults, from insanity to wickedness (as opposed to natural and exceptional moral qualities of “our” sovereign); foreign landscapes to be conquered are all extremely harsh, for their inaccessible rocky or desert nature; and, ultimately, world order cannot be achieved except by a humble adherence to pax assyriaca. In short, the Assyrians created an overall system of historiographical discourse, which included and circumscribed the reality of the time and place in which they lived. There remain, however, some unresolved elements that give rise to a host of problems for the modern historian: what was the intended audience for these texts, written in multiple copies in a literary Babylonian dialect, that decorated limestone slabs, clay tablets and sometimes precious metal artefacts found throughout the Assyrian empire? And why, in addition to the abundance of literary depictions and topical propaganda employed, did these texts never find space for a detailed description of the military techniques that were the "flagship" of the Assyrian power, leaving instead the illustration of the most minute details of sieges, pursuits, pitched battles with the enemy to the astonishingly realistic bas-reliefs on the walls of the royal palaces? Could it be that these bombastic military conquest texts were to be read while at the same time contemplating the sculpted 'films' engraved on the walls of the Throne Rooms?
Diffusion of the Cuneiform system: Anatolia, Elam, Urartu
Anatolia
The early instances of the cuneiform system of writing in Anatolia date to the nineteenth to eighteenth centuries, when Assyrian merchants established their main commercial entrepot in Kanesh, Cappadocia. However, despite the large number of clay tablets written there and which have survived to our days, there is no trace leading back to possible seats of teaching of writing for the benefit of the local inhabitants of Kanesh; presumably these communicated among themselves and with the Assyrian using the indigenous idiom, Hattic. After the middle of the second millennium the Hittites – one of the many Indo-European populations reaching Anatolia from the East around 2000 BC – took control of the Anatolian productive and commercial systems; they were based in the more central and convenient location of Hattusa (present day Bogazköy) on the river Kizilirmak, starting to attain the same level as the other Western Asian great states (Egypt, Mittani, Babylonia and Assyria). From the middle of the fourteenth century, finally, after the defeat of Mittani, their main political and military adversary, the Hittites took possession of several Syrian cities and established a viceroyalty in Karkemish, on the Upper Euphrates. The peculiar orthography of cuneiform Hittite texts comes from the contact with scribal environments from Northern Syria, these number ca. 30,000 exemplars, primarily from the archives of Hattusa: this is one of the few archives of which scriptorial and administrative practice can be followed over various centuries. Some documents have reached us in various copies: these include historiographic
them nowadays, essentially for the great variety of ordering principles established in one or the other list; but also, because the role of memory in the practical-scholastic training for the mastering of cuneiform and the relevant texts was paramount, while modern dictionaries (especially the pocket ones) compensate for memory by reducing its exercise. However, it must be clear: without the bilingual Sumerian-Akkadian lexical lists, with their equivalences including whole sentences, Western scholars of the late nineteenth century would have faced a longer and more difficult struggle to find a “key” to Sumerian, a language without known kinship. At least initially then, the mechanisms of Sumerian were explained to us by the edubba texts, and we should be grateful to the Mesopotamian school for this gift.
Texts of historiographic intent
Mesopotamian historiography was born, and found its primary and constant track of development, out of self-celebratory intents. In the basic textual form of this kind we may range inscriptions where a king celebrates – for an internal audience, aiming at prestige – his activities in public and monumental building, accomplished in the light of a superior personal efficiency and great divine favour. Such inscriptions could be publicly displayed or else become part of the “foundation deposits”, that is destined to the innermost recesses of the new buildings; thus being visible to the gods, but not to human eyes, at least until – centuries away – the building would be reconstructed by a “future prince” (cap. 39). Elsewhere, beyond a sense for the future, such inscriptions supply a glance on the past: for instance, when the self-celebrating king is also the one who discovers earlier inscriptions while renovating the building, whose mudbricks (albeit plastered and strengthened with fired bricks and some stone) had surrendered to the elements over time. It is Assyria, of the two “halves” of Mesopotamia, that has left us the richest and most complex trove of historiographic texts. Here, in fact, the tendency of rulers to annex narratives of military feats which took place “at that time” to building inscriptions slowly developed: a trend that definitely became rooted in the Middle Assyrian period (from the thirteenth century onwards), when the northern Mesopotamian state presented itself in a renewed light compared to its past, more oriented towards a theocratic and militaristic vision. It can be argued whether this “change of pace” took place under the influence of the previous Mittani domination (of which however scarce documentation remains) or as an imitation of communication and historiographic models established among other members of “club of great powers” of the Late Bronze age. In effec t, however, the Middle Assyrian period marks the beginning of a historiographic narrative where the king celebrates himself – strictly in the first person singular – especially in his cosmic righteousness towards Ashur and other important Assyrian deities, while glorifying his military feats abroad; at the same time, the accounts of building work tend to move towards the end of the inscriptions, which grow in length and are written out on a variety of materials (stone, clay, metal) in several parallel copies, scattered all over. In the passage to the neo-Assyrian period (first millennium BC) the formal system of royal inscriptions becomes consolidated: the itineraries of the royal campaigns become more detailed (so as to allow their projection on modern maps: Parpola-Porter 2001), as are the names of the vanquished enemy kings. The space devoted to the description of

under the influence of cuneiform in use in Mittani. Finally, in the thirteenth century, a third scriptorial style shows analogies with Assyrian-type signs; probably this is not by chance, since it is at this time –after the demise of Mittani – that the Hittites and the Assyrians faced each other directly on the Upper Euphrates. In essence, given the long life of Hattusa archives, it is now possible – also thanks to the use of digital technologies – to reconstruct the palaeographic “stemmatics” of many types of cuneiform texts in use at the Hittite court; this way we can notice the interesting permanence of some signs over time, while others were more subject to diachronic changes (Weeden 2011, 2016). Linguistically, the adaptation of cuneiform to express forms of an Indo-European language presented some complexity, but it was as a whole successful. Moreover, thanks to the vast number of copies of the same texts - some of which with syllabic spellings - we know how to read in Hittite many s logograms: so, for example, the Sumerian sign A = “water” corresponds in syllables to wa-a-tar. Overall, the Hittite cuneiform corpus represents the oldest Indo-European linguistic ensemble; it thus has imposed a total rethinking of historical paradigms and Indo-Europeistic classifiers, counter to the belief that viewed Greek and Sanskrit as the most archaic manifestations of this language family.
Elam
Texts in the Elamite language come from the modern province of Khuzistan and Fars, in South-western Iran. The largest part comes from the city of Susa and the surrounding Khuzistan plains, from Persepolis and Anshan (present day Tall-i Malyan) in the high Fars valleys, or from
texts, instructions for administrators and soldiers, laws, hymns and prayers, rituals, astrological omens, mythological texts and even teachings related to the care of horses (hippology texts). On the contrary only single copies remains in the sectors of correspondence, land acquisition deeds, lists and registers, depositions in law courts, cultic inventories, oracular reports and non-astrological omens. In practice, both types are documents that represented the exclusive expression of a dominant élite and their direct subordinates, who chose the Hittite language as their internal means of communication, despite being also able to use another variety of Indo-European – Luwian – or even non Indo-European Hattic for their everyday needs or other requirements (Van der Hout 2006). The orthography of Hittite texts in essence took on the forms of the Old Babylonian ductus from Syria, possibly since the time of the military campaigns of king Hattusili I in the area (ca. 1600 BC).Since we know of many cases of travelling scribes in the second millennium BC, moving from place to place to educate the ruling classes in the art of cuneiform writing, we can thus assume that this was the mode of acculturation in the case of Hittite. In fact, several scribes going by an Akkadian name are attested in the Hattusa archives and elsewhere. Naturally, a class of native Hittites scribes, who seem to have been aware of the philological complexity of Akkadian (not so much of those of Sumerian), rapidly formed; also Hurrian and Luwian came to find transcriptions in Cuneiform, although much less than Hittite. But the Hittite ductus also shows an evolution in the course of time: from an earlier phase (from 1600 onward), a middle one follows (fourteenth century) in which some shapes of the signs have changed, maybe also
fig. 14
Variety of texts, languages and scripts 83



Signs Before the Alphabet
I of Persia in Behistun, the term “Elamite” was chosen, after long discussions, in Oriental studies of the nineteenth and early twentieth century, against other references to “Scythians”, “Medes” or “Medo-Scythians” in a Classicist vein, also because the toponym “Elam” appears in the historical books of the Bible (Basello 2004). It must however be noted that the above mentioned “high place” or Elam does not appear in the Mesopotamian texts before the middle of the third millennium BC Hence, even if archaeological evidence on this area considerably predates that period (especially from the future capital Susa, which preceded Uruk by a millennium), the beginning of Elamite history is fixed by convention towards 2600-2500. In this earliest phase, two south-Iranian polities, Elam and Awan, make their appearance: the "Sumerian King List" indicates that the king Enmebaragesi of Kish (ca. 2675 BC) attacked Elam, and later Awan attacked Ur, and ended the dynasty of Mesannepadda (ca. 2500 BC) taking its place until the kingship returned to Kish. In practice, Elam and Awan (perhaps located in the highlands north of Susa) are considered in this text as states so close to Mesopotamia to even share its dynastic history. Shortly after, also Eannatum of Lagash assimilated Elam to Sumerian cities in the list of his military conquests. Differently, at the time of Urukagina, Lagash seems to have entertained commercial dealings with Elam, as demonstrated by administrative texts from the period. In the twenty-fourth century this fluid state of affairs seems to change: in fact, Sargon of Akkad repeatedly attacked Susa and conquered surrounding areas in the context of building his Mesopotamian empire; Rimush and Manishtushu followed in their father's footsteps. This conquest effort, with the appointment of military governors in various Elamite cities, came to incorporate Susa and its region in the political universe of Akkad, spreading Mesopotamian culture in the area. After the fall of Akkad by the hands of the Guteans, probably coming from more northern areas of the same Zagros Mountains, and their brief interregnum in Mesopotamia, an inscription by Ur-Namma of Ur mentions one Puzur-Inshushinak as king of Awan, whom we can therefore date to 2100 BC ca. He was already ensi – ruler - of Susa, was the first native king to attack Mesopotamia, particularly in its central-eastern sector, including the Diyala and the central Tigris, with Eshnunna (modern Tell Asmar) and Tutub (modern Khafajah) as
sites on the main route between Susiana and the Persepolis-Anshan area, or finally from the Fars coast. Multilingual rock inscriptions of Achaemenid age (ca. 520-450 BC) with Elamite versions can also be found in central-western Iran, in the vicinity of Hamadan, and in Eastern Turkey, near Van. Elamite texts on clay tablets, dated around 600550 BC were found in the ruins of the Assyrian city of Nineveh, and elsewhere in North-eastern Iraq, in the Urartian fortress (cf. c, below) of present-day Armavir Blur in Armenia, and finally in the Old City of Kandahar in Afghanistan. Dating of Elamite texts ranges from 2300 BC to 350 BC (Stolper 2004). Three kinds of writing characterize the linguistic expressions of Elam: a pictographic proto-Elamite, very close to proto-Sumerian cuneiform, so far undeciphered; a hieroglyphic script (syllabic, but with some logograms as well) from the end of the third millennium BC, subject to still hypothetical and mutually diverging decipherments; and finally cuneiform writing in two distinct graphical versions, the first one definitely based on an Akkadian model (between the third and second millennium BC) and the second of more simplified kind (neo-Elamite, first millennium BC). Apart from inscriptions, documentation regarding the Elamite language comes form individual words, mainly names of professions, appearing in texts written in Akkadian and Old Persian. The possible relationships of the Elamite language - which is an agglutinative language, like Sumerian and Hurrian-Urartian - are uncertain. A hypothesis that Elamite could be connected to Dravidian languages has been circulating in studies for more than a century and a half, and is still being advocated (see for instance Khačikjan 1998); in contrast, although it never received a thorough critique, other scholars have simply ignored this assumption. The linguistic study of Elamite does not seem, at least so far, to have been affected by the problem of origins. Elam is, in itself, largely an artificial historical and geographical construct, coined by the Mesopotamian scribes with reference to the South-western highland regions of Iran (today's Khuzistan and the central ranges of the Zagros Mountains) and its inhabitants; the name perhaps referred to the idea of a generic “high place” both in Sumerian and in Akkadian, while a self-definition of the country by the indigenous population seems to be lacking. By virtue of the presence of this language in the important trilingual inscription of Darius

“I, Untash-napirisha, son of Khumban-numena, king of Anshan and Susa, I have achieved a long life, I have gained prosperity, .... For this reason, I built a temple with a brick vault, a temple. .., and I gave it to the god Inshushinak of the sacred place. I raised a ziggurat to heaven. May what I have built, and what I have worked on, be pleasing to you, Inshushinak!”
Building inscription by the king of Elam, Untash-napirisha (ca. 1275-1240 BC), the representative of the new Elamite dynasty that settled there in the late 19th century, to commemorate the erection of the ziggurat at Choga-Zanbil, not far from the historic capital of the Elamite kingdom, Susa, on the Ulay river. Untash-napirisha is known because of his attack on Babylonia, launched competitively with the attack by the Assyrians under Tukulti-Ninurta I (1243-1207), in which the Elamite king withdrew, allowing the Assyrians to lay claim to victory.
A divine statue inscribed in Akkadian found at Susa also testifies to this raid in southern Mesopotamia. The deity mentioned in our text, Inshushinak, is the main figure of the pantheon of Susa, and is attested as a component of many personal names of Elamite kings.

had a meaningful influence on the “Amorite” States of the Euphrates and up to Syria. This phase ended with the reign of Hammurabi; the Babylonian king led several successive campaigns for the conquest of Eshnunna, and then against Elam itself. Fig. 3. Elam, corresponding to Khuzistan, East of the Tigris and of the central ranges of the Zagros Mountains, in present-day South-western Iran (from https://iranologie.com/) After some silence of our sources the “Middle Elamite” period (15001100 BC) is marked by successive dynasties of “kings of Anshan and Susa”; this period appears among the best documented in Susa and other sites through 175 official cuneiform inscriptions by the kings. Of special importance here was the central phase, during which king Untash-napirisha founded his new and grandiose capital, on the site of present-day Choga Zanbil; it has an imposing ziggurat dedicated to the gods of the highlands and the lowlands. During the final phase (twelfth century BC) came the defeat of the Babylonian Kassite dynasty at the hand of Shutruk-nakhkhunde, with the pillage of southern Mesopotamian cities: he was who the king who took to Susa the stele with the Hammurabi code, the victory stele of Naram-Sin of Akkad and many more monuments celebrating Mesopotamian history. His son Kutir-nakhkhunde went as far as to remove the statue of the god Marduk from its temple in Babylon. A military revenge however took place under the Babylonian king Nebuchadnezzar I (1225-1104 BC), who defeated his contemporary Elamite king in a field battle on the river Ulay, even though he did not manage to invade Elam. Another pause in the sources takes us to the eighth century, when we witness the first moves of military and political collaboration be
major cities; however, the founder of the Third Dynasty of Ur relates of having defeated him. The region and other neighbouring areas (such as Anshan) was placed under the control of the Ur empire, with inter-dynastic marriage policies, but also with military conquests (implemented by Shulgi); However, a new dynasty in the land of Shimashki (perhaps in Fars) began a hard opposition against the excessive power of the Mesopotamian empire. This phase ended with the fall of the Ur III empire, in which - in addition to the gradual detachment of various provinces and the increased pressure of the Amorites - military attacks by he Shimashki and Elam against the capital played a key role in the 24th year of King Ibbi-Su'en. The following period (nineteenth-eighteenth centuries) marks the pinnacle of Elam’s power; it was able to establish multiple relationships with the new States in the making, such as Eshnunna, Larsa, Assyria and Babylonia. The royal dynasty of Shimashki was possibly overturned by the kings of Susa, who took on the title of sukkalmakh (originally “grand vizier or minister”, in use e.g. in Larsa ). It is possible that there was a “triumvirate” political structure, with the sukkalmakh at the top, followed by the simple sukkal (‘minister’) of Elam and Shimashki and then the sukkal of Susa, in a succession line for the sukkalmakh that was ensured by the upward rotation of the other two and the appointment of a new “minister” in Susa. The period of the reigns of Isin-Larsa shows the deep influence of the Elamite sukkalmakhs over various Mesopotamian States ; and after the death of the strong Assyrian king Shamshi-Adad I (1781 BC) Elam had the opportunity of conquering Eshnunna, possibly even making it its royal seat. Judging by the Mari diplomatic correspondence, the sukkalmakhs might even have

after the establishment of a new polity, the amount of international contracts made it necessary to be equipped with a system of writing and to use a language of a neighbouring country with its own cultural tradition; while the ability to write one’s own language in the writing system just learned would follow quite soon. In Urartu, after borrowing the Assyrian cuneiform system, interest for Akkadian waned and, on the contrary, texts composed to express the Urartu language – a late branch of Hurrian language – prevailed, employing an accessible form of cuneiform, mainly syllabic. The oddity of the Urartian case should in any case be noted, since the popularity peak of cuneiform had already faded in the first millennium BC, while the expressions of writing based on the innovative alphabetic system were advancing. Moreover, the usual baggage of cultural and literary traditions that were normally associated with the cuneiform system did not accompany its adoption in Urartu. But perhaps both these apparent anomalies can be explained insofar as cuneiform was used mostly with a specific function in Urartu: that of celebrating the exploits of kings (Zimansky 2006). The corpus of Urartian texts (Salvini, 2008, 2012) consists of c uneiform inscriptions coming from all of ancient Urartu, which included Eastern Turkey, Armenia and Iranian Azerbaijan, and reached as far as Iraqi Kurdistan. The majority consists of inscriptions chiselled on stone (stelae or stones included in later buildings. etc) or on rock faces; then we find inscriptions on bronze objects (cap. 42), on clay (tablets, cretulae, ceramic vases) or other materials and, finally, inscriptions on seals. The texts record the feats of the various Urartian kings, in the age of the apex of the State’s power, from Sarduri I (834-828 BC) to Ishpuini (828-810), Menua (810-785), Argishti I (785-763), Sarduri II (763-735)
tween the Elamite State and Babylonia, in the attempt to face the exceeding power of Assyria. From this moment on, Elam’s history is inextricably interweaved with the military activities of Tiglath-pileser III, Sargon II, Sennacherib and then Ashurbanipal in Babylonia against the Chaldeans, and in Elam itself. The final conquest of Susa by Ashurbanipal in 646 BC led to the destruction of temples and the city’s ziggurat, to the pillage of royal and divine statues, to the opening of Elamite royal tombs with the destruction of the remains, and to a mass deportation of the population. The looting operation lasted 55 days, according to the Assyrian king. From this moment on Elam ceased to exist as an autonomous State; the affirmation of Medes and then, above all, of the Persians as dominating powers on the Iranian horizon also caused its ethno-linguistic decline, with the inclusion amidst a myriad of other peoples in the Achaemenid Empire, even though the Elamite language would still be used by kings for their commemorative rock-inscriptions (Potts 1999).
Urartu
The arrival of cuneiform writing in Eastern Anatolia, and then in the neighboring Armenian area, was strictly associated with the supremacy of the polity of Biainili-Urartu, around 830 BC. This event was a latecomer for the Near East, since Assyrians and Hittites had utilized this writing system for about a millennium in neighbouring areas just to the South and West. It must be however taken into account the fact that a sufficiently populated and of complex organisation had not hitherto developed in the area. This said, the modality of the adoption of cuneiform in Biainili-Urartu followed a familiar pattern seen elsewhere:
fig. 16
Variety of texts, languages and scripts 87



Signs Before the Alphabet
an period – with subsequent amendments– which was then exported to other regions outside Mesopotamia itself. Proceeding from compositions made with elementary signs up to increasingly complex ones drawn up in the Akkadian language, but without excluding Sumerian - the most ancient learned language, and an indispensable tool to dominate the whole of the cuneiform writing system -, scribes from all over the Near East learned the Mesopotamian scribal techniques and, with them, the texts from the traditional culture of this area (through recopying of proverbs, literary narratives, letters, religious texts etc.). Perhaps a good modern parallel for this extraordinary international acculturation is the learning of Russian and cyrillic script in the schools of all countries of the Warsaw Pact from 1945 to 1989; but this case was the result of political imposition, while in Western Asia before the Late Bronze Age the adoption of Akkadian and cuneiform script for international relations took place freely, as a practical choice dictated by convenience of communication between very different languages, developing even during phases in which the Mesopotamian states (Babylonia and Assyria) were declining in prestige. Educational institutions (including family-based ones) for the learning of Akkadian therefore arose in various external areas, from Hattusa in Anatolia, to Ugarit on the Mediterranean coast, to Emar on the Upper Euphrates - all places connected in various ways to Hittite dominion (Fincke 2012) - but also at El-Amarna in Egypt (Izre'el 1997). The latter
and Rusa I (735-714); and then also – after the defeats suffered at the hands of the Assyrian king Sargon II – in the period of decline, from Argishti II (714-680), Rusa II (680-639) and Rusa III (629-615 ?). Overall, we know of several hundred texts, which are often characterized by fixed clauses unendingly repeated. Worthy of note are the annals of Argishti I, engraved on the southern side of the citadel of Van; the annals of Sarduri II, on a stela, and on the walls of a niche on the north side of the citadel itself, and an inscription of Rusa II, whose full text was identified in 1998. Of the four existing bilingual inscriptions (Assyrian-Urartian) , all associated with the Musasir buffer state, the most important is one on a stela at the pass of Kelishin, on today's mountainous border between Iraq and Iran.
Akkadian as international language of the Late Bronze Age
The final centuries of the second millennium BC (between 1500 and 1200 BC) were marked by a peculiar cultural phenomenon: the adoption of Akkadian (in cuneiform script) as the diplomatic language of communication between the different powers on the international political scene of the time: Egypt, Mitanni, the Hittite Empire, with their respective vassals, especially in the Syrian-Palestinian area. The solid tradition of the Mesopotamian scribal school was behind this diffusion, with its more or less fixed curriculum developed in the Old Babyloni
41
Tablet bearing Akkadian cuneiform script with a prefect's report
The text is a letter sent by the prefect of the state of Amurru - located between the Gebel Bishri and the lower Orontes River – to the prefect of the city-state of Ugarit. In it, the sender informs his colleague of the arrival of a messenger to receive a specific substance, most likely fragrant and valuable, in Ugarit, 'as much of it as you can' as compensation. As such, the document is inserted among the textual material on the international relations of various kinds between the Syro-Palestinian states in the late 2nd millennium BC, relationships that often involved requests for commodities, sendings, 'gifts', etc.
Origin Ugarit 13th century BC Clay 7.2 x 5.6 x 1.7 cm
Obverse
“Thus (speaks) the prefect of Amurru. To the prefect Ugarit, my son, say: greetings to my son, may the gods protect you. Now, I have sent you Addarya about the parrishkhu. My son, send me as much
Reverse (of it) as is at your disposal. I will give you its price. And may he take it from the head-merchant!"
The text is a letter sent by the prefect (shaknu) of Amurru - a state located between the heights of Jebel Bishri and the lower Orontes in Syria – to his counterpart in the city-state of Ugarit, on the Mediterranean coast. In it the sender notifies a colleague about the arrival of a messenger to receive a specific substance (parriskhu), probably fragrant and valuable in Ugarit, “as much of it as is at your disposal”, promising compensation. As such, the document fits within the abundant textual material on the international relations of various kinds of the Syro-Palestinian states in the late 2nd millennium BC: relations that often involved requests for commodities, dispatches, 'gifts' for diplomatic and political reasons, etc.
In addition to the interest of the letter itself, is the fact that it is the "twin" of a previously known message, RS 17,152: but the latter, although written with similar phraseology and for the same
purpose, was sent by the king of Amurru to the king of Ugarit. The two texts could thus have been sent simultaneously; or else the failure to conclude the negotiations between the prefects could have been followed by an intervention 'from on high'. The word parrishkhu appears only here and in the 'twin' letter in the Akkadian lexicon: it was obviously part of the local late-Amorite technical jargon, common to both Syrian states (cf. the Chicago Assyrian Dictionary, vol. 12 (P), Chicago 2005, pp. 191-192).

(today Tell Kazel) on the coast not far from Ugarit; small archives of letters or administrative documents have reached us from 12 different cities in the Palestinian area, including especially Aphek, Hazor and Taanach. Of particular interest here are the epistolary documents, because they bring into prominence the foreign centres with which the Levant was in written correspondence: Ugarit exchanged information with Carchemish and Emar to the northeast, and then on the coast with Alalakh, Tyre, Birutu (now Beirut), Sidon, Byblos, to the south with Qadesh; while the Palestinian cities were receiving letters from various areas - some of which are not represented in the vast archives of El-Amarna – such as Qatna on the Orontes, and again Byblos, Beirut, Sidon, Tyre, Hazor, Acre, Megiddo, Shechem, Gezer, Jerusalem, Ashkelon and Lachish. In essence, the use of Akkadian in this period had brought about a blossoming of correspondence written for political or commercial purposes, such as we do not find even in later ages, when the alphabet came to make writing more immediate and accessible.
The Ugaritic alphabet; the Phoenician/Aramaic/Hebrew alphabet The first alphabet was born in Ugarit. At this site, now known as Ras Shamra (“the fennel promontory”), on the Mediterranean coast of present-day Syria, the fortuitous discovery of some stone blocks by a farmer, led to the decision of the French authorities to open archaeo
(halfway between Memphis and Thebes on the Nile, ancient Akhetaton, the capital city of Amenhotep IV / Akhenaten) is best known for an epistolary archive that documents to the highest degree the practical affirmation of Akkadian as lingua franca used by international chancelleries in the Late Bronze Age: there are in fact about 300 letters written by some forty Syro-Palestinian “minor kings” to Pharaoh, as well as letters from the “Great Kings” of the time, from Mesopotamia, from Mittani, the Hittite Empire, Cyprus and, finally, some drafts of letters in Akkadian coming from Egypt itself. From the El-Amarna texts in Akkadian, relevant to acts of war, the fate of caravans departing and arriving, and inter-dynastic marriages, it is possible to broadly reconstruct the complex web of political relations of the period, made of conflicting interests disguised in the trappings of courteously formal, diplomatic language. These texts further allow us to highlight several interesting cases of an ambiguous use of Akkadian: whether unintentional, under the influence of the writers” native languages (West Semitic, Hurrian, etc.), or with the intended purpose of latently influencing the royal recipient on matters of particular political interest (Liverani 1998-1999). In parallel, more or less recent discoveries indicate that Akkadian was in regular use in various centres of the Levant. The largest archives of “everyday” documents written in cuneiform come from Ugarit (cap. 41), Alalakh at the outlet of the Orontes in the Mediterranean and Emar. We have small archives also from Ras Ibn Hani, Siyannu and Sumur

Tablet bearing Akkadian cuneiform script
The text is a contract allowing a couple to adopt a third individual who is said to be from 'the country of Khatti', i.e. Hittite, even though his name is Semitic. The clauses contain numerous parallels to both Emar itself and elsewhere in the Akkadian area in the mid-2nd millennium BC. For as long as the adopters are alive, the adoptee will have to 'honor' them; after their deaths, he shall be entitled to participate in the sharing of their assets. Then there are two symmetrical clauses of termination in the event of repudiation by one or the other contracting parties, with the same pecuniary penalties. The contract ends with the names of witnesses, part of them Semitic and partly Hurrian, such as in Alalakh and Ugarit in the same period.
Origin Emar region ca. 14th century BC Clay 8.4 x 6 x 2.5 cm
Variety of texts, languages and scripts 91



Signs Before the Alphabet
logical excavations in 1929, which were developed with almost perfect punctuality until 2011. From the very beginning of the vast archaeological excavation endeavour, clay tablets, inscribed in a cuneiform script until then unknown, came to light: these employed only 30 graphemes, compared to nearly 600 of the Sumerian-Akkadian system. A few years after the discoveries, these texts, now clarified in their formal aspect, were tentatively (but correctly) identified as belonging to the Semitic language family, and in particular to the north-western group of which ancient Hebrew is part. Once this 'key' was found, readings were not long in coming, and in 1931 the decipherment of the new language, called “Ugaritic” was announced. In the following years, the notion that Ugaritic was an “ancestor” of Hebrew was pursued with vigour; Ugaritic was used as a crucial interpretative key for many difficult passages in the Old Testament. However as this may be, various aspects of the Bible, especially religious and theological, can find clarification in Ugaritic texts in general: it is certain, for example, that in the period before the monarchy, the religion of Israel was sharing many aspects with the “Canaanite” one of Ugarit. Over the years of archaeological campaigns on the site, many tablets have been added to those initially found: today we count at least 2,500 texts, written both in Ugaritic and in the international Akkadi
44
Tablet of last will in Akkadian cuneiform script
This text is a contract by which Qibi-Dagan makes his own daughter, Sin-kimi, both his 'son' and his 'wife', that is to say, heir of his assets. The operation appears to have been accomplished at the expense of an actual son, Itti-Sharripiya, who was deprived of all substances and warned not to make any complaints.
Origin Emar region ca. mid-14th century BC Clay 8.2 x 6.2 x 2 cm
Obverse
“In the presence of KhishmiTeshub, son of the king, Qibi-Dagan, son of Duda, son of Sinali, in the day when he made (this) contract, declared thus: 'Now, I made Sin-kimi, my daughter, (as) son and wife. Whatever item of my heritage that Itti-Sharri-piya had taken, I gave to my daughter Sin-kime. Itti-Sharri-piya against Sin-kime will not bring legal action'.”
Lower edge
Seal of the king's son (SEALING)
Reverse
Before Khishmi-Teshub, son of the king; before Pukhi-Shenni, head of the country; before Madkali-Dagan son of Tubi-Dagan; before Udkha-abu, son of Iya; before Abi-kabi, son of Khurasi; before Lim-abu son of Kuki ".
The text comes from Emar (today Tell Meskeneh), an ancient Amorite city located on a large bend in the middle of the Euphrates River in north-eastern Syria. Many texts were found at the site, dated between the 14th century BC and the fall of Emar in 1187 BC From both documents and archaeological data, Emar proves to have been an important center of trade in the Late Bronze Age, thanks to its position between the powerful centers of northern Mesopotamia, Hittite Anatolia, and Syria itself. The tablets of Emar, mostly in Akkadian script and dating back to the 13th century BC, are not of royal or official type, but 'everyday' recordings of private transactions, legal deeds, relationships regarding real estate, weddings, wills, and formal adoptions. Another group of Emar texts is, instead, of scribal and scholarly character (literary and lexical texts).
This text is a contract whereby one Qibi-Dagan makes his own daughter, Sin-kimi, simultaneously both 'son' and 'wife', that is, the heir of all his assets. The operation appears to have been accomplished at the expense of an actual son, Itti-Sharri-piya, who was deprived of all substances, and who is admonished not to make legal complaints. The text, like many others of the late 2nd millennium, is of considerable legal interest in light of the complex procedures that characterize hereditary law in that period: a law that arises and is implemented as the reaction of the private sphere against precise 'thrusts' by the coeval public sphere (waning of the firstborn privilege implemented in the palatial sphere).
In detail, the text highlights the presence of two key characters in the texts of Emar, even though there is not much evidence of them in the archive itself. The first is Khishmi-Teshub, in a text (Emar Vl/3, no.19) said to be the 'son of the king (of Emar)' and at the same time 'brother' of the king Ini-Teshub of Carchemish (cf. also ibid. no.18): the latter is a well known character in both Hittite and Ugaritic texts, the great-grandson in the cadet line of the Hittite king Shuppiluliuma II and a contemporary of king Tudkhaliya IV (approx. 1250-1220). As for Pukhi-Shenni, he shows the same title as that in the Emar document no. 181, another text with the 'son of the king' as witness: although in that case, it applied to Tuwata-zitti, another representative of the 'Hittite' dynasty of Emar.
Finally, the 'seal of the son of the king' is attested in the fragmentary text Emar no. 182. Abi-kapi, son of Khurasi, is frequently mentioned in the legal deeds of the site: see especially Emar text no. 194, in which this individual is the owner of land registered on a tablet 'sealed with the seal of the king of Carchemish'. In conclusion, the present text is full of interesting parallels in the legal tradition of Emar.

an of the Late Bronze Age (cf. f, above), but the total number could be much higher, thanks to most recent discoveries. These texts are to be added to various specimens in other languages in use at the time (from Hittite to Luwian, Hurrian, Egyptian, and Cypro- Minoan) and, at least in part, subject to study in the local scribal school. The texts come from various libraries of the coastal city, attached to temples and the royal palace, but also from private homes of high-ranking individuals.
Ugarit fell at the hands of the “Sea Peoples” around 1180 BC and was never rebuilt. However, a similar fate did not befall other cities that dotted the Mediterranean coast and carried out parallel trading activities to those of the Syrian centre. This point leaves open the question of a possible partial overlapping of the Ugaritic alphabet with writing developed in the Phoenician cities on the present-day
coast of Lebanon. In any case, it should be noted that internal comparisons within the Semitic language group that can be established starting from the Ugaritic alphabet, do not always correspond to those of the subsequent linear alphabets. Moreover, the phonological inventory of the two systems is very different: while Ugaritic has partial vocalization, through the use of the three signs [ʼa], [ʼi], [ʼu], the Phoenician alphabetic system (“Canaanite”) has only the consonant sign [ʼ] (= aleph) without attached vowels. Overall, then, the Ugaritic and Phoenician alphabets are largely different when compared: If we then notice that Ugaritic, following the practice of cuneiform Akkadian, was written from left to right, while Phoenician is written from right to left (like all Semitic alphabets to this day) and, in addition, that the Phoenicians signs are not derived in any way from the cuneiform, but are linear, it must be assumed that the “invention” of the Phoenician alphabet took a very different path from the
one developed by the scribes of international 'calibre' from Ugarit. It is commonly believed that a precursor of the Phoenician alphabet (“proto-Canaanite”) might have already been invented in the first half of the second millennium BC by people who had some knowledge of Egyptian hieroglyphs (where we find a number of signs with phonetic value); that it originally had around 27 phonemes, then reduced to 22 over time; that the signs were initially pictographic, mostly with the value derived from the represented design (acrophonic value: e.g. n for nun, “fish”, for a sign in the shape of fish); and that the pictographic idea – as as the case in most archaic Sumerian - allowed to write freely in every direction, from right to left or vice versa, as boustrophedon, or vertically (Naveh 1982: 43). The Phoenician alphabet would appear towards the eleventh century BC, at the end of this long chain of attempts, which left traces in the Sinai and in various sites of the Levant, but also in the Mediterranean area, with the fixing of 22 consonant signs, to be read from right to left (Lemaire 2008). In any case, there is no doubt that the realization of the Phoenician alphabet came to be combined with the substantial commercial prestige of the Phoenician city-states along the Levantine coast and up to Cilicia in the north; this enabled this writing system to spread rapidly, beginning with the early centuries of the first millennium BC, to the surrounding populations of Semitic language, both to the south (Hebrews and peoples of Transjordan) and internally (Arameans). How deep may the degree of diffusion of this form of writing have been, is impossible to establish, since the linear alphabetic signs could be penned with a simple brush on any surface; and most of the writing media employed (papyrus, parchment, wood, etc.) might have been lost to the wear of time. From Phoenicia itself and from Palestine and surrounding regions, we are therefore left essentially with alphabetic testimonials engraved on stone, or penned on pottery sherds (ostraca). Aramaic instead, due to its ‘situational’ proximity with the Assyrian empire – of which it had become the vernacular language - came to use clay tablets for the preparation of their texts; both in parallel to cuneiform texts and for monolingual documents. From Mesopotamia and Syria, therefore, tablets with texts in Aramaic painted with brush on hardened clay have come down to us, as tangible evidence of a deep technical and cultural shift taking place, although still on the same writing media as “before the alphabet”.
