Signs Before the Alphabet
tamia. As with the majority of cuneiform texts from the Third Dynasty of Ur, the Garshana Archives are devoid of an excavated context whose location, considered to be somewhere in the Sumerian province of Umma, remains unknown; thus their collection and publication remain controversial. Previously, similar cuneiform discoveries for this historical period came from the major cities of southern Mesopotamia. However, the source of the Garshana Archives is from the estate of Shu-Kabta, a general and physician, married to the princess, Simat-Ishtaran, the daughter of the king of Sumer, the first such archive discovered so far. These, mostly well-preserved tablets, record the daily, detailed accounts of the estate’s construction of a brewery, flour-mill, textile-mill, kitchen and compound wall, along with numerous additional dwellings in the countryside. Descriptions of the building processes from the making of mud-bricks, digging of foundations, erection of walls, roofs, plastering and waterproofing provide the first contemporary portrait of the creation of an estate complex perhaps to be compared to the later Roman and medieval “latifundia”. In addition to the smaller households and villages within the property, the estate contained tanning and leather-working facilities that produced elaborate goods for the estate’s residents. Agricultural fields, sheep, goats and cattle, and a piggery provided food for the household’s large kitchen to feed the family and the numerous workers on the estate. Boats and sailors were on hand to provide transport and to ship goods to and from towns and villages near and far. Of particular significance is the extraordinary role that women played on the estate, particularly after the death of Shu-Kabta, when his wife, the princess Simat-Ishtaran, became the head of the household. Elaborate records of the funeral banquet for Shu-Kabta provide lists of the varied dishes served and even recipes for different kinds of soup fed to guests and workers alike. Numerous women, servants and hired workers, participated fully in the construction work, brick-hauling, supervision of work gangs (with equal or even higher wages), and many other tasks hitherto documented only for men. Of particular interest is the evidence for the underlying Semitic-Babylonian language on the written agglutinative-Sumerian language of the texts. It revealed the substantial influence of the Babylonian language, the apparent spoken
The Garshana and Irisagrig Archives
David I. Owen Cornell University
During the last decade, two unique, but unprovenanced, archives from Mesopotamia were published. The Garshana Archives were first identified in the late 1990’s by Dr. Rudi Mayr, assistant curator at the Rosen Collection in New York City who, together with Professor David I. Owen of Cornell University, began the study of the archives. In 2000 the tablets were gifted to Cornell University with the establishment of the Jonathan and Jeannette Rosen Ancient Near Eastern Studies Seminar and Tablet Conservation Laboratory. After a period of intensive conservation and continued study, the Garshana Archives, consisting of ca. 1400 tablets, spanning a 20-year period during the final decades of the Third Dynasty of Ur (2100-2000), appeared in a series of four volumes. The recovery of this unique corpus, more or less as a complete unit, constituted a major discovery in the history and culture of late third millennium BCE Mesopo
96



language of the mixed, local Sumerian and Semitic population, which is reflected also in the many Babylonian personal names recorded on the tablets. For the first time the cuneiform record reveals an intimate portrait of daily life in a rural community in Sumer in the last decades of the 21st century BCE.
In 2006 cuneiform tablets dated to a 20-year period during the last decades of the Third Dynasty of Ur, characterized by their unique calendar, began to appear for sale on Internet sites. They caught the attention of Professor David I. Owen who began to record and study the photos posted on these sites. For the following six years, he was able to identify and “reconstitute” an archive of ca. 1500 tablets associated with the city of Irisagrig (along with some from Garshana), located somewhere in the general vicinity of the Sumerian religious center of Nippur. Just as the discovery of the Garshana Archives added a unique perspective for Sumerian society during the Third Dynasty of Ur, so too did the discovery of the Irisagrig Archives that came from an important city governed by Urmes for over twenty years. Irisagrig was the gateway to the northeastern frontier and was situated on a major trade route to the cities of Elam (Iran) with which it had close relations. The tablets were probably plundered in the remains of governor Urmes’ palace and thus provide details on a complex system of royal administration that included hundreds of messengers, who were dispatched from various branches of government offices, to undertake a wide variety of local administrative tasks and diplomatic missions to cities in northeast Mesopotamia and western Iran. An elaborate system of rations consisting of generous portions of meat, fish and soup were provided to the messengers along with onagers to facilitate travel. Evidence for the existence of many temples, including those for deceased kings of Ur, foreign contacts, military officials, visits of the kings and royal family members, and numerous new political and military details hitherto unknown from previously published archives - together constitute an unparalleled discovery.
In sum, these recently published archives preserve detailed and complimentary portraits of two related, albeit different, contemporary communities. They provide rich data that is enhancing the study and understanding of late Sumerian and early Babylonian society a few short years before the Sumerians vanished as an ethnic and political entity in Mesopotamia and the Babylonians emerged as the major ethnic, linguistic, and political group to dominate Mesopotamia for the remainder of its long history. The discovery and rapid publication of these archives
provided an expanded view of the complex political, religious, social, military, and economic affairs of two cities whose exact locations may one day be revealed and excavated properly.
In the pictures, Sumerian tablet T981 from Garshana. List of foods and various substances for the funerary banquet of Shu-Kabta : 2nd regnal year of Ibbi-Su'en (2028 BC). (From Owen - Mayr 2007, p. 305, tab.. XXX-XXXI).

The Garshana and Irisagrig Archives 97






The craft of the seal engraver






to us, the experience level of the craftsmen could vary considerably. The most expert ones worked for high-ranking customers and employed semiprecious stones. Given the scarcity of textual and archaeological data the organisation of a stone engraver’s workshop in Mesopotamia is difficult to reconstruct. In some cases the style of a group of seals is so distinctive and peculiar that we can assume with some certainty that they came from the same workshop (those from Sippar and Aleppo are good examples). The raw materials employed in the seals’ production were first modelled in a cylindrical shape, refining and smoothing them with sand. Having achieved the cylindrical shape the next stage was its longitudinal perforation, starting from both ends to avoid the drill bit overheating at the risk of breaking the stone. This is particularly noticeable in some specimens whose two holes are slightly out of alignment. The procedures in other production areas, such as Crete and Egypt, indicate that the seals were fixed to a sort of frame or adhesive block to be engraved, making a rough sketch on the surface first, so as to allow the craftsman to position the drawing correctly. However, unfinished Mesopotamian cylinder seals often show just a single element of the figurative scene, as if the modelling or engraving were limited to one element of the composition at a time. The etching of the decorative motif was generally the last step in the making of a cylinder seal. The drawing on the seal was engraved in “negative”, so that when it was pressed on fresh clay it appeared in relief or “positive”. Observing the iconographic motifs of the seals it is possible to distinguish two main groups: seals where human and animal figures are rendered in a realistic manner, showing even the most minute details; to achieve this a variety of different size tools was employed, drills and chisels made of metal or flint, whose marks were then polished to give the drawing a smooth appearance. Seals with schematic figures and stylized geometric patterns, which were made with completely different tools, particularly with a drill. A strict correlation between tools employed and style may be noted at least for the oldest examples, where the most refined seals were always made, or at least finished, with fine tips and chisels, while the simpler schematic ones were always executed by drill. During the Middle Uruk period a hand drill with a rounded tip was commonly in use, and this produced very coarse designs (baggy style). In the Late Uruk period, such motifs were polished and refined using fine tips and chisels, which also allowed for the creation of smaller shapes and more densely decorated compositions. The drill soon became the most common tool, and the fastest one for the etching of glyptics. It was essentially a bow drill (fig. 1), whose drilling tip, attached to a wand, could be rotated at high speed using the bow to which it was attached .
The craft of the seal engraver
Roswitha Del Fabbro
The craft of the bur-gul
We know very little about the seal engravers, whose Sumerian name was bur-gul and in Akkadian purkullu or parkullu. A Babylonian text dated around the sixth century BC during Cyrus’s reign tells us they had apprentices. The duration of the apprenticeship can be imagined if we think of the challenge the craftsman was facing in the execution of a minute drawing on the surface of a tiny cylinder and engraved in negative, almost with no visual aid. It is difficult to comprehend how these engravers could carve such small scenes without the help of magnifying instruments. Recent studies are beginning to reveal that the disabled might have been employed in ancient Mesopotamia, and engravers affected by myopia might have been among these, employed in somewhat the same way as scribes who were charged with compiling tablets in minute handwriting. The seal engraver’s technique was possibly the most complicated among those applied in the ancient arts, comparable to that of the “Little Masters” decorating Greek vases. Inscriptions played an important role in the seal’s design at various times. It seems probable that they would be produced by scribes who had been trained not only to carve inscriptions in stone, but specifically to do so on a very small scale and in reverse, so that the inscription would be readable in the seal’s impression on clay. In some cases it is quite obvious that the inscription was made by someone not properly trained, since it looks unclear or some of its cuneiform symbols are oriented in different directions. During the Old Babylonian period it would seem that the figurative motif was carved first in the majority of seals. The customer could choose the design he preferred and then ask for the inscription to his liking to be added in the space provided for the purpose. The concept of adapting a design to a curved surface and develop it so that it could be repeated with no restrictions was totally new and revolutionary vis-a-vis the well defined area in stamp seals. The fact that in the same period we find bas-reliefs on the curved surface of stone vases is not likely to be a coincidence. The appearance of cylinder seals coincides with the resurgence of fashion for refined stone vases, which in turn was linked to improved stone-working techniques. E. Porada (1977) suggested that the development of cylinder seals could be attributed to the makers of stone vases. The second syllable of bur-gul, the Sumerian name for the seal engravers, might refer to an activity associated with stone pottery (Edzard 1959-1960). The stone engraver could have thus been originally a craftsman both carving seals and working on reliefs and stone vases. In Diqdiqqeh and Ur, C.L. Woolley discovered some stone fragments that were presumably used by the seal engraver, or his apprentices, to test and produce a drawing on a flat surface before executing the “fair copy” on the curved surface of a cylinder. Judging by the diverse quality of the seals that have come down
The craft of the seal engraver

Signs Before the Alphabet
difficult, hence the transformation of this operation from vertical to horizontal marked a noticeable progress. It is likely that both the horizontal drill and cutting wheel were motioned by a bow, like the vertical drill. This innovation made the engraving of hard stones much simpler.
It was previously thought that drills and cutting wheels mounted horizontally were in use as far back as the late fourth millennium BC (Nissen 1977). M. Sax demonstrated on the contrary that these important technological innovations did not make their appearance until the late Old Babylonian period. There is no evidence of use of the cutting wheel earlier than approx. 1750 BC. Before that time, soft stones were by far the preferred material and drawings were carved, engraved and smoothed with copper, flint or wooden tools. An essential element completing the stone carvers’ toolbox was the abrasive. Emery (corundum) powder was introduced at the beginning of the Old Babylonian period and this abrasive was still the most popular in late antiquity. It came from the island of Naxos or from India, although other materials such as quartz and iron oxide, albeit rougher if similar in function, were more easily available. Abrasives were also used in the surface finishing phase, called polishing. The cutting wheel consists of a circular grinding wheel tool fixed on a horizontal rotary axis held by two supports (fig. 2). Scholars generally accept that a cutting wheel could only be used if mounted on a horizontal axis. This tool increased engraving speed and clarity; moreover, it could be controlled more easily, since it allowed for the desired pressure to be exerted in a specific point. This invention merely required a sideways rotation of the bow drill and a support at both ends of the axis. The discs could be of various sizes. The vertical drilling of a smooth and curved surface was extremely

Cylinder seals: styles
The success obtained by this implement is demonstrated by the wide diffusion of cylinder seals over a large swath of the Eastern Mediterranean, in all areas touched by the cuneiform culture, where they remained in use for over three millennia. The technology was so easily adaptable and pervasive that it became an essential aspect of various areas of Mesopotamian life: administrative, legal, personal, social and religious. Seals of the Uruk period were often drilled from top to bottom, as it became customary in later periods; others were not and sported a small handle at the top instead, shaped as an animal or sometimes similar to a crest (cap. 47): this, according to some scholars, was fashioned after the distal end of the metapodial bone. It is indeed possible that the prototypes of cylinder seals were made of perishable materials such as bone, and that this peculiar shape of the handle referred to their typical morphology. The cylinder seals of this period are, for the most part, made of fairly soft limestone and, considering their dimensions, are among the largest, at least as far as the diameter is concerned. In a later phase harder stones began being utilized, and the appearance of lapis lazuli is witness to the existence of long distance connections with the mines of the Badakhstan district, in present day Afghanistan. The Late Uruk period is characterized by a great variety, not only from a stylistic point of view but also for the iconography of subjects depicted on the seals. Among the most frequently employed themes were the parades of prisoners filing in front of the king, contest scenes between heroes and beasts or among wild and domestic animals (often a lion approaching and grabbing its prey from behind; cap. 46), rows of animals (cap. 47), processions of offering-bearers to the temple, the feeding of animals, scenes of social conflict or war, and finally scenes of daily life, particularly work activities. The cylinder seals of the Jemdet Nasr period, between the fourth and third millennium BC show subjects and styles that are completely distinct from those of the Late Uruk period, despite being
In a nutshell, the shift from a stone to a metal (copper/bronze) tool set coincided with the introduction of hard stones for the production of seals. It seems likely that the difficulties of engraving hematite first and an even harder material such as quartz (hardness 7 on the Mohs scale) later stimulated the technological progress that was not needed for the production of seals in softer stones. Other related innovations were the use of the cutting wheel mounted horizontally and probably that of emery as an abrasive.
Artistic forms and styles of the seals over the ages
The seal impression not only identifies its owner, being generally a personal item of his, but it also allows us to highlight how taste, figurative symbolism and techniques of miniature engraving could vary in the course of different periods, in all of which the images depicted on seals can be typologically traced back to a limited number of standard scenes. In essence, the iconographic repertoire of the seals diversified and developed over time, changing according to region and style. The engravers did not need a source of visual inspiration to make the carvings; the recurrence and sometimes the monotony of the images produced were the fruit of a consummate art, which reused the patterns that proved most successful and of greater visual impact, constantly revising them. The most ancient type were stamp seals and appeared in Northern Mesopotamia. Their shape, especially during the Neolithic, varied considerably, possibly to counter the repetitivity of the geometrical carved motifs. It was only in the late Chalcolithic that an increase in figurative complexity can be recorded. The first scenes presenting a more complex narrative structure date to the fourth millennium BC; they refer back, through emblematic episodes, to some mythological themes known from written documents. The cylinder seal was invented in the Middle Uruk period, halfway through the fourth millennium BC; it originated in Southern Mesopotamia or Western Iran. This kind of seal, from its inception, acquired a specific structure and narrative functions, and enabled the development of iconographic motifs of greater complexity. The earliest cylinder seals were used to seal bullae, the hollow clay spheres, which contained so-called tokens. Compared to stamp seals the cylinder-shaped ones allowed extending the decorative motive for any chosen length, and to cover a larger surface when rolled over a cretula or on a tablet, thus

Signs Before the Alphabet
Ninurta /Ningirsu, the god of agriculture and war, who recovered the tablet and returned it to its rightful owner. However, there are different versions of this myth. The banquet scene is another theme that appears during the final stages of the Early Dynastic I period. There are versions with figures drinking from cups or with straws from large jars (cap. 149), typical of Southern Mesopotamia, or eating, the latter more specifically Syrian. This theme will have wide circulation during Early Dynastic II and even more so in Early Dynastic III, in compositions of almost narrative character, bearing strong kinship with similar representations to be found on small plaques and inlays, like on the Ur standard. At the end of this period the ordering in registers became popular. During the Early Dynastic II period the so–called Fara style developed, deriving its name from a city in lower Mesopotamia whose ancient name was Shuruppak. The scenes depicting fights between animals or animals and humans are characterized by a larger proportion of shapes and composition. The figures are distinguished by strong verticality, with animals standing up on their hind legs, while the bodies, for instance those of the lions, intertwine in crossed positions. This change of axis in the composition resulted in the development of more complex configurations. Frequently figures such as the bull-man and that of the hero with curly hair (cap. 123) are depicted. The bull with human head probably represents a frontal stylisation of the bison. The human figures assume a heroic status; they are often naked, in the throes of ferocious beasts, fighting bare-handed or armed with the characteristic dagger with a crescent-shaped hilt. During the Early Dynastic III A a stylistic and compositional evolution can be witnessed, whereby figures become more full-bodied (cap. 53) and their positioning is freer compared to those of the previous period. At the end of this period heroes’ faces are presented in frontal view, bearded, with the characteristic hairstyle in two or three curls arranged on both sides. Seals from the Akkadian period (23th-22nd centuries BC) are distinguishable from those of earlier ages particularly for the at
coeval. The two styles are not sequential, they existed simultaneously. The Jemdet Nasr seals are smaller, chunky and marked by rather schematic compositions, made by drill. The stones utilized are darker and sometimes harder. The most usual themes are depictions of squatting women, the so-called “pigtailed ladies”, most probably craftswomen engaged in various work activities, among which production of pottery and weaving; architectural depictions of temple façades; stylised geometric motifs such as fish-bone, concentric circles and diamond patterns; animals processions, particularly goats, gazelles and fish, the latter at times so stylized as to be hardly recognizable (cap. 51). These were usually arranged in horizontal rows on one or more registers. The seals depicting animals moving towards the temple façade (cap. 9) originate mainly in the region of the Diyala river, North-East of Baghdad, but also from Syria. On the Jemdet Nasr stamp seals depictions of animals, produced by drill, are very common (cap. 52, cap. 86). The lapis lazuli seals often belong to the later Early Dynastic period, albeit with a range of distribution limited to Southern Mesopotamia, particularly in Ur. During the Early Dynastic period most common are the scenes with fighting animals, presented in linear horizontal compositions: predators (usually felines) attacking another animal (generally a goat) frontally from one or two sides. Sometimes a human figure is included, or the bullman, portrayed in the act of attacking the beasts or defending the domestic animals. These so-called “contest scenes” carried a deep symbolic meaning and became one of the main themes recurring on cylinder seals. The motif with the lion-headed eagle Imdugud (Anzû in Akkadian) appears, even if in a schematic shape, in a late phase of Early Dynastic I to be then developed in the Early Dynastic III (cap. 72). According to an Akkadian myth this monstrous creature of divine origin was charged to watch over the so-called "tablet of destinies", the symbol of divine power and heavenly order. Covetous of power, Imdugud stole it from the god Enlil (in the Akkadian version, or the god Enki in the Sumerian one) and was killed by

108



dian aristocracy made an effort to merge the Sumerian symbolic and ideological heritage with their own, and this is reflected in the utmost care given to the representation of mythological scenes and of the whole complex pantheon of the time, from the main deities to the minor ones. The reorganisation of the pantheon took place by merging the terrestrial Sumerian deitiets with the Semitic astral ones. The gods thus become protagonists of Akkadian glyptics (cap. 56). Many of the divine beings common on Akkadian glyptics remain obscure, due to our incomplete knowledge of the mythological apparatus of the time. An exception are the sun-god Shamash, the one most frequently portrayed on Akkadian seals, and Ea/Enki, the water god, both of whom are easily identifiable thanks to their peculiarities, with which we will deal in depth later. These lesser known gods probably belonged to the sphere of popular belief, while what has come down to us – thanks to written sources – are merely the most important myths of official religion, generally in versions from later periods. The exponential increase in Akkadian glyptics of minor deities – which had a role as mediators between the human and divine worlds – well reflects the growing complexity taking place in parallel in the Akkadian state. The characteristic themes of previous periods, such as the contest scenes, continue being reproduced – although in mechanical form – in neo-Sumerian times, or the Third Dynasty of Ur (21st century BC). However, the trend is to simplify the complex and articulated narration of mythological and heroic background of the Akkadian age, making them into more streamlined scenes, repeated with minor variations. A tendency to standardisation develops, with recurring characters and schematic motifs, traceable to the ritual sphere. The inscription takes on an increasing weight within the composition. The most popular theme of the period (cap. 57) was without a doubt the introduction of the faithful in the presence of a deity seated on a throne. The faithful is represented wearing the typical
tention that engravers were paying to a naturalistic rendition of the human and animal bodies (cap. 91). The narrative aspect was also given more careful consideration, adding movement to the scene. Another distinctive character of seals from this period is the explicit research of symmetry and balance in the representation (cap. 55). Two of the themes that were already common in the Early Dynastic period and that continue being developed in Akkadian times are the banquet scenes and the contest scenes. The latter became the favourite depiction of the Akkadian palace élite. In the contest scenes the pairs of fighters are characterized by increasingly standardized human and animal figures. The interweaving of human and animal bodies loosens and the compositions gain a wider breadth. From the relatively chaotic fights of the Early Dynastic period the tendency to represent individual human-animal duels is established. The decorative continuity of the scene is sacrificed in order to dramatize the depiction of the individual fight. Prevailing is the symmetrical arrangement of two pairs of fighters. A linear style, with highly schematic subjects, is flanked by a naturalistic style, characterized by great care for the realism of the subject represented, for instance in the rendition of muscles and other anatomic details. Serpentine was the preferred stone for contest scenes, but more precious materials were employed as well, such as red and green jasper, rock crystal, spotted black and white diorite and even lapis lazuli, although the latter appears to be less readily available than during the Early Dynastic period. The so-called presentation scenes developed in a more mature phase of the Akkadian age. These scenes had already appeared albeit sporadically – at the end of the Early Dynastic III. They tend to replace the banquet scenes, still popular at the beginning of this period. The presentation theme would enjoy considerable popularity in the following neo-Sumerian period, under the Third Dynasty of Ur, when it assumed considerable institutional importance. Deep religious changes were taking place at this time; the Akka

110



The scene of presentation (or “mediated audience”) is also typical of the Isin-Larsa period, but in this phase some meaningful variations develop. The interceding goddess Lama is portrayed with both hands raised, and she does not lead the subject or official by the hand any longer, she now stands behind the latter, who then is placed exactly in front of the divinized ruler sitting on a stool (non-mediated audience). Fill-in elements are often included, such as a ball, rod and vase. Other fill-in motifs that are typical of this period are the club with a double lion head, a decapitated human head, a monkey, a bow-legged dwarf, a triton, birds, dogs, flies, porcupines, mullets. The use of hematite becomes widespread at this stage: it will be one of the most widely used stones for the seals in the first half of the second millennium BC. In the Old Babylonian period mediated and non-mediated audiences were gradually replaced by scenes where only standing figures were represented. The two main characters were the king
rolled-brim turban, well known also from the statues of the time, or with a shaved head and beardless; his left arm is held by an interceding goddess who leads him towards the main deity, while his right arm is lifted in a reverential gesture or left dangling by the side. There are no specific markers which help us to attribute a precise identity to the seated divinity, who wears a horned tiara and, usually, the typical flounced wool dress. The seated deity can be replaced by a divinized king, wearing the characteristic headgear of the time and seated on a flounced wool-covered stool. He holds a goblet or small vase in his right hand, representing the symbol of the protection granted to his subordinate and with some relation to the banquet theme. This was an integral part of a ceremonial system intended to consolidate the central authority and the recognition of the established hierarchy. The scene thus carries a precise ideological and political meaning. These seals likely belonged to high rank officials.
59-60-61
Cylinder seal with inscription
At the center of the scene the so-called 'naked goddess' is represented, with the suppliant goddess Lama on her right, while on the left, a king in martial posture is depicted with a mace in his hand. A 'bull-man', bearing a goat in its arms, is on the right side of the goddess Lama.
Old Babylonian Goethite 2.7 x 1.5 cm
The so-called 'naked goddess' - in the classic Babylonian iconography, or that is, full frontal nudity - is represented on a pedestal, thus indicating its function as a statue or in any case, a simulacrum, and it is smaller in size than the other persons depicted. Many hypotheses have been put forth concerning the possible functions of this figure, which often appears in the seals of the Old Babylonian period and the Kassite period (during which the body is depicted frontally and the head in profile): as a deity, a divine simulacrum, or the counterpart of the Naked Hero.
From the iconography of the female frontal nude figure, it is possible to presume her identification with Ishtar in her specific aspect as goddess of love and sexuality. Ishtar was an extremely complex deity, with features as both goddess of war and goddess of love. This dual nature. also evident in the mythological corpus, might correspond to a double representation of the deity. If the 'naked goddess' is the representation of Ishtar as goddess of love and sexuality, her representation next to Ishtar in warlike garb – as in some seals - intended to show the goddess in all her functions and all her powers.
The 'naked goddess' is flanked by the suppliant goddess Lama on the right and, on the other side, by a figure in a martial posture, probably a king, holding a mace with his left hand. To the right of the goddess Lama, there is a 'bull-man' with a human head and torso in frontal view and a lower half with taurine legs depicted in profile. The figure is holding a goat in his arms. Two birds complete the scene, in the upper part of the seal. In the seal space, there is also a cuneiform inscription in three lines:
a-li-ki-im-ti DUMU hi-da-ti-ia ÌR d.IŠKUR “Ali-kimti, son of Hidatiya, servant of Ishkur/Adad”

61






Signs Before the Alphabet
Edith Porada subdivided the glyptic production of the Mittani period in two styles, the common and the elaborated ones. The seals in the common style are characterized by the repetition of decorative motifs. These were made out in easily fashionable materials, such as steatite (soap stone) or sintered quartz (often described, less accurately as faience; cap. 62), which made mass production possible. Sintered quartz made its appearance in this period. Animals laying down, placed facing each other, were depicted on their own or as part of a composition. A stylized tree is often the focus of the depiction. The most common kind is the so-called bouquet tree. By the two sides of the tree recumbent animal or human figures are arranged in a strongly symmetrical composition. Other scenes frequently depicted are rows of marching men (cap. 82), fish or birds (cap. 175). The elaborate style shows a wider range of themes and the materials employed in the production are different too, generally semi-precious stone. Some of the motifs are directly derived from the Babylonian tradition, such as the deities wearing the typical dress with flounces and the praying figures. Others yet are typical of the Assyrian repertory, such as the trees and winged figures. This style is typified by a veritable horror vacui, wide use is made of fill-ins. The seals from the Kassite period can be classified into four separate styles. The first Kassite style (end of fifteenth century BC to first half of thirteenth century BC) originates from the previous Old Babylonian glyptic tradition, simplifying it. Characteristic is the presence of a vertical inscription occupying most of the figurative area, leaving little space to the drawing. They are generally prayers, often addressed to a personal god, which reflect the new religious climate of this period. The figures (up to a maximum of three) are shown standing or sitting and are more elongated, to adjust to the available space, or the scene is divided in two registers. The identification of the protagonists of the scene is problematic as they are often isolated while the background is filled with a growing number of symbolic elements. The pseudo-Kassite style is derived from the first style and is
and the suppliant goddess, sometimes the only two protagonists on the scene. It is the beginning of a reduction and simplification of the glyptic repertory. At this stage religious themes linked to templar environments were developed. The most frequently represented deities are the sun-god Shamash, walking on a stylized mountain and holding a saw in his right hand; the goddess of love and war Ishtar, armed and with a foot placed on her animal symbol , the lion; and the storm-god Adad holding symbols recalling atmospheric phenomena. Frequent is the depiction, in front of the goddess Lama, of a royal hero. He wears a rolled-brim turban and a triangular dress (probably a warrior garb), he is characterized by a martial posture and by a club or scepter held in the left hand, a symbol of his authority (cap. 60). During the reign of Samsu-iluna (ca. 1750-1712 BC), successor of Hammurabi of Babylonia, seal engraving techniques underwent a radical change: we find evidence of the use of the horizontal drill and the cutting wheel while previously seals were engraved with manual tools. The new technology allowed a faster production of the drawings, but resulted in an impoverishment of the artistic quality in the scenes represented. Starting from the fourth quarter of the eighteenth century BC a distinctive style and iconography developed in Syria (cap. 58). Such characteristics most probably reflect at first the political stability ensured by the powerful kingdom of Yamkhad (Aleppo). Several seals of the Syrian group have all or a part of the drawing laid horizontally, as in cap. 101 belonging to the second Syrian group. This seal shows a figure that became very popular in the Old Syrian seals (during the Middle Bronze age) and Middle Syrian (Late Bronze age), that is the naked goddess represented in the act of disrobing. The scheme with auxiliary motifs separated by guilloche or more complex braids into two rows is particularly common (cap. 67). A winged solar disk is often depicted above a stylized tree.

Two human figures are separated by an ibex standing on its hind legs. The scene is completed by two ibexes in heraldic position with their heads turned backwards and looking at one another. Beneath them, a grazing deer is depicted.

scenes, even of mythological character, with an archer (cap. 125) in kneeling position, or standing or on a wagon; 3) banquet scenes, sometimes sacred (cap. 69); 4) schematic representations, often characterized by a heraldic scheme, as in the case of kings, winged genies and animals arranged by the sides of the sacred tree, topped by the winged disk, symbol of the god Ashur; 5) devotional scenes (to be defined 1) with representations of the various gods of the Assyrian pantheon. Towards the end of the ninth century BC the motifs were engraved using the drill and cutting wheel (linear style). Stamp seals reappear at this time and – starting from the eighth century BC – gradually replace the cylinder ones as the main kind of seal. The most notable are certainly the so-called “royal seals”: circular in shape, they are known only thanks to the impressions on cretulae and tablets with royal edicts. It is possible that these seals were made of gold and that none reached us as they were likely melted to reuse the precious metal. They sometimes show a short cuneiform inscription and depict on the left the king stabbing by sword a rearing lion before him and symbolizes the enemy. The seal utilised for the administration of the queen’s goods instead show the king and queen facing the goddess Ishtar seated on a throne placed over a lion. On the upper part of the seal is depicted a scorpion, the queen’s symbol. The most direct influence on the Neo-Babylonian glyptic is the Middle Assyrian one. The use of the cutting wheel and the drill resulted at this time in a schematic style similar to the linear one of neo-Assyrian period. The contest scene between heroes and beasts or mythological creatures remains one of the most common themes, as well as the presentation scenes, which sometimes have the king as main actor. The contest scenes could also be made in a modelled style. Some stylistic and iconographic details of this style are typically Neo-Babylonian: for instance the tall headgear with feathers worn by gods and semi-divine creatures and the horned tiaras, the cherubs' wings which - unlike the neo-Assyrian ones – are always of equal length (cap. 63), the duplication of figures, the animals in contorted postures and small plants resembling cacti. Moreover, compared to the Neo-Assyrian seals, the Neo-Baby
distinguished by a lower quality of both execution and materials employed, and a greater level of decoration. The figurative repertory is the same while the inscription is shorter and more standardized. The second Kassite style (ca. 1350-1200 BC) partially overlaps the first style chronologically . New iconographic themes appear, such as the scenes of daily life (for instance hunting and plowing) as well as new characters, like chthonic deities with gushing vessels and hybrid creatures. The scene - often characterized by a tripartite structure - becomes more lively and horizontal inscriptions make their appearance. The third Kassite style starts developing in the thirteenth century BC and fades around 1150 BC, at the end of the Kassite age. Heraldic patterns are formalised at this time, with two animals (or mythical creatures) arranged symmetrically on either side of a plant element. The scenes become more natural and lively. Over time, there is thus a progressive distancing from the Old Babylonian model and an approach to the Middle Assyrian one. Characteristic of the Middle Assyrian period are the contest scenes between heroes, animals and hybrid anthropomorphic creatures (for example, the winged lion, the griffin and the winged bull). A new motif that is introduced in this period, is that of an animal walking or jumping toward a tree. The trend of the time, with the continuing quest for symbolic forms, is towards a form of abstract representation, especially starting at the end of the thirteenth century BC. Other typical elements of glyptic production for the period are the presence of arboreal vegetation and architectural elements (particularly places of worship, in front of which ritual scenes or devotional manifestations directed to the deity who lived in them were held). The latter theme is possibly linked to the fact that building activity becomes more intense in the thirteenth century BC, starting with the reign of Shalmaneser I. The inscriptions, where present, are engraved horizontally in the upper portion of the scene. In the cylinder seals of the neo-Assyrian period the scene is often framed by a horizontal line at the two ends of the seal. The popularity of inscriptions declines. The main themes of the intaglios are as follows: 1) narrative compositions, depicting sieges of enemy strongholds or battles on chariots; 2) hunting

cides with the period of most intense administrative use of this practice in the ancient Near East, that is the first centuries of the second millennium BC, when hematite became by far the most favoured material for the seals. The large concave seals of the Akkadian period (cap. 55, cap. 124, cap. 111, cap. 73) were particularly difficult to unroll on the clay surface, and it is not for a chance that there is little evidence of their administrative use. The seals of this period were, however, engraved on a great variety of beautiful semi-precious stones such as jasper, lapis lazuli, diorite and rock crystal. It is thus possible that some seals of this time were owned more as status symbol than for any practical scope. The use of seals as a status indicator is marked, starting from the Akkadian period, by the frequent addition of ornamental metal capsules attached to both ends of the seal by means of an adhesive that, in Babylonia, was usually bitumen. These capsules leave characteristic parallel grooves along the top and bottom of the impression. In the following neo-Sumerian period, some seals were carved including a stone pseudo-capsule at the ends in imitation of metal prototypes. In the Levant or in Egypt a different system for mounting the seals was developed, it was a metal ring fastened to the cylinder at both ends and allowing its rolling. These supports were made in different ways, in any case starting from a thin metal wire which was inserted through the perforation of the seal and then braided into a ring, otherwise the ends of the wire could be inserted via two through holes at the ends of a thick ring and then wrapped around it (cap. 64). These rings were not simply a handle, but they also allowed to hang the seals on strings or chains and to be worn as rings. It is likely that the idea of the signet rings (of the stamp type) of later periods was born from these prototypes. However, these holders are not necessarily contemporary with the seals, but could have been added at a later time, even much later, and could even be modern.
lonian ones are characterised by a greater dynamism. The hero is almost invariably presented in the act of placing a foot on the body of a creature he is fighting with. The heraldic scheme becomes predominant, with a winged genius at the centre overlooking two animals or mythological creatures to the sides, or the animal at the centre with two genies by its sides. Another typical subject of the Neo-Babylonian period is the priest, beardless and with shaved head, paying homage to the symbols of various divinities. This theme, common on both stamp and cylinder seals, will continue to be present in the Achaemenid period, when this kind of seal was frequently reused. At the end of the Neo-Babylonian period, the iconography of seals becomes increasingly free, with less motifs and much more empty space on the figurative surface. This tendency is even more noticeable in the Achaemenid period. It is undoubtedly an influence of the stamp seal, which – with the disappearance of the cuneiform writing and the introduction of the alphabetical one – becomes again the tool of choice, as it had been before the invention of the cylinder seal. Cylinder seals: shapes and media. Many of the most ancient cylinder seals had a concave surface. This is probably due to the fact that contemporary tablets were convex on their surface. These were shaped in the guise of a soap bar, with rounded edges. In later times the tablets, especially the larger ones, became almost flat on both sides, with thin and straight edges. For this reason a seal that was perfectly cylindrical was best suited to this shape and became commonly used. Starting from the Old Babylonian period we have the first examples of convex cylinder seals (cap. 117), or barrel-shaped. When small enough these could be mounted on a ring. The dimensions of a cylinder seal are a good indication of how widespread the use of sealing was. Sizes could vary from a few centimetres to seals big enough to almost fill the palm of a hand, their shape from short and stocky to thin and long. Smaller seals and the larger ones were difficult to roll, and the ideal size coin

Seals: a particular chapter of Mesopotamian art
Mesopotamian artists thought on a small scale, and their main vehicles of artistic expression were portable objects: vases, clay tablets, seals. As we have seen, the latter are particularly important, because many samples showing different styles and subjects survive for each period. While having a practical application, these finds represent a unique body of artefacts prior to Greek and Roman gems. Furthermore, by preserving images that no longer exist in other figurative representations, they serve as a visual chronicle of style and iconography, for instance, for clothing and peculiar headgear (cap. 68), religious symbols (cap. 69) and so on. Seals represent a unique source of information on the conditions of life in ancient Mesopotamia. No other corpus of images, in fact, offers such an abundance of documentation spanning over a comparable period of time. Glyptic production is undoubtedly one of the most characteristic outputs from the cultures of the ancient Near East; it uses a totally unique figurative language that distinguishes it from other art forms, (see for instance the arrangement of figures in different orientations – vertical and horizontal – cap. 70). It most peculiar aspect consists in the fact that the themes of the chosen representations does not seem to derive from the repertory of Mesopotamian sculpture: the seals’ iconography is almost completely independent from the large sculptural art. To cite just one example: the subjects depicted in the bas-reliefs that decorated Assyrian palaces are often completely different from what we find in neo-Assyrian seals, e.g. some curious hybrid creatures of great decorative impact, as the snake-dragon (cap. 71). As E. Porada suggests, the political circumstances could influence a period’s style. As “miniature art”, the iconography of the seals was, from an ideological and political point of view, consistent with the artistic forms of larger scale such as sculpture, reliefs and wall paintings, while having different themes and subjects. Moreover, it integrated other aspects, such as mythology and religion. The portability of this art enriched its ideologi
cal value, since it was ubiquitous, omnipresent, and lasting. The wish to commemorate significant events and the idea of pictorial representation of a story existed since prehistoric times, but it is only startring with the late fourth millennium that the Mesopotamian artists began to carve on seals what can be defined narrative scenes, even if these particular themes are few compared to those with purely decorative or symbolic aims. From the beginnings of Babylonian narrative art two methods of representation were employed. The preferred one was more indirect than explicit, using the most representative scene - a particular time in history, or the culmination of a series of events - to indicate the whole story. This was undoubtedly meant to awaken in the viewer’s mind the memory of the complete story, a bit like the crucifix in our society, which serves to evoke
The craft of the seal engraver
fig. 20
123



Signs Before the Alphabet
73
124



the entire passion of Christ and to symbolize the redemption of mankind through his sacrifice. The second method displays the consecutive episodes of a story, often juxtaposed with no clear demarcation: a group of scenes, generally two or three, sums up the whole. Very often narrative scenes refer to mythological tales. An example is represented by a seal depicting the myth of Etana (cap. 74), of which there are several comparisons in seals of the Akkadian period. According to the "Sumerian King List”, Etana, "the shepherd who ascended to heaven and consolidated all the foreign lands" was the thirteenth king of the first dynasty of Kish. On seals, Etana is depicted ascending to heaven carried by an eagle. This image strongly resembles that of the abduction of Ganymede by Zeus (in the form of an eagle), represented in numerous paintings (Correggio, Rubens, Rembrandt), but also in bas-reliefs of Roman age. A drawing by Michelangelo bears a strong resemblance with the iconography of Etana, also due to the presence of a dog. The original drawing has been lost, but several copies and engravings exist.

73-74
Cylinder seal with the Etana myth
Several scenes from the myth of Etana, the 13th king of the first dynasty of Kish. Etana is depicted while being led into the sky by the eagle. The other scenes recall the pastoral sphere, and in particular, the preparation of dairy products.
Akkadian Shell (aragonite) 4 x 2.4 cm
The seal clearly refers to the myth of Etana, also indicated in the 'Sumerian king list' (cf. ch. 7b) as the thirteenth king of the first dynasty of Kish.
Legend has it that the ruler of Kish, distressed by the fact of not being able to have an offspring due to the infertility of the queen, set off in search of the 'plant of birth', which had appeared to him in a dream. He appealed to Shamash, the sun god, who sent him to seek an eagle. The bird of prey had been thrown into a well with its wings torn by a snake, as revenge for the fact that the eagle had devoured its young. Etana freed the eagle, which out of gratitude decided to carry him to heaven in search of the 'plant of birth'. As for the ending, there are several versions: according to the tragic one, Etana began his ascent transported by the animal and reached his destination but, for reasons that are not clear, at some point they fell to the ground. In the version with a happy ending, instead, Etana was brought before Ishtar. The goddess gave him the 'plant of birth', which he was to eat together with the queen. Thus Etana had a son.
On the seal, the king is depicted as he is being carried to the sky by the eagle. His shepherd dogs lying down, watch him from below by lifting up their heads, along with a person in erect position. In the figurative space, there are also other scenes that recall the pastoral sphere: a herd inside a pen, a seated figure intent on milking a goat with two vases that were probably used to hold the milk, the preparation of cheese in a large bowl by another seated figure, and cheeses placed to dry on a roof.
These dairy preparations, although not described in the myth, are probably a reference to the fact that in the 'Sumerian king list', Etana is defined as 'the shepherd who ascended to heaven and consolidated all the foreign lands'.

The scribe and seal engraver’s materials



Signs Before the Alphabet
75
128



was considered the most precious stone of the ancient Near East. In Sumerian and Akkadian literature the comparative expression ‘like lapis lazuli’ implies great luxury. Given the difficulty of sourcing this stone and its particular value, it was generally employed to make small objects: seals, personal ornaments such as beads and amulets, while in statuary it was used to fashion the eyes, hair and beard. Furthermore, for the same reasons, this material was systematically hoarded - both as a raw material and in the shape of processed objects - and recycled. This is proven also by the progressive decrease in the size of cylinder seals in the third millennium BC and their re-use as beads. The great prestige and the magical value attributed to lapis lazuli gave the stone a special role in cult activities: it was used, for example, for the foundation inscriptions of religious buildings. Aside from rare cases, such as that of lapis lazuli, it is not easy to identify the precise places of origin of the stones, but their availability must have influenced fashion, and we can gain a great deal of information on the evolution of commerce through the study of the types of stones used for the seals in different periods. The most popular stone in the Akkadian period was without a doubt serpentine (cap. 77), usually in shades of dark green, almost black. The seals could also be made of green stone (cap. 78); sometimes black and white speckled diorite and the cores of large shells (aragonite) were used, as well as quartz crystals of various kinds, especially jasper (red and green) and rock crystal (cap. 55). Lapis lazuli appears to be less readily available than in the Early Dynastic period. In the post-Akkadian and Ur III periods, serpentine was almost completely replaced by visually similar, although slightly softer, chlorite. Towards the end of the third millennium BC a new material appeared: hematite. It is a iron oxide, dark steel-grey in color, and has a sheen that makes it similar to metal, creating a bright surface iridescence. It became the ultimate material for the production of seals in the Near East during the first four centuries of the second millennium BC (cap. 79). One of its main features is the good rendition of the visual motif, which is clearly visible on the stone and not only on the imprint of the seal itself. Hematite is undoubtedly the most characteristic material of the Old Babylonian period, but in this period also other iron oxides appear, such as goethite and magnetite, which came from the Anatolian volcanic regions accessible via the upper Euphrates . Goethite was used in Mesopotamia, but probably not in Syria and Turkey. Magnetite appeared towards the end of the nineteenth century BC, but was used for less than a century. The softer stones used were mainly black limestone (calcite). A wider variety is found among the hard stones: rock crystal, chalcedony, obsidian, mottled white and red jasper, and, later in the period, amethyst (probably from Egypt). Carnelian and agate (cap. 117) were
The scribe and seal engraver’s materials
Frederick Mario Fales - Roswitha Del Fabbro
Raw materials for the seals: stones and faience.
Seals were generally made in stone, but could also be made in faience, glass, clay, shell, bone, ivory and possibly wood, although the latter did not survive time. The stones in use were generally relatively soft, to ease engraving and processing. The fact that the majority of seals that reached us are mainly in stone does not necessarily reflect – if not in general terms – the actual distribution of the source materials employed in their making. Seals engraved in easily breakable materials, such as clay or glass, had little chances to reach us because of their fragility, while those made of perishable materials – for instance wood – did not survive because of Mesopotamia’s pedoclimatic conditions. As for the shell seals, they are often found worn, chipped or burnt. The kind of stones in use for the seal making were diverse: lapis lazuli, hematite, carnelian, chlorite, marble, quartz, serpentine, jasper (red or green), diorite (white and black), magnetite, chalcedony, agate, steatite, rock crystal, limestone, obsidian. The popularity of stones changed according to place and time. It depended primarily on the availability of raw materials and on the technology employed in the seal’s production. By far the largest part of these stones were not local to Mesopotamia and surely not from the southern flood plain, which had no natural stone resources. The stones where thus mostly imported via long-distance trade in a multi-regional context. It is not surprising then to observe how the artifacts made with materials coming from remote regions were highly prized by the ancient, and represented true status symbols. The technical evolution surely dictated the choice of stones to be used. When the first cylinder seals appeared, the materials used for the perforation – aided by abrasives – were micro-tips in chipped stone and wooden tips. A marked tendency towards a greater use of precious stones was recorded in the more advanced stage of the third millennium BC; this must have required a change in the materials used for the drill bits, i.e. a shift to copper or copper alloys, still using abrasives (possibly oiled emery). The cylinder seals of the Uruk period are, for the most part, obtained from relatively soft limestone. In a late phase harder stones started being employed, and the appearance of lapis lazuli is witness to the existence of long-distance trade. Compared to the Uruk seals, during the Jemdet Nasr period darker and sometimes harder stones (such as rock crystal) were used. Lapis lazuli (cap. 76) started being imported in large quantities and enjoyed great popularity in seal productions during the second quarter of the third millennium BC (Early Dynastic II). These came from a distant land, that is Badakhshan, a region situated in the north-western part of modern Afghanistan and in the south-eastern part of Tajikistan. The lapis lazuli mines of Badakhshan were famous in antiquity. Marco Polo described them too. Lapis lazuli
The scribe and seal engraver’s materials 129



Signs Before the Alphabet
in Ugarit, on the Syrian coast. The Mittanian seals in “elaborate style” instead continued to be made in semi-precious stones, such as hematite, agate, carnelian, chalcedony and jasper. Chalcedony and jasper were also used for the Kassite seals in the south and for the Middle Assyrian ones in northern Mesopotamia. Chalcedony (cap. 85) clearly predominates among the materials of Neo-Assyrian and Neo-Babylonian seals. The range of softer stones employed at this time includes serpentine (especially used for the Neo-Assyrian seals in the linear style of the ninth century BC), various kinds of limestone, chlorite, calcite, and soapstone. At the end of the eighth century BC, when stamp seals began to return in use in Mesopotamia, they were carved in a large variety of stones. However, when forms and motifs became strictly standardized, as in the Neo-Babylonian period, chalcedony of various colors was the most commonly used, with shades of blue being highly prevalent. This preference for chalcedony persisted even in the Achaemenid period, when stained glass became more and more popular for the manufacture of seals, especially in Syria, usually in dull colors that recalled the most favorite harder stones. Limestone was the favorite among soft stones. Sometimes it was black limestone, perhaps imported from Fars in Iran, where this stone was used for the sculptures of Persepolis. From the second half of the sixth century BC, for about two hundred years, the Persians controlled a huge empire. This allowed them easy access to raw materials. They made extensive use of barrel-shaped agate for seals (cap. 83), probably imported as beads and sometimes dyed artificially, but, surprisingly enough, the seals in lapis lazuli from this period are very rare. The taste for colored or exotic materials in the production of seals clearly indicates that there were aesthetic considerations not only about their imprint, but also with respect to how they could appear when they were worn on the chest with pins or around the neck as elements of a necklace (cap. 84). The earliest seals were sometimes in the shape of animals (cap. 86) and often came from a funerary context; these have been interpreted as amulets which ensured protection and luck to their
imported as barrel-shaped beads from the west coast of India (Gulf of Cambay), while the much rarer amazonite (cap. 80) came from the Urals and Kashmir. Clay seals were used occasionally in all periods, but particularly in the Old Babylonian period. Carnelian (cap. 81) is a variety of chalcedony. If we give credit to the textual evidence, and evaluating some Mesopotamian archaeological discoveries in the countries bordering the Persian Gulf, it seems to come from Iran and sub-continental India, via the sea route - the Persian Gulf itself - or following the land route, at least during the third and the second millennium BC. In the series HAR-ra = hubullu (XXII 35), Meluhha, which should correspond to the current Indus Valley, is poetically called ‘the country of carnelian’. The Old Babylonian lexical series abnu shikinshu, a ‘dictionary of the stones’, offers a description of the stones by analogy and specifies their practical use; the outward aspect of carnelian is described as similar to berries of boxwood, making clear reference to redness (Schuster-Brandis 2008, p. 34: 5). This stone was used to make seals, amulets, necklace beads of different shapes and to decorate tiaras and brooches. Carnelian was much appreciated in the Mesopotamian world, and represented, along with lapis lazuli and agate, one of the three most commonly used ornamental stones in the ancient Near East. Towards the middle of the second millennium BC it became possible to mass-produce an opaque material, known as faience (cap. 82), although it would be more accurate to call it sintered quartz, since it has no relation with the terracotta objects decorated with glaze and enamel, such as Faenza ceramics, whence the name. It is a term that has been improperly used for a long time to describe a type of composite material consisting of a crystalline body constituted by a layered structure of sintered quartz and at times glazed on the surface. However inadequate, the term is too deeply rooted in the literature to be abandoned. Sintered quartz was simple to engrave and the seals could therefore be easily produced in large quantities. This led to a decrease in artistic quality and to the repetition of some popular motifs. A workshop for the production of sintered quartz seals and other objects was excavated

The inscription in Akkadian cuneiform mentions Nabûmukin-apli, king of the Eighth Dynasty of Babylonia (978-943 BC).

132



manufacture of the tablet could be simply achieved by kneading a single lump of clay, or with a more sophisticated technique, with small strips joined one to the other by wetting them: the external ‘envelopes’ (used to contain texts inside) were made this way. Sometimes a core of coarser clay was lined with finer material (Taylor – Cartwright 2011). The drawbacks associated with the use of clay as a writing medium were considerable: corrections could be made only instantaneously, and once the surface was dry, adding to it was impossible. The shape and the size of a tablet had to be planned in advance, in relation to the length of text to be written on it; in this respect some problem could surely arise when (not a rare case) the message was dictated to the scribe on the fly, such as the epistolary messages destined to immediate forwarding (Charpin 2004:41). The largest number of tablets were rectangular, with writing on consecutive lines along the short side (only in Late Babylonian it was along the long side). After the Obverse, the lower edge was inscribed, then the Reverse and, finally, possibly the upper edge; the tablet was scrolled along its horizontal axis, not rotated front to back like our pages, although some rare examples of this kind of use exist. As for sizes, on average a tablet could fit in the palm; on the other hand some specimens had sides that could reach 30 or 40 centimetres and be up to 8 centimetres thick (Taylor 2011: 6). Moreover, the great plasticity of clay allowed making a great variety of shapes on which to apply writing; if in Sumerian times cone or nail-shaped texts were frequent (cap. 90), in the first millennium BC large multi-sided prisms or cylinders were shaped (cap. 17), bearing the deeds of Assyrian and later kings, while coeval loan contracts could also be written on small triangular tablets. Inscriptions could even be carved on architectural decorations, in the shape of a stretched hand, with a row for each finger (Frame 1991) and, finally, on realistic replicas of sheep livers as a ‘practical guide’ for extispicy techniques. Administrative and epistolary texts were sometimes enclosed within an envelope made with a thin strip of clay (cap. 89) to
owners. A text of the Neo-Assyrian period (KAR 185) clearly illustrates some of the magic powers (not always positive) that were attributed to the precious stones:
« A seal of hematite (portends) that the man shall lose what he has acquired. A seal of lapis lazuli (portends) that he shall have power; his god shall rejoice over him. A seal of rock crystal (portends) that he shall enlarge profits; his name shall be good. A seal of ZU.TU.UD.AŠ* (portends) that he shall walk in joy of heart. A seal of green marble (portends) that (until he comes) to the grave, favor upon favor shall be bestowed upon him. A seal of carnelian (portends) that the ‘persecutor’ shall not be released from the body of the man. » (Goff 1956, p. 27)
* the word refers to an unidentified stone
The magical significance attributed to certain stones must have guided the choice of raw materials to be used for the seals. Protective properties, linked to fate in general or to health, could be attributed both to the material itself and to the decorative motifs (Reiner, 1995: 127).
The scribe’s tools: clay, reed, metal, wood
The primary material for the production of tablets was clay, readily available in the damp Mesopotamian flood plain (on the banks of rivers and canals) and prized for its plasticity. However, this material had to be carefully polished, to purge it of impurities or encased small stones, straw, shells etc. before it could be inscribed: in some texts from Mari (eighteenth century BC) we find complaints by someone who has run out of clay, and the reference is to the supply of structured material ready to be used. Signs Before the Alphabet
copies, with an amount yet to be published possibly just a little lower (Streck 2010). Perhaps reed is, after clay, the defining element of ancient Mesopotamia. Two distinct species, Phragmites australis and Arundo donax, are well attested in the Mesopotamian flood plain for different purposes, but the second, the so-called ‘giant reed’, appears to be the one used to make the stylus (in Akkadian qan tuppi, literally ‘tablet reed’) with which cuneiform writing would be made our. A part of the stem was cut with a saw, and then split with a chisel. The external surface of the reed, shiny and
protect the texts from damage, or to keep the content hidden and, as necessary, to allow the affixing of a seal on the casing. The exterior had an inscription that was a summary of content or information on the identity of the writer. To prevent the tablet and envelope from sticking together, it is likely that the scribes waited for the text to be completely dry before preparing the envelope (Taylor in Radner-Robson 2011: 20). The archiving of the clay tablets (e.g. baskets, cap. 88) followed rules of storage space and times of preservation, related to contingencies and content: surely the libraries of tablets, which included materials to be repeatedly consulted, were planned for a long-term permanence, whether they were of scientific, predictive, literary, lexical or even historiographic nature; these tablets could also be oven-baked to make them last longer and, in any case, often new copies were made. On the contrary, the archives of common use (including accounting, administrative, legal texts and letters) were not normally designed by their authors for a long life (on average ca. 30 years), even if they were related to public institutions (Pedersen 1998); once their bureaucratic or communicative function was exhausted, the tablets could be thrown in mass into landfills, if not reused as ‘tiles’ to line a floor and raise its level. Another possibility was that of ‘recycling’ the tablets, which was obtained by soaking the surface and reformatting the clay for a new writing job (Faivre 1998). After all, because their drying was done by air (but not in the too strong sun), even when well-compacted and solid, clay surfaces could, under certain environmental conditions, be exposed to a gradual corrosion, or to fragmentation due to accidental falls or even deliberately (e.g. contracts were cancelled by breaking the tablet they were written on). However, as we know, the burning of public Mesopotamian buildings caused by enemy fury reduced to ashes many types of objects, but not the tablets, which indeed received a ‘biscuiting’ - in the potters’ lexicon – which proved very useful for their long-term conservation. This essentially explains why the number of tablets that reached down to us (and so far published) amount to more than half a million

deer, an abundant animal species in the highland areas such as Anatolia: not surprisingly, the texts in Hittite cuneiform do not show traces of residual organic reed as sometimes found in those written in Mesopotamia (Cammarosano 2014). On the other hand, clay was not the only material used as a surface for writing in Mesopotamia and neighboring regions: extensive use was in fact also made of wooden, bone or ivory boards covered with wax. As we know from the texts themselves and especially from archaeological discoveries (from the Uluburun shipwreck of the late second millennium or from Nimrud and Assur in the Neo-Assyrian period), small boards with raised edges and filled with beeswax to be written (and then to be erased and re-used) were often joined in pairs to form a diptych – or perhaps even more extended ‘triptychs’ or ‘polyptychs’ - and widely employed for short-term notations, plans and drawings, or even temporary texts to be later copied in their final form on clay tablets. The discovery of bronze styluses that ended with a sharp tip (and at the opposite extreme with a sort of spatula) in the Hittite
waterproof, prevented the absorption of moisture from the clay and its sticking to the writing surface, exempting the scribes from cleaning and drying, which would be required with a wooden stylus. The length of the stylus is estimated at around 5-6 cm, but could have been shorter. As for the writing technique, one may ask: was the stylus held in the palm of the hand or sticking out of it? And was the palm facing up or down, or maybe somewhere in between the two? Physiologically, the supine position (palm down) seems easier when moving from horizontal to vertical sections, especially in the case of a short stylus to be held in the palm. But the problem is still open, although 3-D digital photography techniques are taking research closer to the understanding of this ancient technique. Next to reed other materials for the making of the writing stylus on clay were in use in the ancient Near East: archaeological finds from various periods demonstrate the use of bone (especially in Syria) and literary texts mention the existence of gold and silver styluses, which might also have existed, but should only have had ceremonial or cultic functions. It is currently believed, also through experimental archaeology, that horn and bone were used too, specifically of

capital Hattusa shows that other forms of writing (such as hieroglyphic in the Luwian language or the alphabet in the Aramaic language) could be penned on these writing surfaces. Their content is forever lost, except for fortuitous survivals, such as at Nimrud, where a fragment of wax of the most famous series of Assyrian astronomical texts (Enuma Anu Enlil) remained attached to the surface of an ivory diptych, surviving the millennia for having been thrown into a city well. Finally, cuneiform writing was also used on corrosive-resistant materials such as metal and stone. In these cases (cap. 75) the cuneiform signs were to be scratched or engraved with completely different techniques from those used for clay or wax; e.g. for metal an iron punch could be used, which in subsequent rounds produced the ‘wedge’ and then the ‘line’ typical of cuneiform signs. Although the signs kept their three-dimensional shape on these hard surfaces, their appearance was radically different from their counterparts on soft media. A possibly exceptional case is represented by the reproduction of cuneiform signs in ink (executed
