Mankind and the gods in Mesopotamia






more) animals, and also the major Mesopotamian demons, the hideous hybrids Lamaštu and Pazuzu, were of divine substance. But this is not all: identified as divine were also numerous elements that today we would classify as inanimate, such as stars, planets, and other celestial bodies, as well as mountains and rivers and even man-made objects (temple and city gates, cultic platforms, crowns, beds, weapons, musical instruments, and war emblems) and even some illnesses (such as epilepsy, which also in the Greek world was long considered the 'sacred disease' par excellence). However, many of these identifications with the 'divine' seem to be only occasional or to change with the different periods: thus the Tigris River is often deified, but not always, whereas the Euphrates almost never is, unlike its two tributaries, the Balih and Habur. And finally, in a few cases, we find a type worship that was deliberately aniconic, such as for the 'cult of the empty throne' attested at Mari and Ebla in the late 3rd millennium; here the numinous element is constituted by the iconographic absence of a divine figure, or its replacement with a symbol, e.g. during the periodic ritual offerings of foodstuffs (Durand 2005). However, in order to emerge from the veritable 'jungle' of divine attributes in so many, and so different, aspects of ancient Mesopotamian reality, it may be worthwhile to reflect on the fact that a numinous aura of power was largely attributed to all those forces that were thought to have an impact,for better or for worse, on everyday life: therefore, not only the great anthropomorphic deities (e.g,. the god of justice Shamash), but also secondary or peripheral divine figures (cap. 96). Another characteristic of Mesopotamian deities was that of instilling a 'reverential' fear - a mixture of actual fear, fascination, and a humbling sense of mankind's limits - as in the case of the deified cosmic forces, from heaven (Anu) to the sun (Shamash), from the moon (Sîn) to the storm (Adad), but also as for tangible forces operating in the world, such as Truth and Justice; and naturally, as for forces totally opposite to them, such as the agents of chaos and destruction. Another aspect of the divine is linked to awe: its brightness or radiance, so as to amaze, stun, or fascinate the onlooker by virtue of its power, not only as a prerogative of the anthropomorphic gods but also of the astral forces. One last point to note is that a deification by association could take place: thus even the doors of a god's house of worship or the platform where its statue was placed could be deified; and the latter, insofar as it 'contained' the god in effigy, was believed to emanate the same radiance as the divine figure itself.
Mankind and the gods in Mesopotamia
Frederick Mario Fales - Roswitha Del Fabbro
The divine was all-pervasive in the society of ancient Mesopotamia, yet despite the abundance of literary references (myths, hymns, prayers) and iconography (seals, bas-reliefs, statuary) in which it figured, it is still largely indefinable from the conceptual point of view (cap. 93, cap. 94, cap. 92, cap. 97). The divine sphere was extremely broad and fluid: to the extent that even the answer to the fundamental question, "What was a god in Mesopotamia?", is likely to be neither simple nor short (Porter 2009). Certainly, all the cultures of the ancient Near East accepted the polytheistic system as a shared form of their religious identification (Biga - Capomacchia 2008). Polytheism, based on an intrinsic perception of the gods as active agents with specific functions and roles according to their own structural, collective, and institutional dynamics, allowed for the entry of more and more new cults and divine figures, even though the local practices and theological superstructures were framed by local or regional traditions (Pongratz-Leisten 2011). But to what extent was a god perceived as 'ours' and therefore venerable with a sense of exclusivity and when, instead, was it considered as a god 'of others' or - as a further solution - regarded as a figure accepted as the heritage 'of everyone'? There does not seem to be a single or fixed 'rule' on the plurality of forms of perception and veneration of the divine. However, an effort to sum up the aspects that represent the definition of a deity in Mesopotamia, at least from the mid3rd millennium BC, has established that there were three: (a) the deity has its own distinctive name, marked by a particular prefixed sign (the 'determinative', which is DINGIR in Sumerian, and ilu in Akkadian); (B) it is explicitly defined as a 'god' or may behave in the manner of a deity, such as giving blessings or receiving prayers; (C) it may receive, as an exclusive prerogative, an offering of foodstuffs from the faithful at its sanctuaries. The latter criterion certainly applies to the perception of the god by the ruling class through official cults held in various cities. The common people must have also participated in mass in the annual festivities in honor of the gods, but for now, we know little or nothing about this aspect (Oppenheim 1980). On the other hand, it may be noticed that a god did not always have exclusively anthropomorphic characteristics; for example, there are representations that show gods with a human torso with lower parts that depict natural elements (mountains, water, etc.) or that blend anthropomorphic parts with symbols (such as the winged sun disk, etc.). In addition, some deities were depicted as hybrids between humans and animals or between two (or
Mankind and the gods in Mesopotamia
91
143



Signs Before the Alphabet
spread 'cultural network' between the Mediterranean and Western Asia (especially in the 1st millennium BC). In most cases, on the other hand, the distinctive elements of Mesopotamian mythology are numerous and profound. First of all, it has been shown (Jacobsen 1986) that each of the Sumerian city-states of the 3rd millennium BC had its own specific tutelary deity, based in a central sanctuary; however, this correspondence (one city = one god) did not prevent the entire country of Sumer from possessing a unified pantheon, even beyond political divisions, which were at times bitter, between the cities themselves. Just how this dual arrangement came to be created is not clear, but there you have it: certainly, major Sumerian gods personified forces/natural aspects of collective interest, such as the sun (Utu / Shamash), wheat (the goddess Nisaba), love and beauty (Inanna /Ishtar), and the cosmic water that is believed to exist under the earth and, as such, they were for everyone. The gods were connected through relationships and genealogies, mostly within three generations; different local variations were then slowly eliminated over the course of the 3rd millennium, also as a result of political changes (W.G. Lambert). The 'rule' of one city = one god functioned mainly in the sense that the chief god gave his patronage and protection to its urban location; this did not exclude other gods, considered to be of lesser rank, from also being worshiped in his temple. A case of double patronage reigned in Uruk, home to both the father of the gods Anu and the goddess Inanna: perhaps the original subdivision of the town into two villages can explain this diarchy. In other cases, it is possible that ethno-linguistic affiliation may have created distinctions: thus the Sun-god Utu was revered in the Sumerian city of Larsa, while its Semitic counterpart Shamash was the patron of Sippar, further to the north. In conclusion, we can talk about some contiguity (clan kinship - generations - functional differences) between the Greek pantheon on Olympus and the one in Mesopotamia, but not of any real matches. A key reason for the differences lies in the fact that Homer's Iliad and Hesiod's Theogony reflect individual po
In a nutshell, in Mesopotamia the deity was viewed as the sum of its parts: and the larger the number of its parts, the greater the 'density' of the deity in question. All this, as has been said, is implemented according to a perspective of 'addition'; there is no Mesopotamian text that has left us with any image of the divine figure according to a comprehensive and coherent synthesis, such as to satisfy our Western mindset. The deities were present in many forms and in multiple locations at any time: thus, Shamash was present both in the sun, in his temple, and in the various manifestations of worship dedicated to him (e.g., in his actions of divine justice, such as his approval given to the 'code of laws' written by Hammurabi of Babylonia). What is clear, nevertheless, is that each deity operated in its own sphere of primary attributes provided with limits that could not be crossed: the moon god Sîn could not invade or cover the sphere of the sun god Shamash and vice versa, and Marduk was not the primary god in Assyria, as on the contrary, Ashur was not the primary god in Babylonia.
Accustomed as we are since having read the Iliad in school to the notion of a Greek pantheon consisting of many gods and goddesses, in which the major gods of Mount Olympus stand out often passionately capricious -, we inevitably tend to search the entirety of divine figures in Mesopotamia for a structure that may be somewhat comparable, perhaps as a prototype or archetype of the divine horizon illustrated by Homer (and by Hesiod). In some cases, this kind of research has provided positive feedback; for example, the Epic of Atrahasis narrates that the gods drew lots with their names for the division of the cosmos: Anu got the sky, Enlil the earth, and Enki the barrier blocking the ocean, and this partition of lots between related gods was taken up with several similarities in the Iliad with regard to Zeus (sky), Poseidon (sea), and Hades (Hell), although there the Earth was considered common to all the gods (Burkert 1999). So here we have a small piece of evidence of how common conceptual and ideological patterns, including ideas about the gods, circulated in a vast and wide

cient populations may have also found some difficulties in this sense. Therefore, to make their identification easier, the deities began to be represented wearing a headdress with one or more pairs of horns, which appear for the first time at the end of the Early Dynastic period. However, this rule was not always observed, and the gods sometimes appear devoid of any distinctive element. In these extreme cases, in order to define a figure as divine, it is necessary to find more clues, such as its formal role within the scene and its relationship with the other characters or elements depicted. The carvers of seals had to deal with a complex pantheon consisting of many gods with very different characteristics on the one hand, and on the other, with a small space that did not allow them excessive iconographic detail. Therefore, they were forced to establish the minimum number of persons to display, adopting a limited figurative apparel and leaving the task of revealing the identity of the protagonists in the scene to their so-called 'attributes' (objects, symbols, or particular animals relating to the deities) and to the action depicted.
Gods of creation and gods of war: Enlil and Marduk; Ashur and Ishtar
The god Enlil was one of the highest deities of the Mesopotamia pantheon, in that he decreed fates and assigned kingships; he is sometimes referred to as the 'King of Heaven and the Netherworld'. The god, mostly quoted as the son of the forefathers of the gods, Anu and the goddess Aruru, is attested since the most archaic texts. An accredited Sumerian etymology of his name is 'Lord of the Wind/Air', thus making him the central element of a possible 'vertical' cosmological triad, composed of Anu (sky) Enlil (atmosphere) - Enki (earth). However, this hypothesis is not accepted by everyone.
etic voices (although probably assembled from various earlier traditions), while the Mesopotamian pantheon encompassed a vast mass of (a) lists of gods, (b) prayer texts, and (c) works of literature, created in different times and environments, between the 3rd and the 1st millennium BC. In short, the pantheon of Sumer and Akkad is documented through a collective theological thinking that was complex and at times contradictory, even with partisan tendencies; it constituted an important sector within the massive effort of ancient Mesopotamian man to describe the world (visible or not) through the written word in all its details. Be this as it may, the extensive Babylonian list called An = Anum, which lists approximately 2,000 deities and was compiled in the late 2nd millennium, allows us to deduce a first inner hierarchy as follows: Anu - Enlil (with Ninurta) - Ninhursag - Ea (with Marduk) - Sîn - Shamash - Adad – Ishtar. Listed for each deity were the name of his wife (or husband for the goddesses Ninhursag and Ishtar) and those of the couples' children; then there were the names of the different divine subordinates officially connected to the main deity (not just the various divine viziers, but even the gods of the individual specializations going back to the chief god, such as the metalworker-gods for Ea). With time, this vast theological framework would undergo some simplifications, often through mergers of many gods into a smaller number of figures. In light of this complexity, the problem of the possibilities and limits of the identification of Mesopotamian deities in iconographic representations arises, especially regarding the vast number of (cylinder or stamp) seals that portray them, but also in other figurative art forms (cap. 95, cap. 98). In general, the inhabitants of ancient Mesopotamia represented the major gods of their pantheon in human form. The visual similarity between gods and men has obviously complicated the efforts of modern observers to understand the scenes represented, and sometimes even to distinguish between the deities and the mortals. The an
94
Votive statuette of a worshiper
Headless statue of a male figure, with Sumerian cuneiform inscription on his right arm in three lengthwise rows.
Early Dynastic III Limestone 23.5 x 13.5 x 11 cm
The figure, standing with his hands clasped on his bare chest, is dressed from the waist down in a flounced woolen skirt (the so-called kaunakes). On the front of the statue are the outlines of two feet, while the sides and back of the stone are smooth, so as to form a broad uncarved area.
A text in Sumerian cuneiform script – which presents abrasions and breaks - is engraved on the right arm from the shoulder to the elbow, in three lengthwise lines with a further division in boxes within the lines: “Lugal-bad? -, son of Ur-é-gibil?, by profession (??) ..., for the [life] of his wife [and children], [has dedi]cated (this statue).”
Given the conditions of preservation, the translation provided is to be considered largely hypothetical. The very structure of the text is open to different hypotheses: we might have to do with a totally private dedicatory inscription, or with a text written by a courtier in favor of his king as well as of his own family. But of course other reconstructions of the context, differing from the two suggested here, are also possible.
The origin of the statuette is also open to interpretation, on the basis of known figurative and textual data. While the text has some points of contact with the private inscriptions of southern Mesopotamia, and especially with those of Nippur, it should also be said that the iconography finds a good parallel with the statuary from the Middle Euphrates region, and particularly with that of Mari (modern Tell Hariri). Around the middle of the 3rd millennium BC, Mari was a major center of economic and cultural connections between Mesopotamia and Syria; therefore, in essence, it cannot be entirely ruled out that the piece may come from Mari itself or neighboring areas.

poem 'Enlil and Ninlil' which tells of the seduction of the goddess by a highly lustful Enlil and the birth of their offspring, which included Ningirsu, the moon god Nanna (Su'en in Semitic), and Nergal, the god of war, death, and plague (also mentioned in the legends on seals, cap. 79, cap. 126). With the rise of the Babylonian dynasty, Enlil gradually lost his importance with respect to Marduk; but the myth of Anzu also tells of the growing cult of his son Ninurta. Nevertheless, the Code of Hammurabi indicates that the king had been designated by Enlil. Finally, in Assyria the preeminence of Enlil went on for centuries, gradually merging with the figure of the local god Ashur.
The god Marduk was the supreme god of Babylon, where his temple, Esagila, and the temple tower Etemenanki (whose size and opulence is commemorated in the biblical story of the Tower of
At all events, the power and the preeminence of Enlil may be grasped in a series of epithets related to nature, to humanity, and to the gods themselves: 'Great Mountain', 'Father of mankind (literally, of black-headed people)' , and 'Father of all the gods'. In the Akkadian myth of Anzu, which is part of the poetic cycle dedicated to his son Ninurta, and which was also part of the theology and annual cultural activities of Nippur, Enlil holds the 'tablet of destinies', the possession of which involves the command of the whole world; while in the self-celebratory inscription by King Lugalzaggesi, it is Enlil who gave him kingship, as he then likewise did for Ur-Namma. Enlil's command was unalterable, without remission; even though in the Babylonian myth of Atrahasis, he had to review his initial decision to destroy humanity through famine, plague, and the Flood. His wife was Ninlil, as we know from the mythological Sumerian

Mankind and the gods in Mesopotamia 149



Signs Before the Alphabet
precise theological-political purposes; the poem served to disseminate, proclaim, and justify Marduk's 'rise to power' as the chief deity of Babylonia, so as to even supplant Enlil at the head of the local pantheon. In essence, the text glorifies the rise of Marduk, first through the reconstructed family genealogy of the god, then through the story of his victorious battle against Tiāmat, and finally, through granting him fifty 'names' or epithets, taken up from the existing lexical lists of deities, which placed the new god solidly within the traditional pantheon (Seri 2006). Precisely this list of names should have ensured that the fame of Marduk would be imperishable, for as long as mankind would still able to read and write (7: 143-48):
« With the name 'Fifty', the great gods proclaimed him whose names are fifty and made his way supreme. Let them be kept in mind and let the elder (scholar) explain them. Let the wise and the intelligent discuss them together. Let the father repeat them and thus instruct his son. Let them be known (even) to the shepherd and the herdsman. » (translation by Michalowski 1990)
In seals that depict him, the figure of the god Marduk is sometimes replaced by one of his symbols, a pointed spade (marru in Akkadian), such as in a scene of worship of the god, also showing the winged disk which is the symbol of the god Ashur (cap. 100; cf. also cap. 69, where we find both the divine symbols). The animal associated with the god is the mythological mushkhushshu, a composite dragon (scaly skin, bird-like hind legs, feline front paws, long neck and tail, with a horned snake's head), which is depicted, for example, on the Ishtar Gate in Babylon, built by Nebuchadnezzar II in 575 BC (now in the Berlin Museum).
The god Ashur, the deity who would become the mirror counterpart and the maximum rival in heaven and on earth of Marduk, was actually born as the simple patron god of the homonymous city built on a rocky outcrop on the middle stretch of the Tigris River. Thus it is a case of a real numen loci, who appears to have had no direct counterpart in ancient Mesopotamia: at most, one could cite the parallel of Athena for Athens in later times (Livingstone 1997). Since the 21st century BC onward, when it was a mere provincial centre in the reign of the Ur III Dynasty, the city of Assur (also Ashur) made itself independent and the temple of the local deity began to be built on its highest area, well visible above the river. The deity was fed with first-fruits from a large agricultural hinterland surrounding its urban seat. In the few official documents that have reached us regarding this first phase (Old Assyrian period, ca. 2000-1800 BC), there is nothing to denote an aggressive religious ideology, based on war and territorial conquest focusing on the god Ashur; and, indeed, in some texts of the rulers at that time, who were considered as mere
Babel) were located. This god is primarily known to us by way of the epic poem Enūma Elish (from the introductory text, Enūma elish la nabû shamāmū, 'when the sky above did not yet have a name'), better known as the 'Epic of Creation'. The text has survived in copies from the first half of the 1st millennium BC, but it was probably composed at the end of the 2nd millennium (the Kassite period) and also shows older materials. It was recited every year at the New Year's Feast (akitu, celebrated around March 15th) in Babylon, according to a tradition that arose in Ur at the end of the 3rd millennium (Sallaberger 1993). During the several days of the Akitu, the ritual and especially the recitation of the poem, were intended to emphasize the restored cosmic order and the renewed institutionalization of the local and supraregional cult for the current year, as a sort of renewed enthronement of the god. The poem is essentially a hymn to Marduk and describes his rise to the top of the Mesopotamian pantheon. Beginning with the creation of the world, when only the divine beings Apsû and Tiāmat, i.e. the fresh terrestrial waters and the salty marine waters, existed. the poem narrates, first with a genealogical and then with an epic approach, how the god Marduk became eventually king of the gods and how his order was finally established in the universe. Apsû was killed by his son Ea, who assumed the crown as the head of the gods himself, with an action that was reproduced in part in the primordial Greek mythology described in the Theogony by the poet Hesiod (8th-7th centuries BC). However, it was Tiāmat who possessed the fundamental 'tablet of destinies', and she entrusted it to her son Kingu. Tiāmat then rallied the older cosmic powers while the younger gods elected as their champion Marduk, who then challenged the goddess-sea monster: in essence, the true epicentre of the narrative is the primordial battle between Marduk and Tiāmat (Col. 4, ll. 93-103): Marduk's victory thus saves the gods and opens the way for the construction of the cosmos, and finally, for the creation of man as a servant working for the gods themselves. The etymology of the divine name 'Marduk' was much discussed (Jacobsen 1968): one might think of an original Sumerian form * amar-utu-(ak) as 'Son of the Sun' or 'Son of the Storm'. Of these, the latter seems the interpretation most appropriate to the deity, as described in Enūma Elish itself. So when Marduk is armed for battle, he is preceded by lightning and fills his body with burning flames (4. 39-40). When speaking of his birth, the text says that he possessed and controlled the Four Winds, bequeathed to him by his grandfather Anu (4.41-44 and 1.105 ff.). He himself produces further natural phenomena: he generates the storm, the whirlwind, the hurricane, and the wind of '4 limbs', the wind of 'seven limbs', the sandstorm, and the 'wind without rivals' (4.45- 46), and his greatest weapon is the 'cyclone' or the 'storm of the Flood' (abūbu, 4.49). However as this may be, Enūma Elish proved to have also had

have known the first development of an ideology of retaliation, perhaps fueled by the experience of armed war in which the Hurrians themselves excelled. What is certain is that after this phase, the religious and political scenario centered on the god Ashur changed radically. And at that stage, and especially at the end of the 14th century BC, we find the first formulation of the idea of kingship on theocratic foundations, according to which Ashur (and other gods of the local pantheon) held the cosmic power and the legitimate authority to incite the king and his people to conquer the surrounding regions. The first hint of this lies in the words addressed to the king by the High Priest during that Middle Assyrian coronation rite:
« May Ashur and Mullissu, the lords of your headdress, cover you with it for a hundred years! May your foot be acceptable in the Ekur and your hands in the bosom of Ashur, your god! Before Ashur your god, may your priesthood and that of your children be acceptable. With your just sceptre, enlarge your land! May Ashur grant you the capacity to command, to listen, to answer prayers, of truth, and of peace » (translation by Fales 2015)
It is this transfer of powers from the god Ashur – risen to the top of a veritable pantheon in the Middle Assyrian period – to the king who, without abandoning his previous role as 'representative', now also assumed personal traits of moral virtue and attributes of physical prowess, thus placing himself within an intermediate cosmic space between the divine and pure and simple humanity, which achieved the elevation of Assyrian kingship to the highest levels in history. In essence, starting in the 14thcentury BC, the vision of a single political and ideological centre, the 'land of Ashur'
'representatives' of the deity, it is rather difficult to distinguish the outlines of this local god from Enlil, the beneficial pan-Mesopotamian figure. Signs Before the Alphabet
- will increasingly take on the role of 'Holy See' and privileged place of pilgrimage for the whole of Assyria. The theological growth of the god Ashur is precisely fixed in the Middle Assyrian period through his equivalence with Enlil, the Sumerian 'king of the gods'. In paral
lel, Ninlil, the wife of Enlil, will become the wife of Ashur, under the local dialectal name of Mullissu; the city of Assur will take on the nickname Libbali, the 'inner city' (or the citadel par excellence), borrowing this designation from Nippur, the ancient religious capital of Sumer; and the central temple will be named Esharra ('home of the universe'), like the sanctuary of Enlil at Nippur. The sacred city thus becomes a new Nippur, on which to build the 'destiny' of the king as the conqueror of the world. One of the most coveted objectives for the political and ideological ideal of unifying all the surrounding territories under Assyrian rule, was actually represented by the conquest of Babylonia itself, with its ancient 'holy cities' and those cultural prerogatives of antiquity, sacredness and knowledge for which the southern region was famous. Crucial in this respect will be the theological rise of Ashur to equal and surpass Marduk, the god of the second generation who had replaced Enlil in the mythological-epical Babylonian complex. This orientation began with Sargon II (722-705 BC), who decreed the equivalence of Ashur with (the similar-sounding) Anshar, one of Marduk's ancestors in the Poem of Creation, and reached its climax with Sennacherib (704-681 BC), who razed to the ground the southern capital in 689. However, the clearly anti-Babylonian policy of Sennacherib - who had moved the New Year's Feast to the religious capital of Assur - was followed by a precise 'counter-reform' of Esarhaddon (680-668 BC), aimed at fully restoring the cults and festivities, as well as the political prerogatives, of Babylon.
The goddess Inanna (in Sumerian) or Ishtar (in Semitic) is one of the most complex divinities of ancient Mesopotamia, with contradictory and even paradoxical traits; she has been described as
began to be established, leading to the development of the practice of a military conquest (seen as the imposition of the 'yoke of Ashur') with regard to a vast periphery, conceived as 'other' and 'different' (Postgate 1992). The iconography of the god Ashur is often expressed through a series of individual or composite symbols: a winged disk, sometimes provided with horns, from which rippling sunrays radiate (cap. 99); a circle or a wheel, suspended from the wings, enclosing a warrior in the act of shooting an arrow with a bow. The symbols of the figure are likewise common to other gods: a rod (curved or maybe soft) and a ring, interpreted as measuring emblems. Other interpretations are based on a pastoral staff (or ferule) and a nose ring. The overall characteristics of the Assyrian ideological agenda were repeated by all the successive kings, although with a number of minute variations, until the violent and definitive fall of the last imperial capital, Nineveh, in 612 BC. Two examples of this may suffice: at the end of the 12th century BC, King Tiglath-pileser I already stated clearly that:
« The god Ashur and the great gods who exalt my kingship, who granted as my lot power and strength, commanded me to extend the borders of their land. They placed in my hands their mighty weapons, deluge in battle.»
While, a few decades before the end of the imperial experience of the Neo-Assyrian period (9th -7th centuries BC), the long-lived King Ashurbanipal (668-631 BC) could still repeat the formula of enthronement of the previous centuries, attributing to the god Ashur's power the essence of his own huge cosmic, political, and military power;
« Ashur is King – indeed Ashur is King! Ashurbanipal is the representative of Ashur, the creation of his hands. May the great gods make firm his reign, may they protect the life of Ashurbanipal, king of Assyria! May they give him a straight sceptre to extend the land and its people. May his reign be renewed, and may the gods consolidate his throne forever! May they bless the king every day, month, and year, and protect his rule! In his reign, may there constantly be rains from the heavens and floods from the underground sources! May our lord Ashurbanipal be granted long days, copious years, strong weapons, a long reign, years of abundance, a good name and fame, happiness and joy, auspicious oracles, and leadership over all (the other) kings! »
Therefore, over time Ashur would evolve from a mere numen loci to an actual 'total god' for the community that he aims to protect, in isolation vis-à-vis the other 'great gods'. The latter are honored in temples and chapels throughout the country, but Ashur is revered only in the eponymous city, which - especially after having lost its original function of political capital under King Ashurnasirpal II (858-824 BC)

Inanna and Enki). However, in other traditions she is defined as the daughter of Anu or of the moon god Nanna/Su'en/Sîn and the latter's wife, Ningal/Nikkal; yet elsewhere, she is the sister of the Sun god Utu - Shamash. The latter god grants Inanna/Ishtar in marriage to the shepherd (and god of vegetation) Dumuzi without her consent, but she then convinces her husband to court her properly. These aspects are contradicted by the figure of the goddess in the Epic of Gilgamesh, written at a later date; here Inanna/Ishtar, taken by the young hero, invites him to become her lover, but Gilgamesh rejects her, accusing her of having had a series of past lovers whom she had abandoned or destroyed. One trait that is common to the various manifestations of this figure is her sexuality and sensuality; not surprisingly, Inanna/Ishtar was the recipient of prayers to solve symptoms of impotence or cases of unrequited love (Leick 1994). The role of the goddess in the legitimization of sexual potency was also expressed in the ceremony of the 'sacred marriage', which celebrated the marriage of the goddess with her husband Dumuzi - represented respectively by the high priestess and by the king – during the New Year's Feast, as a means of ensuring prosperity and the abundance of crops. Even the iconography of Inanna/Ishtar is remarkably intricate. Originally Inanna was the goddess of earth and fertility, an aspect highlighted by the bunch of dates that she often holds in her hand in artistic representations. Her naked representation, linked to fertility, became especially popular in Syria, where the goddess was variously identified with Astarte, Anath, Asherah, and Ashtaroth in West Semitic and Canaanite religion (cap. 108). tween man and animal (Harris 1975). In Sumerian poetry, she is

Signs Before the Alphabet
representation of Inanna/Ishtar. Finally, Ishtar was also an astral deity. The Semitic Ishtar is characterized more specifically as the goddess of love and war, and as an astral deity associated with the planet Venus. Together with Shamash, the sun-god, and Sîn, the moon-god, Ishtar formed an astral triad in support of justice and divination, to which the Storm god Adad,was also partially associated. As stated in a passage from the Old Babylonian text of the Prayer to the gods of the night:
« The gods and goddesses of the land - Shamash, Sîn, Adad, and Ishtar – have entered into the womb of the sky; they do not make judgments, do not render verdicts. The night is veiled.” » (Reiner 1985: 68; Cooley apud Lenzi 2011: 71-85)
Another aspect that has been linked to the goddess is her being on the threshold between life and death, deriving from her function of descending into the Netherworld and returning from it (as we know from the Sumerian poem, Inanna's Descent to the Netherworld and its counterpart in Akkadian). Thus, Ishtar, angry at the refusal of Gilgamesh to her amorous advances, threatens the god Anu that if he does not send the Bull of Heaven to destroy the hero,
« If thou openest not the gate to let me enter, I will break the door, I will wrench the lock, I will smash the door-posts, I will force the doors. I will bring up the dead to eat the living. And the dead will outnumber the living! » (Gilgamesh VI, 97-100; translation by Pettinato 1992: 174, and cf. George 2003: 695)
depicted while raising both hems of her skirt (cap. 101), revealing her nudity, thereby creating a curious image in which it looks like the deity is jumping rope. It is possible that this particular iconography is an intimation of the story of the descent of Inanna/Ishtar to the Netherworld for love of the shepherd Dumuzi, when the goddess took off the 'seven veils' she wore. According to the myth, Inanna/Ishtar passed through seven gates, and at each one, she was required to take off a garment or an ornament, so that by the seventh gate, she was completely naked. But Inanna/Ishtar was also a goddess of war: this aspect of hers seems to express itself above all in politically meaningful contexts, where the deity is praised in relation to royal power and military force, already by Naram-Sin of Akkad, who calls her 'the warlike Ishtar' and then much later by the kings of the Assyrian empire. It might be said that although the sexuality of the goddess is unquestionably feminine, her nature also shows ample masculine traits. Starting with the Old Babylonian period, the warrior aspect of Ishtar is especially emphasized in the iconography through the battle apparel that she wears, with quivers full of arrows hanging on her back (cap. 103, cap. 104), holding a mace with a double lion's head in her raised right hand (cap. 102) and a scimitar in her lowered left hand. The goddess is depicted frontally in an ascending posture, with a foot resting on the back of her animal-symbol (cap. 106), the lion, sometimes holding onto its leash, or onto a bird with a lion's head. This iconography survived with limited variations for more than a millennium in Mesopotamia and also in the surrounding regions (cap. 105). It is thus likely that the well-executed and imaginative terracotta plaque, of unknown archaeological context - acquired in 2004 by the British Museum - known as 'the Queen of the Night' is a

Model of a chariot
Fragment of a chariot model, of which only the parapet with the representation in relief of Ishtar in her warrior aspect has been preserved.
Isin/Larsa or Old Babylonian Terracotta 15 x 7.5 x 2 cm
The goddess holds a bow in her raised left hand and another weapon, perhaps a scimitar, in her lowered right hand, kept at her side; her feet are placed on her assigned animal, probably a lion, though it is poorly preserved. The face and torso of the goddess are in frontal view, while her legs are depicted in profile. The deity is wearing a high conical headdress and a long robe with vertical pleats cascading to her feet, held in place by a belt around her waist. Two 'stars of Ishtar' are engraved at the top of the parapet.
Chariot models represent one of the most common kinds of craft production in the Mesopotamian world from the late 3rd millennium until the end of the 2nd millennium BC. However, the greatest concentration of production was restricted to a period of time ranging, in terms of absolute chronology, from ca. 2112 to 1595 BC, i.e. the periods of the Ur III dynasty, of Isin/Larsa, and until the end of the Old Babylonian period.
With rare exceptions, the surface of the parapet bearing the embossed decoration (usually obtained by a mold) is the internal one. The outer face is often not even flattened.
These small models represented the two-wheeled chariots for which A. Salonen (Die Landfahrzeuge des Alten Mesopotamien, Annales Academiae Scientiarum Fennicae 72 (3), Helsinki 1951) reconstructed the Sumero-Akkadian terms of their various constituent parts. The two-wheeled chariot (narkabtu in Akkadian) was formed by a high parapet, i.e. a rigid part which extended at the front of the chariot. The double camber on the parapet-top (often perforated in two places to allow the passage of the reins) served as a reinforcement and as a handhold for the charioteer. The chariot body (= bit narkabti) of our exemplar has been lost.
A model closely comparable to this artifact was found at Kish (M.T. Barrelet, Figurines et reliefs en terre cuite de la Mesopotamie antique, Paris 1968, pl. 59 n. 623). In addition to presenting the same type of figure, it also shows a close stylistic kinship with the present one. Based on this comparison, this artifact can be dated to the Isin-Larsa period (ca. 2000-1800 BC).

tamian burials contain references to this liminal quality of Ishtar: from rosettes (or 8-pointed stars) to iconographic motifs including lions and lionesses in decorations with red carnelian (at times coupled with lapis lazuli, symbolizing Dumuzi). There are numerous allusions to these 'funerary' aspects of the goddess, from the Royal Tombs of Ur to the 'frit' glazed masks of the Mari period up to the Neo-Assyrian tombs of Ashur, and the decorations on the golden crowns in the tombs of the queens from Nimrud. Ishtar was associated with the cult in various cities and - in each one - seems to have taken on different personifications (or hypostases). In Sumerian literary texts, it is said that, for her mission to the Netherworld, she abandoned all her positions in the temples of several cities in the south of the country, from Uruk in Bad-tibira to Zabalam, in Adab, in Nippur, Kish, in Akkad, in Umma, in Ur, in Kissik, in Girsu/Lagash, in Isin, in Akshak, in Shuruppak, and in Kazallu; the goddess was also worshiped in Babylon (cap. 112). In the Neo-Assyrian period, we know of several local hypostases, such as 'the Assyrian Ishtar' (or: of the city of Assur)', the 'Lady of the Palace' (Belet-ekalli), the 'Queen of the Rising Star' (Sharrat-Nipha), 'Ishtar of Nineveh' and 'Ishtar of Arbela'. Of the latter two, the Nineveh figure had wide circulation (even reaching the Hittite and Hurrian worlds with the name of Shaushka), while the Ishtar of Arbela (today Erbil, in Kurdistan) was connected to the activity of a number of priestesses who, in her name, produced spontaneous utterances, prophetic in nature, much feared and heeded by kings such as Esarhaddon (680-668 BC). The goddess had a divine vizier (sukkal), of female but sometimes male sex, named Nin-shubur (Wiggermann 1998-2001), who could also act as an interceding deity for the faithful, and is also mentioned as such in the inscriptions of the seals from the first half of the 2nd millennium BC (cap. 109,cap. 115, cap. 116, cap. 80) and on mace heads (cap. 113).

Signs Before the Alphabet
deities of the storm and its accompanying phenomena (thunder, lightning, clouds, rain and wind) held a preeminent place in the local pantheons, or were even considered divine rulers, foremost among the gods, and bestowers of kingship on the human monarch. The most important Storm gods were the Semitic *Hadda (in Akkadian, Adad or Addu, in Western Semitic, Haddu or Hadad), the Syro-Palestinian god Ba'lu or Ba'al (cap. 107), the Hurrite Teshub (and the Urartian Teisheba) and lastly the Hittite /Luwian Tarhunt (Schwemer 2008). Apparently unrelated to any of these are the Sumerian god Enlil, his son Ninurta and lastly the head of the Babylonian pantheon Marduk, who show only partially the same characteristics, and who, moreover, express the world of irrigation that depended on storms only to a certain extent. On the other hand, considering the link between the ‘storm’ and rain-fed agriculture in areas where it was sufficiently abundant, it is unsurprising to notice that the Mesopotamian god Adad/Addu was also worshiped as the propagator of floral life. The Akkadian god Adad had, to be sure, a Sumerian ‘ancestor’, known as Ishkur, written with the logogram im = ‘wind, storm’, whose cult is known especially at Lagash and Adab in the Pre-Sargonic period and later in several other cities (Girsu, Umma, Nippur, and Uruk). In some traditions he is a further child of Enlil,
Gods of nature: Adad (storm), Shamash (sun), Nanna/Sîn (moon), Enki/Ea (water)

Cylinder seal with deity and inscription
Two pairs of figures facing one another are separated by a cuneiform inscription. The god Adad is holding the symbol of lightning in his right hand and his right foot is placed on a bull, his animal symbol. In front of him, a faithful is depicted, perhaps a king. In between the two figures is a crescent moon. The other pair consists of an offering-bearer carrying a goat in her arms advancing towards a deity who is holding a stick with seven balls in his right hand. This is probably the god Shamash: at the end of the Old Babylonian period, in fact, the saw that had distinguished him in the previous phase was replaced by the stick with seven balls.

The abode of Adad is obviously ubiquitous. In the Epic of Gilgamesh he is said to reside on the high mountains in the forest of cedars where the monster Huwawa/Humbaba lived. Due to his obvious connections with the weather and the harvest, but also perhaps as god of the winds, Adad also became in time the god of divination, in association with Shamash; and this role spread also to Syria and Anatolia. In the Middle Euphrates city of Mari (18th cent. BC), Adad/Addu was the head of the pantheon, along with Dagan – the father of the gods in Syrian tradition and Itur-Mer, the local dynastic deity. The cult of the Storm god is attested all over Syria in the ‘Amorite’ period, along the Upper Euphrates (Tuttul, Terqa) as far as Qatna beyond the Syro-Arab desert, and later in the coastal cities, from the Hurrian city of Alalakh to Ugarit. The temple of Hadad at Aleppo was also held in great reverence. It has been excavated in recent decades, yielding a rich store of sculpture and epigraphs (Gonnella et al. 2005), from the 2nd millennium to the period of the Aramean states in the next millennium. In Anatolia, lastly, the Myth of Kumarbi indicates this deity (often confused with Dagan) as the father of Teshub, the Hittite Storm god. In Old Babylonian seals, the Storm god is depicted in various
sometimes the twin brother of Enki/Ea, responsible for the celestial waters. The cult of Adad/Addu is unusual in that it extended – like the Semitic languages themselves – throughout the Near East. The first documentation of the god’s name (as 'a-da) came from Ebla in northeastern Syria (23rd cent. BC), but it probably referred to a an even older deity from the Syro-Mesopotamian area. In Mesopotamia proper, the cult of Addu/Adad flourished in coincidence with the coming to power of the dynasties of Amorite origin in the early 2nd millennium BC. From the Old Babylonian period on, this figure was included among the ‘great gods’ whose cults extended throughout the land, from the south to the region of the Diyala River northeast of the Middle Euphrates and lastly to Assur on the Middle Tigris (where a temple to the god stood since the Old Assyrian period). Under Shamshi-Adad I (18th cent. BC) the original temple of Adad in this city was transformed into a dual sanctuary, dedicated to the Storm god but also to the king of the Heavens, Anu. The figure of Adad is an ambivalent one. On the one hand, he is the rain-god, who also controls the underground waters, ensuring fertile fields and the survival of the population; while on the other he can endanger the harvest with storms, flooding or their opposite, drought (Schwemer 2008). An epithet of the god calling him ‘Lord of lightning, the splendor that illuminates the universe’ clearly shows this ambivalence. Even better, it is clarified by a curse found in the final section of the Code of Hammurabi, addressed to any future sovereign who might violate the sense of the text:
« May Adad, Lord of Abundance, inspector of the channels of

Cylinder seal with offering to a deity and inscription
The god Shamash receives the offering of a goat from a figure that is in front of him, while the interceding goddess Lama watches the scene.
Old Babylonian Hematite 2.5 x 1.3 cm
The seal is slightly concave with the hole filled by a copper loop. Given the particular shape of the seal, we may assume that there were capsules in gold (or other precious metal) at the two ends, which were probably removed to be fused and to reuse the raw material.
The decoration presents, at the far right, an image of the sun god Shamash (cf. ch. 6b), standing and dressed in a long robe and wearing a typical divine headdress with horns. His foot is placed on a stylized mountain and he is easily identifiable thanks to one of his most frequent attributes, a knife with a serrated blade brandished in his lowered right hand, with which - according to myth – he cut his way through the mountains in order for the sun to rise at dawn. The god is receiving the offer of a goat - probably destined for sacrifice - from a figure that is standing directly in front of him, with the characteristic round cap with upturned brim and a foot-length ceremonial robe. Behind him is the goddess to whom he is praying, Lama, in her classic iconography with both hands raised to chin height.
Three lines of cuneiform script inform us of the name and the function of the owner of the seal. There is also mention of a minor deity, Nin-shubur, vizier of the goddess Ishtar, who could also act as a god by interceding for the faithful.
Inscription: d.NIN.ŠUBUR SUKKAL Mar-zu-zu KIN kilib.ba ag2.e
“The god Ninshubur, the vizier. Mar-zuzu, who has measured the total labor (?)”

« Great wild bull of the sacred heavens, young Utu, stable as a torch, who offers his own gaze, the sunlight of the earth » (Shulgi Q, l. 7)
But since the sun is a star that moves through the sky all day long, lighting up the earth without a pause, the deity that represents it can see everything that happens; and this link between astral function and observation of human behaviour led Shamash to become the protector of the voyager by land and by sea. The same link between illumination and observation is the key to understanding the role of Shamash in exercising justice, often acting in common with Adad, but more often as the unrivalled god of the sector. This essential role of Shamash, as the benevolent god of justice – sometimes in conjunction with other astral gods, such as the Moon and Venus – began to emerge forcefully from the Old Babylonian period on; and as such it is the Sun god who is celebrated in the prologue of the Code of Hammurabi. It is through his close association with Shamash that the King of Babylonia conducts his task of establishing justice through a set of basic rules governing the private and public spheres of law. And it is expressly the image of this god – with the characteristic rays of light emanating from his shoulders – that stands before the reverent sovereign in bas-relief in the upper part of the tall stele in polished black diorite that bears the text of the Code. The stele was found by French archaeologists at Susa in1902,
ways, which undoubtedly refer to two different traditional conventions. One of these is based on the tradition on the Akkadian period, when the Storm god’s symbolic animal was a hybrid creature, a lion-griffin or – according to other scholars – a leonine winged dragon. In the other tradition, that of the western populations of Anatolia and Syria, which emerged strongly in the second millennium BC, the god was represented, with few exceptions, standing on a bull. Here he appears with a hairdo falling to his back in a long curl, a horned tiara with a point or knob at the top, and a short skirt. A dagger hangs from his belt and he brandishes a mace, while holding a whip and the reins of the bull. Another of the storm god’s most characteristic attributes was the stylized lightning bolt held in his hand (cap. 10). Shamash. This deity, known in Sumerian as Utu, represented the bright light of the sun, which returns each day to shine on the Mesopotamian plain and illuminates human activities. As such, Shamash is the chief figure of a celestial triad including the Moon god Sîn and the star Venus (Inanna/Ishtar), sometimes joined by the Storm god Adad. But a further characteristic of this figure, closely linked to its daily appearance above the Zagros mountains, was that of determining destinies, which led to a specific function in the practice of divination (see below). His most frequent images in Sumerian literature are those of a ‘wild bull’, a ‘bison’ or a ‘young hero’, as in a hymn of Shulgi addressed to the god:

172



but had been brought to the capital of Elam in the 12th cent. BC as the spoils of war from the Mesopotamian city of Sippar, site of the god’s greatest temple, Ebabbar. As the god of justice, Shamash enjoyed great prestige for the entire duration of Mesopotamian civilisation. Lastly, Shamash was one of the chief patrons of divination, and especially of extispicy. Here too, some of the functions pertaining to this role were shared with other deities, notably the storm god Adad, especially in the Old Babylonian period (Charpin 2013). Nonetheless, in the Neo-Assyrian period, it was to Shamash that the rulers of the empire turned. It was Shamash who, through the ‘manual of extispicy’, helped to count the auspicious and inauspicious features to be found in the entrails of the sacrificed goat or sheep, expressing in the end a lapidary judgment: either positive or negative (Starr 1990). In the seals of the Akkadian period, Shamash is the deity most frequently depicted. The god is often shown in the act of rising from the eastern mountains (cap. 127) – as in the Sumerian and Akkadian poetic texts, where he is compared to mountain animals – while two attendants open the gates of the heavens. Alternatively, Shamash is depicted seated (cap. 111) or standing, his foot resting on a stylized mountain (cap. 115, cap. 116) or on a recumbent bull, while he receives a worshiper wearing a round beret with a turned-up brim and a long gown open in the front, revealing his nude leg. Cylinder seal with contest scene
Contest scene between animals and heroes. A bull-man faces a lion, while two naked heroes face two animals with human faces and bull bodies (some scholars believe these are stylized depictions of bisons, whose frontal view of the head is always presented as a human face, because it was more familiar to the engraver than the animal's muzzle), which are arranged symmetrically. The naturalistic rendering of the human body and of the animal body and the explicit search for symmetry and balance in the representation are characteristic of the glyptics in the Akkadian period.
Akkadian Serpentine 3.1 x 2 cm

2013). A simple half-moon would have indicated the new moon, that is, the beginning of the month. In cases where the crescent moon was associated with the solar disk (the astral symbol of the sun-god Utu/Shamash) it would have indicated the only time when the two heavenly bodies were visible in the sky together, i.e., the days of full moon, around the fifteenth day of the month, when the moon rises fully in the east while the sun is setting in the west. To these two lunar phases were associated specific festivities, most notably the esh-esh festival, which was celebrated on the first and the fifteenth day of the month, in coincidence with these two important astral events that occurred each month and that regulated the life of the community. Another symbol of the god, frequently found in the Old Babylonian period, was the bull, due to the resemblance between its horns and the crescent moon. Enki (in Akkadian and in Babylonian mythology Ea) was the god of freshwater, of underground waters (abzu, in Akkadian apsû, from which our term ‘abyss’ seems to derive, through Greek) and of wisdom. He was the son of the god Anu and the brother of Ish
kur/Adad. The god’s main cult centre was the E-abzu in the city of Eridu, near the Persian Gulf. His act of providing freshwater made him a benevolent god in the eyes of Mesopotamian man. Accordingly, his role in the mythological texts – from the epic of
arms, which he offers to the god, and is followed by the interceding goddess Lama. The identification of this god with Shamash is also aided by the rays of light that at times shine from his shoulders, representing the warmth of the sun (as in the Code of Hammurabi). Starting from the Ur III period, Shamash is symbolized by the solar disk and later, under Syrian influence, he is represented by a winged disk. To the god in his symbology of winged sun, mythological creatures of half-human, half-animal appearance were at times associated, placed at the gates of the most important public buildings, to symbolise the entrance and departure of the sun in its daily journey.
The Moon god. The two main cult centres of the Moon god Nanna (in Akkadian, Suen, then Sîn) were located in geographically opposed cities along the axis of the Euphrates: at Ur, in the lower Mesopotamian alluvial plain, which was the site of his first temple, Ekishnugal (cap. 12) and at Harran on the Balikh tributary in northern Mesopotamia. Nanna was the son of Enlil and of his consort Ninlil; his wife was the goddess Ningal (later pronounced Nikkal) and their children were the sun god Utu and Inanna, while at a later period their son, the god Nusku, was the god of fire. The importance of this deity in the Sumerian period is clearly shown in the text The Journey of Nanna/Suen to Nippur, where the Moon brings abundant offerings of first fruits to Enlil, head of the pantheon, receiving gifts in return from him. In the 1st millennium BC instead, the triad Sin-Nikkal-Nusku enjoyed vast prestige with the Aramaic populations in the area around Harran. The temple of Sin at Harran took up a renewed prominence in the post-Assyrian period thanks to the special devotion for the lunar cult of the last Neo-Babylonian king, Nabonidus (556-539 BC), whose mother Adda-guppi was a a priestess and whose daughter became the chief priestess of the sanctuary. The puzzling stay of seven years by Nabonidus far from his throne at the oasis of Teima' in northern Arabia, could, among other things, have been motivated by religious reasons, considering the importance of the Moon cult among the pre-Islamic Arabs, as demonstrated by the lunar emblems found in recent excavations conducted at Teima'. Surprisingly, the representations of this deity in Mesopotamian art are scarce, although the symbol of the lunar crescent, a halfmoon with the bulge turned downward – a standardized representation of the moon that may have replicated its real shape at the latitude of the land of Sumer – appears on innumerable seals from the Akkadian period onward. During the Ur III period, the crescent moon begins to be omnipresent in scenes of intercession. Some scholars have associated this new motif with the widespread dissemination of the lunar calendar at that time. In addition to its value as the astral symbol of the god Nanna/ Suen, the image of the crescent moon could also indicate precisely when the intercession depicted on the seal took place, i.e., on one of the two most favorable days of the month (Pizzimenti
fig. 31

ated with the hybrid animal sukhurmashu, having the head and forelegs of a goat and the body of a fish. This symbol was to pass down to the Hellenistic and Roman age as Capricorn, still present in our zodiac (Black-Green 1992: 93). Enki/Ea appears frequently in mythological scenes in the art of the Akkadian period, but unlike Shamash – more rarely in later times. He can be easily identified in seals by streams of water, represented by parallel wavy lines, flowing from his shoulders or adorning his throne. In other art forms as well, Enki/Ea is recognisable thanks to the presence of water, as in the case of the plaque decorated in relief (cap. 122) from the Urartian period (middle of the 7th cent. BC) depicting a divinity standing on a ram, the animal symbol of Ea. His association with freshwater links him to fish as well; whether by chance or not, vast amounts of fish remains were found on level VI (from the end of the Early Ubaid period) of the temple of Eridu, associated with this god (Lambert in Finkel-Geller 1997:5).
Personal and interceding deities
In the vast, complex relationships between man and the gods in ancient Mesopotamia, a special role was played by the ‘personal deity’. This was a god or goddess perceived as especially close to the worshiper, guiding his interests and his fate and ready to answer the prayers offered to him/her in private cult. Hence this deity was not a part of the public, official polytheistic religion, but played an indispensable role in the special, unique (henotheistic) relationship with his/her protégé (cap. 114). This is clearly shown by the texts that refer to ‘my god’ or ‘his god’, and the positive experience of ‘acquiring a god’, that is, the piety specifically felt by the worshiper, prepared for divine wrath in case of sin or on the
Atra-hasis to the Poem of Gilgamesh – is that of concretely aiding mankind in its escape from the Flood, also by recurring to expedients for eluding the strict precepts established by the pantheon as a whole. In other Sumerian literary works (Inanna and Enki, Enki and World Order) the deity serves the function of organiser and regulator of human life in its manifold aspects. For this role of his, Enki/Ea formed part, along with Anu and Enlil, of the divine triad that gave a name to the celestial ‘paths’, three vertical bands into which the astrologers (from 1000 BC on) divided the sky, for the purpose of placing the planets and stars within 18 zodiacal constellations, precisely identified (and observed in their motion, over the course of 12 months lasting 30 days each). A Neo-Assyrian astrological tablet describes the journey of the Sun over the various ‘paths’ through the seasons:
« From the 1st of Addaru/XII [= approx. February 15] to the 30th of Ayyaru/II [= approx. May 15], the Sun marches on the path of Anu: breezes and mild temperature. From the 1st of Simanu/III [= approx. May 15] to the 30th of Abu/V [= approx. August 15], the Sun marches over the path of Enlil: harvesting and heat. From the 1st of Ululu/VI [= approx. August 15] to the 30th of Arahsamnu/VIII [= approx. November 15], the Sun marches over the path of Anu: breezes and mild temperature. From the 1st of Kislimu/IX [= approx. November 15] to the 30th of Shabatu/XI [= approx. February 15], the Sun marches in the path of Ea: cold weather. » (Fales 2001:253)
In the symbolism of the Kassite period and later, Enki is associ

« My ill luck has increased, and I do not find the right. I called to my god, but he did no;t show his face, I prayed to my goddess, but she did not raise her head » (Lambert 1960: Ludlul, II, 3-5)
The role of mediation or intercession with the great gods is especially clear in the iconography of cylinder seals from the Third Dynasty of Ur (21st cent. BC), which frequently show audience scenes. At the centre of the scene we find an Assyrian deity seated on a throne, dressed in a tufted woollen gown and wearing a multi-layered horned tiara, who thus constitutes the focal point of the representation and can sometimes be identified by his attributes (for example, sacred weapons or flowing streams of
contrary expecting compassion and pardon after having repented. As has been noted, this personal piety should be seen within the context of a family- and (small) community-based religion. The attitude of the worshiper to his own god resembles that of an infant to its parents. It is a relationship of the primordial type, practically indissoluble and based on faith in the divine elements throughout the different stages of life of the individual and his household. It is hard to know, however, whether the personal deity was more helpful with physical problems (perhaps also in the important sphere of reproduction) or with psychological matters and adaptation (as a sort of ‘guardian angel’). It may be no coincidence that the state of misfortune on all fronts described in the Babylonian poem of the ‘Righteous Sufferer’ also contemplates temporary abandonment of the unlucky man by his personal deity:

placed as guardians at the doors of temples and palaces. The best-known case of these hybrid beings is that of the alamdimmu (in modern times often called lamassu, which are however another thing), the winged bulls (but also lions) with bearded human heads that served – in sculptures in the round up to 4 metres' height – as custodians at the entrance to Assyrian royal palaces. They were then replicated in the monumental iconography of the Achaemenid period. We know them from the palaces of Ashurnasirpal II (883-859 BC) down to the time of Esarhaddon (680-668 BC). From the Assyrian bas-reliefs themselves we also learn that the colossal statues of the alamdimmu were sculpted directly on the walls of the limestone quarries on the upper Tigris and were patiently drawn by squads of workers on sleds pulled by ropes, then transferred to sturdy rafts and floated directly downstream to the capitals of the empire, where they were set up in pairs at the entrances to the palace. (Fales 2001). As protection for the entrances of public buildings, also as accompaniment to the alamdimmu at the gates of Assyrian palaces (such as that of Khorsabad, erected by Sargon II in the late 8th cent. BC), stood the figure of the so-called ‘lord of animals’, which comes from a much more ancient tradition, especially common in Mesopotamian seals. This figure was depicted in various ways, although the best-known type is undoubtedly that of the nude man, bearded and wearing an elaborate coiffure stylized in elegant symmetrical curls (usually three per side). His body is depicted in profile, while the face appears in frontal view (cap. 121). This typology – typical of the Akkadian period – seems to derive from a more archaic one (exactly from Early Dynastic II), where the figure has a beardless face shown in profile, with the hair standing up on his head but curving backward at the ends to form a sort of eyelet (cap. 123). This character was commonly called a ‘hero’, a conventional term for a figure whose real identity remains uncertain. Numerous attempts – often frustrated – have been made to identify the figure of the ‘lord of the animals’, based on Sumerian and Akkadian literature, which however provides no detailed physical descriptions of the protagonists of mythology and epical texts. In the past, and in popular literature, he has often been identified with the epic hero Gilgamesh, although with an obvious lack of any concrete match between literary texts and images. And in fact, it is not sure whether the iconography of the nude, bearded hero refers exclusively to a specific personality; hence it is quite useless to attempt to establish a precise, univocal identity for this figure. It should have rather represented a figurative convention that referred, on different occasions, to different figures appearing in mythology and epos, probably of semi-divine rank. Highly interesting among these – considering the obvious connection in numerous seals between the nude hero and the god Enki/Ea – is a group of deities, the Lahmu, who were responsible for watching over Apsu (Abzu), the residence of the freshwater
water in the case of the god Ea), by the heavenly bodies represented, by his symbolic animal or by the shape of his chair (which at times shows a structure symbolizing the facade of a temple). An advancing figure, who represents a worshiper or a priest dressed in a simple fringed gown, draped over one shoulder, his arm bent at the elbow, his hand extended at shoulder level approaches the divinity, led by the hand by another figure who precedes him. This is the interceding female deity, often identified with the protective figure of the goddess Lama or Lamma/ lamassu (Spycket 1980-1983), who is usually dressed in divine robes less luxurious than those of the main god, except in special cases (the garments of a priestess or other figure). The iconography of the intercession scene changed in the Isin-Larsa period (ca. 2000-1800 BC), with the worshiper facing the greater god, behind whom stands his personal deity, often shown with arms raised at face height in the attitude of supplication. In the Old Babylonian period, audience scenes with the god seated on a throne or the king seated on a stool were gradually replaced by much simpler scenes with fewer figures, shown in standing position. The two main characters were the ruler and the supplicating goddess, who were at times the only figures in the scene (cap. 117, cap. 118). In further variations, within an overall vast range of seal production, the goddess appears in central position between the worshiper and the main deity (cap. 119).
In another significant variation of the scene, the main figure does not wear a tufted gown but a fringed one, it remains seated on a stool or chair and most important, does not wear the horned tiara but a round cap, known also from the statuary of the period. This figure can be identified as a ruler (cap. 120), also due to the frequent presence of the bearded face. It is still open to question, however, whether or not this image represents a king subjected to divinization, following a tendency attested in this period, which entailed a cult of statues portraying the rulers and the building of temples in their name. The interceding goddess disappears more or less entirely from Mesopotamian iconography after the Old Babylonian dynasty. The examples, very rare in the Kassite period, terminate in the 9th cent. BC with a tablet in bas-relief for Shamash, in which the Babylonian king Nabû-apal-iddina is led before the god by the city’s highest priest, with the goddess Aya in the role of interceding deity. However, this motif was to remain in use in the bordering countries, from Cappadocia to Mittani to Syria and Ugarit, throughout the 2nd millennium BC.
Heroes and mythological creatures
In a secondary sphere of Mesopotamian religious polytheism, minor divinities of ancient tradition, of only partial anthropomorphic aspect, also find space. They are known for having been
180



129
Mankind and the gods in Mesopotamia 181



Signs Before the Alphabet
defense of domestic animals is the bull-man (or bison-man), also erroneously identified in the past with Enkidu, Gilgamesh’s companion in adventure. In the bison-man, the lower part of the body (cleft hooves and tail) is taurine, while the head (with bovine ears, although wearing horns) and the torso are clearly human; the erect position also is typical of man (cap. 124). This creature appears throughout the span of Mesopotamian history and also in neighboring civilizations (at Tell Halaf/Guzana, in Syria, in the 1st millennium BC). The Sumerian name of the bison-man, gud. dum.dutu (in Akkadian kusarikku), seems to identify him as the offspring of the sun god Shamash/Utu; in Akkadian texts, the kusarikku were the seven guardians of his home. In later periods the bison-man became increasingly identifiable as the custodian of gates, at the entrances and exits of public places in relation to the time of day, that is, to the motion of the Sun god. In the Neo-Assyrian period, it is frequently portrayed holding up the winged solar disk. From the texts and iconography of this period, we know that,
deity. The root lh ̆m can be translated as ‘thick-haired’ and this
term undoubtedly recalls the flowing hair that is the most obvious characteristic of the nude hero in Mesopotamian glyptic art. At times the hero appearing on the seals might be identified with a ruler, thanks to an exchange that recurs often in the culture of the Land of the Twin Rivers. Beginning with the Middle Assyrian period, the hero is usually winged and is often to be seen as one of the so-called genies, perhaps images or symbolic evocations of the legendary Seven Sages who lived before the Flood, known as apkallu (Black – Green 1994: 163-164; Green in Finkel-Geller 1997: 140). The series of genies, highly developed in the Neo-Assyrian period, included figures of hybrid nature (such as the demon-griffin, by some seen as a vulture) but more often anthropomorphic, such as male figures wearing a horned tiara, either standing or kneeling, carrying a bucket and a ‘pinecone'- sprinkler (in Akkadian mullilu) with a magical-apotropaic function, which may have been related to a stylized tree of life (cap. 129). The contests between heroes and animals are of basically symbolic nature. This representation emphasizes the superiority of man – the emblem of order and civilisation – over the chaotic animal and floral realm, a superiority acquired thanks to the favor shown by the gods to mankind. In other words, what is defined is the dominion of man (represented by the nude hero) over the animal world. Mankind, by utilizing animal resources, is able to draw from them the wealth that allows it to establish an organized civilization, subordinated to the cult and to the gods. Hence scenes of this kind have, from the very beginning, subtle religious meanings (cap. 55). The hero’s chief ally in his struggles with wild beasts and his
fig. 35
fig. 34
182



archers (sometimes winged cherubim, sometimes centaurs) shooting their arrows at hybrid creatures (griffins, lion-dragons, snakelike monsters, or winged creatures with a human head and a scorpion’s body, the so-called girtablullû). In the Sumerian
poem Lugal-e, the Asag (Akkadian asakku) is a monstrous demon that is defeated by the god Ninurta/Ningirsu (in another version, by Adad/Ishkur). Asag was born from the coupling of An and Ki (heaven and earth), and Asag himself mated with kur (the mountains) to produce offspring. He was accompanied by an army of allies made of stone (the stones of the mountains). The defeat of Asag/asakku by Ninurta might be represented on the reliefs erected in the 9th century BC by the Assyrian King Ashurnasirpal II on both sides of the main entrance to the temple of Ninurta at Kalhu (today Nimrud). Here a god (Ninurta or Adad) holding stylized lightening bolts is attacking a leonine bird, perhaps Anzû or Asakku. A correlated scene is found on seals of the Neo-Assyrian period, where Ninurta – portrayed as an archer – shoots his arrows at the demon (cap. 125).
Divination and the sense of man’s future
The divination of omens consists of a group of techniques for observing terrestrial and celestial phenomena (mainly of casual or exceptional nature) and interpreting their reflexes on the future of human events. Interpretation is based on systems for inferring a consequence from a specific cause, formulated in hypothetical terms (if this phenomenon occurs, then the consequences are the following), in a positive or negative key. Divination is attested in Mesopotamia since the 3rd millennium BC From here it migrated to the neighboring regions (Elam, Syria, Anatolia), developing in various ways, and continued up to the last examples of literature in cuneiform script, at the dawn of the Christian era (Fales 2001: 244). The world of techniques developed over this long period is remarkably rich. Various forms of deductive divination, deriving from
among the hybrid creatures playing a role in the mythology linked to the god Shamash, only three of them held up the winged disk: the bison-man, the fish-man and the scorpion-man. The fish-man has human form in the upper part, with a bearded head, and a body with the scales and tail of a fish; the tail was sometimes curved as a symbol of swimming. The scorpion-man has the feet and legs (and sometimes) the wings of a bird, but the rest of its body is that of a scorpion with uplifted tail, ready to sting; here too the head is human and bearded. The Epic of Gilgamesh (Pl. 9: II, 1-9) narrates the arrival of the heroes at the mountain Mashu, “which daily protects the exit of Shamash”, with scorpion-men “whose aura is frightful, whose gaze is death itself”, which protect the sun god “from dawn to dusk”. Giants are only rarely depicted on seals. This is probably due to the fact that it is extremely difficult to render the idea of an enormous body size on such a small artifact and, at the same time, to create a well-balanced composition. When they are represented on seals, the expedient used is that of portraying them crosswise, or horizontally (cap. 70). In most forms of religion, various types of supernatural beings exist, on a level midway between gods and men. The term 'demon', in its original Greek meaning (daimōn 'supernatural being', 'spirit') is an approximative translation of the Akkadian term rābis.u (Sumerian maškim), which can refer to either a good or an evil demon. Demons appear only rarely in Mesopotamian mythology. In general, the ‘evil’ demons seem to have been conceived as simple executors of the will of the gods. Their role was that of implementing a punishment ordained by the gods for some sin that had been committed. The customary method of attacking mankind consisted of inflicting diseases, as in the case of the Lamashtu-demon (cap. 130). Demons were not often portrayed, perhaps in the belief that their images could endanger someone. Demons and monsters, in fact, combine both human and animal features, usually blending the most frightful or potent elements of different creatures. More frequently, however, a human head, which represents intelligence, is superimposed on an animal’s body. The bull-man is the best-known example of this type and – as we have seen – it was the ally of the (human) hero in contest scenes. Bird-men are rare, except during the Akkadian period, but wings are often added to men and animals. Parts of various animals are then combined to create entirely hybrid figures, or Mischwesen. The most ancient combination of this type is the lion-headed eagle Imdugud (Anzû in Akkadian), which appears on the seals of the Early Dynastic period (cap. 72). Combinations of birds, lions and reptiles have produced various kinds of dragons, which became the symbolic animals of various deities, the most famous of them being the mushkhushshu of the god Marduk. On the seals of the Neo-Assyrian and Neo-Babylonian period, mythological hunting scenes (cap. 125) are very frequent, with
fig. 36
Mankind and the gods in Mesopotamia 183



Signs Before the Alphabet
astrologists grouped for “vigils” of the sky, or from the elaborate apotropaic rituals that protected the sleep of kings and the fate of peoples, common man must have also perceived the numinous nature of the stars with their influence on mankind (cap. 128) or – in other geographical or linguistic horizons – must have attempted to ward off evil with amulets (cap. 131) or good-luck charms hung over doors or elsewhere (cap. 132). It is most likely, then, that even among the general population, the beliefs enunciated in the erudite Babylonian “Diviner’s Manual” from the 7th cent. BC were widespread:
« The signs on earth, like the signs in the sky, give us signals. [...] The sky and the earth also produce portents; even if they appear separately, they are not separate (since) the sky and the earth are related. The sign, which is negative in the sky, is negative on earth as well; the sign that is negative on earth is equally negative in the sky. » (Fales 2001: 245)
observation of the real world, included astrology, ornithomancy, teratology and physiognomy. Other methods, based on the examination of offerings or sacrificial materials, included hepatoscopy or extispicy, palmomancy, lecanomancy (drops of oil poured into water), libanomancy (incense smoke) and aleuromancy (forms assumed by flour when poured out). Lastly, in the 1st millennium BC we find a smaller series of divinely inspired expressions, either verbal or not (interpretation of dreams, and prophetic enunciations, such as those of the priestesses of Ishtar at Arbail). Divination was thus an activity applied to the whole universe of society and intellectually esteemed in every period of Mesopotamian history, to judge by the many collections of omens that have come down to us. Its utilisation as a tool of prediction in the public sphere, up to the highest official levels, appears unique in the ancient world as a whole. This ensemble of techniques was thus destined to leave conspicuous traces in memory and in the collective imagination of other ancient civilisations (the world of the Bible, the Greco-Roman world) as a characteristic peculiar to the Babylonian cultural horizon. Alongside this ‘scientific’ horizon, which rotated around groups of sages assembled at the courts of Babylonia and Assyria (scribes and scholars of traditional lists of predictions; astrologers, haruspices, exorcists, physicians, etc.), the practice of divination also exerted an influence on common man in Mesopotamia and its neighboring regions. Although far removed from the
