Amulet with bas relief and inscription
Fragment of an amulet with Akkadian cuneiform inscription (a spell) on one surface; and on the other, a scene in bas relief representing the female demon Lamashtu.
Neo-Assyrian Steatite 3.7 x 2.8 x 1.5 cm
The fragment bears on the Obverse 6 lines of Akkadian cuneiform script, partially preserved (approx. two-thirds). The Reverse is devoid of any inscription but is covered with a scene in bas-relief, also partially preserved: visible is the lower part of the body of a standing anthropomorphic figure standing whose feet (of theriomorphic type) are resting on the back of a crouching donkey. From above the scene a small mammal and a serpent seem to “hang down”- but they are actually supported by the figure or attached to it. The scene presents a remarkable attention to detail, especially as regards the donkey's head.
The text, divided by horizontal rulings after each line, is the final part of a well-attested incantation to be found in Babylonian literature against the demonic figure Lamashtu. The visible signs are part of ll. 3-8 of the standard inscription on these texts (for a total of eight lines), of which a full translation is here provided:
“Incantation: he who has exceeded the length of my bed, who frightened me, who made me quake on the ground, who has related frightening dreams, may he be delivered to Nedu, the great gate-guard of the earth; by the order of the god Ninurta, the eldest son, the beloved offspring, by the order of the god Marduk, who dwells in the temple Esagila and in Babylon. O door and latch, know that I am protected by the divine order of my lords. Spell.”
Both the iconography and the text on our piece allow us to be reconstructed entirely, on the basis of many existing parallels. The standing figure is that of the female demon Lamashtu, which had a lioness' head, a human body, and an eagle's feet, which are placed on the donkey instructed to lead her into the desert; the animal in turn is crouching in the boat that leads to the underworld. Both the serpent and the small mammal are duplicated on the left side of the figure: the reptiles squirm in the demon's fists, while the two mammals bite the demon's hip. The scene could be on a large scale, with the addition of upper registers concerning a sick man cured by exorcists, and processions of demons with beasts' heads, such as in the exemplar Louvre AO 22205, lacking any inscription.

Mankind and the gods in Mesopotamia 187






The kings and their deeds






that were the exclusive prerogative of the king, as well as the ritual functions which he was also called upon to enact for the welfare of his kingdom. In essence, the king had innately the unique ability to be able to 'dialogue', at all times and under all circumstances, with the gods and with men at the same time. That said, in the ancient Near East the king was, and remained, a man - the foremost and most remarkable of men, of course, but still a representative of mankind. It may suffice to recall the central notion of the Poem of Gilgamesh, concerning the desperate and unsuccessful search for immortality by the protagonist, the almighty king of Uruk with a semi-divine nature by birth, to obtain a sense of the insurmountable limit between man and God that deeply resonated in Mesopotamian culture. Unlike Egypt, where there was a very close relationship between the Pharaoh and the organization of Maat - the principle of cosmic order - and therefore the deified kingship was “the locus of convergence between anthropological and cosmic spheres” (Assmann 2016), Mesopotamia and the regions adjacent to it did not conceive of the divinization of the king before the reign of Naram-Sin of Akkad (ca. 2200 BC) and only occasionally later on.
« Because he had ensured the foundations of his city (Agade) against danger, (the citizens) invoked Ishtar in Eanna (= Uruk), Enlil in Nippur, Dagan in Tuttul, Nikhursag in Kish, Ea in Eridu, Sin in Ur, Shamash in Sippar, and Nergal in Kutha, so that he (= Naram-Sin) would be made god of their city, and then they built a temple to him within the city of Agade » (Frayne 1993: pp. 113-114, n. 10, ll. 20-57)
None of the later kings of Akkad was divinized. However, the tradition was revived by King Shulgi of the Ur III dynasty, perhaps as a move to limit the (ideological) damage caused by the death of his father Ur-Namma in battle (cap. 133, cap 134), which could have indicated the displeasure of the great gods. As in the preceding period, divinization had a largely political significance: it identified the ruler with the core values of the surrounding society, for example, through the erection of the statue of the king beside those of the gods in the major sanctuaries and with the creation of temple farms in the name of the deified king. Finally, hymns to the divinized king became part of the school curriculum as a celebration and propaganda tool (Michalowski 1987). As mentioned, if we assume that Sumerian civilization, from the Late Uruk period forward, worked on three social levels (authorities in power – high officials - common people), the divinization of kings was another way to establish royalty as fourth dimension, which was superior to all the local particularities, external or internal (Winter 1987). The tradition of divinizing kings continued with the successors of Shulgi (cap. 29) in the dynasties of Isin and Larsa (20th - 19th centuries BC); moreover, kings – such as Lipit-Ishtar of Isin - were revered
The kings and their deeds
Frederick Mario Fales
« The well-known proverb says: “Man is the shadow of God”; but man is (just) the shadow of man (himself): (Only) the king is the perfect image of God » (From an Assyrian letter, ca. 670 BC; Fales 2001.42)
As is well known, kingship - the dominion of a single individual (and his supporters) over people by virtue of a claimed (but generally accepted) superiority or uniqueness sanctioned by divine or natural forces - is probably one of the most enduring institutional forms of human history. As is well known, it has left traces in archaeological and artistic data, in textual expressions, and in the ethno-anthropological traditions of a great variety of cultures across time and space. In Mesopotamia, the political power of the absolute type had its base and application beginning with the formation of the city and the state, albeit initially with uncertainties and fluctuations between diverse terms and/or powers. Thus, for a large part of the 3rd millennium, the Sumerian titlles en, ensi and lugal were alternated with diverse meanings and hierarchies in the southern city-states (and even in polities as distant as Ebla in Syria), while it appears that no ambiguity ever surrounded the Semitic term sharrum, already present in the proper name of Sargon of Akkad, and from then on, for over two thousand years, translatable as king'. The numerous written sources of information on the kings are invaluably complemented by a visual culture of various types. Thus, the iconographic representations of the Mesopotamian kings in bas-reliefs and sculptures in the round, in the (unfortunately scarcely preserved) wall paintings, and in various branches of the minor arts, contribute to our understanding of the assessment and representation that the rulers of the ancient Near East conveyed of themselves in their physical features and their attributes (tiaras, crowns, robes, ornaments). The sense of the impressive power of a Mesopotamian king can then be obtained, although less directly, through the major building projects that have come down to us, such as palace and temple complexes, works of fortification or of hydraulic engineering, up to the foundation of entire cities. The importance of the person of the king is also made explicit in all of these sources of information (and often in a combination of written and archaeological data) by virtue of the efforts of the king himself to preserve his actions and fame for posterity. So, beyond the data, which was not always continuous over time and sometimes very fragmentary, it is certainly possible to draw a multi-faceted picture of royal power in different periods and regions in the history of the ancient Near East, from the early Sumerians to the last transformations of Babylonia under the Persians and Seleucids. A dual relationship with the king at the center can frequently be observed, even in very different chronological contexts of Mesopotamian history: on one hand, between the kings and the gods and on the other– symmetrically – between the king and humanity. Placed on the axis of this dual relationship are the actual political powers
I sovrani e le loro gesta
133
191



Signs Before the Alphabet
But beyond this aspects, the list unequivocally indicates that only one city at a time was to be endowed with kingship, with a dynastic succession 'by turns' that denies and contradicts the actual existence in parallel of several royal locations, and of dynasties simultaneously in power in southern Mesopotamia in the 3rd millennium BC, as evidenced by all the other contemporary sources. What could have been the reason be for this particular historical view? Noting that the text ends with the kings of Isin - whose ruling line was founded by Ishbi-Erra through secession from the Ur III dynasty, which fell in 2004 BC – it has been claimed that these kings thus wanted to justify their full historical legitimacy: after that of Ur, it would have been the turn of kingship of Isin (Michalowski 1983). Also shorter and more succinct king lists (cap. 137) shed light on the full and direct continuity between the kings of Ur III and those of Isin; and the literary text of the Lamentation on the fall of Sumer and Ur (in Sumerian language, but from the Old Babylonian period) clarifies the reasons for the 'turn-taking system', in the words of the god Enlil:
« “Ur was given the kingship, but it was not given an everlasting kingdom. From ancient days, when the earth was founded, until now when people have multiplied, who has (ever) seen a kingdom reign forever?”»
Thus, even with its many mythological-literary aspects, the 'Sumerian King List' is an interesting indicator of Mesopotamian mentality regarding the descent of royalty from the sphere of the divine. Even more, the text points out the central role of the royal institution in the history of humanity, marked by a very distant past, and - except for the hiatus represented by the legendary Flood - by a linear and continuous flow of time, coinciding with the sum of the various kings' regnal years. In conclusion, one can still agree with the basic idea expressed by the archaeologist Henri Frankfort: “The ancient Near East considered kingship as the very foundation of civilization” (Frankfort 1948, 3).
Ideals of kingship
Sargon and Naram-Sin of Akkad (2350- 2250 BC). Sargon of Akkad, and then his grandson Naram-Sin, brought about an innovative conception of kingship compared to that of the rulers who had preceded them in southern Mesopotamia. Written and figurative monuments in celebration of the dynasty were placed in the major temples; furthermore, royal laboratories made multiple copies of them to be sent to various centers in the country.
« Sargon, king of Agade, conquered the city of Uruk, and was victorious in battle. Further, he personally captured 50 governors and the king (of Uruk). As for the one who will remove this inscription, may the gods Enlil and Shamash tear out his foundations and destroy his progeny » (Frayne 1993, p. 21 n. 7)
« Naram-Sin the mighty, king of Agade: when the four quarters of the world revolted against him, through the love which the goddess Ishtar showed him, he was victorious in nine battles in one year, and those kings that they ( = the rebels) had raised against him, he captured » (Frayne 1993, p. 113, n. 10, ll. 1-12)
In both the texts and the official iconography which has come down to us, the two major kings of Akkad describe themselves as leaders at the
as the siblings or children of the great gods, and often, as lovers or husbands of the goddess Inanna/Ishtar.
Kingship as an institution: the sense of the past and the flow of time
Mesopotamian kingship was conceived as a civilizing institution, that men had obtained from the gods. This is clearly explained by the so-called 'Sumerian King List', which in fact is a mixed composition, part literary text and part actual list, which relates the history of the kingship in Mesopotamia from its beginnings up to the early centuries of the 2nd millennium BC (Jacobsen 1939; Michalowski 1983; Marchesi 2010). Below is the beginning and some salient passages:
« After the kingship descended from heaven, the kingship was in Eridug. In Eridug, Alulim became king; he ruled for 28,800 years. Alaljar ruled for 36,000 years. 2 kings; they ruled for 64,800 years. Then Eridug fell and the kingship was taken to Bad-tibira. In Bad-tibira, En-menlu-ana ruled for 43,200 years. En-men-gal-ana ruled for 28,800 years. Dumuzid, the shepherd, ruled for 36,000 years. 3 kings; they ruled for 108,000 years .... (Total,) in 5 cities 8 kings; they ruled for 241,200 years. Then the flood swept over. After the flood had swept over, and the kingship had descended from heaven, the kingship was in Kish. In Kish, Gushur became king; he ruled for 1200 years ... (Total,) 23 kings; they ruled for 24510 years, 3 months, and 3 1/2 days. Then Kish was defeated and the kingship was taken to Eanna (= Temple of Uruk). In Eanna, Mesh-ki-ag-gasher, the son of Utu, became lord and king; he ruled for 324 years. Mesh-ki-ag-gasher entered the sea and disappeared. Enmerkar, the son of Mesh-ki-ag-gasher, the king of Uruk, who built Uruk, became king; he ruled for 420 years ... Gilgamesh, whose father was a phantom (?), the lord of Kulaba, ruled for 126 years. Ur-Nungal, the son of Gilgamesh, ruled for 30 years. .... (Total), 12 kings: they had ruled for 2,310 years. Then Uruk was defeated and the kingship was taken to Ur ... » (ETCSL 2.1.1, ll. 1-134)
As may be seen, according to this list, kingship 'descended from heaven' so as to be the prerogative of a specific urban venue until its defeat, after which the royal institution moved to another city, and so on for the rest of the long text. The initial section (ll. 1-40) relates to the antediluvian age: at this time, in a certain sense 'before history', the rulers had very long lives. Subsequently, kingship returned to earth and continued its passage from one city to the next; however, the 'post-diluvian' age is characterized by progressively shorter kingdoms. However, both phases present legendary characters – for example, Gilgamesh - or even mythological ones, such as Dumuzi and Meshkiaggasher, said to be the son of the sun god Utu. It is only later on that rulers known from other documents will begin to be mentioned and their years of reign will drop to quite normal and exact lengths. In practice, the text outlines a comprehensive history of kingship in Mesopotamia that proceeds diachronically from a 'dawn of time', absolutely amazing in the durations of the kingdoms and in its personages, up to the ages closer to the drafting of the text, with kings that were well-known and precisely recorded in administrative documents.
192



head of their armies, with the aim of conquering the world. In this same vein, Naram-Sin boasted that he had reached the Mediterranean coast to wash his weapons in the sea. 'King of totality' or 'king of the four parts of the world' were their innovative and beloved titles. This was a significant ideological change compared to the utterances of earlier Sumerian kings, merely tied to the Mesopotamian horizon; and the language of these conquering kings, marked by the centrality of Akkad on a very vast horizon, would serve as a model for the imperial feats of 'universalistic' type by kings in later millennia (Liverani 1993). In particular, posterity would report their deeds with adventurous and heroic terms and images that may be compared with those used in the Gilgamesh epic. The main achievements of the dynasty of Akkad not only included the unification of Mesopotamia under a single dominion, but also the establishment of a socio-political organization capable of coping with crises such as those that had caused the sudden fall of the previous kingdoms,
fig. 37 fig. 38

Brick with royal inscription
Celebratory document of the kingship of Ur-Namma, founder of the Third Dynasty of Ur (2112-2095 BC) on the building of the temple of the god Enlil in the city of Nippur. This inscription was also written on stone tablets, as well as on bronze canephore figurines. The presence of such a text on a foundation brick could be explained by the use - firmly established since the age of Gudea of Lagash - of making small brick capsules containing tablets of stone and metal figurines with the same inscription, as a sort of deposit placed under the foundations of a building.
Origin Nippur (?) Ur III Clay 13.5 x 12 x 2.5 cm
135
Clay cone with inscription
Celebratory record of LipitIshtar, the fifth king of the first dynasty of Isin (1934-1924 BC), listing extensive titles of the king himself with reference to the most important centers of cultural and historical tradition of southern Mesopotamia (Nippur, Ur, Eridu , Uruk). The text goes on to mention the 'restoration of justice' carried out by the king with a clear reference to the promulgation of a code of laws in his name and ends with the mention of a building called the 'house of justice' for which the text was drafted.

Signs Before the Alphabet
such as Lugalzagesi of Umma. Power over the individual cities of the Akkadian empire - left on purpose without defensive walls - was delegated to a number of ensis ('princes' or 'city-heads'), who acted as local administrators on behalf of the state and took charge of tributes from agricultural revenues as well as military conscription; even the capital had its own ensi. The economic hierarchy of the kingdom was family-based, with the subjects as managers of their own 'households'; the high officials of the kingdom, including the ensi, enjoyed agricultural estates granted by the king, who had acquired them through conquest or expropriation. In summary, for the first time, the agrarian regime of Akkad broke away from the previous local particularism by redistributing land to the employees of the Crown in many different areas. As for the local communities, individual families had quotas in various fields for a fair distribution of risks, work activities, and income, thus resulting as members of the 'extended household' of the king, even if not as dependents. Hammurabi of Babylonia (1792-1750 BC). Hammurabi represents the culmination of the 'intermediate' period in which the small kingdoms of Mesopotamia were in the hands of rulers of Amorite origin. The Amorite people, of pastoral and tribal origin, had been definitely advantaged in their penetration by the political fragmentation and the collapse of urban life following the fall of Ur III. They appear to have found different arrangements in Mesopotamia: in some places as an integral part, and also leaders, of the 'sedentary' city and state formations, but elsewhere maintaining to a certain extent their original identity as populations of gentilic structure, primarily practicing pastoral transhumance (such as around Mari on the middle Euphrates). Essentially, then, the Amorites slowly became the socio-political protagonists of this 'new' Mesopotamia. Their Amorite origin was repeatedly emphasized by the rulers of Larsa and other locations with various titles, for example, 'Sheikh (rabianum) of the Amorites' or, as in the case of Sin-kashid of Uruk (1865-1835 BC), 'King of the tribe of Amnanum'. This type of genealogical indication, with reference to a nomadic and tribal past - thus in fact antithetical to any link with urban kingship as outlined in the 'Sumerian King List' would extend until the 18th century BC. Thus Shamshi-Adad of Assyria, stated that his most remote ancestors included '17 kings who lived in tents', while in his genealogy, Hammurabi indicated a succession from 'the dynastic time of the Guteans', 'of the Haneans' and finally, 'of the Amorites' (with Amnanum as oldest ancestor) which led to himself (Finkelstein 1966). Fictitious or inaccurate as they may have been, these genealogical indications show consciousness of a 'new beginning' in terms of an ethnic and linguistic identity that marked Mesopotamia after the fall of Ur III; but that did not prevent all of these 'Amorite' kings from quickly appropriating for themselves the cultural and religious foundations of most ancient Mesopotamia. With the new reunification of Mesopotamia carried out during his reign, which mainly concerned the 'land of Sumer and Akkad' of the earlier tradition of Ur III, but with a broader geographical scope like that of his antecedents in the empire of Sargon of Akkad, Hammurabi gave rise to an innovative political and territorial entity: Babylonia, a region dominated by the eponymous town (Babylon) on the Euphrates. The significance of the unified political framework built by Hammurabi, - and the organizational model that accompanied it - was handed down, ideally, by all successive rulers for over a millennium, until the coming of the Persian age. Hammurabi is particularly known thanks to the discovery of his renowned 'Law Code', the antecedents of which were already in the socalled 'reforms' of Urukagina of Lagash and Ur-Namma of Ur, and later fig. 39
196



on of Lipit-Ishtar of Isin. Like the latter king in particular, Hammurabi did not enact legal measures to be put into effect (shown by the contrast between many of his penalties and those imposed in contemporary legal deeds): in point of fact, his 'code' is a long list of customary rules of a self-celebratory nature, in which Hammurabi appears as the one who 'restored justice and equity' in his own country, especially by canceling in one stroke private and public debts. Undoubtedly at the base of the 'code' was the king's concern for a series of socio-economic failures that plagued the period (the breakdown of family structure, cyclically occurrence indebtedness without remedy, scarcity of agricultural wages). A similar practice was also adopted by one of his successors, Ammi-Saduqa (1646-1626), of whom we possess a draft of his edict of remission. Tiglath-Pileser III. Historians agree in assigning Tiglath-Pileser III a key role in the formation of the Assyrian Empire. In fact, this king managed to revive the fortunes of the Assyrian kingdom, weakened for more than half a century, and managed to make it become the power which between the eighth and seventh century BC ruled unopposed over the 'four parts of the world' then known. At the end of his reign, Assyria had acquired a precise imperial profile and, with his successors, would come to dominate the Near East from the Zagros Mountains to the shores of the Levant - also exerting political influence on Cyprus – and from the border with Egypt to central-eastern Anatolia and to the Persian Gulf, even establishing commercial relations with the oases in the Arabian desert. Starting with Tiglath-Pileser III, the conquered territory was organized according to a mixed system, where polities entrusted to pro-Assyrian rulers were subjected to the traditional tribute in alternation with actual provinces, ruled by governors appointed by the king, who were responsible for military and civil management (including the coordination of tax revenues). Vast deportations were implemented to eradicate and disperse the defeated upper classes throughout the empire. The defense of

Tablet with envelope, record of work payment
The document - dated to the 46th regnal year of Shulgi - is the administrative record of a certain amount (about 150 kg) of fresh reeds, used as payment for 'excess' work. Reeds were a material that was abundant in southern Mesopotamia, and therefore used much more frequently than the more precious wood. The inscription of the seal bears the name of Ur-Sin, a scribe and at the same time 'superintendent' of reeds.

tive hub of Puzrish- Dagan (today Drehem). and the reform of weights and measures, as well as of the calendar, which became the same for the whole kingdom (Steinkeller 1987). But Shulgi has also left us an image of himself as a king who was educated and proud of his achievements in the field of writing and knowledge in general. He had happy memories of his youth – to quote from his self-celebratory hymns - in the scribal schools (edubba) and claimed to have been the best student in the class.
« As a youth, I studied the scribal art in the edubba, From the tablets of Sumer and Akkad, Of all the nobles, none was able to write a tablet like me, In the place where people attend to learn the scribal art. Sums, subtraction, counting and accounting, I completed all (the courses); The beautiful Nanibgal, the goddess Nisaba, Generously endowed me with wisdom and intelligence » (Klein 1981:15)
Surely, as king, he became an enthusiastic patron of the arts and letters; he himself founded the Sumerian science academies in Ur and Nippur, although he certainly adapted them to his objectives, speaking of them as his 'libraries'. In these academies, officials from highly placed families learned to read propaganda texts concerning the dynasty of Ur, including hymns to Shulgi himself, for a proper political education which also included the figure of the king as an excellent 'scribe'. However, on a more basic level, Shulgi became interested in the standardization of writing (choice of signs and formulas) in the administrative texts of his time: and this goal was achieved, as can be seen from an overview of the texts of the period. Ashurbanipal. In addition to his military exploits, the last great Assyrian king is remembered for the formation of his own 'library', i.e. the huge collection of texts of the Sumerian-Akkadian learned tradition in the palaces in Nineveh. But this work was innovative only in the sense of its size: in fact, interest in techniques and works of ancient Mesopotamian wisdom can already be attributed to Middle Assyrian kings such as Tukulti-Ninurta I and Tiglath-Pileser I , who established extensive collections of literary and divination tablets in their palaces. And in the 1st millennium, Sennacherib had already presented Nineveh as the “abode of reserved wisdom, where every memorable works of artistic production, all the cults and secrets of the god Lalgar are gathered together” (Frahm 1997, 280). And Esarhaddon had already started gathering, copying, and archiving documents of Babylonian libraries to collect them in the Assyrian capital. But Ashurbanipal - in addition to continuing vigorously his father's work (Frame - George 2005) - viewed his interest in ancient Sumerian-Akkadian scribal culture also in a practical sense, by actually learning to read and write tablets. He thus ended a text of his own, a compendium of extispicy:
« “Tablet of Ashurbanipal, great king, mighty king, king of Assyria, the beloved of the great gods, to whom Shamash and Adad granted a broad understanding, and who learned and mastered extispicy, the secret of heaven and earth, the wisdom of Shamash and Adad. This tablet he himself wrote, revised, collated, and placed inside his palace” » (Fales 2001, 41-42)
the extensive imperial spaces was secured by internal and frontier forts and by the formation of a permanent army, also made up of contingents drawn from the vanquished populations. Tiglath-Pileser also marked a new period for the Assyrian kingdom, not only by personally engaging in the annual military campaigns and in the restructuring of the administrative system, but by also reining in any claim of internal autonomy on the part of the Magnates, who had reached peaks of autonomy – at the limits of insubordination - under his predecessors. With the early appointment of his son Shalmaneser (V) as crown prince, the king also identified who would have to take care of the internal administration of the state while he himself was engaged in military operations abroad. This model would be followed by his successor, Sargon II, with his son Sennacherib. Finally, Tiglath-Pileser was the Assyrian king who managed to maintain the best relations with the Babylonian region, divided between the inhabitants of the ancient cities, the Aramaic tribes, and the bellicose Chaldean groups. The ideological, political, cultural, and religious prestige of Babylonia led the king to allow the city special treatment, different from that accorded to the other provinces of the empire. In fact, after defeating the Chaldean rebel chief Mukin-zeri, the king rejected the appointment of a loyal vassal to the throne of Babylonia and made his own triumphal entry into the city, choosing the innovative formula of a dual monarchy ('King of Sumer and Akkad') linked to his own person (albeit with a different dynastic name, Pulu); in this capacity, he took part in the New Year's Feast in 729 and 728 BC. This move proved to be successful: Tiglath-Pileser was acclaimed by the population as legitimate king and Shalmaneser V, who succeeded him in 727, enjoyed the same status, under the dynastic name Ululayu.
Kingship as wise management
The ideology of kingship in Mesopotamia tended to present the king as a perfect representative of the gods for the maintenance of divine order on Earth. Thus, the ideal king was a combination of physical perfection and mental balance. In an Assyrian literary composition on the creation of man, the king appears to have been a being quite distinct from common man. Nonetheless, it is likely that most of the Mesopotamian kings had not developed their 'cultural' side to perfection; and in fact, that very few of them were able to read (and still less to write) the many texts that they dictated, promulgated, or promoted, by delegating these activities to their trustworthy scribes. Although it is impossible to settle this matter thoroughly for lack of precise information, we know, on the contrary, of two famous cases (distant in time from one another) in which the rulers claimed to have mastered the art of reading and writing: Shulgi of Ur and Ashurbanipal of Assyria. Shulgi di Ur. This long-lived king (2094-2047 BC) was known for his work in profoundly restructuring his state, which centered on the city of Ur and later expanded throughout southern Mesopotamia, to the Middle Tigris, and north-eastern areas, from Eshnunna to Gasur (Nuzi). The state of Ur III under Shulgi (cap. 138, cap. 139, cap. 140) was bureaucratically divided into different specialized centers, for the collection for primary products and for secondary industrial production under strict central control. His main reforms include: the creation of a standing army, the reorganization of the temple farmsteads, the creation of a single administrative system for the north and south of the country, the introduction of the bala system of taxation in favor of the state, centered on the administra
200



rebuke addressed to her sister-in-law Libbali-sharrat, the wife of young Ashurbanipal, about the latter's poor academic commitment:
« Why don't you write your tablets and recite your exercise? Otherwise, people will say: 'But is this (really) the sister(-in-law) of Sherua-etirat, the eldest daughter of the succession line of Ashur-etel-ilani-mukinni (= other name of Esarhaddon), the great king, the legitimate king, the king of the world, the king of Assyria?' And (remember that) you are (also) a daughter-in-law, the (future) Queen of Ashurbanipal, the great Crown Prince of the succession line of Esarhaddon, king of Assyria! » (Fales 1992, 72; Livingstone 2007, 105)

Elsewhere Ashurbanipal drew up his own 'autobiographical' profile, in which he claimed to have learned the art of Adapa, the first of the legendary Seven Sages (apkallu) who had flourished before the Flood (and his courtier blandished him, defining him a descendent “scion of an apkallu, in fact, of Adapa himself”). Alongside the different martial techniques (archery, use of the spear, driving a war chariot), as a young prince his education had also been broadened by the study of languages and disciplines that formed traditional Sumero-Akkadian wisdom. In particular, he stated:
« I have observed and discussed the signs of heaven and earth in the assembly of teachers. I debate with divination experts (the work called) 'the liver, picture of heaven'; I can resolve reciprocals and calculate complex problems that lack solution. I have read sophistcated texts in obscure Sumerian, and in a barely understandable Akkadian; I have studied inscriptions on stone from the time before the Flood, which are a mixture of impenetrable wisdom (kakku sakku) » (Villard 1997, 137).
But was everything the king boasted about true? Recently, emphasis has been placed on some letters in which the court scholars comment on particular words to be explained to the king or in which, on the contrary, the king himself poses questions to his experts, indicating that some concepts are not clear to him. Finally, there is a letter that seems to have been written by the king himself, but with an uncertain 'calligraphy' and an elementary choice of signs (Zamazalová in Radner-Robson 2011). In any case, in the late Neo-Assyrian Empire, it would seem that it had become customary for the royal princes to learn to read and write. As a result, families and small communities from Syria or Upper Mesopotamia gradually moved further south, eventually forming a quite consistent group of migrants that caused the kings of the Ur III dynasty to erect a wall in the territory to keep them out (cap. 13). During this period, the Sumerian writers referred to the Amorites come from afar in satyrical but concerned tones, as in the myth of the Marriage of Martu:

Common man
Life, prosperity, and health
Frederick Mario Fales - Roswitha Del Fabbro
Nomadic and sedentary populations. The Ancient Near East has left much information on the nomadic way of life. Ancient Near Eastern nomadism was a complex phenomenon combining distinctive characteristics. Social organisation was of the gentilic type, binding the individual to an extended family or clan, then to a tribe or tribal confederation. The predominant form of occupation was shepherding with sheep and goats, although with some recourse to agriculture at certain times and places. Mobility ranged from seasonal transhumance over the steppes or on the hills, riding donkeys, to the full penetration of desert zones after the domestication of the dromedary (the one-humped camel) in the 1st millennium BC. Today we tend to consider nomadism from the viewpoint of economic adaptability, as one of the possible life choices available to ancient man, under the pressure of various contingencies and with many operational variations. The traditional theories positing the arrival of nomadic peoples from the desert, moving to conquer fertile Mesopotamia in successive waves from the 3rd to the 1st millennium, have all been discredited. At most, in the case
fig. 41
Common man. Life, prosperity, and health 207



Signs Before the Alphabet
places (tents) next to houses built of mud bricks, as also demonstrated by some modern anthropological cases. Another significant factor was that of political and institutional interaction. It is customary to indicate the many cases in which membership in a tribe – a communal structure par excellence – implied opposition to the social and political organization of the State, with its power-sharing hierarchies. Here too, however, complex situations could arise. In the archives of the middle Euphrates city-state of Mari (18th cent. BC), we find various forms of interaction – at times collaborative, at times oppositional – between the monarchy and the tribal groups of Amorites that occupied the surrounding steppe (Heimpel 2003: 13-37; Fleming in Szuchman 2009). City and country: village life. Along with the dichotomy between nomadic and sedentary peoples, the relationship between city and country has been much debated. Did actual rural communities, with their own productive, economic and administrative features, exist? Or were the farming villages little more than offshoots of the cities, useful only for providing agricultural products and men-at-arms to the urban ruling classes that operated through bureaucrats and governors? This question has become more urgent in recent decades, when archaeological excavations in areas far from the major urban centres (Northern Iraq, Syria and Kurdistan) have revealed the widespread presence, on the regional level, of small villages and
who eats raw meat, who has no house of his during his lifetime, and who will not be brought to any burial place when he dies! » (Klein 1997; Jahn 2007)
What is certain is that nomadism in Mesopotamia and the surrounding areas, especially in the 3rd and 2nd millennia BC, was of the so-called ‘enclosed’ type, entirely unlike the ‘open’ type, free of barriers, characteristic of nomadic peoples in Central Asia, from the Scythians to the Mongols (Rowton 1974). Within the Fertile Crescent instead, the itineraries of the Ancient Near Eastern nomads were restricted to areas not devoted to agriculture or occupied by settlements. With their animals, used for pasture and transport, the nomads could not remain entirely, or even at length, in desertic zones (such as inland Syria) but were forced to roam across the steppes bordering the rural villages and cultivated fields of the sedentary population. Accordingly, agricultural land and fields used for pasturage were closely linked; shepherds frequently shared the land they roamed over with farmers. Obviously, such interaction between nomads and sedentary peoples gave rise to frequent conflict, but also to more complex modes of cohabitation. For instance, nomads sometimes settled in agricultural villages and owned their own land, but without foregoing their bonds of tribal kinship; or they kept their temporary dwelling
144
Tablet listing cereal rations in Akkadian cuneiform script
The text consists of a list of cereal rations in two columns: on the left appears the quantity, in emâru, sutu, e qa(ʼu), on the right the recipient (either an individual, a group of persons or a sector of the palace). This list concerns the personnel of the royal palace. The amounts provided at the end of the text are much larger than the sum of the individual ‘items’ in the document. To ‘balance the books’, the scribes’ note on the obverse is essential. This is in fact a ‘carryover’ relevant to the quantities missing on the tablet. The document in question was thus part of a longer series listing rations for the palace.
Middle Assyrian or Neo-Assyrian (early) Clay 9.2 x 7.9 x 2.7 cm

BC to record loans of cereals to be repaid with interest, or amounts of silver. Life and work in rural areas
Although, as previously mentioned, cuneiform tablets and cylinder seals provide a good picture of productive activities in the urban milieu (temples and palaces), information on daily life in the rural zones of Mesopotamia and around it is not entirely lacking, for all the historic phases of Antiquity. Mainly in the texts but also in figurative scenes, various operations or aspects of the agricultural seasonal cycle are described and illustrated. A text from the Neo-Assyrian period, dealing with barley, describes harvesting operations (cap. 147), while a document from the Middle Assyrian (or the early Neo-Assyrian period) lists rations of cereals (cap. 144). Ploughing scenes (cap. 148) are common in seals from various periods, as proof of the widespread use of the seeder plough, documented for the first time at the beginning of the 2nd Early Dynastic period. Stock raising was also a fundamental resource for the ancient Mesopotamians. Pastoralism may be defined as a subsistence system based mainly on the products (meat, milk and by-products) of domesticated animals. It has been supposed (Sherratt 1981) that a ‘revolution in secondary products’ took place in the ancient Near East during the Neolithic period (5th and 4th millennia BC). Before this time, animals are thought to have been raised mainly for meat.
hamlets. Here, unlike the much more densely populated agricultural hinterland of Southern Mesopotamia – where the countryside seems to have been actually urbanized, especially under the Ur III Dynasty (Steinkeller 2007) – the rural centres seem linked to the city only by long trails over the steppe, probably used to transport foodstuffs or other goods; but apart from this, they must have been fully self-sufficient, albeit at a low subsistence level. An important feature in the historical evaluation of the city-countryside relationship is security, or the ability of the rural communities to defend themselves against hostile thrusts from nomads or invasion by foreign armies. This is demonstrated by a number of fortified farms (dimtu in Akkadian: Kolinski 2001) built on the steppes of northern Mesopotamia, from the Mittanian to the Middle Assyrian period; and later, from the early 1st millennium onward, by the fortification of small villages all over this area and beyond the Euphrates in the Levant, as defense against Assyrian invaders who – as we know from the inscriptions of their rulers – frequently pillaged rural villages to find provisions for their armies. Summing up, much still remains to be done to understand the economic, social and administrative dynamics of the Mesopotamian rural areas in different historical periods, both in themselves and in relation to the nearby cities, The Near Eastern village was undoubtedly a complex phenomenon. Encapsulating diverse activities and specializations, it also comprised varying social components; moreover, it could interact in various ways with different political-economic agents (the royal palace above all, but sometimes the temple as well). It is however probable that, for most of its history – when it had not yet been transformed into a qua
148
Cylinder seal with ploughing scene
The seal was originally larger in size, with decoration appearing on two overlapping registers. Of the upper register only a small band remains, showing human feet (one figure was probably seated), animal hooves and a floral element. This may have been an offering scene. The lower register is decorated with a scene of farming with a seeder plough. In the figurative setting there is also an armed person, resting on a stick held in his left hand.

Cylinder seal with beer drinkers
On the seal there are two registers, separated by two horizontal lines. On both registers, pairs of men seated on stools and facing each other at the sides of a large jar, most likely containing beer, are drinking from it with long straws. The scene closes with a floral motif.

Signs Before the Alphabet
– who not only hunted them but also referred to them as comparison for their own strength and aggressiveness – and thus must have been relatively abundant on the steppes, such as the Jezirah of northern Mesopotamia. Other Near Eastern bovids, present at least in the piedmont or plateau environment, were the bison (bison bison caucasicus), which probably give rise to the hybrid figure of the man-bison in art. The written texts mention animals that were ‘fattened on grass’ (cap. 13) or ‘on barley’ (cap. 145). In the figurative arts we find various scenes linked to this practice, for example, domestic animals led by men in processions and probably going to be sacrificed (cap. 154), or milking and cheese-making activities, usually linked to the myth of Etana (cap.73, cap. 74). Scenes of this kind were probably related to concepts of fertility and prosperity. The subject matter of the seals shows how a population closely bound to agricultural and pastoral activities as its source of sustenance, based its favorite legends on heroes intent on taming wild animals and protecting domestic ones. The motif of the contest between animals and between men and animals, as we have seen, has remote origins in Mesopotamian seals. The earliest cylinder seals, especially those of the Jemdet Nasr period, also depict scenes of work and craftsmanship, such as the production of pottery by women (cap. 49). Since making terracotta vases (cap. 155, cap. 181) did not require highly sophisticated tools or real workshops, this activity was probably carried out in houses or courtyards. Obviously, making stone vases (cap. 156, cap. 159) instead called for special utensils and more complex procedures, which likely took place in palaces or temples. Another very familiar subject in Mesopotamian is the banqueting scene, showing persons intent on eating or drinking from vases and cups (cap. 157, cap. 158, cap. 161) or using straws to drink from large jars that must have contained beer (cap. 149). The pre
Utilizing domestic animals for their sub-products – leather, wool, milk and perhaps bones – thus represented a fundamental change, with reflections on eating habits when milk began to be consumed and used to make such dairy products as butter, cheese and yoghurt. Another related innovation, dating from Neolithic times, is the use of cattle as work force, that is, for plowing the fields yoked to a cart – and as local means of transporting foodstuffs; donkeys were also used for the latter purpose. Raising small animals (goats and sheep, which in Akkadian are called by the same word, senu) was more common, since they were better suited to the semi-arid conditions and markedly seasonal rainfall typical of the entire Near East, and to the alternation of valleys or plains with hilly areas. Goats and sheep, both belonging to the Ungulate family and, individually, subject to a wide range of fertile hybridization (although not to cross-breeding, perhaps for anatomical reasons) could graze in the same ecological zones, although sheep favor level fields with low-rising vegetation while goats prefer the steep hills that are safer from predators. Both of these species, however, although feeding mainly on grass in pastures, can also consume leafy vegetation, such as undergrowth in the forests (Gilbert 2002). Bovids, on the contrary, had to be raised near permanent sources of water, since they drink more than sheep and goats; and they could be kept near the village or city where they were stabled, allowing better overall control (of the number of head born/dead each year, of feeding, butchering, etc.) as compared to sheep and goats. Domesticated bovids were crucially important economically in every period of Mesopotamian history, for two different reasons: they pulled the plough, and they provided meat for precious offerings to the temples and the gods. Although definitively extinct four centuries ago, the wild bulls (bos primigenius, in Akkadian rimu) were frequently mentioned by Mesopotamian kings

216



ture, animals shared with human beings such attributes as youth, old age and mortality; pride, anger, aggression, sorrow and fear; domesticity, discipline and vulnerability. Some animals became symbolic of, or synonymous with, human qualities; among these were the lion and the bull, which denoted courage and aggression, and the bat, symbol of a fluttering shiver of fear. Like human beings, animals could be either indigenous or foreigners, they could have personal traits and emotions, families and even their own names (cap. 146). As property, animals could be brought to life or killed, could be bought and sold. They could bring wealth, prestige, protection or healing. The animals were protected by the gods but owed them no service, unlike men. Each individual animal was representative of its own group, in contrast to the human being’s specific individuality. Accordingly, the ‘serpent’ is indicated in magic spells as a genus entirely hostile to man:
« It (the serpent) came in by a crevice, It went out by a drain. It struck the "gazelle" (human infant?) while it slept, It secreted itself in a withered oak. The serpent lurks in a beam, The serpent lurks in the wool. »
In a sharp-witted dispute between the Bird and the Fish, the latter is quick to describe the annoying behavior and sounds of birds:
cise meaning of this ceremony is still obscure, although it almost certainly represented a communal gathering of political nature. Sharing food and drink with other individuals of the same social class served to reinforce existing bonds. It cannot be ruled out, however, that this scene may have represented the banquet held during funeral ceremonies, the kispum ritual mentioned in texts from the second millennium BC.
Wild and domestic animals
The texts written in cuneiform show various reactions to the presence of animals as well as the influence of animal life on human experience and in the collective imagination. The literary texts, in both Sumerian and Akkadian, sharply distinguish animals from human beings, demons, and deities. In the lexical texts, long lists of names and typologies of animals were compiled. In divination, which represented an important scientific endeavor in Mesopotamia, the physical characteristics of animals and their behavior were subjected to observation. An omen series (Shumma alu) was based on encounters between man and various animals, ranging from the most common ones (birds, cattle, sheep and goats) to reptiles (lizards, geckos), mongooses, mice, shrewmice, insects of various kinds, worms, crickets, grasshoppers, and chameleons, with either favorable or unfavorable outcomes (Freedman 1998). In expressive and commemorative litera

Stone bas-relief with banqueting scene
Decorative plaque in soft stone. The central panel, bordered by rosettes, is divided into two larger registers, separated by a narrower register with two bulls converging toward the centre. In the upper register there are two seated figures holding a goblet (for one of the figures, only the hands remain), flanked by two assistants (the one on the far left holds a standard, the one on the right a sword). On the lower register two winged sphinxes confront each other, flanked on the right by a lion-demon (ugallu) brandishing a mace and a dagger, and by a deity wearing a horned tiara, who also holds a raised dagger in the act of attacking.

Common man. Life, prosperity, and health

Signs Before the Alphabet
of artistic representations. One of man’s first requisites, in fact, was that of exactly depicting what he saw around him, things whose beauty surprised him and filled him with awe. Moreover, through hunting and fishing first and domestication later, animals provided one of the main sources of food for ancient man, and as such were the most suitable repertoire for a rural population. Scenes of hunting, probably having magical and propitiatory significance to ensure the success of the undertaking, were always especially prevalent in figurative art. As concerns the figurative representations of animals, it is fundamental to distinguish between two levels of semantic attribution. The first level reflects the real world, that is, an animal means exactly what it seems to mean. But man also found symbolic and allegorical significance in the animal world. Animals, for instance, could serve as symbols of a deity or represent its specific aspects. The attributes of a god could appear beside it or could take its place, as an entirely zoomorphic manifestation. Thus, in Mesopotamian art, the deities were sometimes replaced by their symbolic animals (for instance, the dog for the goddess of medicine Gula). Animals were also represented for their intrinsic characteristics, such as strength, fierceness, speed or dignity. In the first millennium BC, for instance, the scorpion – which in Mesopotamia was associated with fertility, but also with an apotropaic function, to ward off evil – represented the queen, while the lion confronted by the Assyrian king in the characteristic stamp seals (or in hunting scenes on Assyrian bas-reliefs) was the valiant enemy fated to succumb. At times, the bodies of one or more animals and the human body were blended together, to form a hybrid being, possessing both anthropomorphic and zoomorphic features. The motif of animals in heraldic confrontation appears frequently in ancient art (cap. 162). In the field of art, a composition formed of two figures confronting each other forms what is commonly known as a heraldic motif. From a purely geometric viewpoint, this kind of representation obeys the laws of the simplest form of symmetry, termed bilateral mirror symmetry. In Mesopotamian seals
« Bird, you know no shame; you fill the courtyard with your droppings, The sweeper-boy, who cleans the courtyard, chases you with ropes. By your call the household is disturbed; they flee from your din! ... With your ugly screech you disturb the night; no one sleeps well »
But the Bird’s answer is no less venomous, ridiculing the physique and smell of the Fish:
« Your mouth is a heap; though your snout goes around in a circle, you can't see behind you. Your hips are amputated, not to mention your arms, hands, and feet— try to tell your neck from your feet! Your stench is revolting, you make people gag, they wrinkle up their noses at you! No trough would hold the kind of slops you eat, And he who has carried you hates to let his hand touch his skin! »
Domestic animals or those in some way connected to man (such as dogs and pigs), but also wild animals, are often made anthropomorphous in Mesopotamian literary compositions as symbols or examples of human conduct. The following Sumerian ‘fable’, with the fox appearing as the exemplification of shrewdness, is a forerunner of the Greek and Latin compositions of Aesop and Phaedrus, and even recalls the eighteenth-century work of La Fontaine, although without conveying any moral:
« Once there were nine wolves, but ten sheep, one too many to divide among them. When the fox came upon them, he said, "Let me divide the shares. For you, being nine, there is one. I, being one, will take nine. That is my preferred share”. »
Since prehistoric times, animals have always been a favorite subject

220



have been as widespread as their representations in art (cap. 160) and in literature would make it seem. Lions were also considered an appropriate decoration for the heads of ceremonial maces (cap. 170). The dedications inscribed on some of them clearly show that the lion was not the emblem of any particular god, but indicated the warlike nature latent in the character of most of the gods. The lion was also a symbol closely linked to regal power. Among the animals most frequently represented, the ungulates predominated. Their images can sometimes be identified as goats, rams (cap. 166), antelopes, gazelles (cap. 50), deer, oryxes, mouflons or ibexes, based on the shape of their horns, but is often hard to determine the species with certainty (the animal depicted in cap. 182, for example, could be either a mouflon or an ibex) due both to stylistic differences (in some periods, animals are highly stylized; see for ex. cap. 173, cap. 172) and to the minute size of the seals. In the Iron Age, horses appear as well (cap. 180). Less frequent are representations of wild boars, which lived in the marshes of southern Mesopotamia. The bull was the original victim of the lion in the conflict between wild and domestic animals. At first, it is the bull that the hero is called upon to protect. Later, in contest scenes, it also became an enemy. The bull is commonly found in all periods. It appears not only on cylinder seals but also on stamp seals, which in the Jemdet Nasr period are frequently shaped in the form of this animal; and in other artistic expressions, such as stone vases (cap. 183), amulets (cap. 163; in this case the animal depicted is probably a calf) and objects made of ivory (cap. 171). The creature commonly indicated in studies on seals as a bull with a human head is more probably a bison from the Zagros mountains. For a brief time during the Akkadian period, bisons were represented very realistically, making them easy to distinguish. Zebus instead appear only very rarely. A seal from the Achaemenid period (cap. 83) depicts a zebu advancing to the right. Note the exceptional skill of the seal-maker, who renders the musculature and other anatomical details with consummate ability. For its refined incision and spa
the iconographical motif of two animals in heraldic confrontation sometimes displays a central figure, which could be the so-called “ lord of animals” (cap. 150) or a winged genius (cap. 151), or at other times the sacred tree (Giovino 2007) occupying the centre of the scene. In these cases, the central figure or the tree served as axis of symmetry. The scene with animals appearing on either side of a central plant was widespread during later periods as well, appearing in Persian, Muslim and Byzantine art; in the latter periods, this motif, having lost its original religious significance, was based on the concepts of symmetry, abstraction and decorative preciousness that are its salient artistic features. Felines began to appear very early on Mesopotamian seals (cap. 164). They usually symbolized the dangerous, threatening aspect of wild nature, while horned animals evoked the peaceful side of nature, domestication and fertility. The motif of the lion attacking a domestic animal appears very frequently in the Late Uruk period (cap. 48), showing how this animal was seen by the ancient stock-breeders as a serious threat to their herds. Note, however, that struggles between animals alone without the involvement of human beings also held intimations to Mesopotamian astronomical concepts. The predators most often appearing in contest scenes are undoubtedly lions, which represent the chief adversary of the hero (or the bull-man) and a constant threat to domestic animals in every period. From the end of the Early Dynastic II period, their heads began to be represented as if seen from above (cap. 53, cap. 76, cap. 54), while starting from the Akkadian II period, they are always shown in profile with gaping jaws, in a threatening attitude (cap. 152). In the Akkadian period (ca. 2350-2200 BC), animals were represented with extreme scientific precision and remarkable naturalistic attention to anatomical details such as musculature. More rarely found are leopards (cap. 182), which appear in contest scenes only for a limited time, more precisely in the Early Dynastic II period (cap. 153). Lions and leopards became extinct in the Near East only about a hundred years ago. However, they do not seem to

the animals in their most typical attitudes, using stereotypes, the artists attempted to reduce the various components of the world to single concepts. This was most likely a subconscious phenomenon. Lastly, it should be noted that seals have often provided the only proof of the existence of a particular faunal species. As previously mentioned, no other means offers such a rich, diversified source of images as seals. Even when their significance remains largely enigmatical, the scenes are dominated by representations of animals. In spite of the difficulty of precisely identifying certain species, the attention to detail and to anatomical features characteristic of certain periods makes it very tempting to utilize these data to enrich our knowledge of ancient Mesopotamian fauna.
Life expectancy. Human health and healing
« Life is connected to death, death is connected to life. Man does not live forever; the days of his life are numbered. Since terrible diseases plague humanity, if man were immortal would it not be torture for him? » (Hittite prayer of Kantuzzili, 15th cent. BC, from Singer – Hoffner 2002, 32) It is recognized today (Fales 2016) that our knowledge of the health of the peoples of ancient Mesopotamia is still far from any precise historical-scientific analysis. Only the latest results of palaeo-anthropological analysis, and specifically palaeo-pathological examination of the skeletons found in archaeological excavations, could change this picture in the near future. It is certain, however, that the enjoyment of a long life, healthy in body and mind was an ideal in Mesopotamian culture; to such a point that the priestess Adda-guppi, mother of King Nabonidus of Babylonia, who reached the venerable age of 104 between the middle of the 7th and the middle of the 6th century BC, proudly declared:
« My eyesight was good, my hearing excellent, my hands and feet were sound, my words well chosen, food and drink agreed with me, my health was fine and my mind happy. »
cious background, this image is reminiscent of Greek gemstones from the 5th century BC. Birds, including wading birds (cap. 167, cap. 175) and raptors (cap. 168, cap. 165), are naturally linked to the celestial sphere, while snakes (cap. 182) – appearing from time to time in every period refer to the chthonic world of subterranean waters but also to the idea of danger, death and the cycle of rebirth. Many scholars have noted a funerary aspect in the representation of some animals, especially serpents and raptors. Fish, often associated with the god of the subterranean waters Ea/Enki, are used as a filler motif and, mainly on the Jemdet Nasr seals (cap. 51) and those of the Mittanic Period, as designs in themselves. As for the arachnids, scorpions – which are closely related to the erotic and procreational sphere – were popular in most periods, especially in Syria (cap. 176), while spiders are found only on Jemdet Nasr seals (cap. 174), where they are sometimes associated with the so-called ‘pigtailed ladies’ and may represent Uttu, the goddess of weaving in Sumerian mythology. Insects, such as the flies serving as filler elements on Isin-Larsa seals, are only rarely represented. Other animals appear less frequently and sometimes their interpretation, considering the scarcity of the available material, remains dubious. This is the case of monkeys (cap. 120), which were imported to the Near East in various periods and became a symbol of prestige in the 3rd and early 2nd millennium BC. A fossil tooth of an animal carved in the shape of a bear, resembling other examples found in the Middle Euphrates city of Mari (the modern Tell Hariri), dates from the time of the shakkanakkus (second half of the 3rd millennium BC), while another figurine of a seated bear (cap. 169) comes from Tell Brak, in the Upper Habur region, and is datable to the 4th millennium BC. Except for Assyrian art, the representation of animals in the various artistic forms is never of narrative nature. The artists did not attempt to depict animals for decorative or naturalistic purposes. On the contrary, they chose some species which they represented with special care and attention, emphasizing, for example, the horns of bulls, goats and deer, the open jaws of lions (to suggest their terrifying roar) and the connection of reptiles to the earth. By portraying

226



ate seeds, members of the pea/bean family, cucumbers and melons, green and root vegetables, along with a great variety of spices (cumin, coriander, fenugreek, etc.). In contrast to the overall good quality of the food, both in itself and in relation to health, the ancient Mesopotamian peoples were exposed to a broad range of potential health hazards. These risks kept the infant mortality rate high, both at birth and in early childhood (cap. 188, cap. 185) and the life expectancy of an adult must have been very short by modern standards. Disease was often recorded in texts dealing with daily life. The possible causes were attributed to nutritional deficiencies, inadequate food preservation, or hazards from water, parasites, animals (e.g. scorpions, snakes, or rabid dogs), and lastly, infections contracted by chance. For instance, tetanus (shashshatu) must have been widespread due to the custom of walking barefoot in an environment abounding in tetanus spores. A definition of this disease is found in a Late Babylonian medical text from Uruk:
« Shashshatu afflicts that one’s limbs like a snake whose head a man has beaten. He (=the sick person) does not drink good bread, he does not drink good water, he does not sleep good sleep. He spends every day in woe. He cannot turn his limbs; they (feel as if they) had been cut apart the way a lying rush bends it neck. »
Lastly, an Assyrian incantation (derived from an original from the preceding millennium) describes the mythological ‘genesis’ of the ergot – the dangerous fungus named Claviceps purpurea – within the whole cycle of the creation of nature and agriculture:
« Incantation. In the beginning, before creation (itself), the rural work-song (alala) came down to the land. The seeder-plow bore the seed furrow, the furrow bore the germ, the germ the rootstock, the root-stock the node, the node the ear, the ear the ergot. The Sun-God reaped (and) the Moon-God gleaned; when the Sun-God was reaping (and) the Moon-God was gleaning, the ergot entered into the eye of the man. Stand by, Sun-God and MoonGod, so that the ergot may come out! »
A specific connection between nutrition, regular diet and good health was also recognized (as in the modern saying, ‘an apple a day keeps the doctor away’). This can be seen in the following letter from the exorcist Adad-šumu-us.ur to the Assyrian King Esarhaddon (680-669 BC), who was in poor health – or perhaps merely a hypochondriac:
« Eating of bread and drinking of wine will soon remove the illness of the king. Good advice is to be heeded: restlessness, not eating and not drinking disturbs the mind and adds to illness. In this matter the king should listen to his servant. »
Conversely, in a psalm by Ashurbanipal I of Assyria (1070-1032 BC) dedicated to the goddess Ishtar of Nineveh, a description of the sovereign’s many disorders includes the inability to appreciate food, accompanied by music:
« I do not go near the meal I am to eat, [ ] / Beer, the support of life, has become disgusting to me, / I loathe the sennet and sounding of strings, which are kingship’s due, / Thus am I deprived of the joys of living! »
However, looking beyond the rulers’ attitudes toward nourishment and health (as are reflected in figurative scenes, see for example cap. 161), we find an extremely rich and varied taxonomy of nutritional substances emerging from the texts and from bone deposits found in archaeological excavations. The nutritional panorama of ancient Mesopotamia includes cereals (especially barley but also wheat, spelt and wild species used to make all kinds of bread) as well as beer, animal products (milk and butter from goats and sheep, honey from bees), meat from butchered animals (again, sheep and goats, with the every-present mutton) and from bovids, wild and domesticated swine, poultry, ducks, geese, gazelles, birds, fish and shellfish. Plant residues found on archaeological sites indicate the presence of chickpeas, lentils, fava beans, apricots, dates (also employed for beer), figs, grapes, olives, walnuts, sesame seeds (used to make oil), vetches, acorns, onions and garlic, capers, pomegran

Common man. Life, prosperity, and health

Signs Before the Alphabet
institutionally ‘religious’ ones, as instead would become the case for later civilizations, beginning with the Graeco-Roman age. Many of the chief gods in the vast Sumero-Akkadian pantheon, as well as personal deities linked to the patient, his family and his ancestors, represented ‘hands’, and thus were identified as responsible for physically ‘touching’ (or at times ‘seizing’, even ‘striking’) the body and thus causing physical and mental affliction. This action was due to the god’s displeasure or anger with the patient, whether he had violated a taboo or transgressed the social order, even unwittingly.
« If he was wounded on the skull and (consequently) his ears do not hear – “hand” of Ishtar. »
Other agents of disease – possibly belonging to a lower rank in the ‘hierarchy’ of the supernatural – consisted of a wide array of malevolent demons, active especially at nighttime, interfering in the mechanisms of sleep, along with ghosts and spirits of the dead. The demons, who – unlike the gods – were uninterested in receiving worship or prayers, could be countered only by powerful activities of magical nature. Apotropaic practices, exorcism, and transferring the evil elsewhere, at times through the friendly intercession of a propritiated god, were employed alternatively to ward off, or if possible overcome, these influences. Such activities were carried out by exorcists, priests and physicians. (cap. 187).
« If his temple afflicts him so that he continually cries out, his temporal blood vessels (feel like they) are pulsating greatly, and the seam of his head (feels like it) is open – “hand” of a ghost. He (=the patient) will die »
Most of the information on the Mesopotamian approach to health comes from a vast repertory of medical texts handed down to us from the Assyro-Babylonian civilization. Indisputably, these texts are highly useful for perceiving how poor health, disease and suffering were understood in Mesopotamia, as well as the methods adopted to survive or coexist with these problems. At the basis of this repertory, accumulated over the centuries (and known to us mainly from records from the 1st millennium BC), we find a ‘personalistic’ approach to the causes (aetiology) of diseases. They were seen as provoked by the active, purposeful intervention of one or more agents against the patient – who was thus seen as a victim – with little or no room for accident or chance in the process of contracting an illness. The signs and symptoms of many and diverse afflictions were referred back to specific agents (‘hands’) originating from outside the person and physically entering in contact with his body, albeit invisibly. Now, of course, this external etiological sphere for the attribution of observable symptoms and its outcomes pertains to what we (on the basis of our Western cultural heritage) term the nonhuman, the supernatural or the metaphysical; however, it must be firmly underscored that the Mesopotamians viewed it as a fully constituent part of reality. This broad domain was ‘empirically’ perceived as swarming with divinities, demons, ghosts and spirits, divided into complex groups by rank and function, although free to operate chaotically or at random on human health. With these premises, obviously, any method for countering the ‘hands’, from the use of pharmacological substances to magical practices, was employed by the healers specialized in different fields. And in the end, no rift was considered to exist between ‘magical’ activities and
172

dums, describing medical substances and their preparation, and (3) the three ‘manuals’ of the šhikinšhu series, constructed according to the explanatory scheme ‘(the element x) – its appearance (is like the element y)’, which correspond to the lapidaries, herbariums and bestiaries of later traditions. Included in the latter group is the abnu shikinshu series, a ‘lapidary’ that lists the names of stones based on their apparent resemblance to other natural elements (for example, ‘the stone whose appearance is like a carp in a river – alabaster is its name’), with sub-specifications in color, design, and texture. The medical importance of these stones often is barely hinted at (as ‘for childbirth’) or remains implicit. Lastly, a third category consists of lexical lists, usually bilingual (in Sumerian with Akkadian beside it) of names of body parts and names of diseases.
Pharmacopeia
The materia medica of the Mesopotamian healing specialists was consistent with worldwide folk tradition of natural medicine, based on centuries-long practice in the observation, selection and functional processing of specific plant products, animal parts, and mineral elements. In this domain, moreover, a series of fixed choices may be presumed on the basis of the local biological inventory and of Near Eastern environmental conditions, especially as regards the availability of the main component, plants. Mesopotamia however, unlike Graeco-Roman antiquity, has left us no theoretical work or treatise on the manner of extracting and preparing these natural ingredients, or combining them into recipes. What we know of this subject can only be deduced from the multiple descriptions of
Texts. The Mesopotamian medical texts comprise a vast number of documents of different kinds, coming mainly from Assyria and Babylonia in the 1st millennium BC, but with earlier antecedents and branches extending elsewhere (Syria, Anatolia), for a total of several thousand tablets. Accordingly, these texts contain incomparably greater and more varied information on medical knowledge than can be found in Egyptian papyri, despite the fame of the latter. Pre-eminent is the collection of symptomatology for the whole body (titled Sakikkū, ‘symptoms’), which was originally compiled by the erudite Esagil-kīn-apli of Borsippa in southern Mesopotamia around the middle of the 11th century BC. Over the course of time it became a ‘canon’ of 40 tablets with over 3000 entries. Sakikkū – of which two passages are given above by way of example– appears fully in line with the Babylonian tradition of divination, having an observational-deductive nature with an a-b-c pattern: first of all (a) the symptoms shown by the patient are described in detail ‘from head to foot’ in hypothetical form (‘if...’); from them (b) a diagnosis is derived with reference to the ‘hand’ responsible for these symptoms, and lastly (c) a prognosis is offered, in terms of healing or death. A second corpus consists of collections of therapeutical texts (bultu), divided into sub-groups dealing with the different parts of the body, from head to feet. Basically, both of these collections can be classified as texts concerned with initially describing the clinical signs, and deducing from them ulterior consequences in terms of the patient’s healing or death (cap. 178, cap. 179). A second category consists of texts devoted mainly to listing specific therapeutical means, either isolated or with reference to their main usage. These include (1) lexical lists of substances with parallel linguistic information, (2) therapeutic repertories or memoran

Cylinder seal with scorpions

In the Ancient Near East the scorpion could be associated with fertility, but also had the apotropaic function of warding off evil.

the applications themselves – i.e. from the individual case-studies, which could be written out at length, or not, possibly even depending on individual scribal whims. Plants – which also included herbs, trees and bushes, spices, grasses, algae, aromatic species, and even fungi of numerous types were used dry or fresh, and their parts (roots, shoots, stems, leaves, seeds, blossoms, fruits) or even by-products (flour, powder, chips, and ashes) could be ground, sifted, soaked, boiled or grilled. For oral or anal administration, inert carriers or diluents such as water, beer, vinegar, honey, milk were used. Mesopotamian pharmacopoeia also included numerous mineral substances (various types of plaster, bitumen, salts, alum, nitre, verdigris, crushed stones, specific types of mud, etc.) also formed Mesopotamian pharmacopoeia. However, the nature of such mineral ingredients is especially difficult to determine, as is their precise therapeutic use, in relation to the purity, or viceversa to their nature as compounds (e.g. sulphur, arsenic, etc.), and finally to their quality as precious stones or not. Finally, further healing ingredients were represented by animal matter, although it is difficult to assess exactly which and how many substances of animal origin were used for healing purposes. Alongside body parts and fluids/organs (blood, bile) of domesticated animals such as sheep, goat, pig, cow, and donkey, also parts of wild fauna such as birds, fish, reptiles, and amphibians, as well as insects, arachnids, crustacea, and molluscs, with their products, secretions or constituents (honey, eggshells and animal shells, coral, etc.), could have found employment in healing alongside a purely culinary utilization.

memorate war are decidedly numerous. This may be seen starting from the pre-Sargonic period and the images of Naram-Sin of Akkad, up to the hundreds of limestone slabs carved in bas-relief that decorated the Assyrian royal palaces, where the theme of violent conquest predominates with great detail and crude realism. The offers of weapons to the gods (cap. 189) also clearly indicate that warfare was an integral part of a widespread sense of urban and territorial defense, in Assyria and Babylonia (cap. 195, cap. 196) as well as in the bordering regions (cap. 197, cap. 193, cap. 190). This defensive feeling included protection of the local cults from invasion by enemies, and the plea for divine aid in battle (cap. 177). Today, while detailed and in-depth analysis of traditional military history continues (type of weapons, of battles, tactics and strategies, etc.), a new stage of research has begun, focusing on the social, economic and cultural aspects, and most notably on the ideological and religious facets, of war in the ancient Near East. (Vidal 2010; Fales 2011) Documentation of a of a condition of peace, or at least of the hope of peace, appears instead mainly in the written texts. In keeping with a recent approach (Foster 2007), peace in ancient Mesopotamia may be examined from various points of view: (1) as the aim of formal pacts and treaties establishing agreements among different groups and/or individuals; (2) as a result within an individual group, thanks to a good and wise political management; and (3) as the subject of intellectual reflection on the order of the world. Pacts and treaties stipulating peace among individuals or different social groups have come down to us in forty or so examples, from the 3rd to the 1st millennium BC, with differences that reflect chang
All of these were subjected to chopping, crushing, mincing and pulverizing. As for the implements used, a recipe prescribes to grind drugs in a lead mortar (esittu); while for mineral substances, a mortar called ursu, probably made of stone, was required. Preparation was laborious in case of extraction of the active components (e.g. plant alkali). The methods utilized were maceration, decoction and digestion (placing substances in ovens at a temperature of 40°-50° C). The patient received these administrations in liquid form with the use of various carriers – as potions or as enemas – or else in solid form, through pills, suppositories, and various powders. Ointments or creams and liniments, as well as poultices and pastes, required semi-liquid dosage. Ointments were meant to soften or soothe the skin; carriers containing ghee, oil and oil extractions, suet, wax, or animal fat, constituted the base of a special form of ointment for the eyes. Oily carriers were also essential components of liniments to treat muscle complaints through massaging, as well as of creams for external use. Pastes, bearing a higher proportion of solid items, such as powders, were presumably used in dilution with oily components and plant pulps and spread onto strips of wool or other fabrics, for introduction into delicate body parts (ears, nostrils, vagina, or anus). Fumigation was also known. Here the medicinal ingredients were put into a censer or burned over a fire, alone or mixed with oily carriers or plant saps (cap. 184).
