Tablet in Akkadian cuneiform
Although the overall subject is childbirth, the tablet consists of two quite different halves: a text of medical prescriptions for a woman in childbirth afflicted by pain is followed by an incantation against the pain of giving birth and a second one, of mythological nature, to be recited at the moment of birth as a magic aid.
Middle Assyrian Clay 11.5 x 7.6 x 1 cm
Mesopotamian pharmacopoeia, in all its nuances and complexities, is behind this important and rare text, given below in an updated translation; it may be defined as a scholarly compilation on medical matters, that perhaps came from the library of the Assyrian king Tiglath-pileser I (12th century BC). The tablet is a copy of previous or parallel exemplars (as clarified in the text itself on the Obverse, l. 32), and as such, presents some errors of scribal transmission. Although the overall subject is childbirth, this compilation consists of two different halves, and of unequal topic. On the Obverse in its entirety, and up to 1.6 (=ll. l-32) on the Reverse, we have a text of medical prescriptions for a woman in labor (alittum) afflicted with colic.
Obverse
(l )"When a pregnant woman has colic ...., her intestines are blocked, her water and blood turns.... . To bring about her release, you mix together fir-turpentine (kukru), juniper (burashu), ata'ishu-plant, sumlalu-spice, sweet reed, ballukku-spice, myrrh (murru), the odorous plant (?)-bushanu, resin of the abukkatu-plant, resin of the baluhhuplant, the root of the ata'ishu-plant, these 11 substances. Then you will put thorn fire in an earthenware jar, you will place these substances on the fire, she will sit over it, and you will rub her down with a woolen cloth. Then you will mix wormwood (sihu), the conifer resin-argannu, the bariratu-balm, watercress (sahlu), and beer bread (billatu) with pure olive oil and pure beer. You will make a compress and you will insert it into her vagina. You will perform this day and night and she will get better. (14) On the second day you will boil it ...., then take it away (from the fire), you will seat the woman over it, and you will rub her down with a woolen cloth. You will sprinkle beer on bitumen (?), And this bitumen (?) will enter her mouth and nostrils. You will anoint with sweet oil, and she will not have flatulence (sehu) and she will get better. (19) If she does not get better, you will fill a cauldron with water and beer, and you shall put in it together: cedar (erenu), cypress (surmenu), juniper drupacea (dapranu), fir turpentine, juniper-burashu, sumlalu-spice, ata'ishu-plant, wormwood, the conifer resin-argannu, the bariratu-balm, mustard (kasu), licorice seed (zer susi), you will boil these 11 substances, and pass them through a sieve. You will sponge her down, (then) get (the woman) up and anoint her with oil. You will bring her to an infirmary (? bit pagri) and she will have flatulence, but it will dissolve.
Reverse
(27) These are all the substances. You will mix the the bariratu-balm with fresh beer bread, you shall place (all) in a jar, you will shut it in the oven, then you will take it out and spread it on her with a woolen cloth. You will bandage her hypogastric regions (emsu) and her buttocks (suhhu) and her kidneys (rapa/tu) and she will be healthy again. (32) This tablet is all copied, but it is not (yet) finished.”
Instead, the rest of the Reverse has a spell for the pains of childbirth (= ll. 33-50), also with very effective images of the physical state and behaviour of a woman in the grip of severe pain (e.g., who no longer wears a veil, common for married women in this period): the mercy of Marduk himself is invoked. Then there is a second spell with a mythological slant: the story of the moon god Sin - whose symbol was the bull (cf. ch. 6b) - and a cow, their mating and the subsequent pregnancy. This myth, known from contemporary copies found at Assur, Nineveh, and in Anatolia, was recited at the time of the birth itself as a magical support for birth (= ll. 51-62).
(33) “The woman who gives birth has pain at delivery; at delivery she has pain, the child is stuck fast, the child is stuck fast: the bolt is fastened to end a life. The door is firmly closed, not letting the baby out...The mother is enveloped in the dust of death: like a chariot, she is enveloped in the dust of battle, like a plough, she is enveloped in dust of the w.oods
(40) Like a warrior in battle, she is cast down in her own blood. Her eyes are diminished, she cannot see; her lips are covered, she cannot open them. The signs of death and destinies (si-mat mu-te u si-ma-ta) become strong; her eyes, and her ... lives in constant fear. Her ears no longer hear, her breast is not longer high (?), her curls are scattered. She no longer wears a veil, she is no longer ashamed. O merciful Marduk, be present (?) and ...: 'This is the time of the battle: I am surrounded, join me!'. Bring forth what is sealed inside, creature of the gods, although a creature of man! That he can come out, that he may see the light!”

Poverty and wealth
The rich man and the pauper are ‘classic’ figures in the Middle Eastern human panorama, as we know also from their later appearance in the events narrated in the New Testament (as in the parable of
and private violence can no longer thrive. Gudea, Prince of Lagash, boasts of this perfect state of things at the time when a temple to the god Ningirsu is erected by the people:
« The ruler issued orders to the city, as to one single person, making the land of Lagash of one accord as if they were sons of the same mother. They took tools in hand, they weeded shrubs, raked the cut weeds together. (Gudea) barred (unnecessary) words and he sent crime back to its “home”. He undid the “tongue” of the whip and the goad and (instead) he laid the wool of ewes in the hands (of the overseers). No mother would have words with her child, and no child would disobey its mother. No master would hit (his) slave on the head when he had blundered, and no mistress would slap the face of a handmaid who had misbehaved towards her » (Edzard 1997: 76-77)
Lastly, a third concept regards peace as an element of the cosmos. This aspect, of a general cosmic order, which included the serene personal lives of the population, regularity in the conduction of assigned trades, fertility in both human beings and animals, constant respectful attention to the deities and their cults, is frequently expressed in precepts of wisdom to the good and just sovereign, or by paternal figures to their children. It also emerges, however, in quite spectacular and contrary manner, in the epic poem on the feats of the god Erra, composed in Babylonian, perhaps in the 1st millennium BC. This deity is described in the poem as ceaselessly engaged in creating and fomenting disorder, chaos and violence on every level of social life, with no respect for people or things. parched have been anointed with oil, the needy have been covered with garments » (Fales – Lanfranchi 1992: 95)
For the people of Mesopotamia, poverty, obviously linked to annual weather conditions, decisive for the success or failure of the crops, was a punishment sent by the gods. This is clearly shown by several other texts issued by kings (Lion 1998) invoking divine clemency against poverty. On the other hand, since the late 3rd millennium BC the rulers had been attentive to the problem of ongoing impoverishment of relatively large sectors of the population (caused by famine, indebtedness, but also by the human losses deriving from war and disease, which left many families in dire poverty). This gave rise, especially in the Old Babylonian period, to the idea of the king as guarantor of justice and restorer of equity (shar kittim u misharim). The concept of justice was concretely expressed in provisions for amnesty, especially the cancellation of debts (except for mercantile ones), which caused the freedom for debtors who had been reduced to bondage, and the revision of sales contracts, with goods returned to their former owners on occasion. These measures taken by the sovereigns to improve the lives of the people as compared to the past were expected to last for all time, as Hammurabi explains in the epilogue to his Law Code: In the future, forever, let every King who shall exist in the country respect the words of justice that I have inscribed on my stele. Let him make no changes in the judgments that I have rendered in the country, nor the verdicts of the country that I have issued. (Charpin 2004: 310) The ‘poor’ have left many traces in Mesopotamian archaeological records from every period, in sets of pottery of great simplicity or terracotta figurines of plain workmanship (cap. 185, cap. 186, cap. 188). Such traces have received special attention in the last thirty years, when Middle Eastern archaeology began to focus not only on palaces and temples but also on rural villages and even nomadic encampments. The traces left by the ‘wealthy’, although fewer in number, are always clearly evident,
Lazarus the beggar and Epulon the rich man: Luke 16:19-31). A precise and effective portrait of the condition of poverty appears at the beginning of the Assyrian story known as The Poor Man of Nippur:
« There was a man of Nippur, poor and humble, Gimil-Ninurta was his name, a miserable man. In his city of Nippur wearily he sat. He had no silver, the pride of his people, he possessed no gold, the pride of mankind. His store-room thirsted for pure grain. With craving for bread his liver was oppressed, with craving for meat and beer his face was disfigured. Daily for lack of food he used to lie hungry. He was clad in garments for which he had no change » (Gurney 1956)
The tale of the Poor Man of Nippur continues in a humorous vein as a satire of social custom. Due to a naive act of Gimil-Ninurta, he is punished and robbed by the Mayor of Nippur, a man ostentatiously rich, arrogant and violent. But the poor man will obtain his revenge more than once, and just as violently, by utilizing the weapon – comically well thought out – of successive disguises. Thus, Gimil-Ninurta will appear before the hateful Mayor first pretending to be equally powerful in wealth or nobility – riding in a brandnew wagon lent him by the king – and then disguised as a scholar arriving from the temple of the goddess of medicine of the city Isin and therefore ranking high in professional status. The ‘moral’ of the story is that barriers between social classes exist and are insurmountable. A “leap upward” along the social ladder can be attained only by trickery, or by accepting the benevolence of kings. Exactly this benevolence is stressed by an exorcist at the Assyrian court, Adad-shumu-usur, in a letter heaping praise on the new king, Ashurbanipal (668-626 BC), not only as one blessed by the gods but also for his great works of social assistance:
« Those who were guilty and have been condemned to death, the King my lord has revived; those who had been imprisoned for many years, you have released; those who had been sick for many days, are now well. The hungry have been sated, those who were
194
Arrowheads with inscription
The inscription in Akkadian cuneiform mentions Nabû-mukin-apli, king of the Eighth Dynasty of Babylonia (978-943 BC).
ca. 950 BC Bronze 6.6 x 1.5 cm 8.2 x 1.6 cm
The inscription mentions a king of Babylonia, Nabu-mukin-apli (= 'Nabu established a legitimate heir'), who reigned for 36 years (978-943 B.C.), founding a new dynasty in the southern Mesopotamian region, more precisely the 8th dynasty of Babylon. The origins of Nabu-mukin-apli are obscure; from his titles, 'king of Babylonia' and 'king of totality' (as in the present texts) one may deduce his desire to place himself in the traditional line of kingship over the region.
The reign of Nabu-mukin-apli occurred in the middle of the so-called Babylonian "dark ages" and consequently the ancient sources concerning him are particularly scarce. The ruler is mentioned in the 'Eclectic Chronicle' (a tablet preserved in the British Museum; BM 27859), but without any historical information concerning him. The 'Religious Chronicle' provides most of the details about his kingdom: it appears to have been characterized by strong pressure from the semi-nomadic groups (Arameans), who managed to prevent the celebration of the New Year's Feast in the city of Babylon, and forced the king to establish his residence further to the south. The Akitu festival or New Year's Feast of Marduk and Nabu was interrupted several times and for a period of nine consecutive years because 'the Arameans were belligerent'. Moreover, during his reign, a tribe known as the Kaldu (later called Chaldeans) invaded southern Mesopotamia and settled there.
In light of a situation of strong political instability such as this, it is certainly interesting that the inscription of this Babylonian king is engraved on a specific type of weapons.

The inscription states that the vase belongs to an individual, whose proper name is followed by his patronymic: ‘Belonging to Bel-nadin-apli, son of Napshiri’. Based on palaeographical aspects, the text can be attributed to the late 2nd millennium, although this could be a case of older forms lasting into the 1st millennium.

References to figures in the chapter
Fig. 41 The ‘Fertile Crescent’, the part of the Near East suitable for agriculture and pasture, due to a combination of factors: (a) Mediterranean climate (to the West), (b) rainfall above the annual minimum (to the North and Northeast) and (c ) the influence of the Twin Rivers (to the Southeast).

being found in specific grave goods or in particular residential structures, both private and public. Hence we have, for example, furnishings from royal palaces, with the name of a ruler or high official engraved on them as a ‘signature’ of ownership (cap. 195), or fragmentary ensembles of precious materials, such as ivory (cap. 171), which the Assyrian kings assembled in great numbers in their palaces (and especially at Nimrud, in the 9th-8th centuries). The question has nowadays been raised (Herrmann – Millard 2003; Reade 2004) as to the reasons behind these ancient collections, containing items of widely varying provenance (Egypt, the Levant, Iran). Did they indicate real preferences of aesthetic nature – as was long believed in the case of the finely carved ivories – or did they reflect, more basically, imperialist satisfaction in boundless possessions?

Fragment of decorated helmet
Front part of a bronze conical helmet in Assyrian style, with embossed and engraved decoratio.
Neo-Assyrian or Urartian Bronze 12 x 18 cm
Fragment of the front part of a conical bronze helmet in Assyrianizing style with a partially preserved brim (height 12 cm, width 18 cm.). The brim is made up of protruding horizontal lines or rings, delimiting at the bottom a band 2.3 cm high, occupied by six chiselled figures of bareheaded dignitaries armed with long swords, some of whom are bearded and others not, advancing toward the left.
In the upper zone, the right half of a scene, also chiselled, with larger figurative dimensions is preserved centered on a Tree of Life topped by a winged sun disk. To the right of the tree is the figure of a king with his right hand raised in reverence, followed by two bearded officers armed with swords; based on Assyrian and Assyrianizing parallels from Urartu, it may be assumed that the residual traces of a figure on the left of the tree also represented a king, symmetrical to the visible one.
To the right of the king and his attendants, and partly overhanging, a well-known 'serpent' decoration is partially preserved, with three raised ribbings that rise in parallel and then curve inward and downward. Only one of the three ribbings - the one to the left - has remained intact, ending in the head of an animal, probably a male sheep, with ornate neck fleece (in lines, dashes and dots). The serpents are surrounded by a line of dots made using a tool with a very thin tip.
Scenes of worship of the tree of life are also to be found on some Urartian helmets from Karmir-blur, dated, thanks to the inscriptions they bear, to the reign of Argishti I (780-756 BC ca.) and Sarduri II (764-735 BC ca.). This suggests that the motif must have been widely represented on Assyrian helmets: in point of fact, on the one hand, it constituted a theme that was often recurrent and extremely familiar to Neo-Assyrian figurative repertoire. On the other hand, the Urartian conical helmet was undoubtedly borrowed from the Assyrian world: not only in the shape of the helmet itself, but also in the figurative repertoire, albeit with its own stylistic renderings. The typological development of Assyrian helmets, once only found in depictions on bas-reliefs from the 9th century BC onwards, has now also found confirmation in a series of updated publications of specimens and fragments of archaeological origin. The decorative repertoire does not seem to have been very varied and complex, but limited to a set of stereotyped motifs.
The chronological positioning of the fragment obviously cannot make use of the crucial element which is the shape of the helmet. However, the stylistic aspects and some iconographic details lead to the conclusion that the fragment in question should be placed between the late 9th century and the early 8th century BC. Typologically, the winged sun above the tree of life appears very similar to specimens known from the stele of Ashurnasirpal II and the black obelisk of Shalmaneser III. Little can be gleaned from the clothing save that in the main it also indicates the 9th century, when placed in comparison with some depictions on tiles and ivory plaques found at Nimrud and assigned to this century by M.E.L. Mallowan. Further tending to confirm this dating, are the hairdo, swept back with a clearly oblique line, and the rich ornaments of the swords of all the depicted officials. Finally, the mace held by the king, whose figure on the left of the tree of life is only partially preserved, is also of the type that was used in the 9th century BC.

Common man. Life, prosperity, and health 251






The decipherment of mesopotamian writing






that a queen named Shammu-ramat did indeed rule – and wisely – the state of Assyria on behalf of her son Adad-nirari III in the early 8th cent. BC. The legendary hanging gardens were, however, probably great parks overlooking the Tigris at Nineveh, laid out by her successors in the 7th century BC (Dalley 2013). But all of this information has come to light only very slowly, after centuries of work and study. As will be seen, by a strange twist of fate, the languages written in Mesopotamian cuneiform script were deciphered by explorers and linguists in inverse order to their antiquity. The first languages to be deciphered were the most recent, gradually followed by earlier ones: from Old Persian of the mid-first millennium BC to Babylonian of the previous two millennia, and then further back to Sumerian, the oldest language in the Land of the Twin Rivers (3rd millennium BC). To the contrary, amateurish attempts to decipher texts written in cuneiform – aided only by intuition – ended in resounding failure, as had happened with Egyptian hieroglyphs before Champollion’s decipherment of the Rosetta Stone. Thus, the first cuneiform monument to arrive in Europe (1786), the so-called Michaux stone – from the name of its discoverer, the botanist André Michaux, sent to the Near East by the French Government (fig. 45) – spurred the most fanciful interpretations, based on a presumed ‘army of the sky’. Today we know that this black basalt stele (called kudurru in Babylonian) merely records a father’s gift of farmland to his daughter as her dowry (ca. 11th cent. BC), with fascinating representations of the Mesopotamian gods appearing on the upper part.
Pietro Della Valle
The Roman polygraph Pietro Della Valle (1586-1652) was the first European to journey to Mesopotamia, as extension of a pilgrimage to the Holy Land that lasted twelve years (1614-26). Thanks to his knowledge of Turkish, Arab and Persian, he established contact with the local cultural milieu (fig. 46). He travelled to Babylon, where he admired the alleged Tower of Babel (fig. 47), and then to Persepolis, where he described the monumental inscriptions of the Persian kings ‘in unknown characters’ (that is, cuneiform). The information he collected was widely disseminated thanks to a publication in 4 volumes (The Travels of Pietro Della Valle, the Pilgrim) in epistolary form, which was translated into French, Dutch, German and English. ‘His observations and judgments were biased in many ways by the limited or non-existent knowledge of the time, but in their ardent commitment they laid the bases for the future development of Near Eastern archaeology’ (Invernizzi 2010, 58).
Ancient Persian: Niebuhr, Grotefend, Rawlinson
The German mathematician and geographer Carsten Niebuhr (17331815) took part in a scientific expedition sent by King Frederick V of Denmark to explore Egypt, Arabia and Syria (fig. 48). Departing in
The decipherment of mesopotamian writing
Frederick Mario Fales
After the cuneiform script and the Assyrian-Babylonian language fell definitively into oblivion, the rediscovery of Ancient Mesopotamia by the Western world took a long time, and became the exclusive privilege of the learned classes, especially from the 15th to the 19th century. Although Arab historians and geographers of the Middle Ages had not entirely forgotten the past of the Land of the Twin Rivers, they were little read in the West from the Renaissance onward. The numerous travels in Mesopotamia, thoroughly described by Jewish mystics searching for the tombs of the Patriarchs (such as Benjamin of Tudela, ca. 1168 A.D.), remained restricted to their own religious tradition. For the post-Renaissance Western World, the names of places and individuals were handed down mainly by the Bible and the Greek, Latin and Byzantine historians, in largely legendary terms. This is the case of the Garden of Eden and of the majestic cities of Nineveh and Babel (whose Tower was the subject of much speculation). Outstanding among the persons were Nebuchadnezzar II, who destroyed Jerusalem and deported its inhabitants (as recalled in Verdi’s opera Nabucco) and Semiramis, the queen fabled for her power, cruelty, and the ‘hanging gardens’ of Babylon attributed to her by Herodotus. Today we know
The decipherment of mesopotamian writing
fig. 44 fig. 45
255



Signs Before the Alphabet
In 1835 Henry C. Rawlinson (1810-1895) was stationed in Persia as a representative of the British East India Company to the Shah of Iran (Fig. 51). Fascinated by cuneiform writing, he systematically copied, suspended 70 metres above the ground, the trilingual inscription of Darius I on the rocky wall of Bisutun. Starting from the results of Grotefend’s readings and armed also with information provided by Herodotus on the sequence and names of the Achaemenid rulers, Rawlinson published a first synthesis on Old Persian writing and edited the Bisutun text in 1847. He managed to identify the Persian cuneiform alphabet almost entirely. The decipherment of Old Persian was then used as the basis for understanding the other two languages appearing in the Bisutun inscription: Akkadian (and Babylonian in particular) and Elamite, an agglutinant language whose kinship to others is still uncertain today (Khačikjan 1998).
The discoveries of Layard and Botta in Assyria
The story of the nineteenth-century discovery of the great Assyrian capitals – and of the ensuing birth of historical and archaeological studies on the Ancient Near East – unfolded against a background of keen rivalry between Napoleon’s France and the British Empire for the territorial control of Mesopotamia, as a potential strategic ‘bridge’ to India, controlled by the British. In this context, a first representative of the East India Company, Claudius James Rich, was sent to Baghdad in 1808 to evaluate French relations with the local authorities. As a geographer by profession, Rich extensively explored first Babylon and then Nineveh, drawing the first reliable topographical map of the latter before dying of cholera in 1821 at the age 34. But the presence of Rich was only the start of a long Anglo-French ‘fencing match’ in Mesopotamia which, although conducted in civil terms, was to leave its mark on the following decades. In the first years of the 1840-50 decade, the young French Consul Paul-Emile Botta (Italian by birth and son of the historian Carlo Botta; fig. 52) and the even younger explorer Austen Henry Layard, an Englishman of Huguenot origin with a good cosmopolitan education who had escaped from the dull career of a London barrister, met in Mosul
1761, the expedition was decimated by disease, and Niebuhr remained the only survivor at Bombay, from where he started home journeying through the Near East. He visited Persepolis all alone, drawing the inscriptions that had been reported by Pietro Della Valle, and saw the rock inscription of Bisutun in 1763-64 (Fig. 49). In these texts he identified three different systems of cuneiform writing, all of which were to be read from left to right. Moreover, he suspected that the simplest writing system, using only forty signs – one of them merely indicating a separation between words – was a type of alphabetic script. He managed to reach Constantinople and then Europe only in 1767. Niebuhr’s discoveries served as basis for the work of the German philologist Georg Friedrich Grotefend (1775-1853), who effectively initiated the decipherment of Ancient Persian cuneiform script (fig. 50). After having studied philology at the University of Göttingen, he became Director of the Hannover lyceum in 1821. Grotefend chose two brief inscriptions copied by Niebuhr at Persepolis, which contained numerous sequences of identical signs, presuming that these were texts written by the Achaemenid kings (6th-4th cent. BC). He surmised that the first groups of signs stood for the names of kings, followed by a title, more or less long, and then by genealogical information. He thus was able to identify the sequences ‘NAME1, King, son of NAME2, King’. Through comparison with Greek sources and basing himself on the forms of the names as transcribed in the Iranian sacred texts of the Avesta (6th cent. A.D.), already well known to the philologists of his day, he managed to read the names of two ancient rulers. In addition, he identified a group of recurrent signs as the word for ‘king’, xashayathaiya (archaic form of the modern Persian shah); these signs appeared both isolated and in a stereotyped titulary. ‘Sovereign / King of Kings’. (I) Darius, the great king, King of Kings, ... son of Istaspe (II) Xerxes, the great king, King of Kings, son of Darius, the King. Grotefend’s readings are remarkably similar to those still adopted today for the Old Persian alphabet. But the decipherment of Old Persian took a great leap forward with the reading of the rock inscription of Bisutun, in today’s region of Kermanshah, carved on the face of a high mountain overlooking the royal road of the Achaemenid kings between Ecbatana and Babylon.
fig. 46 fig. 47
256



group of texts written in cuneiform script. his return to the Ottoman capital at Constantinople, the British ambassador Stratford Canning, informed of his journey, hired him as assistant. To Canning, Layard confided his dream of conducting excavations in Assyria. The British diplomat supported his initiatives, which also offered a chance to oppose French claims to Mesopotamia after Botta’s successful archaeological ventures. For Layard too, excavation at Kuyunjik was prohibited, but he tried his luck at a site further south on the Tigris, known as Nimrud. Here Layard, between 1845 and 1847, operating with the crude digging methods then available (that is, by perforating the tell using techniques similar to mining) replicated if not exceeded the success of his French colleague. This site too contained a vast royal city of the Assyrian Empire, abounding not only in monumental architecture and statuary but also in cuneiform inscriptions on stone and clay. Thus, within a very short time, two different Assyrian capitals were brought to light, which both of the excavators believed (mistakenly, as it turned out) to be the Biblical Nineveh. In the same year, 1847, the two teams tried to remove the monuments and other objects they had found, transporting them on barges to the mouth of the Tigris at Basra, where capacious ships brought them by sea to Europe for the glory of the French and English national museums, the Louvre and the British Museum. The ‘competition’ was won by the Louvre by several months, but in Britain the popularity of the discoveries was much greater, with enormous crowds flocking to see the monuments of an ancient empire brought to light by ‘their’ empire, that of Queen Victoria. Moreover, when Paris was caught up in a tumultuous revolution in 1848 and king Louis Philippe had to leave the throne (while his protégé Botta was relegated to the Consulate of Jerusalem), Layard was enjoying a well-merited success and writing Nineveh and Its Remains (1849). This richly illustrated work in two volumes was to become the first archaeological best-seller in history (being published a good 25 years before the Trojanische Altherthümer, Schliemann’s famous book on Troy!). In the meantime, Layard corresponded with Rawlinson, who was eagerly at work on the great mass of written documents coming from Nimrud, while his discoveries began to challenge the traditional British education, based on the literal acceptance of Scriptural teachings. In the meantime, the art historians were hotly disputing the aesthetic values of the sculptures as compared to the marbles from the Parthenon, brought to London in long-ago 1811. Layard returned to Mosul in 1849 with Hormuzd Rassam, a young Iranian Christian (of the ‘Assyrian’ faith) as his assistant. Here he concentrated on the site of Kuyunjik, having finally obtained Ottoman authorisation to excavate it, and brought to light the immense treasures of the ‘Palace without Rival’ of the Assyrian king Sennacherib. At the same time, his workers were making further discoveries at Nimrud, among them the temple of the Assyrian god Ninurta, which contained numerous cuneiform inscriptions. But quarrels with the British Museum over financing and a failed attempt to excavate in Babylonia finally dampened Layard’s enthusiasm for Near Eastern archaeology. In April 1851, at the age of only 34, the discoverer of Assyrian antiquity left Mesopotamia forever, to devote himself to a long, quarrelsome and disjointed political and diplomatic career (fig. 54) that was to bring him in the end to a serene old age in Venice (Fales – Hickey 1984). Fig. 54. In this ironic cartoon, Layard appears in the act of excavating an Assyrian winged bull, which bears the self-satisfied features of ‘John Bull’, the national personification of the British Empire, from the satir
(Fig. 55). Regardless of the policies of their governments they became good friends, sharing the belief that the great hills rising from the plain of Mosul were not of natural origin, but should conceal the remains of the region’s most ancient civilization, there where medieval Jewish travelers had sought the tombs of the Patriarchs. More specifically, the vast hill facing Mosul from the other side of the Tigris, known as Kuyunjik but also called Nuniya by the locals, promised to conceal the remains of the city of Nineveh, celebrated in the Bible and by the Classical authors as vast and powerful. The suspicious attitude of the Ottoman government authorities and of the local Pasha gave no easy promise of exploration. However, Botta attempted to excavate the site, but without results. His workers then told him of another vast mound (tell in Arabic) a few kilometers to the northeast, known as Khorsabad. Here the Frenchman was lucky; starting in 1844 he unearthed an Assyrian citadel filled with palaces and temples, with monumental statues, bas-reliefs in naturalistic and narrative style, and lastly a first

published his own translation – already announced – of the same text. The Society then asked both Hincks and Oppert to participate in this comparison of translations, bidding them to send their own versions of the same text in sealed envelopes. The envelopes were sent by the four scholars to the President of the scientific association, J. Wilson, who officially opened them on May 25, 1857. The four translations were read on the same day by a specially appointed committee, and, since they all agreed on the basic points, the committee declared itself fully satisfied with the results: Akkadian cuneiform script had been deciphered. From this moment on, the activity of reading and interpreting Assyrian and Babylonian texts that are the perquisite of the scientific discipline still nowadays called “Assyriology” entered a stage of frenetic activity.
The discovery and decipherment of Sumerian
While working on the tablets from Nineveh, Jules Oppert (fig. 56) realized that certain signs did not represent syllables, but whole words. He suspected that these ideograms had been created to reproduce a non-Semitic language even older than Akkadian. Through this intuition he opened the way to deciphering Sumerian, realizing that it was a monosyllabic agglutinative language with a sign for each noun, adjective or verb, as well as signs for various grammatical elements. Oppert thus laid the bases for understanding Sumerian in its principles of homophony (words pronounced more or less the same, but having different meanings) and polyphony/polysemy (the possibility that the same sign might have entirely different phonetic values and meanings). Subsequently he worked on Elamite, the second native language of Iran inscribed on the rock-face of Bisutun and on the monuments of Persepolis. The archaeological discovery of the Sumerians began in 1877, when E.
ical publication Punch (7 April 1855). The last chapter in the endless Anglo-French rivalry in Mesopotamia took place some years later (1852-55). Among numerous quarrels and recriminations over excavation rights, which also involved the local nomadic population, a new generation of archaeologists (the Consul Victor Place for the French, and Hormuzd Rassam for the British) resumed work, the former at Khorsabad, the latter at Kuyunjik. Under Rassam's direction, there emerged the first proofs that this tell was indeed the site of the one and only Nineveh. A final truce between the two teams led to a joint expedition of archaeological materials transported down the Tigris by a French ship in 1855. But by great misfortune the ship with its precious cargo of statuary, most of it destined to the Louvre (Larsen 1994), sank in the Shatt el-Arab after being attacked by rebellious tribes. After this tragedy, the French never again returned to excavating in Assyria, while the British did so only sparingly up to 1949, the year when excavations using modern methodologies began again at Nimrud under M.E.L. (‘Max’) Mallowan, accompanied by his wife, the writer Agatha Christie (Mallowan 1966).
Deciphering Babylonian cuneiform
In the years following Layard’s discoveries and his important contribution of texts to the British Museum, several scholars were independently trying to decipher Akkadian (or Assyrian-Babylonian) in cuneiform script, by patiently studying the several hundred syllabic signs of which it was made up. Outstanding among these scholars was Henry Rawlinson, a man of action and thought, possessing great linguistic talent and a profound knowledge of the Middle East, who had already deciphered Old Persian. Among the others were the Rev. Edward Hincks (1792-1866), pastor of the town of Killyleagh in Ireland and a brilliant linguist, who guessed that the Black Obelisk discovered by Layard mentioned the tribute to the Assyrians paid by Jehu of Israel, known to us from the Bible; the German (later naturalized Frenchman) Jules Oppert (1825-1905), who explored Assyria and Babylonia as a philologist in the years 1852-54, correctly identifying the ruins of Birs Nimrud with the ancient city of Borsippa; and lastly, the brilliant polygraph W. H. Fox Talbot (1800-77), an amateur but highly gifted linguist, known first for his invention of a method called calotype or talbotype, used to obtain several positive images from a single negative one, and later for his development of techniques that would lead to photogravure. In 1857, it was in fact Talbot who sent a sealed envelope containing his translation of an inscription by the Assyrian King Tiglath-pileser I (1113-1074), discovered by Rassam at Assur to the Royal Asiatic Society in London, requesting that it be opened only when H.C. Rawlinson had
fig. 50
fig. 51
258



knowledge of Sumerian. Finally, the German excavations at Uruk, carried out in the 1910s, although interrupted by the World Wars, yielded the most ancient tablets of all, dating from the end of the fourth millennium BC.
Twentieth-century decipherments: Hittite, Ugaritic, and the language of Ebla
While the most important languages written in cuneiform script had been deciphered by the end of the nineteenth century, the next century was to reserve new surprises of various kinds, pertaining to linguistic typology and to the use of cuneiform script for various purposes. The first case is that of the Hittite language. While the vast site of Boğazköy in central-northern Anatolia – later recognized as the seat of the Hittite capital Hattusa – had been identified since the first decades of the nineteenth century, it was only in 1906, with the start of systematic excavations by Hugo Winckler, that a wealth of cuneiform tablets from the 2nd millennium BC began to emerge. Although the signs on the clay tablets of Boğazköy were easy to read, their interpretation immediately appeared arduous, since the language they expressed differed sharply from Akkadian and Sumerian. And even more important, the philologists of the time were not culturally prepared to accept the idea that they these signs might express an Indo-European language. It was only in 1915 that the Czech philologist Bedrich Hrozný, attracted almost by chance to the texts, applied to them the comparative method used in linguistic studies on Indo-European languages, and began to understand their meaning. His work, first greeted with skepticism in the field of Middle Eastern studies, gradually came to be fully accepted. The new Indo-European language revealed by the Boğazköy tablets was given the name Hittite, based on the mention of a people called Hittim in the Bible and on the ‘land of Hatti’ cited in Assyrian texts, although the Hittites themselves used a term linked to this etymology (hattili) to indicate the language and culture of the indigenous people of Anatolia who had preceded them (“Hattic”). From the very first texts deciphered, the comparative/historical classification
de Sarzec, vice-consul of France at Basra, decided to explore the site of Telloh, the ancient Lagash/Girsu, unearthing tens of thousands of inscribed tablets and other objects. Some of these were sent to the Louvre (for example, many statues in the round, with epigraphs of the local ruler Gudea). In these inscriptions, the epigraphists recognized the non-Semitic language that they had begun to decipher starting from the bilingual texts of Nineveh. But the documents coming from southern Iraq were much older than those known at the time. Dating from the 3rd millennium BC, they were written exclusively in Sumerian. The different stages of this language represented at Telloh allowed to establish the evolution of the signs and their notation. At the close of the nineteenth century, A. Amiaud and E. Schrader produced copies, transcriptions and translations of Sumerian royal inscriptions. In 1905, F. Thureau-Dangin continued this work in his book Les inscriptions de Sumer et d’Akkad. To distinguish the different homophonic signs of Sumerian, he developed a system of numeration for the signs, with progressive numerical indices (for example, du, du2, du3, du4, etc.) that are still used today. Throughout the twentieth century, the increase of archaeological campaigns in southern Iraq added to the number of texts available, allowing linguists to perfect their
The decipherment of mesopotamian writing
fig. 52 fig. 53
fig. 54 fig. 55
259



Signs Before the Alphabet
b, g, d...). One tablet, however, lists the same signs in a different order, which is that of the ancient South Arabian language – demonstrating in this way the vastness and variety of Ugarit’s economic and cultural relationships with the entire Levant, as well as with Mesopotamia. The most recent case concerns the language of Ebla. In 1975 an archaeological expedition from the University of Rome headed by P. Matthiae, which had been operating since 1964 at Tell Mardikh, a vast site in northwestern Syria, unearthed a well-conserved archive of some 20,000 cuneiform tablets (see below). All of these texts (administrative, literary and historical) seemed to have been compiled in Sumerian, perhaps taught to the local people by scribes coming from the South-Mesopotamian city of Kish. More detailed study, however, has shown that they were most likely read and recited in the Semitic language of the place (Eblaic), a distant cousin of ancient Akkadian and its contemporary (cf. Rubio 2006). The publication of a bilingual Sumerian-Eblaic vocabulary, with each term matched word for word, reinforced this complex linguistic scenario of so-called ‘alloglottography’, as e.g. paralleled in documents from the Japanese Nara period (700-800 AD) written in Chinese characters but to be read aloud in Japanese. Other sites in Syria, where Sumerian cuneiform script had been exported during this same period, but which utilized a local Semitic language close to Akkadian for reading, were Tell Beydar, the ancient Nabada, on the Syrian steppe, and Tell Hariri, the ancient Mari on the Middle Euphrates. In practice, as has been said (Rubio 2016) Sumerian was the ‘writing catalyst’ for establishing the most ancient literature of this Semitic cultural horizon in Syria.
Is there anything left to be discovered and deciphered?
With nearly a million tablets coming from every part of the Near East, discovered in over a century and a half of excavations in the field or purchased on the antiquities market, we might be tempted to think that most of the work of deciphering the many languages recorded in cuneiform script is now behind us; and this is quite probable, although unexpected discoveries such as that of Ebla might happen at any moment. Moreover, the museums of different countries are still full of tablets yet to be examined or definitively evaluated, which may have further surprises in store for us, of unforeseeable linguistic and philological impact. In this sense a ‘mixed’ case, of recent date, consists of the tablets in Assyrian cuneiform with epigraphs added in Aramaic alphabetical characters, which had already been brought to light by Layard, who however believed them to be ‘Phoenician’ writings. Subjected to rereading and updated interpretation by the museums that possess them (Fales 1986), these bilingual and bi-grapheme texts have greatly increased in quantity, thanks to archives discovered at various archaeological sites of modern Syria in the last thirty years (Lemaire 2001; Fales et al. 2005; Röllig 2014). In any case, there still remain many gaps in our knowledge of the languages compiled in cuneiform. Especially notable are the cases of Amorite and Kassite. Amorite is a West Semitic language which, at the beginning of the 2nd millennium BC, was certainly spoken by the populations of ancient nomadic origin that assumed political control of Mesopotamia. Their coming to power coincided with their ‘Babylonization’, that is, the adoption of Akkadian as their main vehicle of communication. The language of the Amorites has thus survived in the vast
of Hittite – dating from much earlier than the basic Indo-European languages then known, Greek and Sanskrit was, and to some extent remains, the subject of heated debate. At present, three stages of the language are recognized, dating from around 1600 to 1200 BC: Old Hittite (up to about 1450), Middle Hittite (up to about 1320) and New Hittite (up to 1190 BC, when Hattusa was abandoned and the imperial bureaucracy ceased to function). The texts from the Hittite archives, numbering about 30,000 today, display a wide array of types, comprising historical, diplomatic, administrative, juridical, lexical, literary, and mythological documents as well as cultural and religious materials in general. The new archaeological discoveries that have been made for a century now at Hattusa and other political seats of the Hittite Kingdom (then Empire) have added to the quantity year after year, so that Hittitology has now been recognized in many countries as an autonomous scientific and teaching discipline (de Martino 2010). An equally interesting case – though on a smaller scale – is that of Ugaritic. The site of Ras Shamra, on the Mediterranean coast of today’s Syria, was excavated by French archaeologists starting in 1929 and investigated practically uninterruptedly up to 2010. This site, the seat of a city-state with the ancient name of Ugarit, rich in palaces, temples and private residences built of stone, has yielded thousands of cuneiform tablets dating from the 13th century BC, the last century before the city was violently and totally destroyed by raiders arriving by sea (the so-called Sea Peoples). About half the texts found at Ugarit are written in syllabic Akkadian. Other tablets, however, are unusual in having only thirty or so cuneiform signs, very simple in form and quite different from the Akkadian syllabary (fig. 57). They have been identified since 1930 as alphabetical signs, each of which represents a consonant. These signs refer to a local Western Semitic language, which we call Ugaritic (cf. Bordreul – Pardee 2009). At Ugarit, the juridical tablets and texts of international correspondence were written in Akkadian, while the scribes restricted the use of the alphabet and the Ugaritic language to particular types of documents: accounting and administrative texts, local correspondence, religious texts, and lastly the myths and legends of Ugarit itself, such as poems dedicated to the god Baal. Ugaritic alphabetical writing is read from left to right like the other cuneiform texts, with few exceptions –perhaps to be dated during the city’s final stage of life – of a ductus from right to left. A dozen or so tablets are inscribed with ‘abecedaries’, that is, lists of the signs in a fixed order, coinciding almost entirely with what is found in the Phoenician and Hebrew alphabets of some centuries later, which was inherited by the Greeks from the Phoenicians (ʼ,

number of grammatical elements that may be deduced from the personal names of this period (for example, ‘Hammurabi’ was an Amorite name; cf. Gelb 1958) and to a minimum degree, from some isolated Amorite words emerging here and there in the Babylonian texts (most notably in the correspondence of the royal archives of Mari, ca. 1800 BC), which find parallels in later archives (such as the cuneiform and alphabetical texts from Ugarit). The patient search for these words is still in progress, and in this field the future will undoubtedly yield new knowledge. As for Kassite, the original language of a population coming from the Zagros Mountains that conquered Babylonia in the late 16th cent. BC and that then – as in the case of the Amorites – fully absorbed the culture of their new political seat, where they remained up to the 12th century, its kinship is still uncertain. Cuneiform documentation of Kassite is extremely rare. What survives is only a fragmentary bilingual vocabulary (Kassite-Babylonian), with 48 main entries, some lexical entries handed down in Babylonian scribal texts, and a series of names of persons whose significance is at times obscure (Ancilotti 1980). In this case too, any future discoveries of texts made during archaeological excavations, or resulting from the in-depth study of materials in museums, could provide more knowledge of this language, which remains barely above the ‘dead language’ threshold today.

taken Armanum and Ebla ... the god Nergal opened the road to Naram-Sin, the strong, and gave him Armanum and Ebla: he also offered him also the Amanus, the Cedar Mountain, and the Upper Sea. Thanks to the weapon of the god Dagan, that exalts his kingship, Naram-Sin, the powerful one, conquered Armanum and Ebla, and from the bank of the Euphrates up to Ulisum struck down the populations that Dagan delivered into his hands and they carried the breadbasket of Aba, his god, and he wielded dominion over the Amanus and the Cedar Mountain”. The excavations were expanded in 1974, while keeping other sectors of the excavation open. But it was in 1975, after the discovery of the first cuneiform tablets, that the decision was taken to suspend research activities in all the other areas of excavation in order to concentrate the maximum operational effort on the re-emerging structures of what promised to be a discovery of particular importance, since the walls, protected by the slopes of the Old Syrian Citadel and immune to significant overlays later on, appeared to be more than 7 meters high. The 1975 campaign at Tell Mardikh, which would remain in the history of Near Eastern Archaeology, soon led to the discovery, in early September, on the southern side of the corner tower of the Audience Hall, of the so-called Small Archive L.2712, where various hundreds of tablets with cuneiform script, which had fallen to the floor at the time of the destruction of the city from two shelves inserted into the two adjacent walls of the small room, were brought to light along with the discovery of the Monumental Portal, which clearly appeared as the entrance to the palatial complex. Since the Small Archive, located at the northern end of the east side of the Audience Hall, stood a few meters northwards of that imposing entrance, the excavation strategy imposed on us to advance with the excavation southwards – also for the presence of rare tablets scattered about that did not seem to have come from the newly-discovered archive – in order to verify the possible presence of a second archival room symmetrically located with respect to the first Monumental Portal. In the second half of September, even the most optimistic hopes were surpassed by the identification, actually to the south of the Monumental Portal, of a second room larger than the first one, with outer walls preserved, despite their thinness, to a height of 2 meters, and which was none other than the so-called Great Archive L.2769. After a few days, by deepening the excavation along the northern and eastern walls of the room, the tops of piles of large tablets that looked driven into ground came to the surface, at a remarkable height with respect to the ground level of the already known Audience Hall. The appearance of that which, almost like an unreal mirage, has remained in our memory as a sea of tablets in soil dense with the debris and ashes of the terrible fire that ended the life of the mature High Early Syrian towns, made everyone understand that this was the beginning of an epochal discovery. At the end of an exciting campaign, more than 15,500 inventory numbers of written documents and fragments of documents were the treasure that had been collected in two weeks
The discovery of the Royal Archives of Ebla
Paolo Matthiae
Professor of Archaeology and History of Art of the Ancient Near East in the University of Rome
In the early seventies of the last century, there was the curious certainty among scholars that it would be impossible to write a history of Syria in the third millennium BC - in the sense of the events, but also the culture - for one simple and, at that time, incontrovertible reason: that region, the traditional bridge between Mesopotamia and the Mediterranean, knew no writing in those centuries and so one would never be able to count on original written sources. Exactly in those years Europe's greatest Assyriologist, Wolfram von Soden, became an authoritative spokesman for that widespread opinion: there were no hopes of revealing military and political events, economic and social structures, institutional realities, religious beliefs, and ideological attitudes of those centuries crucial for the final formation of the urban civilization of both Syria and Anatolia, of the western and northern outskirts of the regions of Southern Mesopotamia that, with the creation of the first cities in the second half of the fourth millennium BC, in the same decades as Egypt, had pursued the great phenomenon of the first urbanization in human history. That pessimistic and bleak prediction began to be disproved first by a discovery of 42 cuneiform tablets, made in the summer of 1974 at Tell Mardikh, about 60 km south of Aleppo, by the Italian Archaeological Mission in Syria of the Sapienza University of Rome when it was expanding a survey opened the previous year during the briefest of the excavations at Tell Mardikh, due to the Mission's concurrent involvement on a smaller site in the Euphrates valley, Tell Fray, recognized by UNESCO, so as to participate in the international rescue campaign of archaeological sites threatened by the construction of the large Tabqa Dam on the Euphrates. The survey opened on the slopes of the Acropolis of that vast archaeological site in inner western Syria, practically unknown until the beginning of the Italian excavations, where Sapienza University's Mission had begun to operate back in 1964, allowed us to reach, just a few centimeters below the surface, the summit of what proved to be the high and massive corner tower of the large Audience Hall of the Royal Palace of the Early Syrian town in the 24th century BC, a monumental center of the Administrative District of the large palatial complex. In fact, after the identification in 1968 – following the discovery of the basaltic bust of Ibbit-Lim king of Ebla – of Tell Mardikh with the ancient city of Ebla, mentioned in the triumphal Akkadian inscriptions by Sargon and Naram-Sin of Akkad, in the Sumerian building inscriptions by Gudea of Lagash, in the lists of Egyptian conquests by Thutmis III, and in fragmentary Hittite inscriptions by Hattusili I or Mursili I, the Italian Mission undertook a number of attempts to reach the most ancient levels of the urban settlement that flourished approximately from at least the mid-3rd millennium to the mid-2nd millennium BC. The survey in 1973 unexpectedly led to the discovery of important rooms, in an excellent state of preservation, of that powerful city of Syria, the conquest of which Naram-Sin of Akkad had celebrated as an extraordinary event: “Ever since the foundation of humanity, no king among kings had
262



tion of those tablets could be reconstructed the Syro-Mesopotamiatogether with the structure and, above all, the physical organization of the Great Archives, which must have housed no fewer than 4,000 documents. On the epigraphic level, it was quite clear from the early days of the discovery that what had been found was not the archive of a somewhat large administrative or palatial structure, or temple, as had often happened since the mid-nineteenth century, but rather the Central State Archive of an important kingdom in a crucial age in the history of the Syro-Mesopotamian area, significantly called Proto-Imperial, insofar as it existed right on the threshold of the formation of the Empire of Akkad by the great Sargon. Starting with the earliest years of studies on the unexpected discovery made hundreds of kilometers from the heart of the land of Sumer, it was possible to undertake an initial preliminary reading of all the texts and so to identify the nature of the documents preserved in the Royal Archives of Ebla. Thus it was possible to ascertain that a very large majority of them obviously regarded economic and administrative accounting, for not less than 90% of the total, but that a considerable number, equal to several dozens of tablets, often in excellent condition, were of a lexical nature and that some of these various lists were bilingual, in Sumerian and Eblaic - the oldest bilingual dictionaries of the history of humanity - and, finally, that a few tablets were literary texts, hymns, and above all, incantations. In the international political situation, and particularly, the regional one, which was very difficult at the time, a fiery controversy had raged for several months, especially in the United States of America and to a lesser extent in Europe, concerning alleged and totally false difficulties posed by the cultural authorities of the Syrian Arab Republic regarding the publication of the tablets for alleged and non-existent relationships with the Biblical texts of the Old Testament. These controversies – for a full and transparent explanation of the real situation of the studies on the immense epigraphic treasure of Ebla–, were ended in 1977 by the very authorities in Damascus on the one hand, with the repeated affirmation of full rights to complete publication of the texts of the Royal Archives to be carried out by the Expedition of the Sapienza University of Rome, as the protagonist of the discovery, and on the other, with the appointment of an International Committee of major philologists from different countries worldwide that, as an entity independent from the Expedition, would guarantee the full autonomy and the progressive development of research on the tablets –which would clearly take several decades for a complete and comprehensive scientific publication, as had happened for other major discoveries of cuneiform archives in the twentieth century. While hundreds of articles with partial publication of the texts from the Archive and a growing number of studies on the many diverse historical and linguistic problems arising from the discovery in Ebla have appeared in major international scientific journals, the official series of text editions by the Sapienza University of Rome - “Royal Archives of Ebla. Texts,” alongside the series “Royal Archives of Ebla.
of uninterrupted exhausting recoveries, day and night, so that nothing of what the destroyers of the Royal Palace, after relentless looting, had left under the rubble of the burned complex would be lost. Before returning to Damascus, there were 99 boxes of tablets lined up in the courtyard of the Expeditions's house before embarking on the trip to deliver the spectacular finds to the Archaeological Museum of Aleppo. In 1976, using the same strategy followed in 1975, the concentration of the excavations in the eastern side of the Audience Hall led to both the completion of the excavation of the larger archival room, L.2769, and the vestibule, L.2875, which was also the entrance to the Administrative District, and to the discovery of the Trapezoidal Archive L.2764, located inside the District itself. The discovery of cuneiform tablets in the as yet unexcavated limited southern sector of the Great Archive L. 2769, in the vestibule L.2875 that must have also had a function as a scriptorium, in the Trapezoidal Archives L.2764, and also in the Audience Hall in front of the Monumental Portal, meant that about 1,500 other inventory numbers were added to the extraordinary spoils of these epigraphic discoveries. In the following years, new epigraphic discoveries were sporadically made, in both the Southern District south of the Administrative District, and in some small rooms arranged against the eastern wall of the Throne Room L.2866, which brought the total amount of tablets recovered to around 17,050 inventory numbers. On the basis of the complex and prolonged work of recomposition of the tablets damaged by the destruction and subsequent collapse of the structures of the Audience Hall and the Administrative District, which was continued in the museums of Aleppo and Idlib in all the subsequent campaigns of excavation and restoration until 2010 by the Expedition's philologists and restorers, it has been possible to reconstruct that originally, at the time of the destruction of the Royal Palace G, the tablets in the Royal Archives must have been an indeterminable number, between about 4,000 and 5,000 written documents. At the time of the discovery, the tablets found intact or with very limited peripheral chipping numbered just under 2,000. All the other inventory numbers are related to large fragments, even with many columns and lines of writing, amounting to 5,000/6,000 pieces, while the remaining inventory numbers concern minor fragments or superficial flakes with a few traces of lines of writing. If one considers that the total amount of cuneiform tablets relating to the period of the Akkad Dynasty, immediately following the destruction of the Royal Palace of Ebla and regarding more than 125 years, of very disparate provenance from the many sites in the countries of Sumer and Akkad, is considered to be about 5,000 documents, one may get an idea of the extraordinary importance of the discovery of the Royal Archives of Ebla. The value of the discovery of the Ebla Archives is all the more remarkable, on the archaeological level, because the situation of recovery the tablets in the various rooms was such that, for the first time in the history of the discoveries of n archives in the Syro-Mesopotamian world, the original systems of preserva
The discovery of the Royal Archives of Ebla 263



Signs Before the Alphabet
ries and finally venturing on a complex military operation against the powerful city Mari which controlled access to the countries of Akkad and Sumer. This expedition ended in a major victory after a pitched battle near Terqa, but there was no conquest of the large city on the Euphrates and, a few years later, despite an impressive network of dynastic marriages that placed it at the center of a vast network of diplomatic relations, Ebla fell and suffered a total devastation. presumably in the years of the full affirmation on the political scene of the great Sargon of Akkad, who was most likely, directly or indirectly, the author of that conquest. In those years, Ebla had a very broad extension of political and diplomatic relations, from Afghanistan to Egypt, as evidenced first by the 42 kilos of raw lapis lazuli which came from the remote mountains of Badakshan, discovered among the treasures in the Royal Palace which had escaped looters, and secondly, by the precious vases of alabaster and clear diorite of Egyptian production, two of which bore hieroglyphic titles: one of Chephren, the famous builder of the second pyramid of Giza who lived a little less than two hundred years before Ishar-Damu, and the other of Pepi I, the third Pharaoh of the 6th Dynasty of Egypt, who was certainly a contemporary of Sargon of Akkad and Ishar-Damu of Ebla. These precious gifts sent by the great Pharaohs of the Old Kingdom, even many years before the apogee of Ebla's political power shortly before its eclipse, indicate that even several dozens of years before, the city had been powerful and feared: and further important evidence of the antiquity of Ebla's power, its continuity, and its institutional and political traditions is provided by the presence of a King List of Ebla with more than twenty predecessors of Igrish-Khalab, found among the texts of the Archives. The discovery of the Royal Archives of Ebla, which occurred during the eleventh and twelfth excavation campaigns out of the 47 conducted continuously by the Expedition of Sapienza University of Rome from 1964 to 2010 on the large site in Western Syria, was certainly an event of exceptional archaeological and historical importance that deeply renewed and enriched knowledge of the history of archaic urbanization in the 3rd millennium BC in Western Asia, and created a great impression even beyond scientific circles throughout the world. The potential impact of the discovery of Ebla on the interpretation of centuries that were crucial especially for the economy and society of the ancient East in the 3rd millennium BC was clear to the greatest scholars of those years. Already in 1977, Igor M. Diakonoff, the undisputed Master of Oriental Studies at the Academy of Sciences of the USSR, who had the first studies on the Ebla discoveries immediately translated into Russian, stated that the discovery had brought an extraordinary revolution of knowledge, and in the same months, Ignace J. Gelb, the dean of Assyriological Studies at the Oriental Institute of Chicago, declared that "at Ebla, the Italians had discovered a new language, a new culture, and a new history”.
Previous page A stage in the excavations conducted by Paolo Matthiae.
Studies”- saw the light with the publication of approximately twenty volumes to date, accompanied by an extensive photographic and graphic documentation, in which all the texts were repeatedly collated with the originals. The intense progress of publications of texts and sectoral studies has brought to light, on the one hand, the initially unsuspected wealth of information of all kinds - historical, social, institutional, religious - beyond the purely accounting and economic aspects contained in the administrative documents. On the other hand, the extraordinary lexical treasure, both Semitic and Sumerian, of the bilingual lexical lists – with about 1,500 entries in both languages, often not easy to interpret - opened up broad discussion and a critical evaluation of the historical position of Eblaic within the oldest documented Semitic languages, and also on the reconstruction of the prehistory and history of those Semitic languages in the 3rd millennium BC. The initial pioneering readings, the various publications of texts that had almost always not been collated, and the many historical and linguistic interpretations, often exceedingly bold and sometimes decidely adventurous, by Giovanni Pettinato, the Expedition's first epigraphist, and the powerful and accurate publishing work and study of hundreds of texts by Alfonso Archi, by Maria Giovanna Biga, and, especially for the chancellery texts and the interpretations of the Eblaix language by Pelio Fronzaroli, have made an increasing number of the vast complex of texts of the Royal Archives of Ebla, which is now a sector of primary importance, in all respects, of Sumerology, Assyriiology, and Semitic studies, available to the international scientific community. Although, as mentioned, a large majority of the texts of the Ebla Archives are economic in nature, the numerous detailed studies by the Expedtion's epigraphists have recently brought to light that they also contain a great deal of information concerning political, diplomatic, and military events, religious and institutional ceremonies, the most various episodes of social and economic life from weddings to travel, up to festivals, which allow us to reconstruct in detail not only the sophisticated mechanisms of a centralized administration spread over a wide area of inner western Syria, from the Taurus Mountains in the north to the region of Homs in the south, but also many aspects of the political history of a period less than fifty years during the reigns of three kings of Ebla. Athough the Royal Archives include mostly documents of the last two or three decades, they regard the times of the kings Igrish-Khalab, Irkab-Damu, and of Ishar-Damu, who must have been the last ambitious but unlucky king to reign with the invaluable and highly efficient collaboration of two viziers, Ibrium and Ibbi-Zikir, and who must have witnessed the conquest, the plunder, and the destruction of the city probably in the years immediately following 2350/2340 BC Just before that tragic event, Ishar-Damu, under the guidance of Ibbi-Zikir and with the alliance of Nagar in Upper Mesopotamia and Kish in Lower Mesopotamia, had launched an impressive series of military campaigns, first in the Euphrates region to the north, then to the south in central Syria, conquering ample territo

Bibliography 271



Idols
“Eye idols”, typical of the site of Tell Brak (ancient Nagar, in the Upper Khabur region, in northeastern Syria). These artifacts were found in large quantities in the so-called "Eye Temple" by British archaeologist Max Mallowan. The specimens with holes for the eyes (dated to the Late Uruk period) were perhaps used for weaving. Idols with engraved eyes (of the Middle Uruk period) were probably votive offerings.
